G., Chief of Ordnance.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEXPLANATORY\r\n\r\n\r\nIn this book a number of dialects are used, to wit: the Missouri negro\r\ndialect; the extremest form of the backwoods Southwestern dialect; the\r\nordinary \xe2\x80\x9cPike County\xe2\x80\x9d dialect; and four modified varieties of this\r\nlast. The shadings have not been done in a haphazard fashion, or by\r\nguesswork; but painstakingly, and with the trustworthy guidance and\r\nsupport of personal familiarity with these several forms of speech.\r\n\r\nI make this explanation for the reason that without it many readers\r\nwould suppose that all these characters were trying to talk alike and\r\nnot succeeding.\r\n\r\n   The Author.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nHUCKLEBERRY FINN\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I\r\n\r\n\r\nYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know about me without you have read a book by the name of\r\n_The Adventures of Tom Sawyer;_ but that ain\xe2\x80\x99t no matter. That book was\r\nmade by Mr. Mark Twain, and he told the truth, mainly. There was things\r\nwhich he stretched, but mainly he told the truth. That is nothing. I\r\nnever seen anybody but lied one time or another, without it was Aunt\r\nPolly, or the widow, or maybe Mary. Aunt Polly\xe2\x80\x94Tom\xe2\x80\x99s Aunt Polly, she\r\nis\xe2\x80\x94and Mary, and the Widow Douglas is all told about in that book,\r\nwhich is mostly a true book, with some stretchers, as I said before.\r\n\r\nNow the way that the book winds up is this: Tom and me found the\r\nmoney that the robbers hid in the cave, and it made us rich. We got\r\nsix thousand dollars apiece\xe2\x80\x94all gold. It was an awful sight of money\r\nwhen it was piled up. Well, Judge Thatcher he took it and put it out\r\nat interest, and it fetched us a dollar a day apiece all the year\r\nround\xe2\x80\x94more than a body could tell what to do with. The Widow Douglas\r\nshe took me for her son, and allowed she would sivilize me; but it was\r\nrough living in the house all the time, considering how dismal regular\r\nand decent the widow was in all her ways; and so when I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand\r\nit no longer I lit out. I got into my old rags and my sugar-hogshead\r\nagain, and was free and satisfied. But Tom Sawyer he hunted me up and\r\nsaid he was going to start a band of robbers, and I might join if I\r\nwould go back to the widow and be respectable. So I went back.\r\n\r\nThe widow she cried over me, and called me a poor lost lamb, and she\r\ncalled me a lot of other names, too, but she never meant no harm by it.\r\nShe put me in them new clothes again, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do nothing but\r\nsweat and sweat, and feel all cramped up. Well, then, the old thing\r\ncommenced again. The widow rung a bell for supper, and you had to come\r\nto time. When you got to the table you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go right to eating,\r\nbut you had to wait for the widow to tuck down her head and grumble\r\na little over the victuals, though there warn\xe2\x80\x99t really anything the\r\nmatter with them\xe2\x80\x94that is, nothing only everything was cooked by itself.\r\nIn a barrel of odds and ends it is different; things get mixed up, and\r\nthe juice kind of swaps around, and the things go better.\r\n\r\nAfter supper she got out her book and learned me about Moses and the\r\nBulrushers, and I was in a sweat to find out all about him; but by and\r\nby she let it out that Moses had been dead a considerable long time; so\r\nthen I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care no more about him, because I don\xe2\x80\x99t take no stock in\r\ndead people.\r\n\r\nPretty soon I wanted to smoke, and asked the widow to let me. But she\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. She said it was a mean practice and wasn\xe2\x80\x99t clean, and I must\r\ntry to not do it any more. That is just the way with some people. They\r\nget down on a thing when they don\xe2\x80\x99t know nothing about it. Here she\r\nwas a-bothering about Moses, which was no kin to her, and no use to\r\nanybody, being gone, you see, yet finding a power of fault with me for\r\ndoing a thing that had some good in it. And she took snuff, too; of\r\ncourse that was all right, because she done it herself.\r\n\r\nHer sister, Miss Watson, a tolerable slim old maid, with goggles\r\non, had just come to live with her, and took a set at me now with a\r\nspelling-book. She worked me middling hard for about an hour, and then\r\nthe widow made her ease up. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stood it much longer. Then for\r\nan hour it was deadly dull, and I was fidgety. Miss Watson would say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t put your feet up there, Huckleberry\xe2\x80\x9d; and \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t scrunch up like\r\nthat, Huckleberry\xe2\x80\x94set up straight\xe2\x80\x9d; and pretty soon she would say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t gap and stretch like that, Huckleberry\xe2\x80\x94why don\xe2\x80\x99t you try to\r\nbehave?\xe2\x80\x9d Then she told me all about the bad place, and I said I wished\r\nI was there. She got mad then, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean no harm. All I wanted\r\nwas to go somewheres; all I wanted was a change, I warn\xe2\x80\x99t particular.\r\nShe said it was wicked to say what I said; said she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say it for\r\nthe whole world; _she_ was going to live so as to go to the good place.\r\nWell, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no advantage in going where she was going, so I\r\nmade up my mind I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t try for it. But I never said so, because it\r\nwould only make trouble, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do no good.\r\n\r\nNow she had got a start, and she went on and told me all about the good\r\nplace. She said all a body would have to do there was to go around all\r\nday long with a harp and sing, forever and ever. So I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think much\r\nof it. But I never said so. I asked her if she reckoned Tom Sawyer\r\nwould go there, and she said not by a considerable sight. I was glad\r\nabout that, because I wanted him and me to be together.\r\n\r\nMiss Watson she kept pecking at me, and it got tiresome and lonesome.\r\nBy and by they fetched the niggers in and had prayers, and then\r\neverybody was off to bed. I went up to my room with a piece of candle,\r\nand put it on the table. Then I set down in a chair by the window and\r\ntried to think of something cheerful, but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use. I felt so\r\nlonesome I most wished I was dead. The stars were shining, and the\r\nleaves rustled in the woods ever so mournful; and I heard an owl, away\r\noff, who-whooing about somebody that was dead, and a whippowill and\r\na dog crying about somebody that was going to die; and the wind was\r\ntrying to whisper something to me, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out what it was,\r\nand so it made the cold shivers run over me. Then away out in the woods\r\nI heard that kind of a sound that a ghost makes when it wants to tell\r\nabout something that\xe2\x80\x99s on its mind and can\xe2\x80\x99t make itself understood,\r\nand so can\xe2\x80\x99t rest easy in its grave, and has to go about that way\r\nevery night grieving. I got so downhearted and scared I did wish I had\r\nsome company. Pretty soon a spider went crawling up my shoulder, and I\r\nflipped it off and it lit in the candle; and before I could budge it\r\nwas all shriveled up. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t need anybody to tell me that that was\r\nan awful bad sign and would fetch me some bad luck, so I was scared\r\nand most shook the clothes off of me. I got up and turned around in my\r\ntracks three times and crossed my breast every time; and then I tied\r\nup a little lock of my hair with a thread to keep witches away. But I\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t no confidence. You do that when you\xe2\x80\x99ve lost a horseshoe that\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve found, instead of nailing it up over the door, but I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\nheard anybody say it was any way to keep off bad luck when you\xe2\x80\x99d killed\r\na spider.\r\n\r\nI set down again, a-shaking all over, and got out my pipe for a smoke;\r\nfor the house was all as still as death now, and so the widow wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow. Well, after a long time I heard the clock away off in the town\r\ngo boom\xe2\x80\x94boom\xe2\x80\x94boom\xe2\x80\x94twelve licks; and all still again\xe2\x80\x94stiller than\r\never. Pretty soon I heard a twig snap down in the dark amongst the\r\ntrees\xe2\x80\x94something was a-stirring. I set still and listened. Directly I\r\ncould just barely hear a \xe2\x80\x9c_me-yow! me-yow!_\xe2\x80\x9d down there. That was good!\r\nSays I, \xe2\x80\x9c_me-yow! me-yow!_\xe2\x80\x9d as soft as I could, and then I put out the\r\nlight and scrambled out of the window on to the shed. Then I slipped\r\ndown to the ground and crawled in among the trees, and, sure enough,\r\nthere was Tom Sawyer waiting for me.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\n\r\n\r\nWe went tiptoeing along a path amongst the trees back toward the end\r\nof the widow\xe2\x80\x99s garden, stooping down so as the branches wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nscrape our heads. When we was passing by the kitchen I fell over a\r\nroot and made a noise. We scrouched down and laid still. Miss Watson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbig nigger, named Jim, was setting in the kitchen door; we could see\r\nhim pretty clear, because there was a light behind him. He got up and\r\nstretched his neck out about a minute, listening. Then he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho dah?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe listened some more; then he came tiptoeing down and stood right\r\nbetween us; we could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 touched him, nearly. Well, likely it was\r\nminutes and minutes that there warn\xe2\x80\x99t a sound, and we all there so\r\nclose together. There was a place on my ankle that got to itching, but\r\nI dasn\xe2\x80\x99t scratch it; and then my ear begun to itch; and next my back,\r\nright between my shoulders. Seemed like I\xe2\x80\x99d die if I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t scratch.\r\nWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve noticed that thing plenty times since. If you are with the\r\nquality, or at a funeral, or trying to go to sleep when you ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsleepy\xe2\x80\x94if you are anywheres where it won\xe2\x80\x99t do for you to scratch, why\r\nyou will itch all over in upward of a thousand places. Pretty soon Jim\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, who is you? Whar is you? Dog my cats ef I didn\xe2\x80\x99 hear sumf\xe2\x80\x99n.\r\nWell, I know what I\xe2\x80\x99s gwyne to do: I\xe2\x80\x99s gwyne to set down here and\r\nlisten tell I hears it ag\xe2\x80\x99in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he set down on the ground betwixt me and Tom. He leaned his back\r\nup against a tree, and stretched his legs out till one of them most\r\ntouched one of mine. My nose begun to itch. It itched till the tears\r\ncome into my eyes. But I dasn\xe2\x80\x99t scratch. Then it begun to itch on\r\nthe inside. Next I got to itching underneath. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how I\r\nwas going to set still. This miserableness went on as much as six or\r\nseven minutes; but it seemed a sight longer than that. I was itching\r\nin eleven different places now. I reckoned I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand it more\xe2\x80\x99n\r\na minute longer, but I set my teeth hard and got ready to try. Just\r\nthen Jim begun to breathe heavy; next he begun to snore\xe2\x80\x94and then I was\r\npretty soon comfortable again.\r\n\r\nTom he made a sign to me\xe2\x80\x94kind of a little noise with his mouth\xe2\x80\x94and we\r\nwent creeping away on our hands and knees. When we was ten foot off\r\nTom whispered to me, and wanted to tie Jim to the tree for fun. But I\r\nsaid no; he might wake and make a disturbance, and then they\xe2\x80\x99d find out\r\nI warn\xe2\x80\x99t in. Then Tom said he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t got candles enough, and he would\r\nslip in the kitchen and get some more. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want him to try. I said\r\nJim might wake up and come. But Tom wanted to resk it; so we slid in\r\nthere and got three candles, and Tom laid five cents on the table for\r\npay. Then we got out, and I was in a sweat to get away; but nothing\r\nwould do Tom but he must crawl to where Jim was, on his hands and\r\nknees, and play something on him. I waited, and it seemed a good while,\r\neverything was so still and lonesome.\r\n\r\nAs soon as Tom was back we cut along the path, around the garden fence,\r\nand by and by fetched up on the steep top of the hill the other side\r\nof the house. Tom said he slipped Jim\xe2\x80\x99s hat off of his head and hung\r\nit on a limb right over him, and Jim stirred a little, but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwake. Afterward Jim said the witches bewitched him and put him in a\r\ntrance, and rode him all over the state, and then set him under the\r\ntrees again, and hung his hat on a limb to show who done it. And next\r\ntime Jim told it he said they rode him down to New Orleans; and, after\r\nthat, every time he told it he spread it more and more, till by and by\r\nhe said they rode him all over the world, and tired him most to death,\r\nand his back was all over saddle-boils. Jim was monstrous proud about\r\nit, and he got so he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly notice the other niggers. Niggers\r\nwould come miles to hear Jim tell about it, and he was more looked up\r\nto than any nigger in that country. Strange niggers would stand with\r\ntheir mouths open and look him all over, same as if he was a wonder.\r\nNiggers is always talking about witches in the dark by the kitchen\r\nfire; but whenever one was talking and letting on to know all about\r\nsuch things, Jim would happen in and say, \xe2\x80\x9cHm! What you know \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\nwitches?\xe2\x80\x9d and that nigger was corked up and had to take a back seat.\r\nJim always kept that five-center piece round his neck with a string,\r\nand said it was a charm the devil give to him with his own hands, and\r\ntold him he could cure anybody with it and fetch witches whenever he\r\nwanted to just by saying something to it; but he never told what it was\r\nhe said to it. Niggers would come from all around there and give Jim\r\nanything they had, just for a sight of that five-center piece; but they\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t touch it, because the devil had had his hands on it. Jim was\r\nmost ruined for a servant, because he got stuck up on account of having\r\nseen the devil and been rode by witches.\r\n\r\nWell, when Tom and me got to the edge of the hilltop we looked away\r\ndown into the village and could see three or four lights twinkling,\r\nwhere there was sick folks, maybe; and the stars over us was sparkling\r\never so fine; and down by the village was the river, a whole mile\r\nbroad, and awful still and grand. We went down the hill and found Joe\r\nHarper and Ben Rogers, and two or three more of the boys, hid in the\r\nold tanyard. So we unhitched a skiff and pulled down the river two mile\r\nand a half, to the big scar on the hillside, and went ashore.\r\n\r\nWe went to a clump of bushes, and Tom made everybody swear to keep the\r\nsecret, and then showed them a hole in the hill, right in the thickest\r\npart of the bushes. Then we lit the candles, and crawled in on our\r\nhands and knees. We went about two hundred yards, and then the cave\r\nopened up. Tom poked about amongst the passages, and pretty soon ducked\r\nunder a wall where you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 noticed that there was a hole. We\r\nwent along a narrow place and got into a kind of room, all damp and\r\nsweaty and cold, and there we stopped. Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, we\xe2\x80\x99ll start this band of robbers and call it Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x99s Gang.\r\nEverybody that wants to join has got to take an oath, and write his\r\nname in blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverybody was willing. So Tom got out a sheet of paper that he had\r\nwrote the oath on, and read it. It swore every boy to stick to the\r\nband, and never tell any of the secrets; and if anybody done anything\r\nto any boy in the band, whichever boy was ordered to kill that person\r\nand his family must do it, and he mustn\xe2\x80\x99t eat and he mustn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep\r\ntill he had killed them and hacked a cross in their breasts, which was\r\nthe sign of the band. And nobody that didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to the band could\r\nuse that mark, and if he did he must be sued; and if he done it again\r\nhe must be killed. And if anybody that belonged to the band told the\r\nsecrets, he must have his throat cut, and then have his carcass burnt\r\nup and the ashes scattered all around, and his name blotted off the\r\nlist with blood and never mentioned again by the gang, but have a curse\r\nput on it and be forgot forever.\r\n\r\nEverybody said it was a real beautiful oath, and asked Tom if he got\r\nit out of his own head. He said some of it, but the rest was out of\r\npirate-books and robber-books, and every gang that was high-toned had\r\nit.\r\n\r\nSome thought it would be good to kill the _families_ of boys that told\r\nthe secrets. Tom said it was a good idea, so he took a pencil and wrote\r\nit in. Then Ben Rogers says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s Huck Finn, he hain\xe2\x80\x99t got no family; what you going to do \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\nhim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, hain\xe2\x80\x99t he got a father?\xe2\x80\x9d says Tom Sawyer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he\xe2\x80\x99s got a father, but you can\xe2\x80\x99t never find him these days. He\r\nused to lay drunk with the hogs in the tanyard, but he hain\xe2\x80\x99t been seen\r\nin these parts for a year or more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey talked it over, and they was going to rule me out, because they\r\nsaid every boy must have a family or somebody to kill, or else it\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be fair and square for the others. Well, nobody could think of\r\nanything to do\xe2\x80\x94everybody was stumped, and set still. I was most ready\r\nto cry; but all at once I thought of a way, and so I offered them Miss\r\nWatson\xe2\x80\x94they could kill her. Everybody said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she\xe2\x80\x99ll do. That\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Huck can come in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they all stuck a pin in their fingers to get blood to sign with,\r\nand I made my mark on the paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d says Ben Rogers, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s the line of business of this Gang?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing only robbery and murder,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut who are we going to rob?\xe2\x80\x94houses, or cattle, or\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStuff! stealing cattle and such things ain\xe2\x80\x99t robbery; it\xe2\x80\x99s burglary,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsays Tom Sawyer. \xe2\x80\x9cWe ain\xe2\x80\x99t burglars. That ain\xe2\x80\x99t no sort of style. We\r\nare highwaymen. We stop stages and carriages on the road, with masks\r\non, and kill the people and take their watches and money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust we always kill the people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly. It\xe2\x80\x99s best. Some authorities think different, but mostly\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s considered best to kill them\xe2\x80\x94except some that you bring to the\r\ncave here, and keep them till they\xe2\x80\x99re ransomed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRansomed? What\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. But that\xe2\x80\x99s what they do. I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen it in books; and so\r\nof course that\xe2\x80\x99s what we\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how can we do it if we don\xe2\x80\x99t know what it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, blame it all, we\xe2\x80\x99ve _got_ to do it. Don\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you it\xe2\x80\x99s in the\r\nbooks? Do you want to go to doing different from what\xe2\x80\x99s in the books,\r\nand get things all muddled up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s all very fine to _say_, Tom Sawyer, but how in the nation\r\nare these fellows going to be ransomed if we don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to do it to\r\nthem?\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the thing _I_ want to get at. Now, what do you _reckon_ it\r\nis?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. But per\xe2\x80\x99aps if we keep them till they\xe2\x80\x99re ransomed,\r\nit means that we keep them till they\xe2\x80\x99re dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, that\xe2\x80\x99s something _like._ That\xe2\x80\x99ll answer. Why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you said\r\nthat before? We\xe2\x80\x99ll keep them till they\xe2\x80\x99re ransomed to death; and a\r\nbothersome lot they\xe2\x80\x99ll be, too\xe2\x80\x94eating up everything, and always trying\r\nto get loose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you talk, Ben Rogers. How can they get loose when there\xe2\x80\x99s a guard\r\nover them, ready to shoot them down if they move a peg?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA guard! Well, that _is_ good. So somebody\xe2\x80\x99s got to set up all night\r\nand never get any sleep, just so as to watch them. I think that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfoolishness. Why can\xe2\x80\x99t a body take a club and ransom them as soon as\r\nthey get here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it ain\xe2\x80\x99t in the books so\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s why. Now, Ben Rogers, do you\r\nwant to do things regular, or don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the idea. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nreckon that the people that made the books knows what\xe2\x80\x99s the correct\r\nthing to do? Do you reckon _you_ can learn \xe2\x80\x99em anything? Not by a good\r\ndeal. No, sir, we\xe2\x80\x99ll just go on and ransom them in the regular way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind; but I say it\xe2\x80\x99s a fool way, anyhow. Say, do we\r\nkill the women, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Ben Rogers, if I was as ignorant as you I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let on. Kill\r\nthe women? No; nobody ever saw anything in the books like that. You\r\nfetch them to the cave, and you\xe2\x80\x99re always as polite as pie to them; and\r\nby and by they fall in love with you, and never want to go home any\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if that\xe2\x80\x99s the way I\xe2\x80\x99m agreed, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t take no stock in it.\r\nMighty soon we\xe2\x80\x99ll have the cave so cluttered up with women, and fellows\r\nwaiting to be ransomed, that there won\xe2\x80\x99t be no place for the robbers.\r\nBut go ahead, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t got nothing to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLittle Tommy Barnes was asleep now, and when they waked him up he was\r\nscared, and cried, and said he wanted to go home to his ma, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to be a robber any more.\r\n\r\nSo they all made fun of him, and called him cry-baby, and that made him\r\nmad, and he said he would go straight and tell all the secrets. But Tom\r\ngive him five cents to keep quiet, and said we would all go home and\r\nmeet next week, and rob somebody and kill some people.\r\n\r\nBen Rogers said he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get out much, only Sundays, and so he\r\nwanted to begin next Sunday; but all the boys said it would be wicked\r\nto do it on Sunday, and that settled the thing. They agreed to get\r\ntogether and fix a day as soon as they could, and then we elected Tom\r\nSawyer first captain and Joe Harper second captain of the Gang, and so\r\nstarted home.\r\n\r\nI clumb up the shed and crept into my window just before day was\r\nbreaking. My new clothes was all greased up and clayey, and I was\r\ndog-tired.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\n\r\n\r\nWell, I got a good going-over in the morning from old Miss Watson on\r\naccount of my clothes; but the widow she didn\xe2\x80\x99t scold, but only cleaned\r\noff the grease and clay, and looked so sorry that I thought I would\r\nbehave awhile if I could. Then Miss Watson she took me in the closet\r\nand prayed, but nothing come of it. She told me to pray every day,\r\nand whatever I asked for I would get it. But it warn\xe2\x80\x99t so. I tried\r\nit. Once I got a fish-line, but no hooks. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t any good to me\r\nwithout hooks. I tried for the hooks three or four times, but somehow\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make it work. By and by, one day, I asked Miss Watson to\r\ntry for me, but she said I was a fool. She never told me why, and I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make it out no way.\r\n\r\nI set down one time back in the woods, and had a long think about it.\r\nI says to myself, if a body can get anything they pray for, why don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nDeacon Winn get back the money he lost on pork? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t the widow get\r\nback her silver snuff-box that was stole? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t Miss Watson fat up?\r\nNo, says I to myself, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing in it. I went and told the\r\nwidow about it, and she said the thing a body could get by praying for\r\nit was \xe2\x80\x9cspiritual gifts.\xe2\x80\x9d This was too many for me, but she told me\r\nwhat she meant\xe2\x80\x94I must help other people, and do everything I could for\r\nother people, and look out for them all the time, and never think about\r\nmyself. This was including Miss Watson, as I took it. I went out in the\r\nwoods and turned it over in my mind a long time, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no\r\nadvantage about it\xe2\x80\x94except for the other people; so at last I reckoned\r\nI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t worry about it any more, but just let it go. Sometimes the\r\nwidow would take me one side and talk about Providence in a way to make\r\na body\xe2\x80\x99s mouth water; but maybe next day Miss Watson would take hold\r\nand knock it all down again. I judged I could see that there was two\r\nProvidences, and a poor chap would stand considerable show with the\r\nwidow\xe2\x80\x99s Providence, but if Miss Watson\xe2\x80\x99s got him there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no help\r\nfor him any more. I thought it all out, and reckoned I would belong\r\nto the widow\xe2\x80\x99s if he wanted me, though I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out how he was\r\na-going to be any better off then than what he was before, seeing I was\r\nso ignorant, and so kind of low-down and ornery.\r\n\r\nPap he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been seen for more than a year, and that was comfortable\r\nfor me; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to see him no more. He used to always whale me\r\nwhen he was sober and could get his hands on me; though I used to take\r\nto the woods most of the time when he was around. Well, about this time\r\nhe was found in the river drownded, about twelve mile above town, so\r\npeople said. They judged it was him, anyway; said this drownded man\r\nwas just his size, and was ragged, and had uncommon long hair, which\r\nwas all like pap; but they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make nothing out of the face,\r\nbecause it had been in the water so long it warn\xe2\x80\x99t much like a face at\r\nall. They said he was floating on his back in the water. They took him\r\nand buried him on the bank. But I warn\xe2\x80\x99t comfortable long, because I\r\nhappened to think of something. I knowed mighty well that a drownded\r\nman don\xe2\x80\x99t float on his back, but on his face. So I knowed, then, that\r\nthis warn\xe2\x80\x99t pap, but a woman dressed up in a man\xe2\x80\x99s clothes. So I was\r\nuncomfortable again. I judged the old man would turn up again by and\r\nby, though I wished he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\nWe played robber now and then about a month, and then I resigned. All\r\nthe boys did. We hadn\xe2\x80\x99t robbed nobody, hadn\xe2\x80\x99t killed any people, but\r\nonly just pretended. We used to hop out of the woods and go charging\r\ndown on hog-drivers and women in carts taking garden stuff to market,\r\nbut we never hived any of them. Tom Sawyer called the hogs \xe2\x80\x9cingots,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nand he called the turnips and stuff \xe2\x80\x9cjulery,\xe2\x80\x9d and we would go to the\r\ncave and powwow over what we had done, and how many people we had\r\nkilled and marked. But I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no profit in it. One time Tom\r\nsent a boy to run about town with a blazing stick, which he called a\r\nslogan (which was the sign for the Gang to get together), and then\r\nhe said he had got secret news by his spies that next day a whole\r\nparcel of Spanish merchants and rich A-rabs was going to camp in Cave\r\nHollow with two hundred elephants, and six hundred camels, and over\r\na thousand \xe2\x80\x9csumter\xe2\x80\x9d mules, all loaded down with di\xe2\x80\x99monds, and they\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t have only a guard of four hundred soldiers, and so we would lay\r\nin ambuscade, as he called it, and kill the lot and scoop the things.\r\nHe said we must slick up our swords and guns, and get ready. He never\r\ncould go after even a turnip-cart but he must have the swords and guns\r\nall scoured up for it, though they was only lath and broomsticks, and\r\nyou might scour at them till you rotted, and then they warn\xe2\x80\x99t worth a\r\nmouthful of ashes more than what they was before. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe we\r\ncould lick such a crowd of Spaniards and A-rabs, but I wanted to see\r\nthe camels and elephants, so I was on hand next day, Saturday, in the\r\nambuscade; and when we got the word we rushed out of the woods and down\r\nthe hill. But there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no Spaniards and A-rabs, and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nno camels nor no elephants. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t anything but a Sunday-school\r\npicnic, and only a primer class at that. We busted it up, and chased\r\nthe children up the hollow; but we never got anything but some\r\ndoughnuts and jam, though Ben Rogers got a rag doll, and Joe Harper\r\ngot a hymn-book and a tract; and then the teacher charged in, and made\r\nus drop everything and cut. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see no di\xe2\x80\x99monds, and I told Tom\r\nSawyer so. He said there was loads of them there, anyway; and he said\r\nthere was A-rabs there, too, and elephants and things. I said, why\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we see them, then? He said if I warn\xe2\x80\x99t so ignorant, but had\r\nread a book called _Don Quixote_, I would know without asking. He said\r\nit was all done by enchantment. He said there was hundreds of soldiers\r\nthere, and elephants and treasure, and so on, but we had enemies which\r\nhe called magicians, and they had turned the whole thing into an infant\r\nSunday-school, just out of spite. I said, all right; then the thing for\r\nus to do was to go for the magicians. Tom Sawyer said I was a numskull.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9ca magician could call up a lot of genies, and they\r\nwould hash you up like nothing before you could say Jack Robinson. They\r\nare as tall as a tree and as big around as a church.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cs\xe2\x80\x99pose we got some genies to help _us_\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t we lick\r\nthe other crowd then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you going to get them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. How do _they_ get them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, they rub an old tin lamp or an iron ring, and then the genies\r\ncome tearing in, with the thunder and lightning a-ripping around and\r\nthe smoke a-rolling, and everything they\xe2\x80\x99re told to do they up and do\r\nit. They don\xe2\x80\x99t think nothing of pulling a shot-tower up by the roots,\r\nand belting a Sunday-school superintendent over the head with it\xe2\x80\x94or any\r\nother man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho makes them tear around so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, whoever rubs the lamp or the ring. They belong to whoever rubs\r\nthe lamp or the ring, and they\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do whatever he says. If he\r\ntells them to build a palace forty miles long out of di\xe2\x80\x99monds, and fill\r\nit full of chewing-gum, or whatever you want, and fetch an emperor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndaughter from China for you to marry, they\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do it\xe2\x80\x94and they\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngot to do it before sun-up next morning, too. And more: they\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\nwaltz that palace around over the country wherever you want it, you\r\nunderstand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d says I, \xe2\x80\x9cI think they are a pack of flatheads for not keeping\r\nthe palace themselves \xe2\x80\x99stead of fooling them away like that. And what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x94if I was one of them I would see a man in Jericho before I would\r\ndrop my business and come to him for the rubbing of an old tin lamp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you talk, Huck Finn. Why, you\xe2\x80\x99d _have_ to come when he rubbed it,\r\nwhether you wanted to or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! and I as high as a tree and as big as a church? All right, then;\r\nI _would_ come; but I lay I\xe2\x80\x99d make that man climb the highest tree\r\nthere was in the country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use to talk to you, Huck Finn. You don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to\r\nknow anything, somehow\xe2\x80\x94perfect saphead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought all this over for two or three days, and then I reckoned I\r\nwould see if there was anything in it. I got an old tin lamp and an\r\niron ring, and went out in the woods and rubbed and rubbed till I sweat\r\nlike an Injun, calculating to build a palace and sell it; but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nno use, none of the genies come. So then I judged that all that stuff\r\nwas only just one of Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x99s lies. I reckoned he believed in the\r\nA-rabs and the elephants, but as for me I think different. It had all\r\nthe marks of a Sunday-school.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nWell, three or four months run along, and it was well into the winter\r\nnow. I had been to school most all the time and could spell and read\r\nand write just a little, and could say the multiplication table up to\r\nsix times seven is thirty-five, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t reckon I could ever get any\r\nfurther than that if I was to live forever. I don\xe2\x80\x99t take no stock in\r\nmathematics, anyway.\r\n\r\nAt first I hated the school, but by and by I got so I could stand\r\nit. Whenever I got uncommon tired I played hookey, and the hiding I\r\ngot next day done me good and cheered me up. So the longer I went to\r\nschool the easier it got to be. I was getting sort of used to the\r\nwidow\xe2\x80\x99s ways, too, and they warn\xe2\x80\x99t so raspy on me. Living in a house\r\nand sleeping in a bed pulled on me pretty tight mostly, but before the\r\ncold weather I used to slide out and sleep in the woods sometimes, and\r\nso that was a rest to me. I liked the old ways best, but I was getting\r\nso I liked the new ones, too, a little bit. The widow said I was coming\r\nalong slow but sure, and doing very satisfactory. She said she warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nashamed of me.\r\n\r\nOne morning I happened to turn over the salt-cellar at breakfast.\r\nI reached for some of it as quick as I could to throw over my left\r\nshoulder and keep off the bad luck, but Miss Watson was in ahead of\r\nme, and crossed me off. She says, \xe2\x80\x9cTake your hands away, Huckleberry;\r\nwhat a mess you are always making!\xe2\x80\x9d The widow put in a good word for\r\nme, but that warn\xe2\x80\x99t going to keep off the bad luck, I knowed that well\r\nenough. I started out, after breakfast, feeling worried and shaky, and\r\nwondering where it was going to fall on me, and what it was going to\r\nbe. There is ways to keep off some kinds of bad luck, but this wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\none of them kind; so I never tried to do anything, but just poked along\r\nlow-spirited and on the watch-out.\r\n\r\nI went down to the front garden and clumb over the stile where you go\r\nthrough the high board fence. There was an inch of new snow on the\r\nground, and I seen somebody\xe2\x80\x99s tracks. They had come up from the quarry\r\nand stood around the stile awhile, and then went on around the garden\r\nfence. It was funny they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come in, after standing around so. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make it out. It was very curious, somehow. I was going to\r\nfollow around, but I stooped down to look at the tracks first. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnotice anything at first, but next I did. There was a cross in the left\r\nboot-heel made with big nails, to keep off the devil.\r\n\r\nI was up in a second and shinning down the hill. What I wanted to know\r\nwas, what he was going to do, and was he going to stay? Jim got out\r\nhis hair-ball and said something over it, and then he held it up and\r\ndropped it on the floor. Jim got down on his knees, and put his ear against it and\r\nlistened. But it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use; he said it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t talk. Every little\r\nwhile he locked me in and went down to the store, three miles, to the\r\nferry, and traded fish and game for whisky, and fetched it home and got\r\ndrunk and had a good time, and licked me. He got to going away so much, too, and\r\nlocking me in. Once he locked me in and was gone three days. It was\r\ndreadful lonesome. I judged he had got drownded, and I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\ngoing to get out any more. I was scared. I made up my mind I would\r\nfix up some way to leave there. I had tried to get out of that cabin\r\nmany a time, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find no way. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t a window to it\r\nbig enough for a dog to get through. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get up the chimbly; it\r\nwas too narrow. The door was thick, solid oak slabs. Pap was pretty\r\ncareful not to leave a knife or anything in the cabin when he was away;\r\nI reckon I had hunted the place over as much as a hundred times; well,\r\nI was most all the time at it, because it was about the only way to put\r\nin the time. But this time I found something at last; I found an old\r\nrusty wood-saw without any handle; it was laid in between a rafter and\r\nthe clapboards of the roof. I greased it up and went to work. There\r\nwas an old horse-blanket nailed against the logs at the far end of\r\nthe cabin behind the table, to keep the wind from blowing through the\r\nchinks and putting the candle out. I got under the table and raised\r\nthe blanket, and went to work to saw a section of the big bottom log\r\nout\xe2\x80\x94big enough to let me through. Well, it was a good long job, but I\r\nwas getting toward the end of it when I heard pap\xe2\x80\x99s gun in the woods.\r\nI got rid of the signs of my work, and dropped the blanket and hid my\r\nsaw, and pretty soon pap come in.\r\n\r\nPap warn\xe2\x80\x99t in a good humor\xe2\x80\x94so he was his natural self. He said he was\r\ndown-town, and everything was going wrong. His lawyer said he reckoned\r\nhe would win his lawsuit and get the money if they ever got started\r\non the trial; but then there was ways to put it off a long time, and\r\nJudge Thatcher knowed how to do it. And he said people allowed there\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbe another trial to get me away from him and give me to the widow for\r\nmy guardian, and they guessed it would win this time. This shook me up\r\nconsiderable, because I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to go back to the widow\xe2\x80\x99s any more\r\nand be so cramped up and sivilized, as they called it. Then the old\r\nman got to cussing, and cussed everything and everybody he could think\r\nof, and then cussed them all over again to make sure he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t skipped\r\nany, and after that he polished off with a kind of a general cuss all\r\nround, including a considerable parcel of people which he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthe names of, and so called them what\xe2\x80\x99s-his-name when he got to them,\r\nand went right along with his cussing.\r\n\r\nHe said he would like to see the widow get me. He said he would watch\r\nout, and if they tried to come any such game on him he knowed of a\r\nplace six or seven mile off to stow me in, where they might hunt till\r\nthey dropped and they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find me. That made me pretty uneasy\r\nagain, but only for a minute; I reckoned I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay on hand till\r\nhe got that chance.\r\n\r\nThe old man made me go to the skiff and fetch the things he had got.\r\nThere was a fifty-pound sack of corn meal, and a side of bacon,\r\nammunition, and a four-gallon jug of whisky, and an old book and two\r\nnewspapers for wadding, besides some tow. I toted up a load, and went\r\nback and set down on the bow of the skiff to rest. I thought it all\r\nover, and I reckoned I would walk off with the gun and some lines, and\r\ntake to the woods when I run away. I guessed I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay in one\r\nplace, but just tramp right across the country, mostly night-times, and\r\nhunt and fish to keep alive, and so get so far away that the old man\r\nnor the widow couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever find me any more. I judged I would saw out\r\nand leave that night if pap got drunk enough, and I reckoned he would.\r\nI got so full of it I didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice how long I was staying till the old\r\nman hollered and asked me whether I was asleep or drownded.\r\n\r\nI got the things all up to the cabin, and then it was about dark. While\r\nI was cooking supper the old man took a swig or two and got sort of\r\nwarmed up, and went to ripping again. He had been drunk over in town,\r\nand laid in the gutter all night, and he was a sight to look at. A body\r\nwould \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 thought he was Adam\xe2\x80\x94he was just all mud. Whenever his liquor\r\nbegun to work he most always went for the govment. This time he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall this a govment! why, just look at it and see what it\xe2\x80\x99s like.\r\nHere\xe2\x80\x99s the law a-standing ready to take a man\xe2\x80\x99s son away from him\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s own son, which he has had all the trouble and all the anxiety\r\nand all the expense of raising. Yes, just as that man has got that\r\nson raised at last, and ready to go to work and begin to do suthin\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfor _him_ and give him a rest, the law up and goes for him. And they\r\ncall _that_ govment! That ain\xe2\x80\x99t all, nuther. The law backs that old\r\nJudge Thatcher up and helps him to keep me out o\xe2\x80\x99 my property. Here\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat the law does: The law takes a man worth six thousand dollars and\r\nup\xe2\x80\x99ards, and jams him into an old trap of a cabin like this, and lets\r\nhim go round in clothes that ain\xe2\x80\x99t fitten for a hog. They call that\r\ngovment! A man can\xe2\x80\x99t get his rights in a govment like this. Sometimes\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve a mighty notion to just leave the country for good and all. Yes,\r\nand I _told_ \xe2\x80\x99em so; I told old Thatcher so to his face. Lots of \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nheard me, and can tell what I said. Says I, for two cents I\xe2\x80\x99d leave the\r\nblamed country and never come a-near it ag\xe2\x80\x99in. Them\xe2\x80\x99s the very words. I\r\nsays, look at my hat\xe2\x80\x94if you call it a hat\xe2\x80\x94but the lid raises up and the\r\nrest of it goes down till it\xe2\x80\x99s below my chin, and then it ain\xe2\x80\x99t rightly\r\na hat at all, but more like my head was shoved up through a jint o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstove-pipe. Look at it, says I\xe2\x80\x94such a hat for me to wear\xe2\x80\x94one of the\r\nwealthiest men in this town if I could git my rights.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, this is a wonderful govment, wonderful. Why, looky here.\r\nThere was a free nigger there from Ohio\xe2\x80\x94a mulatter, most as white as\r\na white man. He had the whitest shirt on you ever see, too, and the\r\nshiniest hat; and there ain\xe2\x80\x99t a man in that town that\xe2\x80\x99s got as fine\r\nclothes as what he had; and he had a gold watch and chain, and a\r\nsilver-headed cane\xe2\x80\x94the awfulest old gray-headed nabob in the state. And\r\nwhat do you think? They said he was a p\xe2\x80\x99fessor in a college, and could\r\ntalk all kinds of languages, and knowed everything. And that ain\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\nwust. They said he could _vote_ when he was at home. Well, that let\r\nme out. Thinks I, what is the country a-coming to? It was \xe2\x80\x99lection\r\nday, and I was just about to go and vote myself if I warn\xe2\x80\x99t too drunk\r\nto get there; but when they told me there was a state in this country\r\nwhere they\xe2\x80\x99d let that nigger vote, I drawed out. I says I\xe2\x80\x99ll never vote\r\nag\xe2\x80\x99in. Them\xe2\x80\x99s the very words I said; they all heard me; and the country\r\nmay rot for all me\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll never vote ag\xe2\x80\x99in as long as I live. And to see\r\nthe cool way of that nigger\xe2\x80\x94why, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 give me the road if\r\nI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t shoved him out o\xe2\x80\x99 the way. I says to the people, why ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthis nigger put up at auction and sold?\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what I want to know. And\r\nwhat do you reckon they said? Why, they said he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be sold till\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d been in the state six months, and he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been there that long\r\nyet. There, now\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s a specimen. They call that a govment that can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsell a free nigger till he\xe2\x80\x99s been in the state six months. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngovment that calls itself a govment, and lets on to be a govment, and\r\nthinks it is a govment, and yet\xe2\x80\x99s got to set stock-still for six whole\r\nmonths before it can take a-hold of a prowling, thieving, infernal,\r\nwhite-shirted free nigger, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPap was a-going on so he never noticed where his old limber legs was\r\ntaking him to, so he went head over heels over the tub of salt pork\r\nand barked both shins, and the rest of his speech was all the hottest\r\nkind of language\xe2\x80\x94mostly hove at the nigger and the govment, though he\r\ngive the tub some, too, all along, here and there. He hopped around the\r\ncabin considerable, first on one leg and then on the other, holding\r\nfirst one shin and then the other one, and at last he let out with his\r\nleft foot all of a sudden and fetched the tub a rattling kick. But it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t good judgment, because that was the boot that had a couple of\r\nhis toes leaking out of the front end of it; so now he raised a howl\r\nthat fairly made a body\xe2\x80\x99s hair raise, and down he went in the dirt, and\r\nrolled there, and held his toes; and the cussing he done then laid over\r\nanything he had ever done previous. He said so his own self afterwards.\r\nHe had heard old Sowberry Hagan in his best days, and he said it laid\r\nover him, too; but I reckon that was sort of piling it on, maybe.\r\n\r\nAfter supper pap took the jug, and said he had enough whisky there for\r\ntwo drunks and one delirium tremens. That was always his word. I judged\r\nhe would be blind drunk in about an hour, and then I would steal the\r\nkey, or saw myself out, one or t\xe2\x80\x99other. He drank and drank, and tumbled\r\ndown on his blankets by and by; but luck didn\xe2\x80\x99t run my way. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngo sound asleep, but was uneasy. He groaned and moaned and thrashed\r\naround this way and that for a long time. At last I got so sleepy I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep my eyes open all I could do, and so before I knowed what\r\nI was about I was sound asleep, and the candle burning.\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how long I was asleep, but all of a sudden there was an\r\nawful scream and I was up. There was pap looking wild, and skipping\r\naround every which way and yelling about snakes. He said they was\r\ncrawling up his legs; and then he would give a jump and scream, and say\r\none had bit him on the cheek\xe2\x80\x94but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no snakes. He started\r\nand run round and round the cabin, hollering \xe2\x80\x9cTake him off! take him\r\noff! he\xe2\x80\x99s biting me on the neck!\xe2\x80\x9d I never see a man look so wild in the\r\neyes. Pretty soon he was all fagged out, and fell down panting; then he\r\nrolled over and over wonderful fast, kicking things every which way,\r\nand striking and grabbing at the air with his hands, and screaming and\r\nsaying there was devils a-hold of him. He wore out by and by, and laid\r\nstill awhile, moaning. Then he laid stiller, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t make a sound. I\r\ncould hear the owls and the wolves away off in the woods, and it seemed\r\nterrible still. He was laying over by the corner. By and by he raised\r\nup part way and listened, with his head to one side. He says, very low:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTramp\xe2\x80\x94tramp\xe2\x80\x94tramp; that\xe2\x80\x99s the dead; tramp\xe2\x80\x94tramp\xe2\x80\x94tramp; they\xe2\x80\x99re coming\r\nafter me; but I won\xe2\x80\x99t go. Oh, they\xe2\x80\x99re here! don\xe2\x80\x99t touch me\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t! hands\r\noff\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re cold; let go. Oh, let a poor devil alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he went down on all fours and crawled off, begging them to let him\r\nalone, and he rolled himself up in his blanket and wallowed in under\r\nthe old pine table, still a-begging; and then he went to crying. I\r\ncould hear him through the blanket.\r\n\r\nBy and by he rolled out and jumped up on his feet looking wild, and he\r\nsee me and went for me. He chased me round and round the place with a\r\nclasp-knife, calling me the Angel of Death, and saying he would kill\r\nme, and then I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come for him no more. I begged, and told him\r\nI was only Huck; but he laughed _such_ a screechy laugh, and roared\r\nand cussed, and kept on chasing me up. Once when I turned short and\r\ndodged under his arm he made a grab and got me by the jacket between my\r\nshoulders, and I thought I was gone; but I slid out of the jacket quick\r\nas lightning, and saved myself. Pretty soon he was all tired out, and\r\ndropped down with his back against the door, and said he would rest a\r\nminute and then kill me. He put his knife under him, and said he would\r\nsleep and get strong, and then he would see who was who.\r\n\r\nSo he dozed off pretty soon. By and by I got the old split-bottom chair\r\nand clumb up as easy as I could, not to make any noise, and got down\r\nthe gun. I slipped the ramrod down it to make sure it was loaded, and\r\nthen I laid it across the turnip-barrel, pointing towards pap, and set\r\ndown behind it to wait for him to stir. And how slow and still the time\r\ndid drag along.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGit up! What you \xe2\x80\x99bout?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI opened my eyes and looked around, trying to make out where I was. It\r\nwas after sun-up, and I had been sound asleep. Pap was standing over me\r\nlooking sour\xe2\x80\x94and sick, too. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you doin\xe2\x80\x99 with this gun?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI judged he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know nothing about what he had been doing, so I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomebody tried to get in, so I was laying for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you roust me out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I tried to, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t; I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t budge you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right. Don\xe2\x80\x99t stand there palavering all day, but out with\r\nyou and see if there\xe2\x80\x99s a fish on the lines for breakfast. I\xe2\x80\x99ll be along\r\nin a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe unlocked the door, and I cleared out up the river-bank. I noticed\r\nsome pieces of limbs and such things floating down, and a sprinkling\r\nof bark; so I knowed the river had begun to rise. I reckoned I would\r\nhave great times now if I was over at the town. The June rise used to\r\nbe always luck for me; because as soon as that rise begins here comes\r\ncordwood floating down, and pieces of log rafts\xe2\x80\x94sometimes a dozen logs\r\ntogether; so all you have to do is to catch them and sell them to the\r\nwoodyards and the sawmill.\r\n\r\nI went along up the bank with one eye out for pap and t\xe2\x80\x99other one out\r\nfor what the rise might fetch along. Well, all at once here comes\r\na canoe; just a beauty, too, about thirteen or fourteen foot long,\r\nriding high like a duck. I shot head-first off of the bank like a frog,\r\nclothes and all on, and struck out for the canoe. I just expected\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d be somebody laying down in it, because people often done that\r\nto fool folks, and when a chap had pulled a skiff out most to it\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d raise up and laugh at him. But it warn\xe2\x80\x99t so this time. It was a\r\ndrift-canoe sure enough, and I clumb in and paddled her ashore. Thinks\r\nI, the old man will be glad when he sees this\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s worth ten dollars.\r\nBut when I got to shore pap wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in sight yet, and as I was running\r\nher into a little creek like a gully, all hung over with vines and\r\nwillows, I struck another idea: I judged I\xe2\x80\x99d hide her good, and then,\r\n\xe2\x80\x99stead of taking to the woods when I run off, I\xe2\x80\x99d go down the river\r\nabout fifty mile and camp in one place for good, and not have such a\r\nrough time tramping on foot.\r\n\r\nIt was pretty close to the shanty, and I thought I heard the old man\r\ncoming all the time; but I got her hid; and then I out and looked\r\naround a bunch of willows, and there was the old man down the path a\r\npiece just drawing a bead on a bird with his gun. So he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen\r\nanything.\r\n\r\nWhen he got along I was hard at it taking up a \xe2\x80\x9ctrot\xe2\x80\x9d line. He abused\r\nme a little for being so slow; but I told him I fell in the river, and\r\nthat was what made me so long. I knowed he would see I was wet, and\r\nthen he would be asking questions. We got five catfish off the lines\r\nand went home.\r\n\r\nWhile we laid off after breakfast to sleep up, both of us being about\r\nwore out, I got to thinking that if I could fix up some way to keep pap\r\nand the widow from trying to follow me, it would be a certainer thing\r\nthan trusting to luck to get far enough off before they missed me; you\r\nsee, all kinds of things might happen. Well, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see no way for a\r\nwhile, but by and by pap raised up a minute to drink another barrel of\r\nwater, and he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother time a man comes a-prowling round here you roust me out, you\r\nhear? That man warn\xe2\x80\x99t here for no good. I\xe2\x80\x99d a shot him. Next time you\r\nroust me out, you hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he dropped down and went to sleep again; what he had been saying\r\ngive me the very idea I wanted. I says to myself, I can fix it now so\r\nnobody won\xe2\x80\x99t think of following me.\r\n\r\nAbout twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock we turned out and went along up the bank. The\r\nriver was coming up pretty fast, and lots of driftwood going by on the\r\nrise. By and by along comes part of a log raft\xe2\x80\x94nine logs fast together.\r\nWe went out with the skiff and towed it ashore. Then we had dinner.\r\nAnybody but pap would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 waited and seen the day through, so as to\r\ncatch more stuff; but that warn\xe2\x80\x99t pap\xe2\x80\x99s style. Nine logs was enough\r\nfor one time; he must shove right over to town and sell. So he locked\r\nme in and took the skiff, and started off towing the raft about half\r\npast three. I judged he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come back that night. I waited till I\r\nreckoned he had got a good start; then I out with my saw, and went to\r\nwork on that log again. Before he was t\xe2\x80\x99other side of the river I was\r\nout of the hole; him and his raft was just a speck on the water away\r\noff yonder.\r\n\r\n[Illustration: HUCKLEBERRY FINN]\r\n\r\nI took the sack of corn meal and took it to where the canoe was hid,\r\nand shoved the vines and branches apart and put it in; then I done the\r\nsame with the side of bacon; then the whisky-jug. I took all the coffee\r\nand sugar there was, and all the ammunition; I took the wadding; I took\r\nthe bucket and gourd; took a dipper and a tin cup, and my old saw and\r\ntwo blankets, and the skillet and the coffee-pot. I took fish-lines and\r\nmatches and other things\xe2\x80\x94everything that was worth a cent. I cleaned\r\nout the place. I wanted an ax, but there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any, only the one out\r\nat the woodpile, and I knowed why I was going to leave that. I fetched\r\nout the gun, and now I was done.\r\n\r\nI had wore the ground a good deal crawling out of the hole and dragging\r\nout so many things. So I fixed that as good as I could from the outside\r\nby scattering dust on the place, which covered up the smoothness and\r\nthe sawdust. Then I fixed the piece of log back into its place, and put\r\ntwo rocks under it and one against it to hold it there, for it was bent\r\nup at that place and didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite touch ground. If you stood four or\r\nfive foot away and didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it was sawed, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t never notice\r\nit; and besides, this was the back of the cabin, and it warn\xe2\x80\x99t likely\r\nanybody would go fooling around there.\r\n\r\nIt was all grass clear to the canoe, so I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t left a track. I\r\nfollowed around to see. I stood on the bank and looked out over the\r\nriver. All safe. So I took the gun and went up a piece into the\r\nwoods, and was hunting around for some birds when I see a wild pig;\r\nhogs soon went wild in them bottoms after they had got away from the\r\nprairie-farms. I shot this fellow and took him into camp.\r\n\r\nI took the ax and smashed in the door. I beat it and hacked it\r\nconsiderable a-doing it. I fetched the pig in, and took him back nearly\r\nto the table and hacked into his throat with the ax, and laid him down\r\non the ground to bleed; I say ground because it _was_ ground\xe2\x80\x94hard\r\npacked, and no boards. Well, next I took an old sack and put a lot of\r\nbig rocks in it\xe2\x80\x94all I could drag\xe2\x80\x94and I started it from the pig, and\r\ndragged it to the door and through the woods down to the river and\r\ndumped it in, and down it sunk, out of sight. You could easy see that\r\nsomething had been dragged over the ground. I did wish Tom Sawyer was\r\nthere; I knowed he would take an interest in this kind of business, and\r\nthrow in the fancy touches. Nobody could spread himself like Tom Sawyer\r\nin such a thing as that.\r\n\r\nWell, last I pulled out some of my hair, and blooded the ax good, and\r\nstuck it on the back side, and slung the ax in the corner. Then I took\r\nup the pig and held him to my breast with my jacket (so he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndrip) till I got a good piece below the house and then dumped him into\r\nthe river. Now I thought of something else. So I went and got the bag\r\nof meal and my old saw out of the canoe, and fetched them to the house.\r\nI took the bag to where it used to stand, and ripped a hole in the\r\nbottom of it with the saw, for there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no knives and forks on the\r\nplace\xe2\x80\x94pap done everything with his clasp-knife about the cooking. Then\r\nI carried the sack about a hundred yards across the grass and through\r\nthe willows east of the house, to a shallow lake that was five mile\r\nwide and full of rushes\xe2\x80\x94and ducks too, you might say, in the season.\r\nThere was a slough or a creek leading out of it on the other side that\r\nwent miles away, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where, but it didn\xe2\x80\x99t go to the river.\r\nThe meal sifted out and made a little track all the way to the lake. I\r\ndropped pap\xe2\x80\x99s whetstone there too, so as to look like it had been done\r\nby accident. Then I tied up the rip in the meal-sack with a string, so\r\nit wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t leak no more, and took it and my saw to the canoe again.\r\n\r\nIt was about dark now; so I dropped the canoe down the river under some\r\nwillows that hung over the bank, and waited for the moon to rise. I\r\nmade fast to a willow; then I took a bite to eat, and by and by laid\r\ndown in the canoe to smoke a pipe and lay out a plan. I says to myself,\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll follow the track of that sackful of rocks to the shore and then\r\ndrag the river for me. And they\xe2\x80\x99ll follow that meal track to the lake\r\nand go browsing down the creek that leads out of it to find the robbers\r\nthat killed me and took the things. They won\xe2\x80\x99t ever hunt the river\r\nfor anything but my dead carcass. They\xe2\x80\x99ll soon get tired of that, and\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t bother no more about me. All right; I can stop anywhere I want\r\nto. Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s Island is good enough for me; I know that island pretty\r\nwell, and nobody ever comes there. And then I can paddle over to town\r\nnights, and slink around and pick up things I want. Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s Island\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe place.\r\n\r\nI was pretty tired, and the first thing I knowed I was asleep. When I\r\nwoke up I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know where I was for a minute. I set up and looked\r\naround, a little scared. Then I remembered. The river looked miles and\r\nmiles across. The moon was so bright I could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 counted the drift-logs\r\nthat went a-slipping along, black and still, hundreds of yards out from\r\nshore. Everything was dead quiet, and it looked late, and _smelt_ late.\r\nYou know what I mean\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know the words to put it in.\r\n\r\nI took a good gap and a stretch, and was just going to unhitch and\r\nstart when I heard a sound away over the water. I listened. Pretty soon\r\nI made it out. It was that dull kind of a regular sound that comes from\r\noars working in rowlocks when it\xe2\x80\x99s a still night. I peeped out through\r\nthe willow branches, and there it was\xe2\x80\x94a skiff, away across the water.\r\nI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell how many was in it. It kept a-coming, and when it was\r\nabreast of me I see there warn\xe2\x80\x99t but one man in it. Thinks I, maybe\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s pap, though I warn\xe2\x80\x99t expecting him. He dropped below me with the\r\ncurrent, and by and by he came a-swinging up shore in the easy water,\r\nand he went by so close I could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 reached out the gun and touched\r\nhim. Well, it _was_ pap, sure enough\xe2\x80\x94and sober, too, by the way he laid\r\nhis oars.\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t lose no time. The next minute I was a-spinning down-stream\r\nsoft, but quick, in the shade of the bank. I made two mile and a half,\r\nand then struck out a quarter of a mile or more toward the middle of\r\nthe river, because pretty soon I would be passing the ferry-landing,\r\nand people might see me and hail me. I got out amongst the driftwood,\r\nand then laid down in the bottom of the canoe and let her float. I\r\nlaid there, and had a good rest and a smoke out of my pipe, looking\r\naway into the sky; not a cloud in it. The sky looks ever so deep when\r\nyou lay down on your back in the moonshine; I never knowed it before.\r\nAnd how far a body can hear on the water such nights! I heard people\r\ntalking at the ferry-landing. I heard what they said, too\xe2\x80\x94every word\r\nof it. One man said it was getting towards the long days and the short\r\nnights now. T\xe2\x80\x99other one said _this_ warn\xe2\x80\x99t one of the short ones, he\r\nreckoned\xe2\x80\x94and then they laughed, and he said it over again, and they\r\nlaughed again; then they waked up another fellow and told him, and\r\nlaughed, but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t laugh; he ripped out something brisk, and said\r\nlet him alone. The first fellow said he \xe2\x80\x99lowed to tell it to his old\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x94she would think it was pretty good; but he said that warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnothing to some things he had said in his time. I heard one man say\r\nit was nearly three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and he hoped daylight wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wait more\r\nthan about a week longer. After that the talk got further and further\r\naway, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out the words any more; but I could hear the\r\nmumble, and now and then a laugh, too, but it seemed a long ways off.\r\n\r\nI was away below the ferry now. I rose up, and there was Jackson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nIsland, about two mile and a half down-stream, heavy-timbered and\r\nstanding up out of the middle of the river, big and dark and solid,\r\nlike a steamboat without any lights. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t any signs of the bar\r\nat the head\xe2\x80\x94it was all under water now.\r\n\r\nIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t take me long to get there. I shot past the head at a ripping\r\nrate, the current was so swift, and then I got into the dead water and\r\nlanded on the side towards the Illinois shore. I run the canoe into a\r\ndeep dent in the bank that I knowed about; I had to part the willow\r\nbranches to get in; and when I made fast nobody could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 seen the\r\ncanoe from the outside.\r\n\r\nI went up and set down on a log at the head of the island, and looked\r\nout on the big river and the black driftwood and away over to the\r\ntown, three mile away, where there was three or four lights twinkling.\r\nA monstrous big lumber-raft was about a mile up-stream, coming along\r\ndown, with a lantern in the middle of it. I watched it come creeping\r\ndown, and when it was most abreast of where I stood I heard a man say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStern oars, there! heave her head to stabboard!\xe2\x80\x9d I heard that just as\r\nplain as if the man was by my side.\r\n\r\nThere was a little gray in the sky now; so I stepped into the woods,\r\nand laid down for a nap before breakfast.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe sun was up so high when I waked that I judged it was after eight\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock. I laid there in the grass and the cool shade thinking about\r\nthings, and feeling rested and ruther comfortable and satisfied. I\r\ncould see the sun out at one or two holes, but mostly it was big trees\r\nall about, and gloomy in there amongst them. There was freckled places\r\non the ground where the light sifted down through the leaves, and the\r\nfreckled places swapped about a little, showing there was a little\r\nbreeze up there. A couple of squirrels set on a limb and jabbered at me\r\nvery friendly.\r\n\r\nI was powerful lazy and comfortable\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to get up and cook\r\nbreakfast. Well, I was dozing off again when I thinks I hears a deep\r\nsound of \xe2\x80\x9cboom!\xe2\x80\x9d away up the river. I rouses up, and rests on my elbow\r\nand listens; pretty soon I hears it again. I hopped up, and went and\r\nlooked out at a hole in the leaves, and I see a bunch of smoke laying\r\non the water a long ways up\xe2\x80\x94about abreast the ferry. And there was the\r\nferryboat full of people floating along down. I knowed what was the\r\nmatter now. \xe2\x80\x9cBoom!\xe2\x80\x9d I see the white smoke squirt out of the ferryboat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nside. You see, they was firing cannon over the water, trying to make my\r\ncarcass come to the top.\r\n\r\nI was pretty hungry, but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t going to do for me to start a fire,\r\nbecause they might see the smoke. So I set there and watched the\r\ncannon-smoke and listened to the boom. The river was a mile wide there,\r\nand it always looks pretty on a summer morning\xe2\x80\x94so I was having a good\r\nenough time seeing them hunt for my remainders if I only had a bite to\r\neat. Well, then I happened to think how they always put quicksilver in\r\nloaves of bread and float them off, because they always go right to\r\nthe drownded carcass and stop there. So, says I, I\xe2\x80\x99ll keep a lookout,\r\nand if any of them\xe2\x80\x99s floating around after me I\xe2\x80\x99ll give them a show.\r\nI changed to the Illinois edge of the island to see what luck I could\r\nhave, and I warn\xe2\x80\x99t disappointed. A big double loaf come along, and I\r\nmost got it with a long stick, but my foot slipped and she floated out\r\nfurther. Of course I was where the current set in the closest to the\r\nshore\xe2\x80\x94I knowed enough for that. But by and by along comes another one,\r\nand this time I won. I took out the plug and shook out the little dab\r\nof quicksilver, and set my teeth in. It was \xe2\x80\x9cbaker\xe2\x80\x99s bread\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94what the\r\nquality eat; none of your low-down corn-pone.\r\n\r\nI got a good place amongst the leaves, and set there on a log, munching\r\nthe bread and watching the ferryboat, and very well satisfied. And then\r\nsomething struck me. I says, now I reckon the widow or the parson or\r\nsomebody prayed that this bread would find me, and here it has gone\r\nand done it. So there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no doubt but there is something in that\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94that is, there\xe2\x80\x99s something in it when a body like the widow or\r\nthe parson prays, but it don\xe2\x80\x99t work for me, and I reckon it don\xe2\x80\x99t work\r\nfor only just the right kind.\r\n\r\nI lit a pipe and had a good long smoke, and went on watching. The\r\nferryboat was floating with the current, and I allowed I\xe2\x80\x99d have a\r\nchance to see who was aboard when she come along, because she would\r\ncome in close, where the bread did. When she\xe2\x80\x99d got pretty well along\r\ndown towards me, I put out my pipe and went to where I fished out the\r\nbread, and laid down behind a log on the bank in a little open place.\r\nWhere the log forked I could peep through.\r\n\r\nBy and by she come along, and she drifted in so close that they could\r\n\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 run out a plank and walked ashore. Most everybody was on the boat.\r\nPap, and Judge Thatcher, and Bessie Thatcher, and Joe Harper, and Tom\r\nSawyer, and his old Aunt Polly, and Sid and Mary, and plenty more.\r\nEverybody was talking about the murder, but the captain broke in and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook sharp, now; the current sets in the closest here, and maybe he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwashed ashore and got tangled amongst the brush at the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge. I\r\nhope so, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t hope so. They all crowded up and leaned over the rails, nearly\r\nin my face, and kept still, watching with all their might. I could\r\nsee them first-rate, but they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see me. Then the captain sung\r\nout: \xe2\x80\x9cStand away!\xe2\x80\x9d and the cannon let off such a blast right before\r\nme that it made me deef with the noise and pretty near blind with the\r\nsmoke, and I judged I was gone. If they\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 had some bullets in, I\r\nreckon they\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got the corpse they was after. Well, I see I warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhurt, thanks to goodness. The boat floated on and went out of sight\r\naround the shoulder of the island. I could hear the booming now and\r\nthen, further and further off, and by and by, after an hour, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhear it no more. The island was three mile long. I judged they had got\r\nto the foot, and was giving it up. But they didn\xe2\x80\x99t yet awhile. They\r\nturned around the foot of the island and started up the channel on the\r\nMissouri side, under steam, and booming once in a while as they went. I\r\ncrossed over to that side and watched them. When they got abreast the\r\nhead of the island they quit shooting and dropped over to the Missouri\r\nshore and went home to the town.\r\n\r\nI knowed I was all right now. Nobody else would come a-hunting after\r\nme. I got my traps out of the canoe and made me a nice camp in the\r\nthick woods. I made a kind of a tent out of my blankets to put my\r\nthings under so the rain couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get at them. I catched a catfish\r\nand haggled him open with my saw, and towards sundown I started my\r\ncamp-fire and had supper. Then I set out a line to catch some fish for\r\nbreakfast.\r\n\r\nWhen it was dark I set by my camp-fire smoking, and feeling pretty well\r\nsatisfied; but by and by it got sort of lonesome, and so I went and set\r\non the bank and listened to the current swashing along, and counted the\r\nstars and drift-logs and rafts that come down, and then went to bed;\r\nthere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no better way to put in time when you are lonesome; you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t stay so, you soon get over it.\r\n\r\nAnd so for three days and nights. No difference\xe2\x80\x94just the same thing.\r\nBut the next day I went exploring around down through the island. I\r\nwas boss of it; it all belonged to me, so to say, and I wanted to know\r\nall about it; but mainly I wanted to put in the time. I found plenty\r\nstrawberries, ripe and prime; and green summer grapes, and green\r\nrazberries; and the green blackberries was just beginning to show. They\r\nwould all come handy by and by, I judged.\r\n\r\nWell, I went fooling along in the deep woods till I judged I warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfar from the foot of the island. I had my gun along, but I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t shot\r\nnothing; it was for protection; thought I would kill some game nigh\r\nhome. About this time I mighty near stepped on a good-sized snake, and\r\nit went sliding off through the grass and flowers, and I after it,\r\ntrying to get a shot at it. I clipped along, and all of a sudden I\r\nbounded right on to the ashes of a camp-fire that was still smoking.\r\n\r\nMy heart jumped up amongst my lungs. I never waited for to look\r\nfurther, but uncocked my gun and went sneaking back on my tiptoes as\r\nfast as ever I could. Every now and then I stopped a second amongst the\r\nthick leaves and listened, but my breath come so hard I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear\r\nnothing else. I slunk along another piece further, then listened again;\r\nand so on, and so on. If I see a stump, I took it for a man; if I trod\r\non a stick and broke it, it made me feel like a person had cut one of\r\nmy breaths in two and I only got half, and the short half, too.\r\n\r\nWhen I got to camp I warn\xe2\x80\x99t feeling very brash, there warn\xe2\x80\x99t much sand\r\nin my craw; but I says, this ain\xe2\x80\x99t no time to be fooling around. So I\r\ngot all my traps into my canoe again so as to have them out of sight,\r\nand I put out the fire and scattered the ashes around to look like an\r\nold last-year\xe2\x80\x99s camp, and then clumb a tree.\r\n\r\nI reckon I was up in the tree two hours; but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see nothing, I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t hear nothing\xe2\x80\x94I only _thought_ I heard and seen as much as a\r\nthousand things. Well, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay up there forever; so at last\r\nI got down, but I kept in the thick woods and on the lookout all the\r\ntime. All I could get to eat was berries and what was left over from\r\nbreakfast.\r\n\r\nBy the time it was night I was pretty hungry. So when it was good and\r\ndark I slid out from shore before moonrise and paddled over to the\r\nIllinois bank\xe2\x80\x94about a quarter of a mile. I went out in the woods and\r\ncooked a supper, and I had about made up my mind I would stay there\r\nall night when I hear a _plunkety-plunk_, _plunkety-plunk_, and says\r\nto myself, horses coming; and next I hear people\xe2\x80\x99s voices. I got\r\neverything into the canoe as quick as I could, and then went creeping\r\nthrough the woods to see what I could find out. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t got far when I\r\nhear a man say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe better camp here if we can find a good place; the horses is about\r\nbeat out. Let\xe2\x80\x99s look around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait, but shoved out and paddled away easy. I tied up in the\r\nold place, and reckoned I would sleep in the canoe.\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep much. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, somehow, for thinking. And every time\r\nI waked up I thought somebody had me by the neck. So the sleep didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndo me no good. By and by I says to myself, I can\xe2\x80\x99t live this way; I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\na-going to find out who it is that\xe2\x80\x99s here on the island with me; I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nfind it out or bust. Well, I felt better right off.\r\n\r\nSo I took my paddle and slid out from shore just a step or two, and\r\nthen let the canoe drop along down amongst the shadows. The moon was\r\nshining, and outside of the shadows it made it most as light as day.\r\nI poked along well on to an hour, everything still as rocks and sound\r\nasleep. Well, by this time I was most down to the foot of the island.\r\nA little ripply, cool breeze begun to blow, and that was as good as\r\nsaying the night was about done. I give her a turn with the paddle and\r\nbrung her nose to shore; then I got my gun and slipped out and into the\r\nedge of the woods. I sat down there on a log, and looked out through\r\nthe leaves. I see the moon go off watch, and the darkness begin to\r\nblanket the river. But in a little while I see a pale streak over the\r\ntree-tops, and knowed the day was coming. I went for it, cautious and slow. By\r\nand by I was close enough to have a look, and there laid a man on the\r\nground. It most give me the fantods. He had a blanket around his head,\r\nand his head was nearly in the fire. I set there behind a clump of\r\nbushes in about six foot of him, and kept my eyes on him steady. It was\r\ngetting gray daylight now. Pretty soon he gapped and stretched himself\r\nand hove off the blanket, and it was Miss Watson\xe2\x80\x99s Jim! I bet I was\r\nglad to see him. I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Jim!\xe2\x80\x9d and skipped out.\r\n\r\nHe bounced up and stared at me wild. Then he drops down on his knees,\r\nand puts his hands together and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoan\xe2\x80\x99 hurt me\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t! You go en git in de\r\nriver ag\xe2\x80\x99in, whah you b\xe2\x80\x99longs, en doan\xe2\x80\x99 do nuffn to Ole Jim, \xe2\x80\x99at \xe2\x80\x99uz\r\nalwuz yo\xe2\x80\x99 fren\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, I warn\xe2\x80\x99t long making him understand I warn\xe2\x80\x99t dead. Make up your camp-fire good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s de use er makin\xe2\x80\x99 up de camp-fire to cook strawbries en sich\r\ntruck? I think I could. How long you ben on de\r\nislan\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince the night I got killed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! W\xe2\x80\x99y, what has you lived on? But you got a gun. Oh, yes, you got a\r\ngun. Dat\xe2\x80\x99s good. Now you kill sumfn en I\xe2\x80\x99ll make up de fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo we went over to where the canoe was, and while he built a fire in\r\na grassy open place amongst the trees, I fetched meal and bacon and\r\ncoffee, and coffee-pot and frying-pan, and sugar and tin cups, and the\r\nnigger was set back considerable, because he reckoned it was all done\r\nwith witchcraft.
