Then\r\nwhen we had got pretty well stuffed, we laid off and lazied.\r\n\r\nBy and by Jim says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut looky here, Huck, who wuz it dat \xe2\x80\x99uz killed in dat shanty ef it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen I told him the whole thing, and he said it was smart. He said Tom\r\nSawyer couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get up no better plan than what I had. Then I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you come to be here, Jim, and how\xe2\x80\x99d you get here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked pretty uneasy, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t say nothing for a minute. Then he\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe I better not tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, dey\xe2\x80\x99s reasons. But you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 tell on me ef I \xe2\x80\x99uz to tell you,\r\nwould you, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlamed if I would, Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I b\xe2\x80\x99lieve you, Huck. I\xe2\x80\x94I _run off_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJim!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut mind, you said you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 tell\xe2\x80\x94you know you said you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntell, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I did. I said I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll stick to it. Honest _injun_,\r\nI will. People would call me a low-down Abolitionist and despise me for\r\nkeeping mum\xe2\x80\x94but that don\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to tell,\r\nand I ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going back there, anyways. So, now, le\xe2\x80\x99s know all about\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see, it \xe2\x80\x99uz dis way. Ole missus\xe2\x80\x94dat\xe2\x80\x99s Miss Watson\xe2\x80\x94she pecks\r\non me all de time, en treats me pooty rough, but she awluz said she\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99 sell me down to Orleans. But I noticed dey wuz a nigger trader\r\nroun\xe2\x80\x99 de place considable lately, en I begin to git oneasy. Well, one\r\nnight I creeps to de do\xe2\x80\x99 pooty late, en de do\xe2\x80\x99 warn\xe2\x80\x99t quite shet, en I\r\nhear old missus tell de widder she gwyne to sell me down to Orleans,\r\nbut she didn\xe2\x80\x99 want to, but she could git eight hund\xe2\x80\x99d dollars for me,\r\nen it \xe2\x80\x99uz sich a big stack o\xe2\x80\x99 money she couldn\xe2\x80\x99 resis\xe2\x80\x99. De widder she\r\ntry to git her to say she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it, but I never waited to hear de\r\nres\xe2\x80\x99. I lit out mighty quick, I tell you.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tuck out en shin down de hill, en \xe2\x80\x99spec to steal a skift \xe2\x80\x99long de\r\nsho\xe2\x80\x99 som\xe2\x80\x99ers \xe2\x80\x99bove de town, but dey wuz people a-stirring yit, so I hid\r\nin de ole tumbledown cooper shop on de bank to wait for everybody to go\r\n\xe2\x80\x99way. Well, I wuz dah all night. Dey wuz somebody roun\xe2\x80\x99 all de time.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Long \xe2\x80\x99bout six in de mawnin\xe2\x80\x99 skifts begin to go by, en \xe2\x80\x99bout eight er\r\nnine every skift dat went \xe2\x80\x99long wuz talkin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout how yo\xe2\x80\x99 pap come over\r\nto de town en say you\xe2\x80\x99s killed. Dese las\xe2\x80\x99 skifts wuz full o\xe2\x80\x99 ladies en\r\ngenlmen a-goin\xe2\x80\x99 over for to see de place. Sometimes dey\xe2\x80\x99d pull up at de\r\nsho\xe2\x80\x99 en take a res\xe2\x80\x99 b\xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99 dey started acrost, so by de talk I got to\r\nknow all \xe2\x80\x99bout de killin\xe2\x80\x99. I \xe2\x80\x99uz powerful sorry you\xe2\x80\x99s killed, Huck, but\r\nI ain\xe2\x80\x99t no mo\xe2\x80\x99 now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI laid dah under de shavin\xe2\x80\x99s all day. I \xe2\x80\x99uz hungry, but I warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nafeard; bekase I knowed ole missus en de widder wuz goin\xe2\x80\x99 to start to\r\nde camp-meet\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 right arter breakfas\xe2\x80\x99 en be gone all day, en dey knows\r\nI goes off wid de cattle \xe2\x80\x99bout daylight, so dey wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99spec to see\r\nme roun\xe2\x80\x99 de place, en so dey wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 miss me tell arter dark in de\r\nevenin\xe2\x80\x99. De yuther servants wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 miss me, kase dey\xe2\x80\x99d shin out en\r\ntake holiday soon as de ole folks \xe2\x80\x99uz out\xe2\x80\x99n de way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, when it come dark I tuck out up de river road, en went \xe2\x80\x99bout two\r\nmile er more to whah dey warn\xe2\x80\x99t no houses. I\xe2\x80\x99d made up my mine \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\nwhat I\xe2\x80\x99s a-gwyne to do. You see, ef I kep\xe2\x80\x99 on tryin\xe2\x80\x99 to git away afoot,\r\nde dogs \xe2\x80\x99ud track me; ef I stole a skift to cross over, dey\xe2\x80\x99d miss dat\r\nskift, you see, en dey\xe2\x80\x99d know \xe2\x80\x99bout whah I\xe2\x80\x99d lan\xe2\x80\x99 on de yuther side, en\r\nwhah to pick up my track. So I says, a raff is what I\xe2\x80\x99s arter; it doan\xe2\x80\x99\r\n_make_ no track.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see a light a-comin\xe2\x80\x99 roun\xe2\x80\x99 de p\xe2\x80\x99int bymeby, so I wade\xe2\x80\x99 in en shove\xe2\x80\x99\r\na log ahead o\xe2\x80\x99 me en swum more\xe2\x80\x99n half-way acrost de river, en got in\r\n\xe2\x80\x99mongst de driftwood, en kep\xe2\x80\x99 my head down low, en kinder swum agin de\r\ncurrent tell de raff come along. Den I swum to de stern uv it en tuck\r\na-holt. It clouded up en \xe2\x80\x99uz pooty dark for a little while. So I clumb\r\nup en laid down on de planks. De men \xe2\x80\x99uz all \xe2\x80\x99way yonder in de middle,\r\nwhah de lantern wuz. De river wuz a-risin\xe2\x80\x99, en dey wuz a good current;\r\nso I reck\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99at by fo\xe2\x80\x99 in de mawnin\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99d be twenty-five mile down de\r\nriver, en den I\xe2\x80\x99d slip in jis b\xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99 daylight en swim asho\xe2\x80\x99, en take to\r\nde woods on de Illinois side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I didn\xe2\x80\x99 have no luck. When we \xe2\x80\x99uz mos\xe2\x80\x99 down to de head er de\r\nislan\xe2\x80\x99 a man begin to come aft wid de lantern. I see it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use\r\nfer to wait, so I slid overboard en struck out fer de islan\xe2\x80\x99. Well, I\r\nhad a notion I could lan\xe2\x80\x99 mos\xe2\x80\x99 anywhers, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94bank too bluff.\r\nI \xe2\x80\x99uz mos\xe2\x80\x99 to de foot er de islan\xe2\x80\x99 b\xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99 I foun\xe2\x80\x99 a good place. I went\r\ninto de woods en jedged I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 fool wid raffs no mo\xe2\x80\x99, long as dey\r\nmove de lantern roun\xe2\x80\x99 so. I had my pipe en a plug er dog-leg en some\r\nmatches in my cap, en dey warn\xe2\x80\x99t wet, so I \xe2\x80\x99uz all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so you ain\xe2\x80\x99t had no meat nor bread to eat all this time? Why\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t you get mud-turkles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow you gwyne to git \xe2\x80\x99m? You can\xe2\x80\x99t slip up on um en grab um; en how\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na body gwyne to hit um wid a rock? How could a body do it in de night?\r\nEn I warn\xe2\x80\x99t gwyne to show mysef on de bank in de daytime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s so. You\xe2\x80\x99ve had to keep in the woods all the time, of\r\ncourse. Did you hear \xe2\x80\x99em shooting the cannon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. I knowed dey was arter you. I see um go by heah\xe2\x80\x94watched um\r\nthoo de bushes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome young birds come along, flying a yard or two at a time and\r\nlighting. Jim said it was a sign it was going to rain. He said it was a\r\nsign when young chickens flew that way, and so he reckoned it was the\r\nsame way when young birds done it. I was going to catch some of them,\r\nbut Jim wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me. He said it was death. He said his father laid\r\nmighty sick once, and some of them catched a bird, and his old granny\r\nsaid his father would die, and he did.\r\n\r\nAnd Jim said you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t count the things you are going to cook for\r\ndinner, because that would bring bad luck. The same if you shook the\r\ntablecloth after sundown. And he said if a man owned a beehive and that\r\nman died, the bees must be told about it before sun-up next morning,\r\nor else the bees would all weaken down and quit work and die. Jim said\r\nbees wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sting idiots; but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe that, because I had\r\ntried them lots of times myself, and they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sting me.\r\n\r\nI had heard about some of these things before, but not all of them. Jim\r\nknowed all kinds of signs. He said he knowed most everything. I said it\r\nlooked to me like all the signs was about bad luck, and so I asked him\r\nif there warn\xe2\x80\x99t any good-luck signs. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMighty few\xe2\x80\x94an\xe2\x80\x99 _dey_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use to a body. What you want to know\r\nwhen good luck\xe2\x80\x99s a-comin\xe2\x80\x99 for? Want to keep it off?\xe2\x80\x9d And he said: \xe2\x80\x9cEf\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99s got hairy arms en a hairy breas\xe2\x80\x99, it\xe2\x80\x99s a sign dat you\xe2\x80\x99s a-gwyne\r\nto be rich. Well, dey\xe2\x80\x99s some use in a sign like dat, \xe2\x80\x99kase it\xe2\x80\x99s so fur\r\nahead. You see, maybe you\xe2\x80\x99s got to be po\xe2\x80\x99 a long time fust, en so you\r\nmight git discourage\xe2\x80\x99 en kill yo\xe2\x80\x99sef \xe2\x80\x99f you didn\xe2\x80\x99 know by de sign dat\r\nyou gwyne to be rich bymeby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you got hairy arms and a hairy breast, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s de use to ax dat question? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see I has?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, are you rich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I ben rich wunst, and gwyne to be rich ag\xe2\x80\x99in. Wunst I had\r\nfoteen dollars, but I tuck to specalat\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99, en got busted out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you speculate in, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, fust I tackled stock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat kind of stock?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, live stock\xe2\x80\x94cattle, you know. I put ten dollars in a cow. But I\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99 gwyne to resk no mo\xe2\x80\x99 money in stock. De cow up \xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 died on my\r\nhan\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you lost the ten dollars.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t lose it all. I on\xe2\x80\x99y los\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout nine of it. I sole de hide\r\nen taller for a dollar en ten cents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had five dollars and ten cents left. Did you speculate any more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. You know that one-laigged nigger dat b\xe2\x80\x99longs to old Misto\r\nBradish? Well, he sot up a bank, en say anybody dat put in a dollar\r\nwould git fo\xe2\x80\x99 dollars mo\xe2\x80\x99 at de en\xe2\x80\x99 er de year. Well, all de niggers\r\nwent in, but dey didn\xe2\x80\x99t have much. I wuz de on\xe2\x80\x99y one dat had much. So\r\nI stuck out for mo\xe2\x80\x99 dan fo\xe2\x80\x99 dollars, en I said \xe2\x80\x99f I didn\xe2\x80\x99 git it I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nstart a bank mysef. Well, o\xe2\x80\x99 course dat nigger want\xe2\x80\x99 to keep me out er\r\nde business, bekase he says dey warn\xe2\x80\x99t business \xe2\x80\x99nough for two banks,\r\nso he say I could put in my five dollars en he pay me thirty-five at de\r\nen\xe2\x80\x99 er de year.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I done it. Den I reck\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99d I\xe2\x80\x99d inves\xe2\x80\x99 de thirty-five dollars right\r\noff en keep things a-movin\xe2\x80\x99. Dey wuz a nigger name\xe2\x80\x99 Bob, dat had\r\nketched a wood-flat, en his marster didn\xe2\x80\x99 know it; en I bought it\r\noff\xe2\x80\x99n him en told him to take de thirty-five dollars when de en\xe2\x80\x99 er de\r\nyear come; but somebody stole de wood-flat dat night, en nex\xe2\x80\x99 day de\r\none-laigged nigger say de bank\xe2\x80\x99s busted. So dey didn\xe2\x80\x99 none uv us git no\r\nmoney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you do with the ten cents, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I \xe2\x80\x99uz gwyne to spen\xe2\x80\x99 it, but I had a dream, en de dream tole me\r\nto give it to a nigger name\xe2\x80\x99 Balum\xe2\x80\x94Balum\xe2\x80\x99s Ass dey call him for short;\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s one er dem chuckleheads, you know. But he\xe2\x80\x99s lucky, dey say, en I\r\nsee I warn\xe2\x80\x99t lucky. De dream say let Balum inves\xe2\x80\x99 de ten cents en he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nmake a raise for me. Well, Balum he tuck de money, en when he wuz in\r\nchurch he hear de preacher say dat whoever give to de po\xe2\x80\x99 len\xe2\x80\x99 to de\r\nLord, en boun\xe2\x80\x99 to git his money back a hund\xe2\x80\x99d times. So Balum he tuck\r\nen give de ten cents to de po\xe2\x80\x99, en laid low to see what wuz gwyne to\r\ncome of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what did come of it, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNuffn never come of it. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99 manage to k\xe2\x80\x99leck dat money no way;\r\nen Balum he couldn\xe2\x80\x99. I ain\xe2\x80\x99 gwyne to len\xe2\x80\x99 no mo\xe2\x80\x99 money \xe2\x80\x99dout I see de\r\nsecurity. Boun\xe2\x80\x99 to git yo\xe2\x80\x99 money back a hund\xe2\x80\x99d times, de preacher says!\r\nEf I could git de ten _cents_ back, I\xe2\x80\x99d call it squah, en be glad er de\r\nchanst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s all right anyway, Jim, long as you\xe2\x80\x99re going to be rich\r\nagain some time or other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; en I\xe2\x80\x99s rich now, come to look at it. I owns mysef, en I\xe2\x80\x99s wuth\r\neight hund\xe2\x80\x99d dollars. I wisht I had de money, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 want no mo\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\nI wanted to go and look at a place right about the middle of the island\r\nthat I\xe2\x80\x99d found when I was exploring; so we started and soon got to it,\r\nbecause the island was only three miles long and a quarter of a mile\r\nwide.\r\n\r\nThis place was a tolerable long, steep hill or ridge about forty foot\r\nhigh. We had a rough time getting to the top, the sides was so steep\r\nand the bushes so thick. We tramped and clumb around all over it, and\r\nby and by found a good big cavern in the rock, most up to the top on\r\nthe side towards Illinois. The cavern was as big as two or three rooms\r\nbunched together, and Jim could stand up straight in it. It was cool in\r\nthere. Jim was for putting our traps in there right away, but I said we\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t want to be climbing up and down there all the time.\r\n\r\nJim said if we had the canoe hid in a good place, and had all the\r\ntraps in the cavern, we could rush there if anybody was to come to the\r\nisland, and they would never find us without dogs. And, besides, he\r\nsaid them little birds had said it was going to rain, and did I want\r\nthe things to get wet?\r\n\r\nSo we went back and got the canoe, and paddled up abreast the cavern,\r\nand lugged all the traps up there. Then we hunted up a place close by\r\nto hide the canoe in, amongst the thick willows. We took some fish off\r\nof the lines and set them again, and begun to get ready for dinner.\r\n\r\nThe door of the cavern was big enough to roll a hogshead in, and on one\r\nside of the door the floor stuck out a little bit, and was flat and a\r\ngood place to build a fire on. So we built it there and cooked dinner.\r\n\r\nWe spread the blankets inside for a carpet, and eat our dinner in\r\nthere. We put all the other things handy at the back of the cavern.\r\nPretty soon it darkened up, and begun to thunder and lighten; so the\r\nbirds was right about it. Directly it begun to rain, and it rained\r\nlike all fury, too, and I never see the wind blow so. It was one of\r\nthese regular summer storms. It would get so dark that it looked all\r\nblue-black outside, and lovely; and the rain would thrash along by so\r\nthick that the trees off a little ways looked dim and spider-webby;\r\nand here would come a blast of wind that would bend the trees down and\r\nturn up the pale underside of the leaves; and then a perfect ripper of\r\na gust would follow along and set the branches to tossing their arms\r\nas if they was just wild; and next, when it was just about the bluest\r\nand blackest\xe2\x80\x94_fst!_ it was as bright as glory, and you\xe2\x80\x99d have a little\r\nglimpse of tree-tops a-plunging about away off yonder in the storm,\r\nhundreds of yards further than you could see before; dark as sin again\r\nin a second, and now you\xe2\x80\x99d hear the thunder let go with an awful crash,\r\nand then go rumbling, grumbling, tumbling, down the sky towards the\r\nunder side of the world, like rolling empty barrels down-stairs\xe2\x80\x94where\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s long stairs and they bounce a good deal, you know.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJim, this is nice,\xe2\x80\x9d I says. \xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want to be nowhere else but\r\nhere. Pass me along another hunk of fish and some hot corn-bread.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 ben here \xe2\x80\x99f it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 ben for Jim. You\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 ben down dah in de woods widout any dinner, en gittin\xe2\x80\x99 mos\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndrownded, too; dat you would, honey. Chickens knows when it\xe2\x80\x99s gwyne to\r\nrain, en so do de birds, chile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe river went on raising and raising for ten or twelve days, till at\r\nlast it was over the banks. The water was three or four foot deep on\r\nthe island in the low places and on the Illinois bottom. On that side\r\nit was a good many miles wide, but on the Missouri side it was the same\r\nold distance across\xe2\x80\x94a half a mile\xe2\x80\x94because the Missouri shore was just a\r\nwall of high bluffs.\r\n\r\nDaytimes we paddled all over the island in the canoe. It was mighty\r\ncool and shady in the deep woods, even if the sun was blazing outside.\r\nWe went winding in and out amongst the trees, and sometimes the vines\r\nhung so thick we had to back away and go some other way. Well, on every\r\nold broken-down tree you could see rabbits and snakes and such things;\r\nand when the island had been overflowed a day or two they got so tame,\r\non account of being hungry, that you could paddle right up and put your\r\nhand on them if you wanted to; but not the snakes and turtles\xe2\x80\x94they\r\nwould slide off in the water. The ridge our cavern was in was full of\r\nthem. We could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 had pets enough if we\xe2\x80\x99d wanted them.\r\n\r\nOne night we catched a little section of a lumber-raft\xe2\x80\x94nice pine\r\nplanks. It was twelve foot wide and about fifteen or sixteen foot long,\r\nand the top stood above water six or seven inches\xe2\x80\x94a solid, level floor.\r\nWe could see saw-logs go by in the daylight sometimes, but we let them\r\ngo; we didn\xe2\x80\x99t show ourselves in daylight.\r\n\r\nAnother night when we was up at the head of the island, just before\r\ndaylight, here comes a frame-house down, on the west side. She was\r\na two-story, and tilted over considerable. We paddled out and got\r\naboard\xe2\x80\x94clumb in at an up-stairs window. But it was too dark to see yet,\r\nso we made the canoe fast and set in her to wait for daylight.\r\n\r\nThe light begun to come before we got to the foot of the island. Then\r\nwe looked in at the window. We could make out a bed, and a table, and\r\ntwo old chairs, and lots of things around about on the floor, and there\r\nwas clothes hanging against the wall. There was something laying on the\r\nfloor in the far corner that looked like a man. So Jim says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it didn\xe2\x80\x99t budge. So I hollered again, and then Jim says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe man ain\xe2\x80\x99t asleep\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s dead. You hold still\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll go en see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went, and bent down and looked, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a dead man. Yes, indeedy; naked, too. He\xe2\x80\x99s ben shot in de back. I\r\nreck\xe2\x80\x99n he\xe2\x80\x99s ben dead two er three days. Come in, Huck, but doan\xe2\x80\x99 look\r\nat his face\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s too gashly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t look at him at all. Jim throwed some old rags over him, but\r\nhe needn\xe2\x80\x99t done it; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to see him. There was heaps of old\r\ngreasy cards scattered around over the floor, and old whisky-bottles,\r\nand a couple of masks made out of black cloth; and all over the walls\r\nwas the ignorantest kind of words and pictures made with charcoal.\r\nThere was two old dirty calico dresses, and a sun-bonnet, and some\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x99s underclothes hanging against the wall, and some men\xe2\x80\x99s clothing,\r\ntoo. We put the lot into the canoe\xe2\x80\x94it might come good. There was a\r\nboy\xe2\x80\x99s old speckled straw hat on the floor; I took that, too. And there\r\nwas a bottle that had had milk in it, and it had a rag stopper for a\r\nbaby to suck. We would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 took the bottle, but it was broke. There was\r\na seedy old chest, and an old hair trunk with the hinges broke. They\r\nstood open, but there warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing left in them that was any account.\r\nThe way things was scattered about we reckoned the people left in a\r\nhurry, and warn\xe2\x80\x99t fixed so as to carry off most of their stuff.\r\n\r\nWe got an old tin lantern, and a butcher-knife without any handle,\r\nand a bran-new Barlow knife worth two bits in any store, and a lot of\r\ntallow candles, and a tin candlestick, and a gourd, and a tin cup,\r\nand a ratty old bedquilt off the bed, and a reticule with needles and\r\npins and beeswax and buttons and thread and all such truck in it, and\r\na hatchet and some nails, and a fish-line as thick as my little finger\r\nwith some monstrous hooks on it, and a roll of buckskin, and a leather\r\ndog-collar, and a horseshoe, and some vials of medicine that didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhave no label on them; and just as we was leaving I found a tolerable\r\ngood currycomb, and Jim he found a ratty old fiddle-bow, and a wooden\r\nleg. The straps was broke off of it, but, barring that, it was a good\r\nenough leg, though it was too long for me and not long enough for Jim,\r\nand we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find the other one, though we hunted all around.\r\n\r\nAnd so, take it all around, we made a good haul. When we was ready\r\nto shove off we was a quarter of a mile below the island, and it was\r\npretty broad day; so I made Jim lay down in the canoe and cover up with\r\nthe quilt, because if he set up people could tell he was a nigger a\r\ngood ways off. I paddled over to the Illinois shore, and drifted down\r\nmost a half a mile doing it. I crept up the dead water under the bank,\r\nand hadn\xe2\x80\x99t no accidents and didn\xe2\x80\x99t see nobody. We got home all safe.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast I wanted to talk about the dead man and guess out how\r\nhe come to be killed, but Jim didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to. He said it would fetch\r\nbad luck; and besides, he said, he might come and ha\xe2\x80\x99nt us; he said a\r\nman that warn\xe2\x80\x99t buried was more likely to go a-ha\xe2\x80\x99nting around than\r\none that was planted and comfortable. That sounded pretty reasonable,\r\nso I didn\xe2\x80\x99t say no more; but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep from studying over it and\r\nwishing I knowed who shot the man, and what they done it for.\r\n\r\nWe rummaged the clothes we\xe2\x80\x99d got, and found eight dollars in silver\r\nsewed up in the lining of an old blanket overcoat. Jim said he reckoned\r\nthe people in that house stole the coat, because if they\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 knowed\r\nthe money was there they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 left it. I said I reckoned they\r\nkilled him, too; but Jim didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to talk about that. I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you think it\xe2\x80\x99s bad luck; but what did you say when I fetched\r\nin the snake-skin that I found on the top of the ridge day before\r\nyesterday? You said it was the worst bad luck in the world to touch a\r\nsnake-skin with my hands. Well, here\xe2\x80\x99s your bad luck! We\xe2\x80\x99ve raked in\r\nall this truck and eight dollars besides. I wish we could have some bad\r\nluck like this every day, Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever you mind, honey, never you mind. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you git too peart. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na-comin\xe2\x80\x99. Mind I tell you, it\xe2\x80\x99s a-comin\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt did come, too. It was a Tuesday that we had that talk. Well, after\r\ndinner Friday we was laying around in the grass at the upper end of the\r\nridge, and got out of tobacco. I went to the cavern to get some, and\r\nfound a rattlesnake in there. I killed him, and curled him up on the\r\nfoot of Jim\xe2\x80\x99s blanket, ever so natural, thinking there\xe2\x80\x99d be some fun\r\nwhen Jim found him there. Well, by night I forgot all about the snake,\r\nand when Jim flung himself down on the blanket while I struck a light\r\nthe snake\xe2\x80\x99s mate was there, and bit him.\r\n\r\nHe jumped up yelling, and the first thing the light showed was the\r\nvarmint curled up and ready for another spring. I laid him out in a\r\nsecond with a stick, and Jim grabbed pap\xe2\x80\x99s whisky-jug and begun to pour\r\nit down.\r\n\r\nHe was barefooted, and the snake bit him right on the heel. That all\r\ncomes of my being such a fool as to not remember that wherever you\r\nleave a dead snake its mate always comes there and curls around it.\r\nJim told me to chop off the snake\xe2\x80\x99s head and throw it away, and then\r\nskin the body and roast a piece of it. I done it, and he eat it and\r\nsaid it would help cure him. He made me take off the rattles and tie\r\nthem around his wrist, too. He said that that would help. Then I slid\r\nout quiet and throwed the snakes clear away amongst the bushes; for I\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t going to let Jim find out it was all my fault, not if I could\r\nhelp it.\r\n\r\nJim sucked and sucked at the jug, and now and then he got out of his\r\nhead and pitched around and yelled; but every time he come to himself\r\nhe went to sucking at the jug again. His foot swelled up pretty big,\r\nand so did his leg; but by and by the drunk begun to come, and so I\r\njudged he was all right; but I\xe2\x80\x99d druther been bit with a snake than\r\npap\xe2\x80\x99s whisky.\r\n\r\nJim was laid up for four days and nights. Then the swelling was all\r\ngone and he was around again. I made up my mind I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever take\r\na-holt of a snake-skin again with my hands, now that I see what had\r\ncome of it. Jim said he reckoned I would believe him next time. And\r\nhe said that handling a snake-skin was such awful bad luck that maybe\r\nwe hadn\xe2\x80\x99t got to the end of it yet. He said he druther see the new\r\nmoon over his left shoulder as much as a thousand times than take up a\r\nsnake-skin in his hand. Well, I was getting to feel that way myself,\r\nthough I\xe2\x80\x99ve always reckoned that looking at the new moon over your left\r\nshoulder is one of the carelessest and foolishest things a body can\r\ndo. Old Hank Bunker done it once, and bragged about it; and in less\r\nthan two years he got drunk and fell off of the shot-tower, and spread\r\nhimself out so that he was just a kind of a layer, as you may say; and\r\nthey slid him edgeways between two barn doors for a coffin, and buried\r\nhim so, so they say, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see it. Pap told me. But anyway it\r\nall come of looking at the moon that way, like a fool.\r\n\r\nWell, the days went along, and the river went down between its banks\r\nagain; and about the first thing we done was to bait one of the big\r\nhooks with a skinned rabbit and set it and catch a catfish that was\r\nas big as a man, being six foot two inches long, and weighed over two\r\nhundred pounds. We couldn\xe2\x80\x99t handle him, of course; he would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 flung\r\nus into Illinois. We just set there and watched him rip and tear around\r\ntill he drownded. We found a brass button in his stomach and a round\r\nball, and lots of rubbage. We split the ball open with the hatchet,\r\nand there was a spool in it. Jim said he\xe2\x80\x99d had it there a long time,\r\nto coat it over so and make a ball of it. It was as big a fish as was\r\never catched in the Mississippi, I reckon. Jim said he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever seen\r\na bigger one. He would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been worth a good deal over at the village.\r\nThey peddle out such a fish as that by the pound in the market-house\r\nthere; everybody buys some of him; his meat\xe2\x80\x99s as white as snow and\r\nmakes a good fry.\r\n\r\nNext morning I said it was getting slow and dull, and I wanted to get\r\na stirring-up some way. I said I reckoned I would slip over the river\r\nand find out what was going on. Jim liked that notion; but he said I\r\nmust go in the dark and look sharp. Then he studied it over and said,\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I put on some of them old things and dress up like a girl?\r\nThat was a good notion, too. So we shortened up one of the calico\r\ngowns, and I turned up my trouser-legs to my knees and got into it. Jim\r\nhitched it behind with the hooks, and it was a fair fit. I put on the\r\nsun-bonnet and tied it under my chin, and then for a body to look in\r\nand see my face was like looking down a joint of stove-pipe. Jim said\r\nnobody would know me, even in the daytime, hardly. I practised around\r\nall day to get the hang of the things, and by and by I could do pretty\r\nwell in them, only Jim said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t walk like a girl; and he said\r\nI must quit pulling up my gown to get at my britches-pocket. I took\r\nnotice, and done better.\r\n\r\nI started up the Illinois shore in the canoe just after dark.\r\n\r\nI started across to the town from a little below the ferry-landing,\r\nand the drift of the current fetched me in at the bottom of the town.\r\nI tied up and started along the bank. There was a light burning in a\r\nlittle shanty that hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been lived in for a long time, and I wondered\r\nwho had took up quarters there. I slipped up and peeped in at the\r\nwindow. There was a woman about forty year old in there knitting by\r\na candle that was on a pine table. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know her face; she was a\r\nstranger, for you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t start a face in that town that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow. Now this was lucky, because I was weakening; I was getting\r\nafraid I had come; people might know my voice and find me out. But if\r\nthis woman had been in such a little town two days she could tell me\r\nall I wanted to know; so I knocked at the door, and made up my mind I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t forget I was a girl.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d says the woman, and I did. She says: \xe2\x80\x9cTake a cheer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI done it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve walked all the way and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m all tired out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHungry, too, I reckon. I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho done it? We\xe2\x80\x99ve heard considerable about these goings-on down in\r\nHookerville, but we don\xe2\x80\x99t know who \xe2\x80\x99twas that killed Huck Finn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I reckon there\xe2\x80\x99s a right smart chance of people _here_ that \xe2\x80\x99d\r\nlike to know who killed him. But before night they changed around and judged it\r\nwas done by a runaway nigger named Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy _he_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI stopped. I reckoned I better keep still. She run on, and never\r\nnoticed I had put in at all:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe nigger run off the very night Huck Finn was killed. So there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\na reward out for him\xe2\x80\x94three hundred dollars. And there\xe2\x80\x99s a reward out\r\nfor old Finn, too\xe2\x80\x94two hundred dollars. Before night\r\nthey wanted to lynch him, but he was gone, you see. Well, next day\r\nthey found out the nigger was gone; they found out he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ben seen\r\nsence ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock the night the murder was done. So then they put it on\r\nhim, you see; and while they was full of it, next day, back comes old\r\nFinn, and went boo-hooing to Judge Thatcher to get money to hunt for\r\nthe nigger all over Illinois with. Well, he\r\nhain\xe2\x80\x99t come back sence, and they ain\xe2\x80\x99t looking for him back till this\r\nthing blows over a little, for people thinks now that he killed his boy\r\nand fixed things so folks would think robbers done it, and then he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nget Huck\xe2\x80\x99s money without having to bother a long time with a lawsuit.\r\nPeople do say he warn\xe2\x80\x99t any too good to do it. Pretty soon she says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you say your name was, honey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cM\xe2\x80\x94Mary Williams.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomehow it didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to me that I said it was Mary before, so I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t look up\xe2\x80\x94seemed to me I said it was Sarah; so I felt sort of\r\ncornered, and was afeard maybe I was looking it, too. I wished the\r\nwoman would say something more; the longer she set still the uneasier I\r\nwas. But now she says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoney, I thought you said it was Sarah when you first come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes\xe2\x80\x99m, I did. Sarah Mary Williams. Sarah\xe2\x80\x99s my first name. Some\r\ncalls me Sarah, some calls me Mary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, that\xe2\x80\x99s the way of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was feeling better then, but I wished I was out of there, anyway. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look up yet.\r\n\r\nWell, the woman fell to talking about how hard times was, and how\r\npoor they had to live, and how the rats was as free as if they owned\r\nthe place, and so forth and so on, and then I got easy again. She was\r\nright about the rats. You\xe2\x80\x99d see one stick his nose out of a hole in the\r\ncorner every little while. She said she had to have things handy to\r\nthrow at them when she was alone, or they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give her no peace.\r\nShe showed me a bar of lead twisted up into a knot, and said she was a\r\ngood shot with it generly, but she\xe2\x80\x99d wrenched her arm a day or two ago,\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t know whether she could throw true now. But she watched for a\r\nchance, and directly banged away at a rat; but she missed him wide, and\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cOuch!\xe2\x80\x9d it hurt her arm so. Then she told me to try for the next\r\none. I wanted to be getting away before the old man got back, but of\r\ncourse I didn\xe2\x80\x99t let on. I got the thing, and the first rat that showed\r\nhis nose I let drive, and if he\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 stayed where he was he\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been\r\na tolerable sick rat. She said that was first-rate, and she reckoned I\r\nwould hive the next one. She went and got the lump of lead and fetched\r\nit back, and brought along a hank of yarn which she wanted me to help\r\nher with. I held up my two hands and she put the hank over them, and\r\nwent on talking about her and her husband\xe2\x80\x99s matters. But she broke off\r\nto say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep your eye on the rats. You better have the lead in your lap,\r\nhandy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo she dropped the lump into my lap just at that moment, and I clapped\r\nmy legs together on it and she went on talking. But only about a\r\nminute. Then she took off the hank and looked me straight in the face,\r\nand very pleasant, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, now, what\xe2\x80\x99s your real name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWh-hat, mum?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your real name? Is it Bill, or Tom, or Bob?\xe2\x80\x94or what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI reckon I shook like a leaf, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know hardly what to do. But\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease to don\xe2\x80\x99t poke fun at a poor girl like me, mum. If I\xe2\x80\x99m in the\r\nway here, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you won\xe2\x80\x99t. Set down and stay where you are. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to\r\nhurt you, and I ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to tell on you, nuther. You just tell me\r\nyour secret, and trust me. I\xe2\x80\x99ll keep it; and, what\xe2\x80\x99s more, I\xe2\x80\x99ll help\r\nyou. So\xe2\x80\x99ll my old man if you want him to. You see, you\xe2\x80\x99re a runaway\r\n\xe2\x80\x99prentice, that\xe2\x80\x99s all. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t anything. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t no harm in it.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been treated bad, and you made up your mind to cut. Bless you,\r\nchild, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell on you. Tell me all about it now, that\xe2\x80\x99s a good\r\nboy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I said it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be no use to try to play it any longer, and I\r\nwould just make a clean breast and tell her everything, but she mustn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngo back on her promise. Then I told her my father and mother was dead,\r\nand the law had bound me out to a mean old farmer in the country thirty\r\nmile back from the river, and he treated me so bad I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand it\r\nno longer; he went away to be gone a couple of days, and so I took my\r\nchance and stole some of his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s old clothes and cleared out,\r\nand I had been three nights coming the thirty miles. I traveled nights,\r\nand hid daytimes and slept, and the bag of bread and meat I carried\r\nfrom home lasted me all the way, and I had a-plenty. I said I believed\r\nmy uncle Abner Moore would take care of me, and so that was why I\r\nstruck out for this town of Goshen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoshen, child? This ain\xe2\x80\x99t Goshen. This is St. Petersburg. Goshen\xe2\x80\x99s ten\r\nmile further up the river. Who told you this was Goshen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, a man I met at daybreak this morning, just as I was going to turn\r\ninto the woods for my regular sleep. He told me when the roads forked I\r\nmust take the right hand, and five mile would fetch me to Goshen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was drunk, I reckon. He told you just exactly wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he did act like he was drunk, but it ain\xe2\x80\x99t no matter now. I got\r\nto be moving along. I\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch Goshen before daylight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold on a minute. I\xe2\x80\x99ll put you up a snack to eat. You might want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo she put me up a snack, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, when a cow\xe2\x80\x99s laying down, which end of her gets up first? Answer\r\nup prompt now\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t stop to study over it. Which end gets up first?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe hind end, mum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, a horse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe for\xe2\x80\x99rard end, mum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich side of a tree does the moss grow on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNorth side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf fifteen cows is browsing on a hillside, how many of them eats with\r\ntheir heads pointed the same direction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe whole fifteen, mum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I reckon you _have_ lived in the country. I thought maybe you\r\nwas trying to hocus me again. What\xe2\x80\x99s your real name, now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge Peters, mum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, try to remember it, George. Don\xe2\x80\x99t forget and tell me it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nElexander before you go, and then get out by saying it\xe2\x80\x99s George\r\nElexander when I catch you. And don\xe2\x80\x99t go about women in that old\r\ncalico. You do a girl tolerable poor, but you might fool men, maybe.\r\nBless you, child, when you set out to thread a needle don\xe2\x80\x99t hold the\r\nthread still and fetch the needle up to it; hold the needle still and\r\npoke the thread at it; that\xe2\x80\x99s the way a woman most always does, but a\r\nman always does t\xe2\x80\x99other way. And when you throw at a rat or anything,\r\nhitch yourself up a-tiptoe and fetch your hand up over your head as\r\nawkward as you can, and miss your rat about six or seven foot. Throw\r\nstiff-armed from the shoulder, like there was a pivot there for it\r\nto turn on, like a girl; not from the wrist and elbow, with your arm\r\nout to one side, like a boy. And, mind you, when a girl tries to\r\ncatch anything in her lap she throws her knees apart; she don\xe2\x80\x99t clap\r\nthem together, the way you did when you catched the lump of lead.\r\nWhy, I spotted you for a boy when you was threading the needle; and I\r\ncontrived the other things just to make certain. Now trot along to your\r\nuncle, Sarah Mary Williams George Elexander Peters, and if you get into\r\ntrouble you send word to Mrs. Judith Loftus, which is me, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\r\nwhat I can to get you out of it. Keep the river road all the way, and\r\nnext time you tramp take shoes and socks with you. The river road\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nrocky one, and your feet \xe2\x80\x99ll be in a condition when you get to Goshen,\r\nI reckon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI went up the bank about fifty yards, and then I doubled on my tracks\r\nand slipped back to where my canoe was, a good piece below the house.\r\nI jumped in, and was off in a hurry. I went up-stream far enough to\r\nmake the head of the island, and then started across. I took off the\r\nsun-bonnet, for I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want no blinders on then. When I was about the\r\nmiddle I heard the clock begin to strike, so I stops and listens; the\r\nsound come faint over the water but clear\xe2\x80\x94eleven. When I struck the\r\nhead of the island I never waited to blow, though I was most winded,\r\nbut I shoved right into the timber where my old camp used to be, and\r\nstarted a good fire there on a high and dry spot.\r\n\r\nThen I jumped in the canoe and dug out for our place, a mile and a half\r\nbelow, as hard as I could go. I landed, and slopped through the timber\r\nand up the ridge and into the cavern. There Jim laid, sound asleep on\r\nthe ground. I roused him out and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGit up and hump yourself, Jim! There ain\xe2\x80\x99t a minute to lose. They\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nafter us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim never asked no questions, he never said a word; but the way he\r\nworked for the next half an hour showed about how he was scared. By\r\nthat time everything we had in the world was on our raft, and she was\r\nready to be shoved out from the willow cove where she was hid. We put\r\nout the camp-fire at the cavern the first thing, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t show a\r\ncandle outside after that.\r\n\r\nI took the canoe out from the shore a little piece, and took a look;\r\nbut if there was a boat around I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see it, for stars and shadows\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t good to see by. Then we got out the raft and slipped along down\r\nin the shade, past the foot of the island dead still\xe2\x80\x94never saying a\r\nword.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII\r\n\r\n\r\nIt must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been close on to one o\xe2\x80\x99clock when we got below the island\r\nat last, and the raft did seem to go mighty slow. If a boat was to come\r\nalong we was going to take to the canoe and break for the Illinois\r\nshore; and it was well a boat didn\xe2\x80\x99t come, for we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever thought\r\nto put the gun in the canoe, or a fishing-line, or anything to eat. We\r\nwas in ruther too much of a sweat to think of so many things. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngood judgment to put _everything_ on the raft.\r\n\r\nIf the men went to the island I just expect they found the camp-fire I\r\nbuilt, and watched it all night for Jim to come. Anyways, they stayed\r\naway from us, and if my building the fire never fooled them it warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nno fault of mine. I played it as low down on them as I could.\r\n\r\nWhen the first streak of day began to show we tied up to a towhead in a\r\nbig bend on the Illinois side, and hacked off cottonwood branches with\r\nthe hatchet, and covered up the raft with them so she looked like there\r\nhad been a cave-in in the bank there. A towhead is a sand-bar that has\r\ncottonwoods on it as thick as harrow-teeth.\r\n\r\nWe had mountains on the Missouri shore and heavy timber on the Illinois\r\nside, and the channel was down the Missouri shore at that place, so\r\nwe warn\xe2\x80\x99t afraid of anybody running across us. We laid there all day,\r\nand watched the rafts and steamboats spin down the Missouri shore, and\r\nup-bound steamboats fight the big river in the middle. I told Jim all\r\nabout the time I had jabbering with that woman; and Jim said she was\r\na smart one, and if she was to start after us herself _she_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nset down and watch a camp-fire\xe2\x80\x94no, sir, she\xe2\x80\x99d fetch a dog. Well, then,\r\nI said, why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t she tell her husband to fetch a dog? Jim said he\r\nbet she did think of it by the time the men was ready to start, and\r\nhe believed they must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 gone up-town to get a dog and so they lost\r\nall that time, or else we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be here on a towhead sixteen or\r\nseventeen mile below the village\xe2\x80\x94no, indeedy, we would be in that same\r\nold town again. So I said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t care what was the reason they didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nget us as long as they didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\nWhen it was beginning to come on dark we poked our heads out of the\r\ncottonwood thicket, and looked up and down and across; nothing in\r\nsight; so Jim took up some of the top planks of the raft and built a\r\nsnug wigwam to get under in blazing weather and rainy, and to keep the\r\nthings dry. Jim made a floor for the wigwam, and raised it a foot or\r\nmore above the level of the raft, so now the blankets and all the traps\r\nwas out of reach of steamboat waves. Right in the middle of the wigwam\r\nwe made a layer of dirt about five or six inches deep with a frame\r\naround it for to hold it to its place; this was to build a fire on in\r\nsloppy weather or chilly; the wigwam would keep it from being seen. We\r\nmade an extra steering-oar, too, because one of the others might get\r\nbroke on a snag or something. We fixed up a short forked stick to hang\r\nthe old lantern on, because we must always light the lantern whenever\r\nwe see a steamboat coming down-stream, to keep from getting run over;\r\nbut we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have to light it for up-stream boats unless we see we\r\nwas in what they call a \xe2\x80\x9ccrossing\xe2\x80\x9d; for the river was pretty high yet,\r\nvery low banks being still a little under water; so up-bound boats\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t always run the channel, but hunted easy water.\r\n\r\nThis second night we run between seven and eight hours, with a current\r\nthat was making over four mile an hour. We catched fish and talked,\r\nand we took a swim now and then to keep off sleepiness. It was kind of\r\nsolemn, drifting down the big, still river, laying on our backs looking\r\nup at the stars, and we didn\xe2\x80\x99t ever feel like talking loud, and it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t often that we laughed\xe2\x80\x94only a little kind of a low chuckle. We\r\nhad mighty good weather as a general thing, and nothing ever happened\r\nto us at all\xe2\x80\x94that night, nor the next, nor the next.\r\n\r\nEvery night we passed towns, some of them away up on black hillsides,\r\nnothing but just a shiny bed of lights; not a house could you see.\r\nThe fifth night we passed St. Louis, and it was like the whole world\r\nlit up. In St. Petersburg they used to say there was twenty or thirty\r\nthousand people in St. Louis, but I never believed it till I see that\r\nwonderful spread of lights at two o\xe2\x80\x99clock that still night. There\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t a sound there; everybody was asleep.\r\n\r\nEvery night now I used to slip ashore toward ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock at some little\r\nvillage, and buy ten or fifteen cents\xe2\x80\x99 worth of meal or bacon or other\r\nstuff to eat; and sometimes I lifted a chicken that warn\xe2\x80\x99t roosting\r\ncomfortable, and took him along. Pap always said, take a chicken when\r\nyou get a chance, because if you don\xe2\x80\x99t want him yourself you can easy\r\nfind somebody that does, and a good deed ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever forgot. I never see\r\npap when he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want the chicken himself, but that is what he used\r\nto say, anyway.\r\n\r\nMornings before daylight I slipped into corn-fields and borrowed a\r\nwatermelon, or a mushmelon, or a punkin, or some new corn, or things of\r\nthat kind. Pap always said it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no harm to borrow things if you\r\nwas meaning to pay them back some time; but the widow said it warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nanything but a soft name for stealing, and no decent body would do it.\r\nJim said he reckoned the widow was partly right and pap was partly\r\nright; so the best way would be for us to pick out two or three things\r\nfrom the list and say we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t borrow them any more\xe2\x80\x94then he reckoned\r\nit wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be no harm to borrow the others. So we talked it over all\r\none night, drifting along down the river, trying to make up our minds\r\nwhether to drop the watermelons, or the cantelopes, or the mushmelons,\r\nor what. But toward daylight we got it all settled satisfactory, and\r\nconcluded to drop crabapples and p\xe2\x80\x99simmons. We warn\xe2\x80\x99t feeling just\r\nright before that, but it was all comfortable now. I was glad the way\r\nit come out, too, because crabapples ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever good, and the p\xe2\x80\x99simmons\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be ripe for two or three months yet.\r\n\r\nWe shot a water-fowl now and then that got up too early in the morning\r\nor didn\xe2\x80\x99t go to bed early enough in the evening. Take it all round, we\r\nlived pretty high.\r\n\r\nThe fifth night below St. Louis we had a big storm after midnight, with\r\na power of thunder and lightning, and the rain poured down in a solid\r\nsheet. We stayed in the wigwam and let the raft take care of itself.\r\nWhen the lightning glared out we could see a big straight river ahead,\r\nand high, rocky bluffs on both sides. By and by says I, \xe2\x80\x9cHel-_lo_,\r\nJim, looky yonder!\xe2\x80\x9d It was a steamboat that had killed herself on a\r\nrock. We was drifting straight down for her. The lightning showed her\r\nvery distinct. She was leaning over, with part of her upper deck above\r\nwater, and you could see every little chimbly-guy clean and clear, and\r\na chair by the big bell, with an old slouch hat hanging on the back of\r\nit, when the flashes come.\r\n\r\nWell, it being away in the night and stormy, and all so\r\nmysterious-like, I felt just the way any other boy would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 felt when\r\nI seen that wreck laying there so mournful and lonesome in the middle\r\nof the river. I wanted to get aboard of her and slink around a little,\r\nand see what there was there. So I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLe\xe2\x80\x99s land on her, Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Jim was dead against it at first. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI doan\xe2\x80\x99 want to go fool\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99long er no wrack. We\xe2\x80\x99s doin\xe2\x80\x99 blame\xe2\x80\x99 well,\r\nen we better let blame\xe2\x80\x99 well alone, as de good book says. Like as not\r\ndey\xe2\x80\x99s a watchman on dat wrack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWatchman your grandmother,\xe2\x80\x9d I says; \xe2\x80\x9cthere ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing to watch but\r\nthe texas and the pilot-house; and do you reckon anybody\xe2\x80\x99s going to\r\nresk his life for a texas and a pilot-house such a night as this, when\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s likely to break up and wash off down the river any minute?\xe2\x80\x9d Jim\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say nothing to that, so he didn\xe2\x80\x99t try. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd besides,\xe2\x80\x9d I says,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwe might borrow something worth having out of the captain\xe2\x80\x99s stateroom.\r\nSeegars, _I_ bet you\xe2\x80\x94and cost five cents apiece, solid cash. Steamboat\r\ncaptains is always rich, and get sixty dollars a month, and _they_\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care a cent what a thing costs, you know, long as they want it.\r\nStick a candle in your pocket; I can\xe2\x80\x99t rest, Jim, till we give her a\r\nrummaging. Do you reckon Tom Sawyer would ever go by this thing? Not\r\nfor pie, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. He\xe2\x80\x99d call it an adventure\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what he\xe2\x80\x99d call\r\nit; and he\xe2\x80\x99d land on that wreck if it was his last act. And wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhe throw style into it?\xe2\x80\x94wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t he spread himself, nor nothing? Why,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d think it was Christopher C\xe2\x80\x99lumbus discovering Kingdom Come. I\r\nwish Tom Sawyer _was_ here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim he grumbled a little, but give in. He said we mustn\xe2\x80\x99t talk any more\r\nthan we could help, and then talk mighty low. The lightning showed us\r\nthe wreck again just in time, and we fetched the stabboard derrick, and\r\nmade fast there.\r\n\r\nThe deck was high out here. We went sneaking down the slope of it to\r\nlabboard, in the dark, towards the texas, feeling our way slow with our\r\nfeet, and spreading our hands out to fend off the guys, for it was so\r\ndark we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no sign of them. Pretty soon we struck the forward\r\nend of the skylight, and clumb on to it; and the next step fetched us\r\nin front of the captain\xe2\x80\x99s door, which was open, and by Jimminy, away\r\ndown through the texas-hall we see a light! and all in the same second\r\nwe seem to hear low voices in yonder!\r\n\r\nJim whispered and said he was feeling powerful sick, and told me to\r\ncome along. I says, all right, and was going to start for the raft; but\r\njust then I heard a voice wail out and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, please don\xe2\x80\x99t, boys; I swear I won\xe2\x80\x99t ever tell!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother voice said, pretty loud:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a lie, Jim Turner. You always want\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x99n your share of the truck, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve always got it, too, because\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve swore \xe2\x80\x99t if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t you\xe2\x80\x99d tell. I was just a-biling with\r\ncuriosity; and I says to myself, Tom Sawyer wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t back out now,\r\nand so I won\xe2\x80\x99t either; I\xe2\x80\x99m a-going to see what\xe2\x80\x99s going on here. So I\r\ndropped on my hands and knees in the little passage, and crept aft\r\nin the dark till there warn\xe2\x80\x99t but one stateroom betwixt me and the\r\ncross-hall of the texas. Then in there I see a man stretched on the\r\nfloor and tied hand and foot, and two men standing over him, and one\r\nof them had a dim lantern in his hand, and the other one had a pistol.\r\nThis one kept pointing the pistol at the man\xe2\x80\x99s head on the floor, and\r\nsaying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d _like_ to! We struck\r\nfor the stern of the texas, and found it, and then scrabbled along\r\nforwards on the skylight, hanging on from shutter to shutter, for the\r\nedge of the skylight was in the water. After a long time the\r\nrain let up, but the clouds stayed, and the lightning kept whimpering,\r\nand by and by a flash showed us a black thing ahead, floating, and we\r\nmade for it.\r\n\r\nIt was the raft, and mighty glad was we to get aboard of it again. We\r\nseen a light now away down to the right, on shore. So I said I would\r\ngo for it. The skiff was half full of plunder which that gang had\r\nstole there on the wreck. We hustled it on to the raft in a pile, and\r\nI told Jim to float along down, and show a light when he judged he had\r\ngone about two mile, and keep it burning till I come; then I manned my\r\noars and shoved for the light. As I went by I see it\r\nwas a lantern hanging on the jackstaff of a double-hull ferryboat. I\r\nskimmed around for the watchman, a-wondering whereabouts he slept; and\r\nby and by I found him roosting on the bitts forward, with his head down\r\nbetween his knees.
