I made the land about a mile\r\nbelow, and been fooling along ever since, trying to get people to do\r\nsomething, but they said, \xe2\x80\x99What, in such a night and such a current?\r\nThere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no sense in it; go for the steam-ferry.\xe2\x80\x99 Now if you\xe2\x80\x99ll go\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jackson, I\xe2\x80\x99d _like_ to, and, blame it, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know but I will; but\r\nwho in the dingnation\xe2\x80\x99s a-going to _pay_ for it? Do you reckon your\r\npap\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy _that\xe2\x80\x99s_ all right. Miss Hooker she tole me, _particular_, that\r\nher uncle Hornback\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat guns! is _he_ her uncle? Looky here, you break for that light\r\nover yonder-way, and turn out west when you git there, and about a\r\nquarter of a mile out you\xe2\x80\x99ll come to the tavern; tell \xe2\x80\x99em to dart you\r\nout to Jim Hornback\xe2\x80\x99s, and he\xe2\x80\x99ll foot the bill. And don\xe2\x80\x99t you fool\r\naround any, because he\xe2\x80\x99ll want to know the news. Tell him I\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nhis niece all safe before he can get to town. Hump yourself, now; I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\na-going up around the corner here to roust out my engineer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI struck for the light, but as soon as he turned the corner I went back\r\nand got into my skiff and bailed her out, and then pulled up shore in\r\nthe easy water about six hundred yards, and tucked myself in among some\r\nwood-boats; for I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t rest easy till I could see the ferryboat\r\nstart. But take it all around, I was feeling ruther comfortable on\r\naccounts of taking all this trouble for that gang, for not many would\r\n\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done it. I wished the widow knowed about it. I judged she would\r\nbe proud of me for helping these rapscallions, because rapscallions\r\nand dead-beats is the kind the widow and good people takes the most\r\ninterest in.\r\n\r\nWell, before long here comes the wreck, dim and dusky, sliding along\r\ndown! A kind of cold shiver went through me, and then I struck out for\r\nher. She was very deep, and I see in a minute there warn\xe2\x80\x99t much chance\r\nfor anybody being alive in her. I pulled all around her and hollered a\r\nlittle, but there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any answer; all dead still. I felt a little\r\nbit heavy-hearted about the gang, but not much, for I reckoned if they\r\ncould stand it I could.\r\n\r\nThen here comes the ferryboat; so I shoved for the middle of the river\r\non a long down-stream slant; and when I judged I was out of eye-reach\r\nI laid on my oars, and looked back and see her go and smell around the\r\nwreck for Miss Hooker\xe2\x80\x99s remainders, because the captain would know her\r\nuncle Hornback would want them; and then pretty soon the ferryboat\r\ngive it up and went for the shore, and I laid into my work and went\r\na-booming down the river.\r\n\r\nIt did seem a powerful long time before Jim\xe2\x80\x99s light showed up; and when\r\nit did show it looked like it was a thousand mile off. By the time I\r\ngot there the sky was beginning to get a little gray in the east; so we\r\nstruck for an island, and hid the raft, and sunk the skiff, and turned\r\nin and slept like dead people.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV\r\n\r\n\r\nBy and by, when we got up, we turned over the truck the gang had stole\r\noff of the wreck, and found boots, and blankets, and clothes, and all\r\nsorts of other things, and a lot of books, and a spy-glass, and three\r\nboxes of seegars. We hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever been this rich before in neither of\r\nour lives. The seegars was prime. We laid off all the afternoon in the\r\nwoods talking, and me reading the books, and having a general good\r\ntime. I told Jim all about what happened inside the wreck and at the\r\nferryboat, and I said these kinds of things was adventures; but he\r\nsaid he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want no more adventures. He said that when I went in\r\nthe texas and he crawled back to get on the raft and found her gone he\r\nnearly died, because he judged it was all up with _him_ anyway it could\r\nbe fixed; for if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t get saved he would get drownded; and if he\r\ndid get saved, whoever saved him would send him back home so as to get\r\nthe reward, and then Miss Watson would sell him South, sure. Well, he\r\nwas right; he was most always right; he had an uncommon level head for\r\na nigger.\r\n\r\nI read considerable to Jim about kings and dukes and earls and such,\r\nand how gaudy they dressed, and how much style they put on, and called\r\neach other and so on, \xe2\x80\x99stead of mister; and Jim\xe2\x80\x99s eyes bugged out, and\r\nhe was interested. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99 know dey was so many un um. I hain\xe2\x80\x99t hearn \xe2\x80\x99bout none un um,\r\nskasely, but ole King Sollermun, onless you counts dem kings dat\xe2\x80\x99s in a\r\npack er k\xe2\x80\x99yards. How much do a king git?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet?\xe2\x80\x9d I says; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, they get a thousand dollars a month if they want\r\nit; they can have just as much as they want; everything belongs to\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ain\xe2\x80\x99_ dat gay? En what dey got to do, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_They_ don\xe2\x80\x99t do nothing! Why, how you talk! They just set around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; is dat so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course it is. They just set around\xe2\x80\x94except, maybe, when there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nwar; then they go to the war. But other times they just lazy around; or\r\ngo hawking\xe2\x80\x94just hawking and sp\xe2\x80\x94 Sh!\xe2\x80\x94d\xe2\x80\x99you hear a noise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe skipped out and looked; but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing but the flutter of a\r\nsteamboat\xe2\x80\x99s wheel away down, coming around the point; so we come back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d says I, \xe2\x80\x9cand other times, when things is dull, they fuss with\r\nthe parlyment; and if everybody don\xe2\x80\x99t go just so he whacks their heads\r\noff. But mostly they hang round the harem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoun\xe2\x80\x99 de which?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHarem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s de harem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe place where he keeps his wives. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know about the harem?\r\nSolomon had one; he had about a million wives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes, dat\xe2\x80\x99s so; I\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99d done forgot it. A harem\xe2\x80\x99s a bo\xe2\x80\x99d\xe2\x80\x99n-house, I\r\nreck\xe2\x80\x99n. Mos\xe2\x80\x99 likely dey has rackety times in de nussery. En I reck\xe2\x80\x99n\r\nde wives quarrels considable; en dat \xe2\x80\x99crease de racket. Yit dey say\r\nSollermun de wises\xe2\x80\x99 man dat ever live\xe2\x80\x99. I doan\xe2\x80\x99 take no stock in\r\ndat. Bekase why: would a wise man want to live in de mids\xe2\x80\x99 er sich a\r\nblim-blammin\xe2\x80\x99 all de time? No\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99deed he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. A wise man \xe2\x80\x99ud take en\r\nbuil\xe2\x80\x99 a biler-factry; en den he could shet _down_ de biler-factry when\r\nhe want to res\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, but he _was_ the wisest man, anyway; because the widow she told\r\nme so, her own self.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI doan\xe2\x80\x99 k\xe2\x80\x99yer what de widder say, he _warn\xe2\x80\x99t_ no wise man nuther. He\r\nhad some er de dad-fetchedes\xe2\x80\x99 ways I ever see. Does you know \xe2\x80\x99bout dat\r\nchile dat he \xe2\x80\x99uz gwyne to chop in two?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the widow told me all about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Well_, den! Warn\xe2\x80\x99 dat de beatenes\xe2\x80\x99 notion in de worl\xe2\x80\x99? You jes\xe2\x80\x99 take\r\nen look at it a minute. Dah\xe2\x80\x99s de stump, dah\xe2\x80\x94dat\xe2\x80\x99s one er de women;\r\nheah\xe2\x80\x99s you\xe2\x80\x94dat\xe2\x80\x99s de yuther one; I\xe2\x80\x99s Sollermun; en dish yer dollar\r\nbill\xe2\x80\x99s de chile. Bofe un you claims it. What does I do? Does I shin\r\naroun\xe2\x80\x99 mongs\xe2\x80\x99 de neighbors en fine out which un you de bill _do_ b\xe2\x80\x99long\r\nto, en han\xe2\x80\x99 it over to de right one, all safe en soun\xe2\x80\x99, de way dat\r\nanybody dat had any gumption would? No; I take en whack de bill in\r\n_two_, en give half un it to you, en de yuther half to de yuther woman.\r\nDat\xe2\x80\x99s de way Sollermun was gwyne to do wid de chile. Now I want to ast\r\nyou: what\xe2\x80\x99s de use er dat half a bill?\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t buy noth\xe2\x80\x99n wid it. En what\r\nuse is a half a chile? I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 give a dern for a million un um.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut hang it, Jim, you\xe2\x80\x99ve clean missed the point\xe2\x80\x94blame it, you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nmissed it a thousand mile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? Me? Go \xe2\x80\x99long. Doan\xe2\x80\x99 talk to _me_ \xe2\x80\x99bout yo\xe2\x80\x99 pints. I reck\xe2\x80\x99n I\r\nknows sense when I sees it; en dey ain\xe2\x80\x99 no sense in sich doin\xe2\x80\x99s as dat.\r\nDe \xe2\x80\x99spute warn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99bout a half a chile, de \xe2\x80\x99spute was \xe2\x80\x99bout a whole\r\nchile; en de man dat think he kin settle a \xe2\x80\x99spute \xe2\x80\x99bout a whole chile\r\nwid a half a chile doan\xe2\x80\x99 know enough to come in out\xe2\x80\x99n de rain. Doan\xe2\x80\x99\r\ntalk to me \xe2\x80\x99bout Sollermun, Huck, I knows him by de back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I tell you you don\xe2\x80\x99t get the point.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame de point! I reck\xe2\x80\x99n I knows what I knows. En mine you, de _real_\r\npint furder\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s down deeper. It lays in de way Sollermun was raised.\r\nYou take a man dat\xe2\x80\x99s got on\xe2\x80\x99y one or two chillen; is dat man gwyne to\r\nbe waseful o\xe2\x80\x99 chillen? No, he ain\xe2\x80\x99t; he can\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99ford it. _He_ know how\r\nto value \xe2\x80\x99em. But you take a man dat\xe2\x80\x99s got \xe2\x80\x99bout five million chillen\r\nrunnin\xe2\x80\x99 roun\xe2\x80\x99 de house, en it\xe2\x80\x99s diffunt. _He_ as soon chop a chile in\r\ntwo as a cat. Dey\xe2\x80\x99s plenty mo\xe2\x80\x99. A chile er two, mo\xe2\x80\x99 er less, warn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nconsekens to Sollermun, dad fetch him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI never see such a nigger. If he got a notion in his head once, there\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t no getting it out again. He was the most down on Solomon of any\r\nnigger I ever see. So I went to talking about other kings, and let\r\nSolomon slide. I told about Louis Sixteenth that got his head cut off\r\nin France long time ago; and about his little boy the dolphin, that\r\nwould \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been a king, but they took and shut him up in jail, and some\r\nsay he died there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPo\xe2\x80\x99 little chap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut some says he got out and got away, and come to America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDat\xe2\x80\x99s good! But he\xe2\x80\x99ll be pooty lonesome\xe2\x80\x94dey ain\xe2\x80\x99 no kings here, is\r\ndey, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDen he cain\xe2\x80\x99t git no situation. What he gwyne to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Some of them gets on the police, and some of them\r\nlearns people how to talk French.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Huck, doan\xe2\x80\x99 de French people talk de same way we does?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_No_, Jim; you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand a word they said\xe2\x80\x94not a single word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, I be ding-busted! How do dat come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t know; but it\xe2\x80\x99s so. I got some of their jabber out of a book.\r\nS\xe2\x80\x99pose a man was to come to you and say Polly-voo-franzy\xe2\x80\x94what would you\r\nthink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 think nuffn; I\xe2\x80\x99d take en bust him over de head\xe2\x80\x94dat is, if he\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t white. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99low no nigger to call me dat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t calling you anything. It\xe2\x80\x99s only saying, do you know\r\nhow to talk French?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, den, why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t he _say_ it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he _is_ a-saying it. That\xe2\x80\x99s a Frenchman\xe2\x80\x99s _way_ of saying it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s a blame ridicklous way, en I doan\xe2\x80\x99 want to hear no mo\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x99bout it. Dey ain\xe2\x80\x99 no sense in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooky here, Jim; does a cat talk like we do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, a cat don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, does a cow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, a cow don\xe2\x80\x99t, nuther.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes a cat talk like a cow, or a cow talk like a cat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, dey don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s natural and right for \xe2\x80\x99em to talk different from each other,\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd ain\xe2\x80\x99t it natural and right for a cat and a cow to talk different\r\nfrom _us_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, mos\xe2\x80\x99 sholy it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, why ain\xe2\x80\x99t it natural and right for a _Frenchman_ to talk\r\ndifferent from us? You answer me that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs a cat a man, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, den, dey ain\xe2\x80\x99t no sense in a cat talkin\xe2\x80\x99 like a man. Is a cow a\r\nman?\xe2\x80\x94er is a cow a cat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, she ain\xe2\x80\x99t either of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, den, she ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no business to talk like either one er the\r\nyuther of \xe2\x80\x99em. Is a Frenchman a man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Well_, den! Dad blame it, why doan\xe2\x80\x99 he _talk_ like a man? You answer\r\nme _dat!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI see it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use wasting words\xe2\x80\x94you can\xe2\x80\x99t learn a nigger to argue.\r\nSo I quit.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV\r\n\r\n\r\nWe judged that three nights more would fetch us to Cairo, at the bottom\r\nof Illinois, where the Ohio River comes in, and that was what we was\r\nafter. We would sell the raft and get on a steamboat and go way up the\r\nOhio amongst the free states, and then be out of trouble.\r\n\r\nWell, the second night a fog begun to come on, and we made for a\r\ntowhead to tie to, for it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do to try to run in a fog; but\r\nwhen I paddled ahead in the canoe, with the line to make fast, there\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t anything but little saplings to tie to. I passed the line around\r\none of them right on the edge of the cut bank, but there was a stiff\r\ncurrent, and the raft come booming down so lively she tore it out by\r\nthe roots and away she went. I see the fog closing down, and it made me\r\nso sick and scared I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t budge for most a half a minute it seemed\r\nto me\xe2\x80\x94and then there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no raft in sight; you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see twenty\r\nyards. I jumped into the canoe and run back to the stern, and grabbed\r\nthe paddle and set her back a stroke. But she didn\xe2\x80\x99t come. I was in\r\nsuch a hurry I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t untied her. I got up and tried to untie her, but\r\nI was so excited my hands shook so I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly do anything with\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nAs soon as I got started I took out after the raft, hot and heavy,\r\nright down the towhead. That was all right as far as it went, but the\r\ntowhead warn\xe2\x80\x99t sixty yards long, and the minute I flew by the foot of\r\nit I shot out into the solid white fog, and hadn\xe2\x80\x99t no more idea which\r\nway I was going than a dead man.\r\n\r\nThinks I, it won\xe2\x80\x99t do to paddle; first I know I\xe2\x80\x99ll run into the bank\r\nor a towhead or something; I got to set still and float, and yet it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmighty fidgety business to have to hold your hands still at such a\r\ntime. I whooped and listened. Away down there somewheres I hears\r\na small whoop, and up comes my spirits. I went tearing after it,\r\nlistening sharp to hear it again. The next time it come I see I warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nheading for it, but heading away to the right of it. And the next time\r\nI was heading away to the left of it\xe2\x80\x94and not gaining on it much either,\r\nfor I was flying around, this way and that and t\xe2\x80\x99other, but it was\r\ngoing straight ahead all the time.\r\n\r\nI did wish the fool would think to beat a tin pan, and beat it all\r\nthe time, but he never did, and it was the still places between the\r\nwhoops that was making the trouble for me. Well, I fought along, and\r\ndirectly I hears the whoop _behind_ me. I was tangled good now. That\r\nwas somebody else\xe2\x80\x99s whoop, or else I was turned around.\r\n\r\nI throwed the paddle down. I heard the whoop again; it was behind me\r\nyet, but in a different place; it kept coming, and kept changing its\r\nplace, and I kept answering, till by and by it was in front of me\r\nagain, and I knowed the current had swung the canoe\xe2\x80\x99s head down-stream,\r\nand I was all right if that was Jim and not some other raftsman\r\nhollering. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell nothing about voices in a fog, for nothing\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t look natural nor sound natural in a fog.\r\n\r\nThe whooping went on, and in about a minute I come a-booming down on a\r\ncut bank with smoky ghosts of big trees on it, and the current throwed\r\nme off to the left and shot by, amongst a lot of snags that fairly\r\nroared, the current was tearing by them so swift.\r\n\r\nIn another second or two it was solid white and still again. I set\r\nperfectly still then, listening to my heart thump, and I reckon I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t draw a breath while it thumped a hundred.\r\n\r\nI just give up then. I knowed what the matter was. That cut bank was an\r\nisland, and Jim had gone down t\xe2\x80\x99other side of it. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t no towhead\r\nthat you could float by in ten minutes. It had the big timber of a\r\nregular island; it might be five or six miles long and more than half a\r\nmile wide.\r\n\r\nI kept quiet, with my ears cocked, about fifteen minutes, I reckon.\r\nI was floating along, of course, four or five miles an hour; but you\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t ever think of that. No, you _feel_ like you are laying dead still\r\non the water; and if a little glimpse of a snag slips by you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink to yourself how fast _you\xe2\x80\x99re_ going, but you catch your breath\r\nand think, my! how that snag\xe2\x80\x99s tearing along. If you think it ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndismal and lonesome out in a fog that way by yourself in the night, you\r\ntry it once\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll see.\r\n\r\nNext, for about a half an hour, I whoops now and then; at last I hears\r\nthe answer a long ways off, and tries to follow it, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndo it, and directly I judged I\xe2\x80\x99d got into a nest of towheads, for I\r\nhad little dim glimpses of them on both sides of me\xe2\x80\x94sometimes just a\r\nnarrow channel between, and some that I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see I knowed was there\r\nbecause I\xe2\x80\x99d hear the wash of the current against the old dead brush and\r\ntrash that hung over the banks. Well, I warn\xe2\x80\x99t long loosing the whoops\r\ndown amongst the towheads; and I only tried to chase them a little\r\nwhile, anyway, because it was worse than chasing a Jack-o\xe2\x80\x99-lantern. You\r\nnever knowed a sound dodge around so, and swap places so quick and so\r\nmuch.\r\n\r\nI had to claw away from the bank pretty lively four or five times, to\r\nkeep from knocking the islands out of the river; and so I judged the\r\nraft must be butting into the bank every now and then, or else it would\r\nget further ahead and clear out of hearing\xe2\x80\x94it was floating a little\r\nfaster than what I was.\r\n\r\nWell, I seemed to be in the open river again by and by, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhear no sign of a whoop nowheres. I reckoned Jim had fetched up on a\r\nsnag, maybe, and it was all up with him. I was good and tired, so I\r\nlaid down in the canoe and said I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t bother no more. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to go to sleep, of course; but I was so sleepy I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it;\r\nso I thought I would take jest one little cat-nap.\r\n\r\nBut I reckon it was more than a cat-nap, for when I waked up the stars\r\nwas shining bright, the fog was all gone, and I was spinning down a\r\nbig bend stern first. First I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know where I was; I thought I was\r\ndreaming; and when things began to come back to me they seemed to come\r\nup dim out of last week.\r\n\r\nIt was a monstrous big river here, with the tallest and the thickest\r\nkind of timber on both banks; just a solid wall, as well as I could see\r\nby the stars. I looked away down-stream, and seen a black speck on the\r\nwater. I took after it; but when I got to it it warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing but a\r\ncouple of saw-logs made fast together. Then I see another speck, and\r\nchased that; then another, and this time I was right. It was the raft.\r\n\r\nWhen I got to it Jim was setting there with his head down between his\r\nknees, asleep, with his right arm hanging over the steering-oar. The\r\nother oar was smashed off, and the raft was littered up with leaves and\r\nbranches and dirt. So she\xe2\x80\x99d had a rough time.\r\n\r\nI made fast and laid down under Jim\xe2\x80\x99s nose on the raft, and began to\r\ngap, and stretch my fists out against Jim, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello, Jim, have I been asleep? Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you stir me up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness gracious, is dat you, Huck? En you ain\xe2\x80\x99 dead\xe2\x80\x94you ain\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndrownded\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99s back ag\xe2\x80\x99in? It\xe2\x80\x99s too good for true, honey, it\xe2\x80\x99s too good\r\nfor true. Lemme look at you chile, lemme feel o\xe2\x80\x99 you. No, you ain\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndead! you\xe2\x80\x99s back ag\xe2\x80\x99in, \xe2\x80\x99live en soun\xe2\x80\x99, jis de same ole Huck\xe2\x80\x94de same\r\nole Huck, thanks to goodness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you, Jim? You been a-drinking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrinkin\xe2\x80\x99? Has I ben a-drinkin\xe2\x80\x99? Has I had a chance to be a-drinkin\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, what makes you talk so wild?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow does I talk wild?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_How?_ Why, hain\xe2\x80\x99t you been talking about my coming back, and all that\r\nstuff, as if I\xe2\x80\x99d been gone away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck\xe2\x80\x94Huck Finn, you look me in de eye; look me in de eye. _Hain\xe2\x80\x99t_ you\r\nben gone away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone away? Why, what in the nation do you mean? _I_ hain\xe2\x80\x99t been gone\r\nanywheres. Where would I go to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, looky here, boss, dey\xe2\x80\x99s sumfn wrong, dey is. Is I _me_, or who\r\n_is_ I? Is I heah, or whah _is_ I? Now dat\xe2\x80\x99s what I wants to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I think you\xe2\x80\x99re here, plain enough, but I think you\xe2\x80\x99re a\r\ntangle-headed old fool, Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI is, is I? Well, you answer me dis: Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tote out de line in de\r\ncanoe fer to make fas\xe2\x80\x99 to de towhead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t. What towhead? I hain\xe2\x80\x99t seen no towhead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou hain\xe2\x80\x99t seen no towhead? Looky here, didn\xe2\x80\x99t de line pull loose en\r\nde raf\xe2\x80\x99 go a-hummin\xe2\x80\x99 down de river, en leave you en de canoe behine in\r\nde fog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, _de_ fog!\xe2\x80\x94de fog dat\xe2\x80\x99s been aroun\xe2\x80\x99 all night. En didn\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nwhoop, en didn\xe2\x80\x99t I whoop, tell we got mix\xe2\x80\x99 up in de islands en one un\r\nus got los\xe2\x80\x99 en t\xe2\x80\x99other one was jis\xe2\x80\x99 as good as los\xe2\x80\x99, \xe2\x80\x99kase he didn\xe2\x80\x99\r\nknow whah he wuz? En didn\xe2\x80\x99t I bust up agin a lot er dem islands en have\r\na turrible time en mos\xe2\x80\x99 git drownded? Now ain\xe2\x80\x99 dat so, boss\xe2\x80\x94ain\xe2\x80\x99t it\r\nso? You answer me dat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, this is too many for me, Jim. I hain\xe2\x80\x99t seen no fog, nor no\r\nislands, nor no troubles, nor nothing. I been setting here talking\r\nwith you all night till you went to sleep about ten minutes ago, and I\r\nreckon I done the same. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got drunk in that time, so of\r\ncourse you\xe2\x80\x99ve been dreaming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDad fetch it, how is I gwyne to dream all dat in ten minutes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, hang it all, you did dream it, because there didn\xe2\x80\x99t any of it\r\nhappen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Huck, it\xe2\x80\x99s all jis\xe2\x80\x99 as plain to me as\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt don\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference how plain it is; there ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing in\r\nit. I know, because I\xe2\x80\x99ve been here all the time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim didn\xe2\x80\x99t say nothing for about five minutes, but set there studying\r\nover it. Then he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, den, I reck\xe2\x80\x99n I did dream it, Huck; but dog my cats ef it ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nde powerfulest dream I ever see. En I hain\xe2\x80\x99t ever had no dream b\xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndat\xe2\x80\x99s tired me like dis one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, that\xe2\x80\x99s all right, because a dream does tire a body like\r\neverything sometimes. But this one was a staving dream; tell me all\r\nabout it, Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Jim went to work and told me the whole thing right through, just as\r\nit happened, only he painted it up considerable. Then he said he must\r\nstart in and \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99terpret\xe2\x80\x9d it, because it was sent for a warning. He said\r\nthe first towhead stood for a man that would try to do us some good,\r\nbut the current was another man that would get us away from him. The\r\nwhoops was warnings that would come to us every now and then, and if\r\nwe didn\xe2\x80\x99t try hard to make out to understand them they\xe2\x80\x99d just take us\r\ninto bad luck, \xe2\x80\x99stead of keeping us out of it. The lot of towheads was\r\ntroubles we was going to get into with quarrelsome people and all kinds\r\nof mean folks, but if we minded our business and didn\xe2\x80\x99t talk back and\r\naggravate them, we would pull through and get out of the fog and into\r\nthe big clear river, which was the free states, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have no\r\nmore trouble.\r\n\r\nIt had clouded up pretty dark just after I got on to the raft, but it\r\nwas clearing up again now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, well, that\xe2\x80\x99s all interpreted well enough as far as it goes, Jim,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nI says; \xe2\x80\x9cbut what does _these_ things stand for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the leaves and rubbish on the raft and the smashed oar. You\r\ncould see them first-rate now.\r\n\r\nJim looked at the trash, and then looked at me, and back at the trash\r\nagain. He had got the dream fixed so strong in his head that he\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to shake it loose and get the facts back into its place\r\nagain right away. But when he did get the thing straightened around he\r\nlooked at me steady without ever smiling, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do dey stan\xe2\x80\x99 for? I\xe2\x80\x99s gwyne to tell you. When I got all wore out\r\nwid work, en wid de callin\xe2\x80\x99 for you, en went to sleep, my heart wuz\r\nmos\xe2\x80\x99 broke bekase you wuz los\xe2\x80\x99, en I didn\xe2\x80\x99 k\xe2\x80\x99yer no\xe2\x80\x99 mo\xe2\x80\x99 what become er\r\nme en de raf\xe2\x80\x99. En when I wake up en fine you back ag\xe2\x80\x99in, all safe en\r\nsoun\xe2\x80\x99, de tears come, en I could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got down on my knees en kiss yo\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfoot, I\xe2\x80\x99s so thankful. En all you wuz thinkin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout wuz how you could\r\nmake a fool uv ole Jim wid a lie. Dat truck dah is _trash_; en trash\r\nis what people is dat puts dirt on de head er dey fren\xe2\x80\x99s en makes \xe2\x80\x99em\r\nashamed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he got up slow and walked to the wigwam, and went in there without\r\nsaying anything but that. But that was enough. It made me feel so mean\r\nI could almost kissed _his_ foot to get him to take it back.\r\n\r\nIt was fifteen minutes before I could work myself up to go and humble\r\nmyself to a nigger; but I done it, and I warn\xe2\x80\x99t ever sorry for it\r\nafterward, neither. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t do him no more mean tricks, and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndone that one if I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 knowed it would make him feel that way.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI\r\n\r\n\r\nWe slept most all day, and started out at night, a little ways behind\r\na monstrous long raft that was as long going by as a procession. She\r\nhad four long sweeps at each end, so we judged she carried as many as\r\nthirty men, likely. She had five big wigwams aboard, wide apart, and an\r\nopen camp-fire in the middle, and a tall flag-pole at each end. There\r\nwas a power of style about her. It _amounted_ to something being a\r\nraftsman on such a craft as that.\r\n\r\nWe went drifting down into a big bend, and the night clouded up and\r\ngot hot. The river was very wide, and was walled with solid timber on\r\nboth sides; you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see a break in it hardly ever, or a light. We\r\ntalked about Cairo, and wondered whether we would know it when we got\r\nto it. I said likely we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, because I had heard say there warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbut about a dozen houses there, and if they didn\xe2\x80\x99t happen to have them\r\nlit up, how was we going to know we was passing a town? Jim said if\r\nthe two big rivers joined together there, that would show. But I said\r\nmaybe we might think we was passing the foot of an island and coming\r\ninto the same old river again. That disturbed Jim\xe2\x80\x94and me too. So the\r\nquestion was, what to do? I said, paddle ashore the first time a light\r\nshowed, and tell them pap was behind, coming along with a trading-scow,\r\nand was a green hand at the business, and wanted to know how far it was\r\nto Cairo. Jim thought it was a good idea, so we took a smoke on it and\r\nwaited.\r\n\r\nThere warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing to do now but to look out sharp for the town, and\r\nnot pass it without seeing it. He said he\xe2\x80\x99d be mighty sure to see it,\r\nbecause he\xe2\x80\x99d be a free man the minute he seen it, but if he missed it\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d be in a slave country again and no more show for freedom. Every\r\nlittle while he jumps up and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDah she is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it warn\xe2\x80\x99t. It was Jack-o\xe2\x80\x99-lanterns, or lightning-bugs; so he set\r\ndown again, and went to watching, same as before. Jim said it made him\r\nall over trembly and feverish to be so close to freedom. Well, I can\r\ntell you it made me all over trembly and feverish, too, to hear him,\r\nbecause I begun to get it through my head that he _was_ most free\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nwho was to blame for it? Why, _me_. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get that out of my\r\nconscience, no how nor no way. It got to troubling me so I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nrest; I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay still in one place. It hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever come home to\r\nme before, what this thing was that I was doing. But now it did; and it\r\nstayed with me, and scorched me more and more. I tried to make out to\r\nmyself that _I_ warn\xe2\x80\x99t to blame, because _I_ didn\xe2\x80\x99t run Jim off from\r\nhis rightful owner; but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use, conscience up and says, every\r\ntime, \xe2\x80\x9cBut you knowed he was running for his freedom, and you could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99\r\npaddled ashore and told somebody.\xe2\x80\x9d That was so\xe2\x80\x94I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get around\r\nthat no way. That was where it pinched. Conscience says to me, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nhad poor Miss Watson done to you that you could see her nigger go off\r\nright under your eyes and never say one single word? What did that poor\r\nold woman do to you that you could treat her so mean? Why, she tried to\r\nlearn you your book, she tried to learn you your manners, she tried to\r\nbe good to you every way she knowed how. _That\xe2\x80\x99s_ what she done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI got to feeling so mean and so miserable I most wished I was dead.\r\nI fidgeted up and down the raft, abusing myself to myself, and Jim\r\nwas fidgeting up and down past me. We neither of us could keep still.\r\nEvery time he danced around and says, \xe2\x80\x9cDah\xe2\x80\x99s Cairo!\xe2\x80\x9d it went through me\r\nlike a shot, and I thought if it _was_ Cairo I reckoned I would die of\r\nmiserableness.\r\n\r\nJim talked out loud all the time while I was talking to myself. He was\r\nsaying how the first thing he would do when he got to a free state he\r\nwould go to saving up money and never spend a single cent, and when\r\nhe got enough he would buy his wife, which was owned on a farm close\r\nto where Miss Watson lived; and then they would both work to buy the\r\ntwo children, and if their master wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t sell them, they\xe2\x80\x99d get an\r\nAb\xe2\x80\x99litionist to go and steal them.\r\n\r\nIt most froze me to hear such talk. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever dared to talk such\r\ntalk in his life before. Just see what a difference it made in him the\r\nminute he judged he was about free. It was according to the old saying,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive a nigger an inch and he\xe2\x80\x99ll take an ell.\xe2\x80\x9d Thinks I, this is what\r\ncomes of my not thinking. Here was this nigger, which I had as good as\r\nhelped to run away, coming right out flat-footed and saying he would\r\nsteal his children\xe2\x80\x94children that belonged to a man I didn\xe2\x80\x99t even know;\r\na man that hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever done me no harm.\r\n\r\nI was sorry to hear Jim say that, it was such a lowering of him. My\r\nconscience got to stirring me up hotter than ever, until at last I says\r\nto it, \xe2\x80\x9cLet up on me\xe2\x80\x94it ain\xe2\x80\x99t too late yet\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll paddle ashore at the\r\nfirst light and tell.\xe2\x80\x9d I felt easy and happy and light as a feather\r\nright off. All my troubles was gone. I went to looking out sharp for a\r\nlight, and sort of singing to myself. By and by one showed. Jim sings\r\nout:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99s safe, Huck, we\xe2\x80\x99s safe! Jump up and crack yo\xe2\x80\x99 heels! Dat\xe2\x80\x99s de good\r\nole Cairo at las\xe2\x80\x99, I jis knows it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll take the canoe and go and see, Jim. It mightn\xe2\x80\x99t be, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe jumped and got the canoe ready, and put his old coat in the bottom\r\nfor me to set on, and give me the paddle; and as I shoved off, he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPooty soon I\xe2\x80\x99ll be a-shout\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 for joy, en I\xe2\x80\x99ll say, it\xe2\x80\x99s all on\r\naccounts o\xe2\x80\x99 Huck; I\xe2\x80\x99s a free man, en I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever ben free ef it\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99 ben for Huck; Huck done it. Jim won\xe2\x80\x99t ever forgit you, Huck;\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99s de bes\xe2\x80\x99 fren\xe2\x80\x99 Jim\xe2\x80\x99s ever had; en you\xe2\x80\x99s de _only_ fren\xe2\x80\x99 ole Jim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngot now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was paddling off, all in a sweat to tell on him; but when he says\r\nthis, it seemed to kind of take the tuck all out of me. I went along\r\nslow then, and I warn\xe2\x80\x99t right down certain whether I was glad I started\r\nor whether I warn\xe2\x80\x99t. When I was fifty yards off, Jim says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDah you goes, de ole true Huck; de on\xe2\x80\x99y white genlman dat ever kep\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhis promise to ole Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, I just felt sick. But I says, I _got_ to do it\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t get _out_\r\nof it. Right then along comes a skiff with two men in it with guns, and\r\nthey stopped and I stopped. One of them says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that yonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA piece of a raft,\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you belong on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny men on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s five niggers run off to-night up yonder, above the head\r\nof the bend. They backed water. \xe2\x80\x9cKeep\r\naway, boy\xe2\x80\x94keep to looard. Confound it, I just expect the wind has\r\nblowed it to us. Your pap\xe2\x80\x99s got the smallpox, and you know it precious\r\nwell. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come out and say so? You float along down about twenty\r\nmiles, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll come to a town on the left-hand side of the river. It\r\nwill be long after sun-up then, and when you ask for help you tell them\r\nyour folks are all down with chills and fever. Don\xe2\x80\x99t be a fool again,\r\nand let people guess what is the matter. He said twenty mile more warn\xe2\x80\x99t far for the raft to go, but he\r\nwished we was already there.\r\n\r\nTowards daybreak we tied up, and Jim was mighty particular about hiding\r\nthe raft good. Then he worked all day fixing things in bundles, and\r\ngetting all ready to quit rafting.\r\n\r\nThat night about ten we hove in sight of the lights of a town away down\r\nin a left-hand bend.\r\n\r\nI went off in the canoe to ask about it. Pretty soon I found a man out\r\nin the river with a skiff, setting a trot-line. I ranged up and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMister, is that town Cairo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCairo? no. You must be a blame\xe2\x80\x99 fool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat town is it, mister?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you want to know, go and find out. No high ground about Cairo, Jim\r\nsaid. I had forgot it. We laid up for the day on a towhead tolerable\r\nclose to the left-hand bank. I begun to suspicion something. So did\r\nJim. I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe we went by Cairo in the fog that night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoan\xe2\x80\x99 le\xe2\x80\x99s talk about it, Huck. So it was all up with\r\nCairo.\r\n\r\nWe talked it all over. It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do to take to the shore; we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake the raft up the stream, of course. So we slept\r\nall day amongst the cottonwood thicket, so as to be fresh for the work,\r\nand when we went back to the raft about dark the canoe was gone!\r\n\r\nWe didn\xe2\x80\x99t say a word for a good while. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t anything to say. We\r\nboth knowed well enough it was some more work of the rattlesnake-skin;\r\nso what was the use to talk about it? It would only look like we was\r\nfinding fault, and that would be bound to fetch more bad luck\xe2\x80\x94and keep\r\non fetching it, too, till we knowed enough to keep still.\r\n\r\nBy and by we talked about what we better do, and found there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nway but just to go along down with the raft till we got a chance to buy\r\na canoe to go back in. But we\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t see no rafts laying up; so we went along during three hours and\r\nmore. Well, the night got gray and ruther thick, which is the next\r\nmeanest thing to fog. You can\xe2\x80\x99t tell the shape of the river, and you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t see no distance. It got to be very late and still, and then along\r\ncomes a steamboat up the river. We lit the lantern, and judged she\r\nwould see it. Up-stream boats didn\xe2\x80\x99t generly come close to us; they go\r\nout and follow the bars and hunt for easy water under the reefs; but\r\nnights like this they bull right up the channel against the whole river.\r\n\r\nWe could hear her pounding along, but we didn\xe2\x80\x99t see her good till she\r\nwas close. She aimed right for us. Often they do that and try to see\r\nhow close they can come without touching; sometimes the wheel bites off\r\na sweep, and then the pilot sticks his head out and laughs, and thinks\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s mighty smart. Well, here she comes, and we said she was going to\r\ntry and shave us; but she didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be sheering off a bit. She was\r\na big one, and she was coming in a hurry, too, looking like a black\r\ncloud with rows of glow-worms around it; but all of a sudden she bulged\r\nout, big and scary, with a long row of wide-open furnace doors shining\r\nlike red-hot teeth, and her monstrous bows and guards hanging right\r\nover us. There was a yell at us, and a jingling of bells to stop the\r\nengines, a powwow of cussing, and whistling of steam\xe2\x80\x94and as Jim went\r\noverboard on one side and I on the other, she come smashing straight\r\nthrough the raft.\r\n\r\nI dived\xe2\x80\x94and I aimed to find the bottom, too, for a thirty-foot wheel\r\nhad got to go over me, and I wanted it to have plenty of room. I could\r\nalways stay under water a minute; this time I reckon I stayed under a\r\nminute and a half. Then I bounced for the top in a hurry, for I was\r\nnearly busting. I popped out to my armpits and blowed the water out of\r\nmy nose, and puffed a bit. Of course there was a booming current; and\r\nof course that boat started her engines again ten seconds after she\r\nstopped them, for they never cared much for raftsmen; so now she was\r\nchurning along up the river, out of sight in the thick weather, though\r\nI could hear her.\r\n\r\nI sung out for Jim about a dozen times, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t get any answer;\r\nso I grabbed a plank that touched me while I was \xe2\x80\x9ctreading water,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nstruck out for shore, shoving it ahead of me. But I made out to see\r\nthat the drift of the current was towards the left-hand shore, which\r\nmeant that I was in a crossing; so I changed off and went that way.\r\n\r\nIt was one of these long, slanting, two-mile crossings; so I was a good\r\nlong time in getting over. I made a safe landing, and clumb up the\r\nbank. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see but a little ways, but I went poking along over\r\nrough ground for a quarter of a mile or more, and then I run across a\r\nbig old-fashioned double log house before I noticed it. I was going to\r\nrush by and get away, but a lot of dogs jumped out and went to howling\r\nand barking at me, and I knowed better than to move another peg.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII\r\n\r\n\r\nIn about a minute somebody spoke out of a window without putting his\r\nhead out, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe done, boys! Who\xe2\x80\x99s there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeorge Jackson, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want nothing, sir. They held the candle, and took\r\na good look at me, and all said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, _he_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t a Shepherdson\xe2\x80\x94no,\r\nthere ain\xe2\x80\x99t any Shepherdson about him.\xe2\x80\x9d Then the old man said he hoped\r\nI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind being searched for arms, because he didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean no harm\r\nby it\xe2\x80\x94it was only to make sure. So he didn\xe2\x80\x99t pry into my pockets, but\r\nonly felt outside with his hands, and said it was all right. He told me\r\nto make myself easy and at home, and tell all about myself; but the old\r\nlady says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, bless you, Saul, the poor thing\xe2\x80\x99s as wet as he can be; and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou reckon it may be he\xe2\x80\x99s hungry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue for you, Rachel\xe2\x80\x94I forgot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the old lady says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetsy\xe2\x80\x9d (this was a nigger woman), \xe2\x80\x9cyou fly around and get him\r\nsomething to eat as quick as you can, poor thing; and one of you girls\r\ngo and wake up Buck and tell him\xe2\x80\x94oh, here he is himself. Buck, take\r\nthis little stranger and get the wet clothes off from him and dress him\r\nup in some of yours that\xe2\x80\x99s dry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBuck looked about as old as me\xe2\x80\x94thirteen or fourteen or along there,\r\nthough he was a little bigger than me. He hadn\xe2\x80\x99t on anything but a\r\nshirt, and he was very frowzy-headed. He came in gaping and digging one\r\nfist into his eyes, and he was dragging a gun along with the other one.\r\nHe says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin\xe2\x80\x99t they no Shepherdsons around?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey said, no, \xe2\x80\x99twas a false alarm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cif they\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 ben some, I reckon I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all laughed, and Bob says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Buck, they might have scalped us all, you\xe2\x80\x99ve been so slow in\r\ncoming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, nobody come after me, and it ain\xe2\x80\x99t right. I\xe2\x80\x99m always kept down;\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t get no show.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, Buck, my boy,\xe2\x80\x9d says the old man, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have show enough,\r\nall in good time, don\xe2\x80\x99t you fret about that. Go \xe2\x80\x99long with you now, and\r\ndo as your mother told you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen we got up-stairs to his room he got me a coarse shirt and a\r\nroundabout and pants of his, and I put them on. While I was at it he\r\nasked me what my name was, but before I could tell him he started to\r\ntell me about a bluejay and a young rabbit he had catched in the woods\r\nday before yesterday, and he asked me where Moses was when the candle\r\nwent out. I said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know; I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t heard about it before, no way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, guess,\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99m I going to guess,\xe2\x80\x9d says I, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I never heard tell of it\r\nbefore?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you can guess, can\xe2\x80\x99t you? It\xe2\x80\x99s just as easy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Which_ candle?\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, any candle,\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know where he was,\xe2\x80\x9d says I; \xe2\x80\x9cwhere was he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, he was in the _dark!_ That\xe2\x80\x99s where he was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if you knowed where he was, what did you ask me for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, blame it, it\xe2\x80\x99s a riddle, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see? Say, how long are you\r\ngoing to stay here? You got to stay always. We can just have booming\r\ntimes\xe2\x80\x94they don\xe2\x80\x99t have no school now. Do you own a dog? I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a\r\ndog\xe2\x80\x94and he\xe2\x80\x99ll go in the river and bring out chips that you throw in. Do\r\nyou like to comb up Sundays, and all that kind of foolishness? You bet\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t, but ma she makes me. Confound these ole britches! I reckon I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbetter put \xe2\x80\x99em on, but I\xe2\x80\x99d ruther not, it\xe2\x80\x99s so warm. Are you all ready?\r\nAll right. Come along, old hoss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCold corn-pone, cold corn-beef, butter and buttermilk\xe2\x80\x94that is what they\r\nhad for me down there, and there ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing better that ever I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ncome across yet. Buck and his ma and all of them smoked cob pipes,\r\nexcept the nigger woman, which was gone, and the two young women.\r\nThey all smoked and talked, and I eat and talked. The young women had\r\nquilts around them, and their hair down their backs. They all asked me\r\nquestions, and I told them how pap and me and all the family was living\r\non a little farm down at the bottom of Arkansaw, and my sister Mary Ann\r\nrun off and got married and never was heard of no more, and Bill went\r\nto hunt them and he warn\xe2\x80\x99t heard of no more, and Tom and Mort died,\r\nand then there warn\xe2\x80\x99t nobody but just me and pap left, and he was just\r\ntrimmed down to nothing, on account of his troubles; so when he died\r\nI took what there was left, because the farm didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to us, and\r\nstarted up the river, deck passage, and fell overboard; and that was\r\nhow I come to be here. So they said I could have a home there as long\r\nas I wanted it. Then it was most daylight and everybody went to bed,\r\nand I went to bed with Buck, and when I waked up in the morning, drat\r\nit all, I had forgot what my name was. So I laid there about an hour\r\ntrying to think, and when Buck waked up I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you spell, Buck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet you can\xe2\x80\x99t spell my name,\xe2\x80\x9d says I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bet you what you dare I can,\xe2\x80\x9d says he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d says I, \xe2\x80\x9cgo ahead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cG-e-o-r-g-e J-a-x-o-n\xe2\x80\x94there now,\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d says I, \xe2\x80\x9cyou done it, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think you could. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nslouch of a name to spell\xe2\x80\x94right off without studying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI set it down, private, because somebody might want _me_ to spell it\r\nnext, and so I wanted to be handy with it and rattle it off like I was\r\nused to it.\r\n\r\nIt was a mighty nice family, and a mighty nice house, too. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nseen no house out in the country before that was so nice and had so\r\nmuch style. It didn\xe2\x80\x99t have an iron latch on the front door, nor a\r\nwooden one with a buckskin string, but a brass knob to turn, the same\r\nas houses in town. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t no bed in the parlor, nor a sign of a\r\nbed; but heaps of parlors in towns has beds in them. There was a big\r\nfireplace that was bricked on the bottom, and the bricks was kept clean\r\nand red by pouring water on them and scrubbing them with another brick;\r\nsometimes they wash them over with red water-paint that they call\r\nSpanish-brown, same as they do in town. They had big brass dog-irons\r\nthat could hold up a saw-log. There was a clock on the middle of the\r\nmantelpiece, with a picture of a town painted on the bottom half of\r\nthe glass front, and a round place in the middle of it for the sun,\r\nand you could see the pendulum swinging behind it. It was beautiful to\r\nhear that clock tick; and sometimes when one of these peddlers had been\r\nalong and scoured her up and got her in good shape, she would start\r\nin and strike a hundred and fifty before she got tuckered out. They\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t took any money for her.\r\n\r\nWell, there was a big outlandish parrot on each side of the clock,\r\nmade out of something like chalk, and painted up gaudy. By one of the\r\nparrots was a cat made of crockery, and a crockery dog by the other;\r\nand when you pressed down on them they squeaked, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t open\r\ntheir mouths nor look different nor interested. They squeaked through\r\nunderneath. There was a couple of big wild-turkey-wing fans spread out\r\nbehind those things. On the table in the middle of the room was a kind\r\nof a lovely crockery basket that had apples and oranges and peaches and\r\ngrapes piled up in it, which was much redder and yellower and prettier\r\nthan real ones is, but they warn\xe2\x80\x99t real because you could see where\r\npieces had got chipped off and showed the white chalk, or whatever it\r\nwas, underneath.\r\n\r\nThis table had a cover made out of beautiful oilcloth, with a red and\r\nblue spread-eagle painted on it, and a painted border all around. It\r\ncome all the way from Philadelphia, they said. There was some books,\r\ntoo, piled up perfectly exact, on each corner of the table. One was a\r\nbig family Bible full of pictures. One was _Pilgrim\xe2\x80\x99s Progress_, about\r\na man that left his family, it didn\xe2\x80\x99t say why. I read considerable in\r\nit now and then. The statements was interesting, but tough. Another\r\nwas _Friendship\xe2\x80\x99s Offering_, full of beautiful stuff and poetry; but I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t read the poetry. Another was Henry Clay\xe2\x80\x99s Speeches, and another\r\nwas Dr. Gunn\xe2\x80\x99s Family Medicine, which told you all about what to do\r\nif a body was sick or dead. There was a hymn-book, and a lot of other\r\nbooks. And there was nice split-bottom chairs, and perfectly sound,\r\ntoo\xe2\x80\x94not bagged down in the middle and busted, like an old basket.\r\n\r\nThey had pictures hung on the walls\xe2\x80\x94mainly Washingtons and Lafayettes,\r\nand battles, and Highland Marys, and one called \xe2\x80\x9cSigning the\r\nDeclaration.\xe2\x80\x9d There was some that they called crayons, which one of\r\nthe daughters which was dead made her own self when she was only\r\nfifteen years old. They was different from any pictures I ever see\r\nbefore\xe2\x80\x94blacker, mostly, than is common. One was a woman in a slim black\r\ndress, belted small under the armpits, with bulges like a cabbage in\r\nthe middle of the sleeves, and a large black scoop-shovel bonnet with\r\na black veil, and white slim ankles crossed about with black tape, and\r\nvery wee black slippers, like a chisel, and she was leaning pensive\r\non a tombstone on her right elbow, under a weeping willow, and her\r\nother hand hanging down her side holding a white handkerchief and a\r\nreticule, and underneath the picture it said \xe2\x80\x9cShall I Never See Thee\r\nMore Alas.\xe2\x80\x9d Another one was a young lady with her hair all combed up\r\nstraight to the top of her head, and knotted there in front of a comb\r\nlike a chair-back, and she was crying into a handkerchief and had a\r\ndead bird laying on its back in her other hand with its heels up, and\r\nunderneath the picture it said \xe2\x80\x9cI Shall Never Hear Thy Sweet Chirrup\r\nMore Alas.\xe2\x80\x9d There was one where a young lady was at a window looking\r\nup at the moon, and tears running down her cheeks; and she had an open\r\nletter in one hand with black sealing-wax showing on one edge of it,\r\nand she was mashing a locket with a chain to it against her mouth, and\r\nunderneath the picture it said \xe2\x80\x9cAnd Art Thou Gone Yes Thou Art Gone\r\nAlas.\xe2\x80\x9d These was all nice pictures, I reckon, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t somehow\r\nseem to take to them, because if ever I was down a little they always\r\ngive me the fantods. Everybody was sorry she died, because she had\r\nlaid out a lot more of these pictures to do, and a body could see by\r\nwhat she had done what they had lost. But I reckoned that with her\r\ndisposition she was having a better time in the graveyard. She was at\r\nwork on what they said was her greatest picture when she took sick,\r\nand every day and every night it was her prayer to be allowed to live\r\ntill she got it done, but she never got the chance. It was a picture of\r\na young woman in a long white gown, standing on the rail of a bridge\r\nall ready to jump off, with her hair all down her back, and looking\r\nup to the moon, with the tears running down her face, and she had two\r\narms folded across her breast, and two arms stretched out in front,\r\nand two more reaching up toward the moon\xe2\x80\x94and the idea was to see which\r\npair would look best, and then scratch out all the other arms; but, as\r\nI was saying, she died before she got her mind made up, and now they\r\nkept this picture over the head of the bed in her room, and every time\r\nher birthday come they hung flowers on it. Other times it was hid with\r\na little curtain. The young woman in the picture had a kind of a nice\r\nsweet face, but there was so many arms it made her look too spidery,\r\nseemed to me.\r\n\r\nThis young girl kept a scrap-book when she was alive, and used to paste\r\nobituaries and accidents and cases of patient suffering in it out of\r\nthe _Presbyterian Observer_, and write poetry after them out of her own\r\nhead. It was very good poetry. This is what she wrote about a boy by\r\nthe name of Stephen Dowling Bots that fell down a well and was drownded:\r\n\r\nODE TO STEPHEN DOWLING BOTS, DEC\xe2\x80\x99D\r\n\r\n    And did young Stephen sicken,\r\n    And did young Stephen die?\r\n    And did the sad hearts thicken,\r\n    And did the mourners cry?\r\n\r\n    No; such was not the fate of\r\n    Young Stephen Dowling Bots;\r\n    Though sad hearts round him thickened,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x99Twas not from sickness\xe2\x80\x99 shots.\r\n\r\n    No whooping-cough did rack his frame,\r\n    Nor measles drear with spots;\r\n    Not these impaired the sacred name\r\n    Of Stephen Dowling Bots.\r\n\r\n    Despised love struck not with woe\r\n    That head of curly knots,\r\n    Nor stomach troubles laid him low,\r\n    Young Stephen Dowling Bots.\r\n\r\n    O no. Then list with tearful eye,\r\n    Whilst I his fate do tell.\r\n    His soul did from this cold world fly\r\n    By falling down a well.\r\n\r\n    They got him out and emptied him;\r\n    Alas it was too late;\r\n    His spirit was gone for to sport aloft\r\n    In the realms of the good and great.\r\n\r\nIf Emmeline Grangerford could make poetry like that before she was\r\nfourteen, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no telling what she could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done by and by.\r\nBuck said she could rattle off poetry like nothing. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\nhave to stop to think. He said she would slap down a line, and if she\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t find anything to rhyme with it would just scratch it out and\r\nslap down another one, and go ahead. She warn\xe2\x80\x99t particular; she could\r\nwrite about anything you choose to give her to write about just so it\r\nwas sadful. Every time a man died, or a woman died, or a child died,\r\nshe would be on hand with her \xe2\x80\x9ctribute\xe2\x80\x9d before he was cold. She called\r\nthem tributes. The neighbors said it was the doctor first, then\r\nEmmeline, then the undertaker\xe2\x80\x94the undertaker never got in ahead of\r\nEmmeline but once, and then she hung fire on a rhyme for the dead\r\nperson\xe2\x80\x99s name, which was Whistler. She warn\xe2\x80\x99t ever the same after\r\nthat; she never complained, but she kinder pined away and did not live\r\nlong. Poor thing, many\xe2\x80\x99s the time I made myself go up to the little\r\nroom that used to be hers and get out her poor old scrap-book and read\r\nin it when her pictures had been aggravating me and I had soured on\r\nher a little. I liked all that family, dead ones and all, and warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngoing to let anything come between us. Poor Emmeline made poetry about\r\nall the dead people when she was alive, and it didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem right that\r\nthere warn\xe2\x80\x99t nobody to make some about her now she was gone; so I\r\ntried to sweat out a verse or two myself, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to make\r\nit go somehow. They kept Emmeline\xe2\x80\x99s room trim and nice, and all the\r\nthings fixed in it just the way she liked to have them when she was\r\nalive, and nobody ever slept there. The old lady took care of the room\r\nherself, though there was plenty of niggers, and she sewed there a\r\ngood deal and read her Bible there mostly.\r\n\r\nWell, as I was saying about the parlor, there was beautiful curtains\r\non the windows: white, with pictures painted on them of castles with\r\nvines all down the walls, and cattle coming down to drink. There was a\r\nlittle old piano, too, that had tin pans in it, I reckon, and nothing\r\nwas ever so lovely as to hear the young ladies sing \xe2\x80\x9cThe Last Link is\r\nBroken\xe2\x80\x9d and play \xe2\x80\x9cThe Battle of Prague\xe2\x80\x9d on it. The walls of all the\r\nrooms was plastered, and most had carpets on the floors, and the whole\r\nhouse was whitewashed on the outside.\r\n\r\nIt was a double house, and the big open place betwixt them was roofed\r\nand floored, and sometimes the table was set there in the middle of\r\nthe day, and it was a cool, comfortable place. Nothing couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nbetter. And warn\xe2\x80\x99t the cooking good, and just bushels of it too!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nCol. Grangerford was a gentleman, you see. He was a gentleman all over;\r\nand so was his family. He was well born, as the saying is, and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nworth as much in a man as it is in a horse, so the Widow Douglas said,\r\nand nobody ever denied that she was of the first aristocracy in our\r\ntown; and pap he always said it, too, though he warn\xe2\x80\x99t no more quality\r\nthan a mudcat himself. Col. Grangerford was very tall and very slim,\r\nand had a darkish-paly complexion, not a sign of red in it anywheres;\r\nhe was clean-shaved every morning all over his thin face, and he had\r\nthe thinnest kind of lips, and the thinnest kind of nostrils, and a\r\nhigh nose, and heavy eyebrows, and the blackest kind of eyes, sunk so\r\ndeep back that they seemed like they was looking out of caverns at\r\nyou, as you may say. His forehead was high, and his hair was gray and\r\nstraight and hung to his shoulders. His hands was long and thin, and\r\nevery day of his life he put on a clean shirt and a full suit from\r\nhead to foot made out of linen so white it hurt your eyes to look at\r\nit; and on Sundays he wore a blue tail-coat with brass buttons on it.\r\nHe carried a mahogany cane with a silver head to it. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nfrivolishness about him, not a bit, and he warn\xe2\x80\x99t ever loud. He was\r\nas kind as he could be\xe2\x80\x94you could feel that, you know, and so you had\r\nconfidence. Sometimes he smiled, and it was good to see; but when he\r\nstraightened himself up like a liberty-pole, and the lightning begun to\r\nflicker out from under his eyebrows, you wanted to climb a tree first,\r\nand find out what the matter was afterwards. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t ever have to\r\ntell anybody to mind their manners\xe2\x80\x94everybody was always good-mannered\r\nwhere he was. Everybody loved to have him around, too; he was sunshine\r\nmost always\xe2\x80\x94I mean he made it seem like good weather. When he turned\r\ninto a cloud-bank it was awful dark for half a minute, and that was\r\nenough; there wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing go wrong again for a week.\r\n\r\nWhen him and the old lady come down in the morning all the family got\r\nup out of their chairs and give them good day, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t set down\r\nagain till they had set down. Then Tom and Bob went to the sideboard\r\nwhere the decanter was, and mixed a glass of bitters and handed it to\r\nhim, and he held it in his hand and waited till Tom\xe2\x80\x99s and Bob\xe2\x80\x99s was\r\nmixed, and then they bowed and said, \xe2\x80\x9cOur duty to you, sir, and madam\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\nand _they_ bowed the least bit in the world and said thank you, and so\r\nthey drank, all three, and Bob and Tom poured a spoonful of water on\r\nthe sugar and the mite of whisky or apple-brandy in the bottom of their\r\ntumblers, and give it to me and Buck, and we drank to the old people\r\ntoo.\r\n\r\nBob was the oldest and Tom next\xe2\x80\x94tall, beautiful men with very broad\r\nshoulders and brown faces, and long black hair and black eyes. They\r\ndressed in white linen from head to foot, like the old gentleman, and\r\nwore broad Panama hats.\r\n\r\nThen there was Miss Charlotte; she was twenty-five, and tall and proud\r\nand grand, but as good as she could be when she warn\xe2\x80\x99t stirred up; but\r\nwhen she was she had a look that would make you wilt in your tracks,\r\nlike her father. She was beautiful.\r\n\r\nSo was her sister, Miss Sophia, but it was a different kind. She was\r\ngentle and sweet like a dove, and she was only twenty.\r\n\r\nEach person had their own nigger to wait on them\xe2\x80\x94Buck too. My nigger\r\nhad a monstrous easy time, because I warn\xe2\x80\x99t used to having anybody do\r\nanything for me, but Buck\xe2\x80\x99s was on the jump most of the time.\r\n\r\nThis was all there was of the family now, but there used to be\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x94three sons; they got killed; and Emmeline that died.\r\n\r\nThe old gentleman owned a lot of farms and over a hundred niggers.\r\nSometimes a stack of people would come there, horseback, from ten\r\nor fifteen mile around, and stay five or six days, and have such\r\njunketings round about and on the river, and dances and picnics in the\r\nwoods daytimes, and balls at the house nights. These people was mostly\r\nkinfolks of the family. The men brought their guns with them. It was a\r\nhandsome lot of quality, I tell you.\r\n\r\nThere was another clan of aristocracy around there\xe2\x80\x94five or six\r\nfamilies\xe2\x80\x94mostly of the name of Shepherdson. They was as high-toned\r\nand well born and rich and grand as the tribe of Grangerfords. The\r\nShepherdsons and Grangerfords used the same steamboat-landing, which\r\nwas about two mile above our house; so sometimes when I went up there\r\nwith a lot of our folks I used to see a lot of the Shepherdsons there\r\non their fine horses.\r\n\r\nOne day Buck and me was away out in the woods hunting, and heard a\r\nhorse coming. We was crossing the road. Buck says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick! Jump for the woods!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe done it, and then peeped down the woods through the leaves. Pretty\r\nsoon a splendid young man came galloping down the road, setting his\r\nhorse easy and looking like a soldier. He had his gun across his\r\npommel. I had seen him before. It was young Harney Shepherdson. I heard\r\nBuck\xe2\x80\x99s gun go off at my ear, and Harney\xe2\x80\x99s hat tumbled off from his\r\nhead. He grabbed his gun and rode straight to the place where we was\r\nhid. But we didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait. We started through the woods on a run. The\r\nwoods warn\xe2\x80\x99t thick, so I looked over my shoulder to dodge the bullet,\r\nand twice I seen Harney cover Buck with his gun; and then he rode away\r\nthe way he come\xe2\x80\x94to get his hat, I reckon, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see. We never\r\nstopped running till we got home. The old gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s eyes blazed a\r\nminute\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99twas pleasure, mainly, I judged\xe2\x80\x94then his face sort of smoothed\r\ndown, and he says, kind of gentle:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like that shooting from behind a bush. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you step\r\ninto the road, my boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Shepherdsons don\xe2\x80\x99t, father. They always take advantage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Charlotte she held her head up like a queen while Buck was telling\r\nhis tale, and her nostrils spread and her eyes snapped. The two young\r\nmen looked dark, but never said nothing. Miss Sophia she turned pale,\r\nbut the color come back when she found the man warn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt.\r\n\r\nSoon as I could get Buck down by the corn-cribs under the trees by\r\nourselves, I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you want to kill him, Buck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I bet I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he do to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHim? He never done nothing to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, what did you want to kill him for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, nothing\xe2\x80\x94only it\xe2\x80\x99s on account of the feud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s a feud?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, where was you raised? Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know what a feud is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever heard of it before\xe2\x80\x94tell me about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d says Buck, \xe2\x80\x9ca feud is this way: A man has a quarrel with\r\nanother man, and kills him; then that other man\xe2\x80\x99s brother kills _him_;\r\nthen the other brothers, on both sides, goes for one another; then the\r\n_cousins_ chip in\xe2\x80\x94and by and by everybody\xe2\x80\x99s killed off, and there ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nno more feud. But it\xe2\x80\x99s kind of slow, and takes a long time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas this one been going on long, Buck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I should _reckon!_ It started thirty year ago, or som\xe2\x80\x99ers along\r\nthere. There was trouble \xe2\x80\x99bout something, and then a lawsuit to settle\r\nit; and the suit went agin one of the men, and so he up and shot the\r\nman that won the suit\xe2\x80\x94which he would naturally do, of course. Anybody\r\nwould.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was the trouble about, Buck?\xe2\x80\x94land?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon maybe\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, who done the shooting? Was it a Grangerford or a Shepherdson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLaws, how do _I_ know? It was so long ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t anybody know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, pa knows, I reckon, and some of the other old people; but\r\nthey don\xe2\x80\x99t know now what the row was about in the first place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas there been many killed, Buck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; right smart chance of funerals. But they don\xe2\x80\x99t always kill. Pa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngot a few buckshot in him; but he don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it \xe2\x80\x99cuz he don\xe2\x80\x99t weigh\r\nmuch, anyway. Bob\xe2\x80\x99s been carved up some with a bowie, and Tom\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nhurt once or twice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas anybody been killed this year, Buck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; we got one and they got one. \xe2\x80\x99Bout three months ago my cousin\r\nBud, fourteen year old, was riding through the woods on t\xe2\x80\x99other side\r\nof the river, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t have no weapon with him, which was blame\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfoolishness, and in a lonesome place he hears a horse a-coming behind\r\nhim, and sees old Baldy Shepherdson a-linkin\xe2\x80\x99 after him with his gun in\r\nhis hand and his white hair a-flying in the wind; and \xe2\x80\x99stead of jumping\r\noff and taking to the brush, Bud \xe2\x80\x99lowed he could outrun him; so they\r\nhad it, nip and tuck, for five mile or more, the old man a-gaining all\r\nthe time; so at last Bud seen it warn\xe2\x80\x99t any use, so he stopped and\r\nfaced around so as to have the bullet-holes in front, you know, and the\r\nold man he rode up and shot him down. But he didn\xe2\x80\x99t git much chance to\r\nenjoy his luck, for inside of a week our folks laid _him_ out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon that old man was a coward, Buck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon he _warn\xe2\x80\x99t_ a coward. Not by a blame\xe2\x80\x99 sight. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\ncoward amongst them Shepherdsons\xe2\x80\x94not a one. And there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no cowards\r\namongst the Grangerfords either. Why, that old man kep\xe2\x80\x99 up his end\r\nin a fight one day for half an hour against three Grangerfords, and\r\ncome out winner. They was all a-horseback; he lit off of his horse and\r\ngot behind a little woodpile, and kep\xe2\x80\x99 his horse before him to stop\r\nthe bullets; but the Grangerfords stayed on their horses and capered\r\naround the old man, and peppered away at him, and he peppered away at\r\nthem. Him and his horse both went home pretty leaky and crippled, but\r\nthe Grangerfords had to be _fetched_ home\xe2\x80\x94and one of \xe2\x80\x99em was dead, and\r\nanother died the next day. No, sir; if a body\xe2\x80\x99s out hunting for cowards\r\nhe don\xe2\x80\x99t want to fool away any time amongst them Shepherdsons, becuz\r\nthey don\xe2\x80\x99t breed any of that _kind_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNext Sunday we all went to church, about three mile, everybody\r\na-horseback. The men took their guns along, so did Buck, and kept\r\nthem between their knees or stood them handy against the wall. The\r\nShepherdsons done the same. It was pretty ornery preaching\xe2\x80\x94all about\r\nbrotherly love, and such-like tiresomeness; but everybody said it was\r\na good sermon, and they all talked it over going home, and had such\r\na powerful lot to say about faith and good works and free grace and\r\npreforeordestination, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what all, that it did seem to me\r\nto be one of the roughest Sundays I had run across yet.\r\n\r\nAbout an hour after dinner everybody was dozing around, some in their\r\nchairs and some in their rooms, and it got to be pretty dull. Buck and\r\na dog was stretched out on the grass in the sun sound asleep. I went\r\nup to our room, and judged I would take a nap myself. I found that\r\nsweet Miss Sophia standing in her door, which was next to ours, and\r\nshe took me in her room and shut the door very soft, and asked me if I\r\nliked her, and I said I did; and she asked me if I would do something\r\nfor her and not tell anybody, and I said I would. Then she said she\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nforgot her Testament, and left it in the seat at church between two\r\nother books, and would I slip out quiet and go there and fetch it to\r\nher, and not say nothing to nobody. I said I would. So I slid out and\r\nslipped off up the road, and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t anybody at the church, except\r\nmaybe a hog or two, for there warn\xe2\x80\x99t any lock on the door, and hogs\r\nlikes a puncheon floor in summer-time because it\xe2\x80\x99s cool. If you notice,\r\nmost folks don\xe2\x80\x99t go to church only when they\xe2\x80\x99ve got to; but a hog is\r\ndifferent.\r\n\r\nSays I to myself, something\xe2\x80\x99s up; it ain\xe2\x80\x99t natural for a girl to be in\r\nsuch a sweat about a Testament. So I give it a shake, and out drops\r\na little piece of paper with \xe2\x80\x9c_Half past two_\xe2\x80\x9d wrote on it with a\r\npencil. I ransacked it, but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find anything else. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake anything out of that, so I put the paper in the book again,\r\nand when I got home and up-stairs there was Miss Sophia in her door\r\nwaiting for me. She pulled me in and shut the door; then she looked\r\nin the Testament till she found the paper, and as soon as she read\r\nit she looked glad; and before a body could think she grabbed me and\r\ngive me a squeeze, and said I was the best boy in the world, and not\r\nto tell anybody. She was mighty red in the face for a minute, and her\r\neyes lighted up, and it made her powerful pretty. I was a good deal\r\nastonished, but when I got my breath I asked her what the paper was\r\nabout, and she asked me if I had read it, and I said no, and she asked\r\nme if I could read writing, and I told her \xe2\x80\x9cno, only coarse-hand,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nthen she said the paper warn\xe2\x80\x99t anything but a book-mark to keep her\r\nplace, and I might go and play now.\r\n\r\nI went off down to the river, studying over this thing, and pretty soon\r\nI noticed that my nigger was following along behind. When we was out of\r\nsight of the house he looked back and around a second, and then comes\r\na-running, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMars Jawge, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll come down into de swamp I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you a whole\r\nstack o\xe2\x80\x99 water-moccasins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThinks I, that\xe2\x80\x99s mighty curious; he said that yesterday. He oughter\r\nknow a body don\xe2\x80\x99t love water-moccasins enough to go around hunting for\r\nthem. What is he up to, anyway? So I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right; trot ahead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI followed a half a mile; then he struck out over the swamp, and waded\r\nankle-deep as much as another half-mile. We come to a little flat piece\r\nof land which was dry and very thick with trees and bushes and vines,\r\nand he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shove right in dah jist a few steps, Mars Jawge; dah\xe2\x80\x99s whah dey\r\nis. I\xe2\x80\x99s seed \xe2\x80\x99m befo\xe2\x80\x99; I don\xe2\x80\x99t k\xe2\x80\x99yer to see \xe2\x80\x99em no mo\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he slopped right along and went away, and pretty soon the trees\r\nhid him. I poked into the place a ways and come to a little open patch\r\nas big as a bedroom all hung around with vines, and found a man laying\r\nthere asleep\xe2\x80\x94and, by jings, it was my old Jim!\r\n\r\nI waked him up, and I reckoned it was going to be a grand surprise to\r\nhim to see me again, but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t. He nearly cried he was so glad, but\r\nhe warn\xe2\x80\x99t surprised. Said he swum along behind me that night, and heard\r\nme yell every time, but dasn\xe2\x80\x99t answer, because he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want nobody to\r\npick _him_ up and take him into slavery again. Says he:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI got hurt a little, en couldn\xe2\x80\x99t swim fas\xe2\x80\x99, so I wuz a considerable\r\nways behine you towards de las\xe2\x80\x99; when you landed I reck\xe2\x80\x99ned I could\r\nketch up wid you on de lan\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99dout havin\xe2\x80\x99 to shout at you, but when I\r\nsee dat house I begin to go slow. I \xe2\x80\x99uz off too fur to hear what dey\r\nsay to you\xe2\x80\x94I wuz \xe2\x80\x99fraid o\xe2\x80\x99 de dogs; but when it \xe2\x80\x99uz all quiet ag\xe2\x80\x99in I\r\nknowed you\xe2\x80\x99s in de house, so I struck out for de woods to wait for day.\r\nEarly in de mawnin\xe2\x80\x99 some er de niggers come along, gwyne to de fields,\r\nen dey tuk me en showed me dis place, whah de dogs can\xe2\x80\x99t track me on\r\naccounts o\xe2\x80\x99 de water, en dey brings me truck to eat every night, en\r\ntells me how you\xe2\x80\x99s a-gittin\xe2\x80\x99 along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell my Jack to fetch me here sooner, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, \xe2\x80\x99twarn\xe2\x80\x99t no use to \xe2\x80\x99sturb you, Huck, tell we could do sumfn\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99s all right now. I ben a-buyin\xe2\x80\x99 pots en pans en vittles, as I got a\r\nchanst, en a-patchin\xe2\x80\x99 up de raf\xe2\x80\x99 nights when\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ raft, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur ole raf\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean to say our old raft warn\xe2\x80\x99t smashed all to flinders?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, she warn\xe2\x80\x99t. She was tore up a good deal\xe2\x80\x94one en\xe2\x80\x99 of her was; but\r\ndey warn\xe2\x80\x99t no great harm done, on\xe2\x80\x99y our traps was mos\xe2\x80\x99 all los\xe2\x80\x99. Ef\r\nwe hadn\xe2\x80\x99 dive\xe2\x80\x99 so deep en swum so fur under water, en de night hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nben so dark, en we warn\xe2\x80\x99t so sk\xe2\x80\x99yerd, en ben sich punkin-heads, as de\r\nsayin\xe2\x80\x99 is, we\xe2\x80\x99d a seed de raf\xe2\x80\x99.
