No; some er de niggers\r\nfoun\xe2\x80\x99 her ketched on a snag along heah in de ben\xe2\x80\x99, en dey hid her in\r\na crick \xe2\x80\x99mongst de willows, en dey wuz so much jawin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout which un\r\n\xe2\x80\x99um she b\xe2\x80\x99long to de mos\xe2\x80\x99 dat I come to heah \xe2\x80\x99bout it pooty soon, so\r\nI ups en settles de trouble by tellin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99um she don\xe2\x80\x99t b\xe2\x80\x99long to none\r\nuv \xe2\x80\x99um, but to you en me; en I ast \xe2\x80\x99m if dey gwyne to grab a young\r\nwhite genlman\xe2\x80\x99s propaty, en git a hid\xe2\x80\x99n for it? Den I gin \xe2\x80\x99m ten cents\r\napiece, en dey \xe2\x80\x99uz mighty well satisfied, en wisht some mo\xe2\x80\x99 raf\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x99ud\r\ncome along en make \xe2\x80\x99m rich ag\xe2\x80\x99in. Dey\xe2\x80\x99s mighty good to me, dese niggers\r\nis, en whatever I wants \xe2\x80\x99m to do fur me I doan\xe2\x80\x99 have to ast \xe2\x80\x99m twice,\r\nhoney. Dat Jack\xe2\x80\x99s a good nigger, en pooty smart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he is. He ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever told me you was here; told me to come, and\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d show me a lot of water-moccasins. If anything happens _he_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmixed up in it. He can say he never seen us together, and it \xe2\x80\x99ll be the\r\ntruth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to talk much about the next day. I reckon I\xe2\x80\x99ll cut it\r\npretty short. I waked up about dawn, and was a-going to turn over and\r\ngo to sleep again when I noticed how still it was\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be\r\nanybody stirring. That warn\xe2\x80\x99t usual. Next I noticed that Buck was up\r\nand gone. Well, I gets up, a-wondering, and goes down-stairs\xe2\x80\x94nobody\r\naround; everything as still as a mouse. Just the same outside. Thinks\r\nI, what does it mean? Down by the woodpile I comes across my Jack, and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s it all about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSays he:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know, Mars Jawge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d says I, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, den, Miss Sophia\xe2\x80\x99s run off! \xe2\x80\x99deed she has. She run off in de\r\nnight some time\xe2\x80\x94nobody don\xe2\x80\x99t know jis\xe2\x80\x99 when; run off to get married\r\nto dat young Harney Shepherdson, you know\xe2\x80\x94leastways, so dey \xe2\x80\x99spec.\r\nDe fambly foun\xe2\x80\x99 it out \xe2\x80\x99bout half an hour ago\xe2\x80\x94maybe a little mo\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94en\xe2\x80\x99\r\nI _tell_ you dey warn\xe2\x80\x99t no time los\xe2\x80\x99. Sich another hurryin\xe2\x80\x99 up guns\r\nen hosses _you_ never see! De women folks has gone for to stir up de\r\nrelations, en ole Mars Saul en de boys tuck dey guns en rode up de\r\nriver road for to try to ketch dat young man en kill him \xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99 he kin\r\ngit acrost de river wid Miss Sophia. I reck\xe2\x80\x99n dey\xe2\x80\x99s gwyne to be mighty\r\nrough times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBuck went off \xe2\x80\x99thout waking me up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I reck\xe2\x80\x99n he _did!_ Dey warn\xe2\x80\x99t gwyne to mix you up in it.\r\nMars Buck he loaded up his gun en \xe2\x80\x99lowed he\xe2\x80\x99s gwyne to fetch home a\r\nShepherdson or bust. Well, dey\xe2\x80\x99ll be plenty un \xe2\x80\x99m dah, I reck\xe2\x80\x99n, en you\r\nbet you he\xe2\x80\x99ll fetch one ef he gits a chanst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI took up the river road as hard as I could put. By and by I begin to\r\nhear guns a good ways off. When I came in sight of the log store and\r\nthe woodpile where the steamboats lands I worked along under the trees\r\nand brush till I got to a good place, and then I clumb up into the\r\nforks of a cottonwood that was out of reach, and watched. There was a\r\nwood-rank four foot high a little ways in front of the tree, and first\r\nI was going to hide behind that; but maybe it was luckier I didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\nThere was four or five men cavorting around on their horses in the open\r\nplace before the log store, cussing and yelling, and trying to get at\r\na couple of young chaps that was behind the wood-rank alongside of the\r\nsteamboat-landing; but they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come it. Every time one of them\r\nshowed himself on the river side of the woodpile he got shot at. The\r\ntwo boys was squatting back to back behind the pile, so they could\r\nwatch both ways.\r\n\r\nBy and by the men stopped cavorting around and yelling. They started\r\nriding towards the store; then up gets one of the boys, draws a steady\r\nbead over the wood-rank, and drops one of them out of his saddle. All\r\nthe men jumped off of their horses and grabbed the hurt one and started\r\nto carry him to the store; and that minute the two boys started on the\r\nrun. They got half-way to the tree I was in before the men noticed.\r\nThen the men see them, and jumped on their horses and took out after\r\nthem. They gained on the boys, but it didn\xe2\x80\x99t do no good, the boys had\r\ntoo good a start; they got to the woodpile that was in front of my\r\ntree, and slipped in behind it, and so they had the bulge on the men\r\nagain. One of the boys was Buck, and the other was a slim young chap\r\nabout nineteen years old.\r\n\r\nThe men ripped around awhile, and then rode away. As soon as they\r\nwas out of sight I sung out to Buck and told him. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat to make of my voice coming out of the tree at first. He was\r\nawful surprised. He told me to watch out sharp and let him know when\r\nthe men come in sight again; said they was up to some devilment or\r\nother\xe2\x80\x94wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be gone long. I wished I was out of that tree, but I\r\ndasn\xe2\x80\x99t come down. Buck begun to cry and rip, and \xe2\x80\x99lowed that him and\r\nhis cousin Joe (that was the other young chap) would make up for this\r\nday yet. He said his father and his two brothers was killed, and two or\r\nthree of the enemy. Said the Shepherdsons laid for them in ambush. Buck\r\nsaid his father and brothers ought to waited for their relations\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nShepherdsons was too strong for them. I asked him what was become of\r\nyoung Harney and Miss Sophia. He said they\xe2\x80\x99d got across the river and\r\nwas safe. I was glad of that; but the way Buck did take on because he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t manage to kill Harney that day he shot at him\xe2\x80\x94I hain\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\nheard anything like it.\r\n\r\nAll of a sudden, bang! bang! bang! goes three or four guns\xe2\x80\x94the men had\r\nslipped around through the woods and come in from behind without their\r\nhorses! The boys jumped for the river\xe2\x80\x94both of them hurt\xe2\x80\x94and as they\r\nswum down the current the men run along the bank shooting at them and\r\nsinging out, \xe2\x80\x9cKill them, kill them!\xe2\x80\x9d It made me so sick I most fell\r\nout of the tree. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to tell _all_ that happened\xe2\x80\x94it would\r\nmake me sick again if I was to do that. I wished I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever come\r\nashore that night to see such things. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever going to get shut of\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94lots of times I dream about them.\r\n\r\nI stayed in the tree till it begun to get dark, afraid to come down.\r\nSometimes I heard guns away off in the woods; and twice I seen little\r\ngangs of men gallop past the log store with guns; so I reckoned the\r\ntrouble was still a-going on. I was mighty downhearted; so I made up\r\nmy mind I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever go a-near that house again, because I reckoned\r\nI was to blame, somehow. I judged that that piece of paper meant that\r\nMiss Sophia was to meet Harney somewheres at half past two and run\r\noff; and I judged I ought to told her father about that paper and the\r\ncurious way she acted, and then maybe he would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 locked her up, and\r\nthis awful mess wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever happened.\r\n\r\nWhen I got down out of the tree I crept along down the river-bank a\r\npiece, and found the two bodies laying in the edge of the water, and\r\ntugged at them till I got them ashore; then I covered up their faces,\r\nand got away as quick as I could. I cried a little when I was covering\r\nup Buck\xe2\x80\x99s face, for he was mighty good to me.\r\n\r\nIt was just dark now. I never went near the house, but struck through\r\nthe woods and made for the swamp. Jim warn\xe2\x80\x99t on his island, so I\r\ntramped off in a hurry for the crick, and crowded through the willows,\r\nred-hot to jump aboard and get out of that awful country. The raft was\r\ngone! My souls, but I was scared! I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get my breath for most a\r\nminute. Then I raised a yell. A voice not twenty-five foot from me says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood lan\xe2\x80\x99! is dat you, honey? Doan\xe2\x80\x99 make no noise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was Jim\xe2\x80\x99s voice\xe2\x80\x94nothing ever sounded so good before. I run along the\r\nbank a piece and got aboard, and Jim he grabbed me and hugged me, he\r\nwas so glad to see me. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLaws bless you, chile, I \xe2\x80\x99uz right down sho\xe2\x80\x99 you\xe2\x80\x99s dead ag\xe2\x80\x99in. Jack\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeen heah; he say he reck\xe2\x80\x99n you\xe2\x80\x99s ben shot, kase you didn\xe2\x80\x99 come home no\r\nmo\xe2\x80\x99; so I\xe2\x80\x99s jes\xe2\x80\x99 dis minute a-startin\xe2\x80\x99 de raf\xe2\x80\x99 down towards de mouf er\r\nde crick, so\xe2\x80\x99s to be all ready for to shove out en leave soon as Jack\r\ncomes ag\xe2\x80\x99in en tells me for certain you _is_ dead. Lawsy, I\xe2\x80\x99s mighty\r\nglad to git you back ag\xe2\x80\x99in, honey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s mighty good; they won\xe2\x80\x99t find me, and they\xe2\x80\x99ll think\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve been killed, and floated down the river\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s something up there\r\nthat \xe2\x80\x99ll help them think so\xe2\x80\x94so don\xe2\x80\x99t you lose no time, Jim, but just\r\nshove off for the big water as fast as ever you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI never felt easy till the raft was two mile below there and out in\r\nthe middle of the Mississippi. Then we hung up our signal lantern, and\r\njudged that we was free and safe once more. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had a bite to eat\r\nsince yesterday, so Jim he got out some corn-dodgers and buttermilk,\r\nand pork and cabbage and greens\xe2\x80\x94there ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing in the world so\r\ngood when it\xe2\x80\x99s cooked right\xe2\x80\x94and whilst I eat my supper we talked and\r\nhad a good time. I was powerful glad to get away from the feuds, and so\r\nwas Jim to get away from the swamp. We said there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no home like a\r\nraft, after all. Other places do seem so cramped up and smothery, but a\r\nraft don\xe2\x80\x99t. You feel mighty free and easy and comfortable on a raft.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX\r\n\r\n\r\nTwo or three days and nights went by; I reckon I might say they swum\r\nby, they slid along so quiet and smooth and lovely. Here is the way we\r\nput in the time. It was a monstrous big river down there\xe2\x80\x94sometimes a\r\nmile and a half wide; we run nights, and laid up and hid daytimes; soon\r\nas night was most gone we stopped navigating and tied up\xe2\x80\x94nearly always\r\nin the dead water under a towhead; and then cut young cottonwoods\r\nand willows, and hid the raft with them. Then we set out the lines.\r\nNext we slid into the river and had a swim, so as to freshen up\r\nand cool off; then we set down on the sandy bottom where the water\r\nwas about knee-deep, and watched the daylight come. Not a sound\r\nanywheres\xe2\x80\x94perfectly still\xe2\x80\x94just like the whole world was asleep, only\r\nsometimes the bullfrogs a-cluttering, maybe. The first thing to see,\r\nlooking away over the water, was a kind of dull line\xe2\x80\x94that was the\r\nwoods on t\xe2\x80\x99other side; you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make nothing else out; then a pale\r\nplace in the sky; then more paleness spreading around; then the river\r\nsoftened up away off, and warn\xe2\x80\x99t black any more, but gray; you could\r\nsee little dark spots drifting along ever so far away\xe2\x80\x94trading-scows,\r\nand such things; and long black streaks\xe2\x80\x94rafts; sometimes you could hear\r\na sweep screaking; or jumbled-up voices, it was so still, and sounds\r\ncome so far; and by and by you could see a streak on the water which\r\nyou know by the look of the streak that there\xe2\x80\x99s a snag there in a swift\r\ncurrent which breaks on it and makes that streak look that way; and you\r\nsee the mist curl up off of the water, and the east reddens up, and\r\nthe river, and you make out a log cabin in the edge of the woods, away\r\non the bank on t\xe2\x80\x99other side of the river, being a wood-yard, likely,\r\nand piled by them cheats so you can throw a dog through it anywheres;\r\nthen the nice breeze springs up, and comes fanning you from over there,\r\nso cool and fresh and sweet to smell on account of the woods and the\r\nflowers; but sometimes not that way, because they\xe2\x80\x99ve left dead fish\r\nlaying around, gars and such, and they do get pretty rank; and next\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve got the full day, and everything smiling in the sun, and the\r\nsong-birds just going it!\r\n\r\nA little smoke couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be noticed now, so we would take some fish off\r\nof the lines and cook up a hot breakfast. And afterwards we would\r\nwatch the lonesomeness of the river, and kind of lazy along, and by\r\nand by lazy off to sleep. Wake up by and by, and look to see what done\r\nit, and maybe see a steamboat coughing along up-stream, so far off\r\ntowards the other side you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell nothing about her only\r\nwhether she was a stern-wheel or side-wheel; then for about an hour\r\nthere wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be nothing to hear nor nothing to see\xe2\x80\x94just solid\r\nlonesomeness. Next you\xe2\x80\x99d see a raft sliding by, away off yonder, and\r\nmaybe a galoot on it chopping, because they\xe2\x80\x99re most always doing it on\r\na raft; you\xe2\x80\x99d see the ax flash and come down\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t hear nothing;\r\nyou see that ax go up again, and by the time it\xe2\x80\x99s above the man\xe2\x80\x99s head\r\nthen you hear the _k\xe2\x80\x99chunk!_\xe2\x80\x94it had took all that time to come over\r\nthe water. So we would put in the day, lazying around, listening to\r\nthe stillness. Once there was a thick fog, and the rafts and things\r\nthat went by was beating tin pans so the steamboats wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t run over\r\nthem. A scow or a raft went by so close we could hear them talking and\r\ncussing and laughing\xe2\x80\x94heard them plain; but we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no sign of\r\nthem; it made you feel crawly; it was like spirits carrying on that\r\nway in the air. Jim said he believed it was spirits; but I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; spirits wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say, \xe2\x80\x99Dern the dern fog.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSoon as it was night out we shoved; when we got her out to about the\r\nmiddle we let her alone, and let her float wherever the current wanted\r\nher to; then we lit the pipes, and dangled our legs in the water, and\r\ntalked about all kinds of things\xe2\x80\x94we was always naked, day and night,\r\nwhenever the mosquitoes would let us\xe2\x80\x94the new clothes Buck\xe2\x80\x99s folks\r\nmade for me was too good to be comfortable, and besides I didn\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\nmuch on clothes, nohow.\r\n\r\nSometimes we\xe2\x80\x99d have that whole river all to ourselves for the longest\r\ntime. Yonder was the banks and the islands, across the water; and\r\nmaybe a spark\xe2\x80\x94which was a candle in a cabin window; and sometimes on\r\nthe water you could see a spark or two\xe2\x80\x94on a raft or a scow, you know;\r\nand maybe you could hear a fiddle or a song coming over from one of\r\nthem crafts. It\xe2\x80\x99s lovely to live on a raft. We had the sky up there,\r\nall speckled with stars, and we used to lay on our backs and look up\r\nat them, and discuss about whether they was made or only just\r\nhappened. Jim he allowed they was made, but I allowed they happened; I\r\njudged it would have took too long to _make_ so many. Jim said the\r\nmoon could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 _laid_ them; well, that looked kind of reasonable, so I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t say nothing against it, because I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen a frog lay most as\r\nmany, so of course it could be done. We used to watch the stars that\r\nfell, too, and see them streak down. Jim allowed they\xe2\x80\x99d got spoiled\r\nand was hove out of the nest.\r\n\r\nOnce or twice of a night we would see a steamboat slipping along in\r\nthe dark, and now and then she would belch a whole world of sparks up\r\nout of her chimbleys, and they would rain down in the river and look\r\nawful pretty; then she would turn a corner and her lights would wink\r\nout and her powwow shut off and leave the river still again; and by\r\nand by her waves would get to us, a long time after she was gone, and\r\njoggle the raft a bit, and after that you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear nothing for\r\nyou couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell how long, except maybe frogs or something.\r\n\r\nAfter midnight the people on shore went to bed, and then for two or\r\nthree hours the shores was black\xe2\x80\x94no more sparks in the cabin windows.\r\nThese sparks was our clock\xe2\x80\x94the first one that showed again meant\r\nmorning was coming, so we hunted a place to hide and tie up right\r\naway.\r\n\r\nOne morning about daybreak I found a canoe and crossed over a chute to\r\nthe main shore\xe2\x80\x94it was only two hundred yards\xe2\x80\x94and paddled about a\r\nmile up a crick amongst the cypress woods, to see if I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nsome berries. Just as I was passing a place where a kind of a cowpath\r\ncrossed the crick, here comes a couple of men tearing up the path as\r\ntight as they could foot it. I thought I was a goner, for whenever\r\nanybody was after anybody I judged it was _me_\xe2\x80\x94or maybe Jim. I was\r\nabout to dig out from there in a hurry, but they was pretty close to\r\nme then, and sung out and begged me to save their lives\xe2\x80\x94said they\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t been doing nothing, and was being chased for it\xe2\x80\x94said there was\r\nmen and dogs a-coming. They wanted to jump right in, but I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you do it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t hear the dogs and horses yet; you\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\ntime to crowd through the brush and get up the crick a little ways;\r\nthen you take to the water and wade down to me and get in\xe2\x80\x94that \xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nthrow the dogs off the scent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey done it, and soon as they was aboard I lit out for our towhead,\r\nand in about five or ten minutes we heard the dogs and the men away\r\noff, shouting. We heard them come along towards the crick, but\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t see them; they seemed to stop and fool around awhile; then,\r\nas we got further and further away all the time, we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly\r\nhear them at all; by the time we had left a mile of woods behind us\r\nand struck the river, everything was quiet, and we paddled over to the\r\ntowhead and hid in the cottonwoods and was safe.\r\n\r\nOne of these fellows was about seventy or upwards, and had a bald head\r\nand very gray whiskers. He had an old battered-up slouch hat on, and a\r\ngreasy blue woolen shirt, and ragged old blue jeans britches stuffed\r\ninto his boot-tops, and home-knit galluses\xe2\x80\x94no, he only had one. He\r\nhad an old long-tailed blue jeans coat with slick brass buttons flung\r\nover his arm, and both of them had big, fat, ratty-looking\r\ncarpet-bags.\r\n\r\nThe other fellow was about thirty, and dressed about as ornery. After\r\nbreakfast we all laid off and talked, and the first thing that come\r\nout was that these chaps didn\xe2\x80\x99t know one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat got you into trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d says the baldhead to t\xe2\x80\x99other chap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99d been selling an article to take the tartar off the\r\nteeth\xe2\x80\x94and it does take it off, too, and generly the enamel along with\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94but I stayed about one night longer than I ought to, and was just\r\nin the act of sliding out when I ran across you on the trail this side\r\nof town, and you told me they were coming, and begged me to help you\r\nto get off. So I told you I was expecting trouble myself, and would\r\nscatter out _with_ you. That\xe2\x80\x99s the whole yarn\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s yourn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99d ben a-runnin\xe2\x80\x99 a little temperance revival thar \xe2\x80\x99bout a\r\nweek, and was the pet of the women folks, big and little, for I was\r\nmakin\xe2\x80\x99 it mighty warm for the rummies, I _tell_ you, and takin\xe2\x80\x99 as\r\nmuch as five or six dollars a night\xe2\x80\x94ten cents a head, children and\r\nniggers free\xe2\x80\x94and business a-growin\xe2\x80\x99 all the time, when somehow or\r\nanother a little report got around last night that I had a way of\r\nputtin\xe2\x80\x99 in my time with a private jug on the sly. A nigger rousted me\r\nout this mornin\xe2\x80\x99, and told me the people was getherin\xe2\x80\x99 on the quiet\r\nwith their dogs and horses, and they\xe2\x80\x99d be along pretty soon and give\r\nme \xe2\x80\x99bout half an hour\xe2\x80\x99s start, and then run me down if they could; and\r\nif they got me they\xe2\x80\x99d tar and feather me and ride me on a rail, sure.\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t wait for no breakfast\xe2\x80\x94I warn\xe2\x80\x99t hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld man,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young one, \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon we might double-team it\r\ntogether; what do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ain\xe2\x80\x99t undisposed. What\xe2\x80\x99s your line\xe2\x80\x94mainly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJour printer by trade; do a little in patent medicines;\r\ntheater-actor\xe2\x80\x94tragedy, you know; take a turn to mesmerism and\r\nphrenology when there\xe2\x80\x99s a chance; teach singing-geography school for a\r\nchange; sling a lecture sometimes\xe2\x80\x94oh, I do lots of things\xe2\x80\x94most\r\nanything that comes handy, so it ain\xe2\x80\x99t work. What\xe2\x80\x99s your lay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve done considerble in the doctoring way in my time. Layin\xe2\x80\x99 on o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhands is my best holt\xe2\x80\x94for cancer and paralysis, and sich things; and\r\nI k\xe2\x80\x99n tell a fortune pretty good when I\xe2\x80\x99ve got somebody along to find\r\nout the facts for me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame\r\n_you_, gentlemen\xe2\x80\x94far from it; I don\xe2\x80\x99t blame anybody. I deserve it\r\nall. Let the cold world do its worst; one thing I know\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ngrave somewhere for me. The world may go on just as it\xe2\x80\x99s always done,\r\nand take everything from me\xe2\x80\x94loved ones, property, everything; but it\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t take that. Some day I\xe2\x80\x99ll lie down in it and forget it all, and\r\nmy poor broken heart will be at rest.\xe2\x80\x9d He went on a-wiping.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrot your pore broken heart,\xe2\x80\x9d says the baldhead; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat are you\r\nheaving your pore broken heart at _us_ f\xe2\x80\x99r? _We_ hain\xe2\x80\x99t done nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I know you haven\xe2\x80\x99t. It\xe2\x80\x99s right I should\r\nsuffer\xe2\x80\x94perfectly right\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t make any moan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrought you down from whar? Whar was you brought down from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you would not believe me; the world never believes\xe2\x80\x94let it\r\npass\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99tis no matter. The secret of my birth\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe secret of your birth! Do you mean to say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d says the young man, very solemn, \xe2\x80\x9cI will reveal it to\r\nyou, for I feel I may have confidence in you. By rights I am a duke!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim\xe2\x80\x99s eyes bugged out when he heard that; and I reckon mine did, too.\r\nThen the baldhead says: \xe2\x80\x9cNo! you can\xe2\x80\x99t mean it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. My great-grandfather, eldest son of the Duke of Bridgewater,\r\nfled to this country about the end of the last century, to breathe the\r\npure air of freedom; married here, and died, leaving a son, his own\r\nfather dying about the same time. The second son of the late duke\r\nseized the titles and estates\xe2\x80\x94the infant real duke was ignored. I am\r\nthe lineal descendant of that infant\xe2\x80\x94I am the rightful Duke of\r\nBridgewater; and here am I, forlorn, torn from my high estate, hunted\r\nof men, despised by the cold world, ragged, worn, heartbroken, and\r\ndegraded to the companionship of felons on a raft!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim pitied him ever so much, and so did I. We tried to comfort him,\r\nbut he said it warn\xe2\x80\x99t much use, he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be much comforted; said if\r\nwe was a mind to acknowledge him, that would do him more good than\r\nmost anything else; so we said we would, if he would tell us how. He\r\nsaid we ought to bow when we spoke to him, and say \xe2\x80\x9cYour Grace,\xe2\x80\x9d or\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Lord,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cYour Lordship\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind it if we called\r\nhim plain \xe2\x80\x9cBridgewater,\xe2\x80\x9d which, he said, was a title anyway, and not a\r\nname; and one of us ought to wait on him at dinner, and do any little\r\nthing for him he wanted done.\r\n\r\nWell, that was all easy, so we done it. All through dinner Jim stood\r\naround and waited on him, and says, \xe2\x80\x9cWill yo\xe2\x80\x99 Grace have some o\xe2\x80\x99 dis\r\nor some o\xe2\x80\x99 dat?\xe2\x80\x9d and so on, and a body could see it was mighty\r\npleasing to him.\r\n\r\nBut the old man got pretty silent by and by\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t have much to say,\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t look pretty comfortable over all that petting that was\r\ngoing on around that duke. He seemed to have something on his mind.\r\nSo, along in the afternoon, he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooky here, Bilgewater,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m nation sorry for you, but you\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t the only person that\xe2\x80\x99s had troubles like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you ain\xe2\x80\x99t. You ain\xe2\x80\x99t the only person that\xe2\x80\x99s ben snaked down\r\nwrongfully out\xe2\x80\x99n a high place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you ain\xe2\x80\x99t the only person that\xe2\x80\x99s had a secret of his birth.\xe2\x80\x9d And,\r\nby jings, _he_ begins to cry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold! What do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBilgewater, kin I trust you?\xe2\x80\x9d says the old man, still sort of\r\nsobbing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the bitter death!\xe2\x80\x9d He took the old man by the hand and squeezed\r\nit, and says, \xe2\x80\x9cThat secret of your being: speak!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBilgewater, I am the late Dauphin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYou bet you, Jim and me stared this time. Then the duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my friend, it is too true\xe2\x80\x94your eyes is lookin\xe2\x80\x99 at this very\r\nmoment on the pore disappeared Dauphin, Looy the Seventeen, son of\r\nLooy the Sixteen and Marry Antonette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou! At your age! No! You mean you\xe2\x80\x99re the late Charlemagne; you must\r\nbe six or seven hundred years old, at the very least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrouble has done it, Bilgewater, trouble has done it; trouble has\r\nbrung these gray hairs and this premature balditude. Yes, gentlemen,\r\nyou see before you, in blue jeans and misery, the wanderin\xe2\x80\x99, exiled,\r\ntrampled-on, and sufferin\xe2\x80\x99 rightful King of France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, he cried and took on so that me and Jim didn\xe2\x80\x99t know hardly what\r\nto do, we was so sorry\xe2\x80\x94and so glad and proud we\xe2\x80\x99d got him with us,\r\ntoo. So we set in, like we done before with the duke, and tried to\r\ncomfort _him._ But he said it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use, nothing but to be dead\r\nand done with it all could do him any good; though he said it often\r\nmade him feel easier and better for a while if people treated him\r\naccording to his rights, and got down on one knee to speak to him, and\r\nalways called him \xe2\x80\x9cYour Majesty,\xe2\x80\x9d and waited on him first at meals,\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t set down in his presence till he asked them. So Jim and me\r\nset to majestying him, and doing this and that and t\xe2\x80\x99other for him,\r\nand standing up till he told us we might set down. This done him heaps\r\nof good, and so he got cheerful and comfortable. But the duke kind of\r\nsoured on him, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t look a bit satisfied with the way things was\r\ngoing; still, the king acted real friendly towards him, and said the\r\nduke\xe2\x80\x99s great-grandfather and all the other Dukes of Bilgewater was a\r\ngood deal thought of by _his_ father, and was allowed to come to the\r\npalace considerable; but the duke stayed huffy a good while, till by\r\nand by the king says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike as not we got to be together a blamed long time on this h-yer\r\nraft, Bilgewater, and so what\xe2\x80\x99s the use o\xe2\x80\x99 your bein\xe2\x80\x99 sour? It \xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nonly make things oncomfortable. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t my fault I warn\xe2\x80\x99t born a\r\nduke, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t your fault you warn\xe2\x80\x99t born a king\xe2\x80\x94so what\xe2\x80\x99s the use to\r\nworry? Make the best o\xe2\x80\x99 things the way you find \xe2\x80\x99em, says I\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\nmotto. This ain\xe2\x80\x99t no bad thing that we\xe2\x80\x99ve struck here\xe2\x80\x94plenty grub and\r\nan easy life\xe2\x80\x94come, give us your hand, duke, and le\xe2\x80\x99s all be friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duke done it, and Jim and me was pretty glad to see it. It took\r\naway all the uncomfortableness and we felt mighty good over it,\r\nbecause it would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been a miserable business to have any\r\nunfriendliness on the raft; for what you want, above all things, on a\r\nraft, is for everybody to be satisfied, and feel right and kind\r\ntowards the others.\r\n\r\nIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t take me long to make up my mind that these liars warn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nkings nor dukes at all, but just low-down humbugs and frauds. But I\r\nnever said nothing, never let on; kept it to myself; it\xe2\x80\x99s the best\r\nway; then you don\xe2\x80\x99t have no quarrels, and don\xe2\x80\x99t get into no trouble.\r\nIf they wanted us to call them kings and dukes, I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nobjections, \xe2\x80\x99long as it would keep peace in the family; and it warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nno use to tell Jim, so I didn\xe2\x80\x99t tell him. If I never learnt nothing\r\nelse out of pap, I learnt that the best way to get along with his kind\r\nof people is to let them have their own way.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX\r\n\r\n\r\nThey asked us considerable many questions; wanted to know what we\r\ncovered up the raft that way for, and laid by in the daytime instead\r\nof running\xe2\x80\x94was Jim a runaway nigger? Says I:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness sakes! would a runaway nigger run _south_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo, they allowed he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. I had to account for things some way, so\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy folks was living in Pike County, in Missouri, where I was born,\r\nand they all died off but me and pa and my brother Ike. Pa, he \xe2\x80\x99lowed\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d break up and go down and live with Uncle Ben, who\xe2\x80\x99s got a little\r\none-horse place on the river forty-four mile below Orleans. Pa was\r\npretty poor, and had some debts; so when he\xe2\x80\x99d squared up there warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnothing left but sixteen dollars and our nigger, Jim. That warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nenough to take us fourteen hundred mile, deck passage nor no other\r\nway. Well, when the river rose pa had a streak of luck one day; he\r\nketched this piece of a raft; so we reckoned we\xe2\x80\x99d go down to Orleans\r\non it. Pa\xe2\x80\x99s luck didn\xe2\x80\x99t hold out; a steamboat run over the forrard\r\ncorner of the raft one night, and we all went overboard and dove under\r\nthe wheel; Jim and me come up all right, but pa was drunk, and Ike was\r\nonly four years old, so they never come up no more. Well, for the next\r\nday or two we had considerable trouble, because people was always\r\ncoming out in skiffs and trying to take Jim away from me, saying they\r\nbelieved he was a runaway nigger. We don\xe2\x80\x99t run daytimes no more now;\r\nnights they don\xe2\x80\x99t bother us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave me alone to cipher out a way so we can run in the daytime if we\r\nwant to. I\xe2\x80\x99ll think the thing over\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll invent a plan that \xe2\x80\x99ll fix\r\nit. So the duke and the king went to overhauling our\r\nwigwam, to see what the beds was like. My bed was a straw tick\xe2\x80\x94better\r\nthan Jim\xe2\x80\x99s, which was a corn-shuck tick; there\xe2\x80\x99s always cobs around\r\nabout in a shuck tick, and they poke into you and hurt; and when you\r\nroll over the dry shucks sound like you was rolling over in a pile of\r\ndead leaves; it makes such a rustling that you wake up. Well, the duke\r\nallowed he would take my bed; but the king allowed he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. He\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 reckoned the difference in rank would a sejested to you\r\nthat a corn-shuck bed warn\xe2\x80\x99t just fitten for me to sleep on. Misfortune has broken my once haughty spirit; I yield, I\r\nsubmit; \xe2\x80\x99tis my fate. I am alone in the world\xe2\x80\x94let me suffer; I can\r\nbear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe got away as soon as it was good and dark. The king told us to stand\r\nwell out towards the middle of the river, and not show a light till we\r\ngot a long ways below the town. We come in sight of the little bunch\r\nof lights by and by\xe2\x80\x94that was the town, you know\xe2\x80\x94and slid by, about a\r\nhalf a mile out, all right. When we was three-quarters of a mile below\r\nwe hoisted up our signal lantern; and about ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock it come on to\r\nrain and blow and thunder and lighten like everything; so the king\r\ntold us to both stay on watch till the weather got better; then him\r\nand the duke crawled into the wigwam and turned in for the night. It\r\nwas my watch below till twelve, but I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 turned in anyway if\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d had a bed, because a body don\xe2\x80\x99t see such a storm as that every day\r\nin the week, not by a long sight. And every second or two there\xe2\x80\x99d come a glare that lit up the\r\nwhite-caps for a half a mile around, and you\xe2\x80\x99d see the islands looking\r\ndusty through the rain, and the trees thrashing around in the wind;\r\nthen comes a _h-whack!_\xe2\x80\x94bum! bum! bumble-umble-um-bum-bum-bum-bum\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthe thunder would go rumbling and grumbling away, and quit\xe2\x80\x94and then\r\n_rip_ comes another flash and another sock-dolager. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t have no trouble about snags; the lightning\r\nwas glaring and flittering around so constant that we could see them\r\nplenty soon enough to throw her head this way or that and miss them.\r\n\r\nI had the middle watch, you know, but I was pretty sleepy by that\r\ntime, so Jim he said he would stand the first half of it for me; he\r\nwas always mighty good that way, Jim was. About\r\ntwo they come up again, though, and Jim was going to call me; but he\r\nchanged his mind, because he reckoned they warn\xe2\x80\x99t high enough yet to\r\ndo any harm; but he was mistaken about that, for pretty soon all of a\r\nsudden along comes a regular ripper and washed me overboard. It most\r\nkilled Jim a-laughing. He was the easiest nigger to laugh that ever\r\nwas, anyway.\r\n\r\nI took the watch, and Jim he laid down and snored away; and by and by\r\nthe storm let up for good and all; and the first cabin-light that\r\nshowed I rousted him out, and we slid the raft into hiding-quarters\r\nfor the day.\r\n\r\nThe king got out an old ratty deck of cards after breakfast, and him\r\nand the duke played seven-up awhile, five cents a game. Then they got\r\ntired of it, and allowed they would \xe2\x80\x9clay out a campaign,\xe2\x80\x9d as they\r\ncalled it. The duke went down into his carpet-bag, and fetched up a\r\nlot of little printed bills and read them out loud. One bill said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe celebrated Dr. Armand de Montalban, of Paris,\xe2\x80\x9d would \xe2\x80\x9clecture on\r\nthe Science of Phrenology\xe2\x80\x9d at such and such a place, on the blank day\r\nof blank, at ten cents admission, and \xe2\x80\x9cfurnish charts of character at\r\ntwenty-five cents apiece.\xe2\x80\x9d The duke said that was _him._ In another\r\nbill he was the \xe2\x80\x9cworld-renowned Shakespearian tragedian, Garrick the\r\nYounger, of Drury Lane, London.\xe2\x80\x9d In other bills he had a lot of other\r\nnames and done other wonderful things, like finding water and gold\r\nwith a \xe2\x80\x9cdivining-rod,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cdissipating witch spells,\xe2\x80\x9d and so on. Le\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncommence right away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the duke he told him all about who Romeo was and who Juliet was,\r\nand said he was used to being Romeo, so the king could be Juliet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if Juliet\xe2\x80\x99s such a young gal, duke, my peeled head and my white\r\nwhiskers is goin\xe2\x80\x99 to look oncommon odd on her, maybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, don\xe2\x80\x99t you worry; these country jakes won\xe2\x80\x99t ever think of that.\r\nBesides, you know, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be in costume, and that makes all the\r\ndifference in the world; Juliet\xe2\x80\x99s in a balcony, enjoying the moonlight\r\nbefore she goes to bed, and she\xe2\x80\x99s got on her nightgown and her ruffled\r\nnightcap.
