The king got the directions, and allowed he\xe2\x80\x99d go and work\r\nthat camp-meeting for all it was worth, and I might go, too.\r\n\r\nThe duke said what he was after was a printing-office. We found it; a\r\nlittle bit of a concern, up over a carpenter-shop\xe2\x80\x94carpenters and\r\nprinters all gone to the meeting, and no doors locked. It was a dirty,\r\nlittered-up place, and had ink-marks, and handbills with pictures of\r\nhorses and runaway niggers on them, all over the walls. The duke shed\r\nhis coat and said he was all right now. So me and the king lit out for\r\nthe camp-meeting.\r\n\r\nWe got there in about a half an hour fairly dripping, for it was a\r\nmost awful hot day. There was as much as a thousand people there from\r\ntwenty mile around. The woods was full of teams and wagons, hitched\r\neverywheres, feeding out of the wagon-troughs and stomping to keep off\r\nthe flies. There was sheds made out of poles and roofed over with\r\nbranches, where they had lemonade and gingerbread to sell, and piles\r\nof watermelons and green corn and such-like truck.\r\n\r\nThe preaching was going on under the same kinds of sheds, only they\r\nwas bigger and held crowds of people. The benches was made out of\r\noutside slabs of logs, with holes bored in the round side to drive\r\nsticks into for legs. They didn\xe2\x80\x99t have no backs. The preachers had\r\nhigh platforms to stand on at one end of the sheds. The women had on\r\nsun-bonnets; and some had linsey-woolsey frocks, some gingham ones,\r\nand a few of the young ones had on calico. Some of the young men was\r\nbarefooted, and some of the children didn\xe2\x80\x99t have on any clothes but\r\njust a tow-linen shirt. Some of the old women was knitting, and some\r\nof the young folks was courting on the sly.\r\n\r\nThe first shed we come to the preacher was lining out a hymn. He lined\r\nout two lines, everybody sung it, and it was kind of grand to hear it,\r\nthere was so many of them and they done it in such a rousing way; then\r\nhe lined out two more for them to sing\xe2\x80\x94and so on. The people woke up\r\nmore and more, and sung louder and louder; and towards the end some\r\nbegun to groan, and some begun to shout. Then the preacher begun to\r\npreach, and begun in earnest, too; and went weaving first to one side\r\nof the platform and then the other, and then a-leaning down over the\r\nfront of it, with his arms and his body going all the time, and\r\nshouting his words out with all his might; and every now and then he\r\nwould hold up his Bible and spread it open, and kind of pass it around\r\nthis way and that, shouting, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the brazen serpent in the\r\nwilderness! Look upon it and live!\xe2\x80\x9d And people would shout out,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGlory!\xe2\x80\x94A-a-_men_!\xe2\x80\x9d And so he went on, and the people groaning and\r\ncrying and saying amen:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come to the mourners\xe2\x80\x99 bench! come, black with sin! (_amen!_)\r\ncome, sick and sore! (_amen!_) come, lame and halt and blind!\r\n(_amen!_) come, pore and needy, sunk in shame! (_a-a-men!_) come, all\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s worn and soiled and suffering!\xe2\x80\x94come with a broken spirit! come\r\nwith a contrite heart! come in your rags and sin and dirt! the waters\r\nthat cleanse is free, the door of heaven stands open\xe2\x80\x94oh, enter in and\r\nbe at rest!\xe2\x80\x9d (_a-a-men! glory, glory hallelujah!_)\r\n\r\nAnd so on. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out what the preacher said any more, on\r\naccount of the shouting and crying. Folks got up everywheres in the\r\ncrowd, and worked their way just by main strength to the mourners\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbench, with the tears running down their faces; and when all the\r\nmourners had got up there to the front benches in a crowd, they sung\r\nand shouted and flung themselves down on the straw, just crazy and\r\nwild.\r\n\r\nWell, the first I knowed the king got a-going, and you could hear him\r\nover everybody; and next he went a-charging up onto the platform, and\r\nthe preacher he begged him to speak to the people, and he done it. He\r\ntold them he was a pirate\xe2\x80\x94been a pirate for thirty years out in the\r\nIndian Ocean\xe2\x80\x94and his crew was thinned out considerable last spring in\r\na fight, and he was home now to take out some fresh men, and thanks to\r\ngoodness he\xe2\x80\x99d been robbed last night and put ashore off of a steamboat\r\nwithout a cent, and he was glad of it; it was the blessedest thing\r\nthat ever happened to him, because he was a changed man now, and happy\r\nfor the first time in his life; and, poor as he was, he was going to\r\nstart right off and work his way back to the Indian Ocean, and put in\r\nthe rest of his life trying to turn the pirates into the true path;\r\nfor he could do it better than anybody else, being acquainted with all\r\npirate crews in that ocean; and though it would take him a long time\r\nto get there without money, he would get there anyway, and every time\r\nhe convinced a pirate he would say to him, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you thank me, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou give me no credit; it all belongs to them dear people in Pokeville\r\ncamp-meeting, natural brothers and benefactors of the race, and that\r\ndear preacher there, the truest friend a pirate ever had!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then he busted into tears, and so did everybody. Then somebody\r\nsings out, \xe2\x80\x9cTake up a collection for him, take up a collection!\xe2\x80\x9d Well,\r\na half a dozen made a jump to do it, but somebody sings out, \xe2\x80\x9cLet\r\n_him_ pass the hat around!\xe2\x80\x9d Then everybody said it, the preacher too.\r\n\r\nSo the king went all through the crowd with his hat, swabbing his\r\neyes, and blessing the people and praising them and thanking them for\r\nbeing so good to the poor pirates away off there; and every little\r\nwhile the prettiest kind of girls, with the tears running down their\r\ncheeks, would up and ask him would he let them kiss him for to\r\nremember him by; and he always done it; and some of them he hugged and\r\nkissed as many as five or six times\xe2\x80\x94and he was invited to stay a\r\nweek; and everybody wanted him to live in their houses, and said\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d think it was an honor; but he said as this was the last day of\r\nthe camp-meeting he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do no good, and besides he was in a sweat\r\nto get to the Indian Ocean right off and go to work on the pirates.\r\n\r\nWhen we got back to the raft and he come to count up he found he had\r\ncollected eighty-seven dollars and seventy-five cents. And then he had\r\nfetched away a three-gallon jug of whisky, too, that he found under a\r\nwagon when he was starting home through the woods. The king said, take\r\nit all around, it laid over any day he\xe2\x80\x99d ever put in in the\r\nmissionarying line. He said it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use talking, heathens don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\namount to shucks alongside of pirates to work a camp-meeting with.\r\n\r\nThe duke was thinking _he\xe2\x80\x99d_ been doing pretty well till the king\r\ncome to show up, but after that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t think so so much. He\r\nhad set up and printed off two little jobs for farmers in that\r\nprinting-office\xe2\x80\x94horse bills\xe2\x80\x94and took the money, four dollars. And he\r\nhad got in ten dollars\xe2\x80\x99 worth of advertisements for the paper, which\r\nhe said he would put in for four dollars if they would pay in\r\nadvance\xe2\x80\x94so they done it. The price of the paper was two dollars a\r\nyear, but he took in three subscriptions for half a dollar apiece on\r\ncondition of them paying him in advance; they were going to pay in\r\ncordwood and onions as usual, but he said he had just bought the\r\nconcern and knocked down the price as low as he could afford it, and\r\nwas going to run it for cash. He set up a little piece of poetry,\r\nwhich he made, himself, out of his own head\xe2\x80\x94three verses\xe2\x80\x94kind of\r\nsweet and saddish\xe2\x80\x94the name of it was, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, crush, cold world, this\r\nbreaking heart\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and he left that all set up and ready to print in the\r\npaper, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t charge nothing for it. Well, he took in nine dollars\r\nand a half, and said he\xe2\x80\x99d done a pretty square day\xe2\x80\x99s work for it.\r\n\r\nThen he showed us another little job he\xe2\x80\x99d printed and hadn\xe2\x80\x99t charged\r\nfor, because it was for us. It had a picture of a runaway nigger with\r\na bundle on a stick over his shoulder, and \xe2\x80\x9c$200 reward\xe2\x80\x9d under it. The\r\nreading was all about Jim and just described him to a dot. It said he\r\nrun away from St. Jacques\xe2\x80\x99s plantation, forty mile below New Orleans,\r\nlast winter, and likely went north, and whoever would catch him and\r\nsend him back he could have the reward and expenses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d says the duke, \xe2\x80\x9cafter to-night we can run in the daytime if we\r\nwant to. Whenever we see anybody coming we can tie Jim hand and foot\r\nwith a rope, and lay him in the wigwam and show this handbill and say\r\nwe captured him up the river, and were too poor to travel on a\r\nsteamboat, so we got this little raft on credit from our friends and\r\nare going down to get the reward. Handcuffs and chains would look\r\nstill better on Jim, but it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go well with the story of us\r\nbeing so poor. Too much like jewelry. Ropes are the correct thing\xe2\x80\x94we\r\nmust preserve the unities, as we say on the boards.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all said the duke was pretty smart, and there couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be no\r\ntrouble about running daytimes. We judged we could make miles enough\r\nthat night to get out of the reach of the powwow we reckoned the\r\nduke\xe2\x80\x99s work in the printing-office was going to make in that little\r\ntown; then we could boom right along if we wanted to.\r\n\r\nWe laid low and kept still, and never shoved out till nearly ten\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock; then we slid by, pretty wide away from the town, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhoist our lantern till we was clear out of sight of it.\r\n\r\nWhen Jim called me to take the watch at four in the morning, he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck, does you reck\xe2\x80\x99n we gwyne to run acrost any mo\xe2\x80\x99 kings on dis\r\ntrip?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d says he, \xe2\x80\x9cdat\xe2\x80\x99s all right, den. I doan\xe2\x80\x99 mine one er two kings,\r\nbut dat\xe2\x80\x99s enough. Dis one\xe2\x80\x99s powerful drunk, en de duke ain\xe2\x80\x99 much\r\nbetter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI found Jim had been trying to get him to talk French, so he could\r\nhear what it was like; but he said he had been in this country so\r\nlong, and had so much trouble, he\xe2\x80\x99d forgot it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was after sun-up now, but we went right on and didn\xe2\x80\x99t tie up. The\r\nking and the duke turned out by and by looking pretty rusty; but after\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d jumped overboard and took a swim it chippered them up a good\r\ndeal. After breakfast the king he took a seat on the corner of the\r\nraft, and pulled off his boots and rolled up his britches, and let his\r\nlegs dangle in the water, so as to be comfortable, and lit his pipe,\r\nand went to getting his \xe2\x80\x9cRomeo and Juliet\xe2\x80\x9d by heart. When he had got\r\nit pretty good him and the duke begun to practise it together. The\r\nduke had to learn him over and over again how to say every speech; and\r\nhe made him sigh, and put his hand on his heart, and after a while he\r\nsaid he done it pretty well; \xe2\x80\x9conly,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cyou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t bellow out\r\n_Romeo!_ that way, like a bull\xe2\x80\x94you must say it soft and sick and\r\nlanguishy, so\xe2\x80\x94R-o-o-meo! that is the idea; for Juliet\xe2\x80\x99s a dear sweet\r\nmere child of a girl, you know, and she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t bray like a jackass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, next they got out a couple of long swords that the duke made out\r\nof oak laths, and begun to practise the sword-fight\xe2\x80\x94the duke called\r\nhimself Richard III.; and the way they laid on and pranced around the\r\nraft was grand to see. But by and by the king tripped and fell\r\noverboard, and after that they took a rest, and had a talk about all\r\nkinds of adventures they\xe2\x80\x99d had in other times along the river.\r\n\r\nAfter dinner the duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Capet, we\xe2\x80\x99ll want to make this a first-class show, you know, so\r\nI guess we\xe2\x80\x99ll add a little more to it. We want a little something to\r\nanswer encores with, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s onkores, Bilgewater?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duke told him, and then says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll answer by doing the Highland fling or the sailor\xe2\x80\x99s hornpipe; and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94well, let me see\xe2\x80\x94oh, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got it\xe2\x80\x94you can do Hamlet\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsoliloquy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHamlet\xe2\x80\x99s which?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHamlet\xe2\x80\x99s soliloquy, you know; the most celebrated thing in\r\nShakespeare. Ah, it\xe2\x80\x99s sublime, sublime! Always fetches the house. I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t got it in the book\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve only got one volume\xe2\x80\x94but I reckon I\r\ncan piece it out from memory. I\xe2\x80\x99ll just walk up and down a minute, and\r\nsee if I can call it back from recollection\xe2\x80\x99s vaults.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he went to marching up and down, thinking, and frowning horrible\r\nevery now and then; then he would hoist up his eyebrows; next he would\r\nsqueeze his hand on his forehead and stagger back and kind of moan;\r\nnext he would sigh, and next he\xe2\x80\x99d let on to drop a tear. It was\r\nbeautiful to see him. By and by he got it. He told us to give\r\nattention. Then he strikes a most noble attitude, with one leg shoved\r\nforwards, and his arms stretched away up, and his head tilted back,\r\nlooking up at the sky; and then he begins to rip and rave and grit his\r\nteeth; and after that, all through his speech, he howled, and spread\r\naround, and swelled up his chest, and just knocked the spots out of\r\nany acting ever _I_ see before. This is the speech\xe2\x80\x94I learned it, easy\r\nenough, while he was learning it to the king:\r\n\r\n    To be, or not to be; that is the bare bodkin\r\n    That makes calamity of so long life;\r\n    For who would fardels bear, till Birnam Wood do come to\r\n        Dunsinane,\r\n    But that the fear of something after death\r\n    Murders the innocent sleep,\r\n    Great nature\xe2\x80\x99s second course,\r\n    And makes us rather sling the arrows of outrageous fortune\r\n    Than fly to others that we know not of.\r\n    There\xe2\x80\x99s the respect must give us pause:\r\n    Wake Duncan with thy knocking! I would thou couldst;\r\n    For who would bear the whips and scorns of time,\r\n    The oppressor\xe2\x80\x99s wrong, the proud man\xe2\x80\x99s contumely,\r\n    The law\xe2\x80\x99s delay, and the quietus which his pangs might take,\r\n    In the dead waste and middle of the night, when churchyards\r\n        yawn\r\n    In customary suits of solemn black,\r\n    But that the undiscovered country from whose bourne no\r\n        traveler returns,\r\n    Breathes forth contagion on the world,\r\n    And thus the native hue of resolution, like the poor cat i\xe2\x80\x99 the\r\n        adage,\r\n    Is sicklied o\xe2\x80\x99er with care,\r\n    And all the clouds that lowered o\xe2\x80\x99er our housetops,\r\n    With this regard their currents turn awry,\r\n    And lose the name of action.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x99Tis a consummation devoutly to be wished. But soft you, the\r\n        fair Ophelia:\r\n    Ope not thy ponderous and marble jaws,\r\n    But get thee to a nunnery\xe2\x80\x94go!\r\n\r\nWell, the old man he liked that speech, and he mighty soon got it so\r\nhe could do it first-rate. It seemed like he was just born for it; and\r\nwhen he had his hand in and was excited, it was perfectly lovely the\r\nway he would rip and tear and rair up behind when he was getting it off.\r\n\r\nThe first chance we got the duke he had some show-bills printed; and\r\nafter that, for two or three days as we floated along, the raft was a\r\nmost uncommon lively place, for there warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing but sword-fighting\r\nand rehearsing\xe2\x80\x94as the duke called it\xe2\x80\x94going on all the time. One\r\nmorning, when we was pretty well down the state of Arkansaw, we come\r\nin sight of a little one-horse town in a big bend; so we tied up about\r\nthree-quarters of a mile above it, in the mouth of a crick which was\r\nshut in like a tunnel by the cypress trees, and all of us but Jim took\r\nthe canoe and went down there to see if there was any chance in that\r\nplace for our show.\r\n\r\nWe struck it mighty lucky; there was going to be a circus there that\r\nafternoon, and the country-people was already beginning to come in, in\r\nall kinds of old shackly wagons, and on horses. The circus would leave\r\nbefore night, so our show would have a pretty good chance. The duke he\r\nhired the court house, and we went around and stuck up our bills. They\r\nread like this:\r\n\r\n\r\n    Shaksperean Revival ! ! !\r\n    Wonderful Attraction!\r\n    For One Night Only!\r\n    The world-renowned tragedians,\r\n    David Garrick the younger, of Drury Lane Theatre, London,\r\n       and\r\n    Edmund Kean the elder, of the Royal Haymarket Theatre,\r\n    Whitechapel, Pudding Lane, Piccadilly, London, and the\r\n    Royal Continental Theatres, in their sublime\r\n    Shaksperean Spectacle entitled\r\n    The Balcony Scene\r\n       in\r\n    Romeo and Juliet ! ! !\r\n    Romeo...................Mr. Garrick\r\n    Juliet..................Mr. Kean\r\n    Assisted by the whole strength of the company!\r\n    New costumes, new scenery, new appointments!\r\n       Also:\r\n    The thrilling, masterly, and blood-curdling\r\n    Broad-sword conflict\r\n    In Richard III. ! ! !\r\n    Richard III.............Mr. Garrick\r\n    Richmond................Mr. Kean\r\n       Also:\r\n    (by special request)\r\n    Hamlet\xe2\x80\x99s Immortal Soliloquy ! !\r\n    By the Illustrious Kean!\r\n    Done by him 300 consecutive nights in Paris!\r\n    For One Night Only,\r\n    On account of imperative European engagements!\r\n    Admission 25 cents; children and servants, 10 cents.\r\n\r\n\r\nThen we went loafing around town. The stores and houses was most all\r\nold, shackly, dried-up frame concerns that hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever been painted;\r\nthey was set up three or four foot above ground on stilts, so as to be\r\nout of reach of the water when the river was overflowed. The houses\r\nhad little gardens around them, but they didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to raise hardly\r\nanything in them but jimpson weeds, and sunflowers, and ash-piles, and\r\nold curled-up boots and shoes, and pieces of bottles, and rags, and\r\nplayed-out tinware. The fences was made of different kinds of boards,\r\nnailed on at different times; and they leaned every which way, and had\r\ngates that didn\xe2\x80\x99t generly have but one hinge\xe2\x80\x94a leather one. Some of the\r\nfences had been whitewashed some time or another, but the duke said\r\nit was in Columbus\xe2\x80\x99s time, like enough. There was generly hogs in the\r\ngarden, and people driving them out.\r\n\r\nAll the stores was along one street. They had white domestic awnings in\r\nfront, and the country-people hitched their horses to the awning-posts.\r\nThere was empty dry-goods boxes under the awnings, and loafers roosting\r\non them all day long, whittling them with their Barlow knives; and\r\nchawing tobacco, and gaping and yawning and stretching\xe2\x80\x94a mighty ornery\r\nlot. They generly had on yellow straw hats most as wide as an umbrella,\r\nbut didn\xe2\x80\x99t wear no coats nor waistcoats; they called one another Bill,\r\nand Buck, and Hank, and Joe, and Andy, and talked lazy and drawly, and\r\nused considerable many cuss-words. There was as many as one loafer\r\nleaning up against every awning-post, and he most always had his hands\r\nin his britches pockets, except when he fetched them out to lend a chaw\r\nof tobacco or scratch. What a body was hearing amongst them all the\r\ntime was:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGimme a chaw \xe2\x80\x99v tobacker, Hank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCain\xe2\x80\x99t; I hain\xe2\x80\x99t got but one chaw left. Ask Bill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMaybe Bill he gives him a chaw; maybe he lies and says he ain\xe2\x80\x99t got\r\nnone. Some of them kinds of loafers never has a cent in the world,\r\nnor a chaw of tobacco of their own. They get all their chawing by\r\nborrowing; they say to a fellow, \xe2\x80\x9cI wisht you\xe2\x80\x99d len\xe2\x80\x99 me a chaw, Jack, I\r\njist this minute give Ben Thompson the last chaw I had\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94which is a lie\r\npretty much every time; it don\xe2\x80\x99t fool nobody but a stranger; but Jack\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t no stranger, so he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ give him a chaw, did you? So did your sister\xe2\x80\x99s cat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngrandmother. You pay me back the chaws you\xe2\x80\x99ve awready borry\xe2\x80\x99d off\xe2\x80\x99n me,\r\nLafe Buckner, then I\xe2\x80\x99ll loan you one or two ton of it, and won\xe2\x80\x99t charge\r\nyou no back intrust, nuther.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I _did_ pay you back some of it wunst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you did\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99bout six chaws. You borry\xe2\x80\x99d store tobacker and paid back\r\nnigger-head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStore tobacco is flat black plug, but these fellows mostly chaws the\r\nnatural leaf twisted. When they borrow a chaw they don\xe2\x80\x99t generly cut\r\nit off with a knife, but set the plug in between their teeth, and gnaw\r\nwith their teeth and tug at the plug with their hands till they get it\r\nin two; then sometimes the one that owns the tobacco looks mournful at\r\nit when it\xe2\x80\x99s handed back, and says, sarcastic:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, gimme the _chaw_, and you take the _plug_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: \xe2\x80\x9cGIMME A CHAW\xe2\x80\x9d]\r\n\r\nAll the streets and lanes was just mud; they warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing else _but_\r\nmud\xe2\x80\x94mud as black as tar and nigh about a foot deep in some places,\r\nand two or three inches deep in _all_ the places. The hogs loafed and\r\ngrunted around everywheres. You\xe2\x80\x99d see a muddy sow and a litter of pigs\r\ncome lazying along the street and whollop herself right down in the\r\nway, where folks had to walk around her, and she\xe2\x80\x99d stretch out and\r\nshut her eyes and wave her ears whilst the pigs was milking her, and\r\nlook as happy as if she was on salary. And pretty soon you\xe2\x80\x99d hear a\r\nloafer sing out, \xe2\x80\x9cHi! _so_ boy! sick him, Tige!\xe2\x80\x9d and away the sow would\r\ngo, squealing most horrible, with a dog or two swinging to each ear,\r\nand three or four dozen more a-coming; and then you would see all the\r\nloafers get up and watch the thing out of sight, and laugh at the fun\r\nand look grateful for the noise. Then they\xe2\x80\x99d settle back again till\r\nthere was a dog-fight. There couldn\xe2\x80\x99t anything wake them up all over,\r\nand make them happy all over, like a dog-fight\xe2\x80\x94unless it might be\r\nputting turpentine on a stray dog and setting fire to him, or tying a\r\ntin pan to his tail and see him run himself to death.\r\n\r\nOn the river-front some of the houses was sticking out over the bank,\r\nand they was bowed and bent, and about ready to tumble in. The people\r\nhad moved out of them. The bank was caved away under one corner of some\r\nothers, and that corner was hanging over. People lived in them yet, but\r\nit was dangersome, because sometimes a strip of land as wide as a house\r\ncaves in at a time. Sometimes a belt of land a quarter of a mile deep\r\nwill start in and cave along and cave along till it all caves into the\r\nriver in one summer. Such a town as that has to be always moving back,\r\nand back, and back, because the river\xe2\x80\x99s always gnawing at it.\r\n\r\nThe nearer it got to noon that day the thicker and thicker was the\r\nwagons and horses in the streets, and more coming all the time.\r\nFamilies fetched their dinners with them from the country, and eat them\r\nin the wagons. There was considerable whisky-drinking going on, and I\r\nseen three fights. By and by somebody sings out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere comes old Boggs!\xe2\x80\x94in from the country for his little old monthly\r\ndrunk; here he comes, boys!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll the loafers looked glad; I reckoned they was used to having fun out\r\nof Boggs. One of them says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWonder who he\xe2\x80\x99s a-gwyne to chaw up this time. If he\xe2\x80\x99d a-chawed up all\r\nthe men he\xe2\x80\x99s ben a-gwyne to chaw up in the last twenty year he\xe2\x80\x99d have\r\nconsiderable ruputation now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother one says, \xe2\x80\x9cI wisht old Boggs \xe2\x80\x99d threaten me, \xe2\x80\x99cuz then I\xe2\x80\x99d know\r\nI warn\xe2\x80\x99t gwyne to die for a thousan\xe2\x80\x99 year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoggs comes a-tearing along on his horse, whooping and yelling like an\r\nInjun, and singing out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCler the track, thar. I\xe2\x80\x99m on the waw-path, and the price uv coffins is\r\na-gwyne to raise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was drunk, and weaving about in his saddle; he was over fifty year\r\nold, and had a very red face. Everybody yelled at him and laughed at\r\nhim and sassed him, and he sassed back, and said he\xe2\x80\x99d attend to them\r\nand lay them out in their regular turns, but he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t wait now\r\nbecause he\xe2\x80\x99d come to town to kill old Colonel Sherburn, and his motto\r\nwas, \xe2\x80\x9cMeat first, and spoon vittles to top off on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe see me, and rode up and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhar\xe2\x80\x99d you come f\xe2\x80\x99m, boy? You prepared to die?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he rode on. I was scared, but a man says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe don\xe2\x80\x99t mean nothing; he\xe2\x80\x99s always a-carryin\xe2\x80\x99 on like that when he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndrunk. He\xe2\x80\x99s the best-naturedest old fool in Arkansaw\xe2\x80\x94never hurt nobody,\r\ndrunk nor sober.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoggs rode up before the biggest store in town, and bent his head down\r\nso he could see under the curtain of the awning and yells:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome out here, Sherburn! Come out and meet the man you\xe2\x80\x99ve swindled.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99re the houn\xe2\x80\x99 I\xe2\x80\x99m after, and I\xe2\x80\x99m a-gwyne to have you, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so he went on, calling Sherburn everything he could lay his tongue\r\nto, and the whole street packed with people listening and laughing and\r\ngoing on. By and by a proud-looking man about fifty-five\xe2\x80\x94and he was a\r\nheap the best-dressed man in that town, too\xe2\x80\x94steps out of the store, and\r\nthe crowd drops back on each side to let him come. He says to Boggs,\r\nmighty ca\xe2\x80\x99m and slow\xe2\x80\x94he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m tired of this, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll endure it till one o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Till one\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, mind\xe2\x80\x94no longer. If you open your mouth against me only once\r\nafter that time you can\xe2\x80\x99t travel so far but I will find you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he turns and goes in. The crowd looked mighty sober; nobody\r\nstirred, and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no more laughing. Boggs rode off\r\nblackguarding Sherburn as loud as he could yell, all down the street;\r\nand pretty soon back he comes and stops before the store, still keeping\r\nit up. Some men crowded around him and tried to get him to shut up, but\r\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t; they told him it would be one o\xe2\x80\x99clock in about fifteen\r\nminutes, and so he _must_ go home\xe2\x80\x94he must go right away. But it didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndo no good. He cussed away with all his might, and throwed his hat down\r\nin the mud and rode over it, and pretty soon away he went a-raging down\r\nthe street again, with his gray hair a-flying. Everybody that could\r\nget a chance at him tried their best to coax him off of his horse so\r\nthey could lock him up and get him sober; but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use\xe2\x80\x94up the\r\nstreet he would tear again, and give Sherburn another cussing. By and\r\nby somebody says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo for his daughter!\xe2\x80\x94quick, go for his daughter; sometimes he\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlisten to her. If anybody can persuade him, she can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo somebody started on a run. I walked down street a ways and stopped.\r\nIn about five or ten minutes here comes Boggs again, but not on his\r\nhorse. He was a-reeling across the street towards me, bareheaded, with\r\na friend on both sides of him a-holt of his arms and hurrying him\r\nalong. He was quiet, and looked uneasy; and he warn\xe2\x80\x99t hanging back any,\r\nbut was doing some of the hurrying himself. Somebody sings out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoggs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked over there to see who said it, and it was that Colonel\r\nSherburn. He was standing perfectly still in the street, and had a\r\npistol raised in his right hand\xe2\x80\x94not aiming it, but holding it out with\r\nthe barrel tilted up towards the sky. The same second I see a young\r\ngirl coming on the run, and two men with her. Boggs and the men turned\r\nround to see who called him, and when they see the pistol the men\r\njumped to one side, and the pistol-barrel come down slow and steady\r\nto a level\xe2\x80\x94both barrels cocked. Boggs throws up both of his hands and\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x9cO Lord, don\xe2\x80\x99t shoot!\xe2\x80\x9d Bang! goes the first shot, and he staggers\r\nback, clawing at the air\xe2\x80\x94bang! goes the second one, and he tumbles\r\nbackwards onto the ground, heavy and solid, with his arms spread out.\r\nThat young girl screamed out and comes rushing, and down she throws\r\nherself on her father, crying, and saying, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99s killed him, he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nkilled him!\xe2\x80\x9d The crowd closed up around them, and shouldered and jammed\r\none another, with their necks stretched, trying to see, and people on\r\nthe inside trying to shove them back and shouting, \xe2\x80\x9cBack, back! give\r\nhim air, give him air!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nColonel Sherburn he tossed his pistol onto the ground, and turned\r\naround on his heels and walked off.\r\n\r\nThey took Boggs to a little drug store, the crowd pressing around just\r\nthe same, and the whole town following, and I rushed and got a good\r\nplace at the window, where I was close to him and could see in. They\r\nlaid him on the floor and put one large Bible under his head, and\r\nopened another one and spread it on his breast; but they tore open his\r\nshirt first, and I seen where one of the bullets went in. He made about\r\na dozen long gasps, his breast lifting the Bible up when he drawed\r\nin his breath, and letting it down again when he breathed it out\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nafter that he laid still; he was dead. Then they pulled his daughter\r\naway from him, screaming and crying, and took her off. She was about\r\nsixteen, and very sweet and gentle looking, but awful pale and scared.\r\n\r\nWell, pretty soon the whole town was there, squirming and scrouging and\r\npushing and shoving to get at the window and have a look, but people\r\nthat had the places wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give them up, and folks behind them was\r\nsaying all the time, \xe2\x80\x9cSay, now, you\xe2\x80\x99ve looked enough, you fellows;\r\n\xe2\x80\x99tain\xe2\x80\x99t right and \xe2\x80\x99tain\xe2\x80\x99t fair for you to stay thar all the time, and\r\nnever give nobody a chance; other folks has their rights as well as\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was considerable jawing back, so I slid out, thinking maybe\r\nthere was going to be trouble. The streets was full, and everybody was\r\nexcited. Everybody that seen the shooting was telling how it happened,\r\nand there was a big crowd packed around each one of these fellows,\r\nstretching their necks and listening. One long, lanky man, with long\r\nhair and a big white fur stovepipe hat on the back of his head, and a\r\ncrooked-handled cane, marked out the places on the ground where Boggs\r\nstood and where Sherburn stood, and the people following him around\r\nfrom one place to t\xe2\x80\x99other and watching everything he done, and bobbing\r\ntheir heads to show they understood, and stooping a little and resting\r\ntheir hands on their thighs to watch him mark the places on the ground\r\nwith his cane; and then he stood up straight and stiff where Sherburn\r\nhad stood, frowning and having his hat-brim down over his eyes, and\r\nsung out, \xe2\x80\x9cBoggs!\xe2\x80\x9d and then fetched his cane down slow to a level, and\r\nsays \xe2\x80\x9cBang!\xe2\x80\x9d staggered backwards, says \xe2\x80\x9cBang!\xe2\x80\x9d again, and fell down\r\nflat on his back. The people that had seen the thing said he done it\r\nperfect; said it was just exactly the way it all happened. Then as much\r\nas a dozen people got out their bottles and treated him.\r\n\r\nWell, by and by somebody said Sherburn ought to be lynched. In about a\r\nminute everybody was saying it; so away they went, mad and yelling, and\r\nsnatching down every clothes-line they come to to do the hanging with.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII\r\n\r\n\r\nThey swarmed up towards Sherburn\xe2\x80\x99s house, a-whooping and raging like\r\nInjuns, and everything had to clear the way or get run over and tromped\r\nto mush, and it was awful to see. Children was heeling it ahead of the\r\nmob, screaming and trying to get out of the way; and every window along\r\nthe road was full of women\xe2\x80\x99s heads, and there was nigger boys in every\r\ntree, and bucks and wenches looking over every fence; and as soon as\r\nthe mob would get nearly to them they would break and skaddle back out\r\nof reach. Lots of the women and girls was crying and taking on, scared\r\nmost to death.\r\n\r\nThey swarmed up in front of Sherburn\xe2\x80\x99s palings as thick as they could\r\njam together, and you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear yourself think for the noise. It\r\nwas a little twenty-foot yard. Some sung out \xe2\x80\x9cTear down the fence! tear\r\ndown the fence!\xe2\x80\x9d Then there was a racket of ripping and tearing and\r\nsmashing, and down she goes, and the front wall of the crowd begins to\r\nroll in like a wave.\r\n\r\nJust then Sherburn steps out onto the roof of his little front porch,\r\nwith a double-barrel gun in his hand, and takes his stand, perfectly\r\nca\xe2\x80\x99m and deliberate, not saying a word. The racket stopped, and the\r\nwave sucked back.\r\n\r\nSherburn never said a word\xe2\x80\x94just stood there, looking down. The\r\nstillness was awful creepy and uncomfortable. Sherburn run his eye\r\nslow along the crowd; and wherever it struck the people tried a little\r\nto outgaze him, but they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t; they dropped their eyes and looked\r\nsneaky. Then pretty soon Sherburn sort of laughed; not the pleasant\r\nkind, but the kind that makes you feel like when you are eating bread\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s got sand in it.\r\n\r\nThen he says, slow and scornful:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe idea of _you_ lynching anybody! It\xe2\x80\x99s amusing. The idea of you\r\nthinking you had pluck enough to lynch a _man!_ Because you\xe2\x80\x99re brave\r\nenough to tar and feather poor friendless cast-out women that come\r\nalong here, did that make you think you had grit enough to lay your\r\nhands on a _man?_ Why, a _man\xe2\x80\x99s_ safe in the hands of ten thousand of\r\nyour kind\xe2\x80\x94as long as it\xe2\x80\x99s daytime and you\xe2\x80\x99re not behind him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo I know you? I know you clear through. I was born and raised in the\r\nSouth, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve lived in the North; so I know the average all around.\r\nThe average man\xe2\x80\x99s a coward. In the North he lets anybody walk over him\r\nthat wants to, and goes home and prays for a humble spirit to bear\r\nit. In the South one man, all by himself, has stopped a stage full\r\nof men in the daytime, and robbed the lot. Your newspapers call you\r\na brave people so much that you think you are braver than any other\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x94whereas you\xe2\x80\x99re just _as_ brave, and no braver. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t your\r\njuries hang murderers? Because they\xe2\x80\x99re afraid the man\xe2\x80\x99s friends will\r\nshoot them in the back, in the dark\xe2\x80\x94and it\xe2\x80\x99s just what they _would_ do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo they always acquit; and then a _man_ goes in the night, with a\r\nhundred masked cowards at his back, and lynches the rascal. Your\r\nmistake is, that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t bring a man with you; that\xe2\x80\x99s one mistake,\r\nand the other is that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t come in the dark and fetch your masks.\r\nYou brought _part_ of a man\xe2\x80\x94Buck Harkness, there\xe2\x80\x94and if you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had\r\nhim to start you, you\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 taken it out in blowing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to come. The average man don\xe2\x80\x99t like trouble and\r\ndanger. _You_ don\xe2\x80\x99t like trouble and danger. But if only _half_ a\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94like Buck Harkness, there\xe2\x80\x94shouts \xe2\x80\x99Lynch him! lynch him!\xe2\x80\x99 you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nafraid to back down\xe2\x80\x94afraid you\xe2\x80\x99ll be found out to be what you\r\nare\xe2\x80\x94_cowards_\xe2\x80\x94and so you raise a yell, and hang yourselves onto that\r\nhalf-a-man\xe2\x80\x99s coat-tail, and come raging up here, swearing what big\r\nthings you\xe2\x80\x99re going to do. The pitifulest thing out is a mob; that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhat an army is\xe2\x80\x94a mob; they don\xe2\x80\x99t fight with courage that\xe2\x80\x99s born in\r\nthem, but with courage that\xe2\x80\x99s borrowed from their mass, and from their\r\nofficers. But a mob without any _man_ at the head of it is _beneath_\r\npitifulness. Now the thing for _you_ to do is to droop your tails and\r\ngo home and crawl in a hole. If any real lynching\xe2\x80\x99s going to be done it\r\nwill be done in the dark, Southern fashion; and when they come they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nbring their masks, and fetch a _man_ along. Now _leave_\xe2\x80\x94and take your\r\nhalf-a-man with you\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94tossing his gun up across his left arm and cocking\r\nit when he says this.\r\n\r\nThe crowd washed back sudden, and then broke all apart, and went\r\ntearing off every which way, and Buck Harkness he heeled it after them,\r\nlooking tolerable cheap. I could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 stayed if I wanted to, but I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t want to.\r\n\r\nI went to the circus and loafed around the back side till the watchman\r\nwent by, and then dived in under the tent. I had my twenty-dollar gold\r\npiece and some other money, but I reckoned I better save it, because\r\nthere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no telling how soon you are going to need it, away from\r\nhome and amongst strangers that way. You can\xe2\x80\x99t be too careful. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nopposed to spending money on circuses when there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no other way,\r\nbut there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use in _wasting_ it on them.\r\n\r\nIt was a real bully circus. It was the splendidest sight that ever was\r\nwhen they all come riding in, two and two, and gentleman and lady,\r\nside by side, the men just in their drawers and undershirts, and no\r\nshoes nor stirrups, and resting their hands on their thighs easy and\r\ncomfortable\xe2\x80\x94there must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been twenty of them\xe2\x80\x94and every lady with\r\na lovely complexion, and perfectly beautiful, and looking just like\r\na gang of real sure-enough queens, and dressed in clothes that cost\r\nmillions of dollars, and just littered with diamonds. It was a powerful\r\nfine sight; I never see anything so lovely. And then one by one they\r\ngot up and stood, and went a-weaving around the ring so gentle and\r\nwavy and graceful, the men looking ever so tall and airy and straight,\r\nwith their heads bobbing and skimming along, away up there under the\r\ntent-roof, and every lady\xe2\x80\x99s rose-leafy dress flapping soft and silky\r\naround her hips, and she looking like the most loveliest parasol.\r\n\r\nAnd then faster and faster they went, all of them dancing, first\r\none foot out in the air and then the other, the horses leaning more\r\nand more, and the ringmaster going round and round the center pole,\r\ncracking his whip and shouting \xe2\x80\x9cHi!\xe2\x80\x94hi!\xe2\x80\x9d and the clown cracking jokes\r\nbehind him; and by and by all hands dropped the reins, and every lady\r\nput her knuckles on her hips and every gentleman folded his arms, and\r\nthen how the horses did lean over and hump themselves! And so one after\r\nthe other they all skipped off into the ring, and made the sweetest bow\r\nI ever see, and then scampered out, and everybody clapped their hands\r\nand went just about wild.\r\n\r\nWell, all through the circus they done the most astonishing things;\r\nand all the time that clown carried on so it most killed the people.\r\nThe ringmaster couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever say a word to him but he was back at him\r\nquick as a wink with the funniest things a body ever said; and how\r\nhe ever _could_ think of so many of them, and so sudden and so pat,\r\nwas what I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t no way understand. Why, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 thought\r\nof them in a year. And by and by a drunken man tried to get into the\r\nring\xe2\x80\x94said he wanted to ride; said he could ride as well as anybody\r\nthat ever was. They argued and tried to keep him out, but he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlisten, and the whole show come to a standstill. Then the people begun\r\nto holler at him and make fun of him, and that made him mad, and he\r\nbegun to rip and tear; so that stirred up the people, and a lot of\r\nmen begun to pile down off of the benches and swarm toward the ring,\r\nsaying, \xe2\x80\x9cKnock him down! throw him out!\xe2\x80\x9d and one or two women begun to\r\nscream. So, then, the ringmaster he made a little speech, and said he\r\nhoped there wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be no disturbance, and if the man would promise\r\nhe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make no more trouble he would let him ride if he thought\r\nhe could stay on the horse. So everybody laughed and said all right,\r\nand the man got on. The minute he was on, the horse begun to rip and\r\ntear and jump and cavort around, with two circus men hanging on to his\r\nbridle trying to hold him, and the drunk man hanging on to his neck,\r\nand his heels flying in the air every jump, and the whole crowd of\r\npeople standing up shouting and laughing till tears rolled down. And at\r\nlast, sure enough, all the circus men could do, the horse broke loose,\r\nand away he went like the very nation, round and round the ring, with\r\nthat sot laying down on him and hanging to his neck, with first one\r\nleg hanging most to the ground on one side, and then t\xe2\x80\x99other one on\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99other side, and the people just crazy. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t funny to me, though;\r\nI was all of a tremble to see his danger. But pretty soon he struggled\r\nup astraddle and grabbed the bridle, a-reeling this way and that; and\r\nthe next minute he sprung up and dropped the bridle and stood! and\r\nthe horse a-going like a house afire, too. He just stood up there,\r\na-sailing around as easy and comfortable as if he warn\xe2\x80\x99t ever drunk in\r\nhis life\xe2\x80\x94and then he begun to pull off his clothes and sling them. He\r\nshed them so thick they kind of clogged up the air, and altogether he\r\nshed seventeen suits. And, then, there he was, slim and handsome, and\r\ndressed the gaudiest and prettiest you ever saw, and he lit into that\r\nhorse with his whip and made him fairly hum\xe2\x80\x94and finally skipped off,\r\nand made his bow and danced off to the dressing-room, and everybody\r\njust a-howling with pleasure and astonishment.\r\n\r\nThen the ringmaster he see how he had been fooled, and he _was_ the\r\nsickest ringmaster you ever see, I reckon. Why, it was one of his\r\nown men! He had got up that joke all out of his own head, and never\r\nlet on to nobody. Well, I felt sheepish enough to be took in so, but\r\nI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been in that ringmaster\xe2\x80\x99s place, not for a thousand\r\ndollars. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know; there may be bullier circuses than what that one\r\nwas, but I never struck them yet. Anyways, it was plenty good enough\r\nfor _me_; and wherever I run across it, it can have all of _my_ custom\r\nevery time.\r\n\r\nWell, that night we had _our_ show; but there warn\xe2\x80\x99t only about twelve\r\npeople there\xe2\x80\x94just enough to pay expenses. And they laughed all the\r\ntime, and that made the duke mad; and everybody left, anyway, before\r\nthe show was over, but one boy which was asleep. So the duke said these\r\nArkansaw lunkheads couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come up to Shakespeare; what they wanted\r\nwas low comedy\xe2\x80\x94and maybe something ruther worse than low comedy, he\r\nreckoned. He said he could size their style. So next morning he got\r\nsome big sheets of wrapping-paper and some black paint, and drawed off\r\nsome handbills, and stuck them up all over the village. The bills said:\r\n\r\n\r\n   AT THE COURT HOUSE!\r\n   FOR 3 NIGHTS ONLY!\r\n   _The World-Renowned Tragedians_\r\n   DAVID GARRICK THE YOUNGER!\r\n      AND\r\n   EDMUND KEAN THE ELDER!\r\n   _Of the London and Continental\r\n   Theatres_,\r\n   In their Thrilling Tragedy of\r\n   THE KING\xe2\x80\x99S CAMELEOPARD,\r\n      OR\r\n   THE ROYAL NONESUCH ! ! !\r\n   _Admission 50 cents._\r\n\r\nThen at the bottom was the biggest line of all, which said:\r\n\r\n   LADIES AND CHILDREN NOT ADMITTED\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d says he, \xe2\x80\x9cif that line don\xe2\x80\x99t fetch them, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nArkansaw!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII\r\n\r\n\r\nWell, all day him and the king was hard at it, rigging up a stage and\r\na curtain and a row of candles for footlights; and that night the\r\nhouse was jam full of men in no time. When the place couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hold no\r\nmore, the duke he quit tending door and went around the back way and\r\ncome onto the stage and stood up before the curtain and made a little\r\nspeech, and praised up this tragedy, and said it was the most\r\nthrillingest one that ever was; and so he went on a-bragging about the\r\ntragedy, and about Edmund Kean the Elder, which was to play the main\r\nprincipal part in it; and at last when he\xe2\x80\x99d got everybody\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexpectations up high enough, he rolled up the curtain, and the next\r\nminute the king come a-prancing out on all fours, naked; and he was\r\npainted all over, ring-streaked-and-striped, all sorts of colors, as\r\nsplendid as a rainbow. And\xe2\x80\x94but never mind the rest of his outfit; it\r\nwas just wild, but it was awful funny. The people most killed\r\nthemselves laughing; and when the king got done capering and capered\r\noff behind the scenes, they roared and clapped and stormed and\r\nhaw-hawed till he come back and done it over again, and after that\r\nthey made him do it another time. Well, it would make a cow laugh to\r\nsee the shines that old idiot cut.\r\n\r\nThen the duke he lets the curtain down, and bows to the people, and\r\nsays the great tragedy will be performed only two nights more, on\r\naccounts of pressing London engagements, where the seats is all sold\r\nalready for it in Drury Lane; and then he makes them another bow, and\r\nsays if he has succeeded in pleasing them and instructing them, he\r\nwill be deeply obleeged if they will mention it to their friends and\r\nget them to come and see it.\r\n\r\nTwenty people sings out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, is it over? Is that _all_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duke says yes. Then there was a fine time. Everybody sings out,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSold!\xe2\x80\x9d and rose up mad, and was a-going for that stage and them\r\ntragedians. But a big, fine-looking man jumps up on a bench and\r\nshouts:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold on! Just a word, gentlemen.\xe2\x80\x9d They stopped to listen. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are\r\nsold\xe2\x80\x94mighty badly sold. But we don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be the laughing-stock of\r\nthis whole town, I reckon, and never hear the last of this thing as\r\nlong as we live. _No_. What we want is to go out of here quiet, and\r\ntalk this show up, and sell the _rest_ of the town! Then we\xe2\x80\x99ll all be\r\nin the same boat. Ain\xe2\x80\x99t that sensible?\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet it is!\xe2\x80\x94the jedge is\r\nright!\xe2\x80\x9d everybody sings out.) \xe2\x80\x9cAll right, then\xe2\x80\x94not a word about any\r\nsell. Go along home, and advise everybody to come and see the\r\ntragedy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNext day you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear nothing around that town but how splendid\r\nthat show was. House was jammed again that night, and we sold this\r\ncrowd the same way. When me and the king and the duke got home to the\r\nraft we all had a supper; and by and by, about midnight, they made Jim\r\nand me back her out and float her down the middle of the river, and\r\nfetch her in and hide her about two mile below town.\r\n\r\nThe third night the house was crammed again\xe2\x80\x94and they warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnew-comers this time, but people that was at the show the other two\r\nnights. I stood by the duke at the door, and I see that every man that\r\nwent in had his pockets bulging, or something muffled up under his\r\ncoat\xe2\x80\x94and I see it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no perfumery, neither, not by a long sight.\r\nI smelt sickly eggs by the barrel, and rotten cabbages, and such\r\nthings; and if I know the signs of a dead cat being around, and I bet\r\nI do, there was sixty-four of them went in. I shoved in there for a\r\nminute, but it was too various for me; I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stand it. Well, when\r\nthe place couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hold no more people the duke he give a fellow a\r\nquarter and told him to tend door for him a minute, and then he\r\nstarted around for the stage door, I after him; but the minute we\r\nturned the corner and was in the dark he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWalk fast now till you get away from the houses, and then shin for\r\nthe raft like the dickens was after you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI done it, and he done the same. We struck the raft at the same time,\r\nand in less than two seconds we was gliding down-stream, all dark and\r\nstill, and edging towards the middle of the river, nobody saying a\r\nword. I reckoned the poor king was in for a gaudy time of it with the\r\naudience, but nothing of the sort; pretty soon he crawls out from\r\nunder the wigwam, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, how\xe2\x80\x99d the old thing pan out this time, duke?\xe2\x80\x9d He hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nup-town at all.\r\n\r\nWe never showed a light till we was about ten mile below the village.\r\nThen we lit up and had a supper, and the king and the duke fairly\r\nlaughed their bones loose over the way they\xe2\x80\x99d served them people. The\r\nduke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreenhorns, flatheads! _I_ knew the first house would keep mum and let\r\nthe rest of the town get roped in; and I knew they\xe2\x80\x99d lay for us the\r\nthird night, and consider it was _their_ turn now. Well, it _is_ their\r\nturn, and I\xe2\x80\x99d give something to know how much they\xe2\x80\x99d take for it. I\r\n_would_ just like to know how they\xe2\x80\x99re putting in their opportunity.\r\nThey can turn it into a picnic if they want to\xe2\x80\x94they brought plenty\r\nprovisions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThem rapscallions took in four hundred and sixty-five dollars in that\r\nthree nights. I never see money hauled in by the wagon-load like that\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nBy and by, when they was asleep and snoring, Jim says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t it s\xe2\x80\x99prise you de way dem kings carries on, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cit don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t it, Huck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it don\xe2\x80\x99t, because it\xe2\x80\x99s in the breed. I reckon they\xe2\x80\x99re all\r\nalike.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Huck, dese kings o\xe2\x80\x99 ourn is reglar rapscallions; dat\xe2\x80\x99s jist what\r\ndey is; dey\xe2\x80\x99s reglar rapscallions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I\xe2\x80\x99m a-saying; all kings is mostly rapscallions, as\r\nfur as I can make out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs dat so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou read about them once\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll see. Look at Henry the Eight; this\r\n\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99s a Sunday-school Superintendent to _him_. And look at Charles\r\nSecond, and Louis Fourteen, and Louis Fifteen, and James Second, and\r\nEdward Second, and Richard Third, and forty more; besides all them\r\nSaxon heptarchies that used to rip around so in old times and raise\r\nCain. My, you ought to seen old Henry the Eight when he was in bloom.\r\nHe _was_ a blossom. He used to marry a new wife every day, and chop\r\noff her head next morning. And he would do it just as indifferent as\r\nif he was ordering up eggs. \xe2\x80\x99Fetch up Nell Gwynn,\xe2\x80\x99 he says. They fetch\r\nher up. Next morning, \xe2\x80\x99Chop off her head!\xe2\x80\x99 And they chop it off.\r\n\xe2\x80\x99Fetch up Jane Shore,\xe2\x80\x99 he says; and up she comes. Next morning, \xe2\x80\x99Chop\r\noff her head\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94and they chop it off. \xe2\x80\x99Ring up Fair Rosamun.\xe2\x80\x99 Fair\r\nRosamun answers the bell. Next morning, \xe2\x80\x99Chop off her head.\xe2\x80\x99 And he\r\nmade every one of them tell him a tale every night; and he kept that\r\nup till he had hogged a thousand and one tales that way, and then he\r\nput them all in a book, and called it Domesday Book\xe2\x80\x94which was a good\r\nname and stated the case. You don\xe2\x80\x99t know kings, Jim, but I know them;\r\nand this old rip of ourn is one of the cleanest I\xe2\x80\x99ve struck in\r\nhistory. Well, Henry he takes a notion he wants to get up some trouble\r\nwith this country. How does he go at it\xe2\x80\x94give notice?\xe2\x80\x94give the\r\ncountry a show? No. All of a sudden he heaves all the tea in Boston\r\nHarbor overboard, and whacks out a declaration of independence, and\r\ndares them to come on. That was _his_ style\xe2\x80\x94he never give anybody a\r\nchance. He had suspicions of his father, the Duke of Wellington. Well,\r\nwhat did he do? Ask him to show up? No\xe2\x80\x94drownded him in a butt of\r\nmamsey, like a cat. S\xe2\x80\x99pose people left money laying around where he\r\nwas\xe2\x80\x94what did he do? He collared it. S\xe2\x80\x99pose he contracted to do a\r\nthing, and you paid him, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t set down there and see that he\r\ndone it\xe2\x80\x94what did he do? He always done the other thing. S\xe2\x80\x99pose he\r\nopened his mouth\xe2\x80\x94what then? If he didn\xe2\x80\x99t shut it up powerful quick\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d lose a lie every time. That\xe2\x80\x99s the kind of a bug Henry was; and if\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 had him along \xe2\x80\x99stead of our kings he\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 fooled that town a\r\nheap worse than ourn done. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say that ourn is lambs, because\r\nthey ain\xe2\x80\x99t, when you come right down to the cold facts; but they ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnothing to _that_ old ram, anyway. All I say is, kings is kings, and\r\nyou got to make allowances. Take them all around, they\xe2\x80\x99re a mighty\r\nornery lot. It\xe2\x80\x99s the way they\xe2\x80\x99re raised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut dis one do _smell_ so like de nation, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they all do, Jim. _We_ can\xe2\x80\x99t help the way a king smells; history\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t tell no way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow de duke, he\xe2\x80\x99s a tolerble likely man in some ways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a duke\xe2\x80\x99s different. But not very different. This one\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nmiddling hard lot for a duke. When he\xe2\x80\x99s drunk there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nnear-sighted man could tell him from a king.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anyways, I doan\xe2\x80\x99 hanker for no mo\xe2\x80\x99 un um, Huck. Dese is all I\r\nkin stan\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the way I feel, too, Jim. But we\xe2\x80\x99ve got them on our hands, and\r\nwe got to remember what they are, and make allowances. Sometimes I\r\nwish we could hear of a country that\xe2\x80\x99s out of kings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat was the use to tell Jim these warn\xe2\x80\x99t real kings and dukes? It\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done no good; and, besides, it was just as I said: you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell them from the real kind.\r\n\r\nI went to sleep, and Jim didn\xe2\x80\x99t call me when it was my turn. He often\r\ndone that. When I waked up just at daybreak he was sitting there with\r\nhis head down betwixt his knees, moaning and mourning to himself. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t take notice nor let on. I knowed what it was about. He was\r\nthinking about his wife and his children, away up yonder, and he was\r\nlow and homesick; because he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever been away from home before in\r\nhis life; and I do believe he cared just as much for his people as\r\nwhite folks does for their\xe2\x80\x99n. It don\xe2\x80\x99t seem natural, but I reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nso. He was often moaning and mourning that way nights, when he judged\r\nI was asleep, and saying, \xe2\x80\x9cPo\xe2\x80\x99 little \xe2\x80\x99Lizabeth! po\xe2\x80\x99 little Johnny!\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s mighty hard; I spec\xe2\x80\x99 I ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever gwyne to see you no mo\xe2\x80\x99, no\r\nmo\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d He was a mighty good nigger, Jim was.\r\n\r\nBut this time I somehow got to talking to him about his wife and young\r\nones; and by and by he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes me feel so bad dis time \xe2\x80\x99uz bekase I hear sumpn over\r\nyonder on de bank like a whack, er a slam, while ago, en it mine me er\r\nde time I treat my little \xe2\x80\x99Lizabeth so ornery. She warn\xe2\x80\x99t on\xe2\x80\x99y \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\nfo\xe2\x80\x99 year ole, en she tuck de sk\xe2\x80\x99yarlet fever, en had a powful rough\r\nspell; but she got well, en one day she was a-stannin\xe2\x80\x99 aroun\xe2\x80\x99, en I\r\nsays to her, I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Shet de do\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe never done it; jis\xe2\x80\x99 stood dah, kiner smilin\xe2\x80\x99 up at me. It make me\r\nmad; en I says ag\xe2\x80\x99in, mighty loud, I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Doan\xe2\x80\x99 you hear me? Shet de do\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe jis stood de same way, kiner smilin\xe2\x80\x99 up. I was a-bilin\xe2\x80\x99! I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I lay I _make_ you mine!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEn wid dat I fetch\xe2\x80\x99 her a slap side de head dat sont her a-sprawlin\xe2\x80\x99.\r\nDen I went into de yuther room, en \xe2\x80\x99uz gone \xe2\x80\x99bout ten minutes; en when\r\nI come back dah was dat do\xe2\x80\x99 a-stannin\xe2\x80\x99 open _yit_, en dat chile\r\nstannin\xe2\x80\x99 mos\xe2\x80\x99 right in it, a-lookin\xe2\x80\x99 down and mournin\xe2\x80\x99, en de tears\r\nrunnin\xe2\x80\x99 down. My, but I _wuz_ mad! I was a-gwyne for de chile, but\r\njis\xe2\x80\x99 den\xe2\x80\x94it was a do\xe2\x80\x99 dat open innerds\xe2\x80\x94jis\xe2\x80\x99 den, \xe2\x80\x99long come de wind\r\nen slam it to, behine de chile, ker-_blam!_\xe2\x80\x94en my lan\xe2\x80\x99, de chile\r\nnever move\xe2\x80\x99! My breff mos\xe2\x80\x99 hop outer me; en I feel so\xe2\x80\x94so\xe2\x80\x94I doan\xe2\x80\x99\r\nknow _how_ I feel. I crope out, all a-tremblin\xe2\x80\x99, en crope aroun\xe2\x80\x99 en\r\nopen de do\xe2\x80\x99 easy en slow, en poke my head in behine de chile, sof\xe2\x80\x99 en\r\nstill, en all uv a sudden I says _pow!_ jis\xe2\x80\x99 as loud as I could yell.\r\n_She never budge!_ Oh, Huck, I bust out a-cryin\xe2\x80\x99 en grab her up in my\r\narms, en say, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, de po\xe2\x80\x99 little thing! De Lord God Amighty fogive po\xe2\x80\x99\r\nole Jim, kaze he never gwyne to fogive hisself as long\xe2\x80\x99s he live!\xe2\x80\x99 Oh,\r\nshe was plumb deef en dumb, Huck, plumb deef en dumb\xe2\x80\x94en I\xe2\x80\x99d ben\r\na-treat\xe2\x80\x99n her so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nNext day, towards night, we laid up under a little willow towhead out\r\nin the middle, where there was a village on each side of the river,\r\nand the duke and the king begun to lay out a plan for working them\r\ntowns. Jim he spoke to the duke, and said he hoped it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t take\r\nbut a few hours, because it got mighty heavy and tiresome to him when\r\nhe had to lay all day in the wigwam tied with the rope. You see, when\r\nwe left him all alone we had to tie him, because if anybody happened\r\non to him all by himself and not tied it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look much like he\r\nwas a runaway nigger, you know. So the duke said it _was_ kind of hard\r\nto have to lay roped all day, and he\xe2\x80\x99d cipher out some way to get\r\naround it.\r\n\r\nHe was uncommon bright, the duke was, and he soon struck it. He\r\ndressed Jim up in King Lear\xe2\x80\x99s outfit\xe2\x80\x94it was a long curtain-calico\r\ngown, and a white horse-hair wig and whiskers; and then he took his\r\ntheater paint and painted Jim\xe2\x80\x99s face and hands and ears and neck all\r\nover a dead, dull solid blue, like a man that\xe2\x80\x99s been drownded nine\r\ndays. Blamed if he warn\xe2\x80\x99t the horriblest-looking outrage I ever see.\r\nThen the duke took and wrote out a sign on a shingle so:\r\n\r\n_Sick Arab\xe2\x80\x94but harmless when not out of his head._\r\n\r\nAnd he nailed that shingle to a lath, and stood the lath up four or\r\nfive foot in front of the wigwam. Jim was satisfied. He said it was a\r\nsight better than lying tied a couple of years every day, and\r\ntrembling all over every time there was a sound. The duke told him to\r\nmake himself free and easy, and if anybody ever come meddling around,\r\nhe must hop out of the wigwam, and carry on a little, and fetch a howl\r\nor two like a wild beast, and he reckoned they would light out and\r\nleave him alone. Which was sound enough judgment; but you take the\r\naverage man, and he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wait for him to howl. Why, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t only\r\nlook like he was dead, he looked considerable more than that.\r\n\r\nThese rapscallions wanted to try the Nonesuch again, because there was\r\nso much money in it, but they judged it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be safe, because\r\nmaybe the news might \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 worked along down by this time. They couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhit no project that suited exactly; so at last the duke said he\r\nreckoned he\xe2\x80\x99d lay off and work his brains an hour or two and see if he\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t put up something on the Arkansaw village; and the king he\r\nallowed he would drop over to t\xe2\x80\x99other village without any plan, but\r\njust trust in Providence to lead him the profitable way\xe2\x80\x94meaning the\r\ndevil, I reckon. We had all bought store clothes where we stopped\r\nlast; and now the king put his\xe2\x80\x99n on, and he told me to put mine on. I\r\ndone it, of course. The king\xe2\x80\x99s duds was all black, and he did look\r\nreal swell and starchy. I never knowed how clothes could change a body\r\nbefore. Why, before, he looked like the orneriest old rip that ever\r\nwas; but now, when he\xe2\x80\x99d take off his new white beaver and make a bow\r\nand do a smile, he looked that grand and good and pious that you\xe2\x80\x99d say\r\nhe had walked right out of the ark, and maybe was old Leviticus\r\nhimself. Jim cleaned up the canoe, and I got my paddle ready. There\r\nwas a big steamboat laying at the shore away up under the point, about\r\nthree mile above the town\xe2\x80\x94been there a couple of hours, taking on\r\nfreight. Says the king:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeein\xe2\x80\x99 how I\xe2\x80\x99m dressed, I reckon maybe I better arrive down from St.\r\nLouis or Cincinnati, or some other big place. Go for the steamboat,\r\nHuckleberry; we\xe2\x80\x99ll come down to the village on her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t have to be ordered twice to go and take a steamboat ride. I\r\nfetched the shore a half a mile above the village, and then went\r\nscooting along the bluff bank in the easy water. Pretty soon we come\r\nto a nice innocent-looking young country jake setting on a log\r\nswabbing the sweat off of his face, for it was powerful warm weather;\r\nand he had a couple of big carpet-bags by him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRun her nose inshore,\xe2\x80\x9d says the king. I done it. \xe2\x80\x9cWher\xe2\x80\x99 you bound\r\nfor, young man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the steamboat; going to Orleans.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGit aboard,\xe2\x80\x9d says the king. \xe2\x80\x9cHold on a minute, my servant \xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99p you with them bags. Jump out and he\xe2\x80\x99p the gentleman,\r\nAdolphus\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94meaning me, I see.\r\n\r\nI done so, and then we all three started on again. The young chap was\r\nmighty thankful; said it was tough work toting his baggage such\r\nweather. He asked the king where he was going, and the king told him\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d come down the river and landed at the other village this morning,\r\nand now he was going up a few mile to see an old friend on a farm up\r\nthere. Well, if ever I\r\nstruck anything like it, I\xe2\x80\x99m a nigger. It was enough to make a body\r\nashamed of the human race.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV\r\n\r\n\r\nThe news was all over town in two minutes, and you could see the\r\npeople tearing down on the run from every which way, some of them\r\nputting on their coats as they come. Pretty soon we was in the middle\r\nof a crowd, and the noise of the tramping was like a soldier march.\r\nThe windows and dooryards was full; and every minute somebody would\r\nsay, over a fence:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it _them?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd somebody trotting along with the gang would answer back and say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen we got to the house the street in front of it was packed, and the\r\nthree girls was standing in the door. Well, when it come to that it worked the crowd like you\r\nnever see anything like it, and everybody broke down and went to\r\nsobbing right out loud\xe2\x80\x94the poor girls, too; and every woman, nearly,\r\nwent up to the girls, without saying a word, and kissed them, solemn,\r\non the forehead, and then put their hand on their head, and looked up\r\ntowards the sky, with the tears running down, and then busted out and\r\nwent off sobbing and swabbing, and give the next woman a show. I never\r\nsee anything so disgusting.\r\n\r\nWell, by and by the king he gets up and comes forward a little, and\r\nworks himself up and slobbers out a speech, all full of tears and\r\nflapdoodle, about its being a sore trial for him and his poor brother\r\nto lose the diseased, and to miss seeing diseased alive after the long\r\njourney of four thousand mile, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a trial that\xe2\x80\x99s sweetened and\r\nsanctified to us by this dear sympathy and these holy tears, and so he\r\nthanks them out of his heart and out of his brother\xe2\x80\x99s heart, because\r\nout of their mouths they can\xe2\x80\x99t, words being too weak and cold, and all\r\nthat kind of rot and slush, till it was just sickening; and then he\r\nblubbers out a pious goody-goody Amen, and turns himself loose and goes\r\nto crying fit to bust.\r\n\r\nAnd the minute the words were out of his mouth somebody over in the\r\ncrowd struck up the doxolojer, and everybody joined in with all their\r\nmight, and it just warmed you up and made you feel as good as church\r\nletting out. Music is a good thing; and after all that soul-butter and\r\nhogwash I never see it freshen up things so, and sound so honest and\r\nbully.\r\n\r\nThen the king begins to work his jaw again, and says how him and his\r\nnieces would be glad if a few of the main principal friends of the\r\nfamily would take supper here with them this evening, and help set up\r\nwith the ashes of the diseased; and says if his poor brother laying\r\nyonder could speak he knows who he would name, for they was names that\r\nwas very dear to him, and mentioned often in his letters; and so he\r\nwill name the same, to wit, as follows, viz.:\xe2\x80\x94Rev. It\xe2\x80\x99s a word that\xe2\x80\x99s made up out\xe2\x80\x99n\r\nthe Greek _orgo_, outside, open, abroad; and the Hebrew _jeesum_, to\r\nplant, cover up; hence in_ter_. I hain\xe2\x80\x99t done nothing to him. He\xe2\x80\x99s told some\r\nstretchers, I reckon, and I said I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t swallow it all; and that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nevery bit and grain I _did_ say. I reckon he can stand a little thing\r\nlike that, can\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care whether \xe2\x80\x99twas little or whether \xe2\x80\x99twas big; he\xe2\x80\x99s here in\r\nour house and a stranger, and it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t good of you to say it. If you\r\nwas in his place it would make you feel ashamed; and so you oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nto say a thing to another person that will make _them_ feel ashamed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Maim, he said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt don\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference what he _said_\xe2\x80\x94that ain\xe2\x80\x99t the thing. The\r\nthing is for you to treat him _kind_, and not be saying things to make\r\nhim remember he ain\xe2\x80\x99t in his own country and amongst his own folks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says to myself, _this_ is a girl that I\xe2\x80\x99m letting that old reptile\r\nrob her of her money!\r\n\r\nThen Susan _she_ waltzed in; and if you\xe2\x80\x99ll believe me, she did give\r\nHare-lip hark from the tomb!\r\n\r\nSays I to myself, and this is _another_ one that I\xe2\x80\x99m letting him rob\r\nher of her money!\r\n\r\nThen Mary Jane she took another inning, and went in sweet and lovely\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x94which was her way; but when she got done there warn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly\r\nanything left o\xe2\x80\x99 poor Hare-lip. So she hollered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, then,\xe2\x80\x9d says the other girls; \xe2\x80\x9cyou just ask his pardon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe done it, too; and she done it beautiful. She done it so beautiful\r\nit was good to hear; and I wished I could tell her a thousand lies, so\r\nshe could do it again.\r\n\r\nI says to myself, this is _another_ one that I\xe2\x80\x99m letting him rob her\r\nof her money. And when she got through they all jest laid theirselves\r\nout to make me feel at home and know I was amongst friends. I felt so\r\nornery and low down and mean that I says to myself, my mind\xe2\x80\x99s made up;\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll hive that money for them or bust.\r\n\r\nSo then I lit out\xe2\x80\x94for bed, I said, meaning some time or another. When\r\nI got by myself I went to thinking the thing over. I says to myself,\r\nshall I go to that doctor, private, and blow on these frauds? No\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t do. He might tell who told him; then the king and the duke would\r\nmake it warm for me. Shall I go, private, and tell Mary Jane? No\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ndasn\xe2\x80\x99t do it. Her face would give them a hint, sure; they\xe2\x80\x99ve got the\r\nmoney, and they\xe2\x80\x99d slide right out and get away with it. If she was to\r\nfetch in help I\xe2\x80\x99d get mixed up in the business before it was done\r\nwith, I judge. No; there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no good way but one. I got to steal\r\nthat money, somehow; and I got to steal it some way that they won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsuspicion that I done it. They\xe2\x80\x99ve got a good thing here, and they\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to leave till they\xe2\x80\x99ve played this family and this town\r\nfor all they\xe2\x80\x99re worth, so I\xe2\x80\x99ll find a chance time enough. I\xe2\x80\x99ll steal\r\nit and hide it; and by and by, when I\xe2\x80\x99m away down the river, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwrite a letter and tell Mary Jane where it\xe2\x80\x99s hid. But I better hive it\r\nto-night if I can, because the doctor maybe hasn\xe2\x80\x99t let up as much as\r\nhe lets on he has; he might scare them out of here yet.\r\n\r\nSo, thinks I, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and search them rooms. Upstairs the hall was\r\ndark, but I found the duke\xe2\x80\x99s room, and started to paw around it with\r\nmy hands; but I recollected it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be much like the king to let\r\nanybody else take care of that money but his own self; so then I went\r\nto his room and begun to paw around there. But I see I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nnothing without a candle, and I dasn\xe2\x80\x99t light one, of course. So I\r\njudged I\xe2\x80\x99d got to do the other thing\xe2\x80\x94lay for them and eavesdrop.\r\nAbout that time I hears their footsteps coming, and was going to skip\r\nunder the bed; I reached for it, but it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t where I thought it\r\nwould be; but I touched the curtain that hid Mary Jane\xe2\x80\x99s frocks, so I\r\njumped in behind that and snuggled in amongst the gowns, and stood\r\nthere perfectly still.\r\n\r\nThey come in and shut the door; and the first thing the duke done was\r\nto get down and look under the bed. Then I was glad I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t found the\r\nbed when I wanted it. And yet, you know, it\xe2\x80\x99s kind of natural to hide\r\nunder the bed when you are up to anything private. They sets down then,\r\nand the king says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what is it? And cut it middlin\xe2\x80\x99 short, because it\xe2\x80\x99s better for\r\nus to be down there a-whoopin\xe2\x80\x99 up the mournin\xe2\x80\x99 than up here givin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em\r\na chance to talk us over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, this is it, Capet. I ain\xe2\x80\x99t easy; I ain\xe2\x80\x99t comfortable. That\r\ndoctor lays on my mind. I wanted to know your plans. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a\r\nnotion, and I think it\xe2\x80\x99s a sound one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, duke?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat we better glide out of this before three in the morning, and\r\nclip it down the river with what we\xe2\x80\x99ve got. Specially, seeing we got\r\nit so easy\xe2\x80\x94_given_ back to us, flung at our heads, as you may say,\r\nwhen of course we allowed to have to steal it back. I\xe2\x80\x99m for knocking\r\noff and lighting out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat made me feel pretty bad. About an hour or two ago it would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbeen a little different, but now it made me feel bad and disappointed.\r\nThe king rips out and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! And not sell out the rest o\xe2\x80\x99 the property? March off like a\r\npassel of fools and leave eight or nine thous\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 dollars\xe2\x80\x99 worth o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nproperty layin\xe2\x80\x99 around jest sufferin\xe2\x80\x99 to be scooped in?\xe2\x80\x94and all good,\r\nsalable stuff, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duke he grumbled; said the bag of gold was enough, and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to go no deeper\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to rob a lot of orphans of\r\n_everything_ they had.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, how you talk!\xe2\x80\x9d says the king. \xe2\x80\x9cWe sha\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99t rob \xe2\x80\x99em of nothing at\r\nall but jest this money. The people that _buys_ the property is the\r\nsuff\xe2\x80\x99rers; because as soon \xe2\x80\x99s it\xe2\x80\x99s found out \xe2\x80\x99at we didn\xe2\x80\x99t own\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94which won\xe2\x80\x99t be long after we\xe2\x80\x99ve slid\xe2\x80\x94the sale won\xe2\x80\x99t be valid, and\r\nit \xe2\x80\x99ll all go back to the estate.
