Why,\r\njest think\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s thous\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99s and thous\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99s that ain\xe2\x80\x99t nigh so well\r\noff. Bless you, _they_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t got noth\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 to complain of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, the king he talked him blind; so at last he give in, and said\r\nall right, but said he believed it was blamed foolishness to stay, and\r\nthat doctor hanging over them. But the king says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCuss the doctor! What do we k\xe2\x80\x99yer for _him?_ Hain\xe2\x80\x99t we got all the\r\nfools in town on our side? And ain\xe2\x80\x99t that a big enough majority in any\r\ntown?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo they got ready to go down-stairs again. The duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think we put that money in a good place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat cheered me up. I\xe2\x80\x99d begun to think I warn\xe2\x80\x99t going to get a hint of\r\nno kind to help me. The king says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause Mary Jane \xe2\x80\x99ll be in mourning from this out; and first you\r\nknow the nigger that does up the rooms will get an order to box these\r\nduds up and put \xe2\x80\x99em away; and do you reckon a nigger can run across\r\nmoney and not borrow some of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour head\xe2\x80\x99s level ag\xe2\x80\x99in, duke,\xe2\x80\x9d says the king; and he comes\r\na-fumbling under the curtain two or three foot from where I was. I\r\nstuck tight to the wall and kept mighty still, though quivery; and I\r\nwondered what them fellows would say to me if they catched me; and I\r\ntried to think what I\xe2\x80\x99d better do if they did catch me. But the king\r\nhe got the bag before I could think more than about a half a thought,\r\nand he never suspicioned I was around. They took and shoved the bag\r\nthrough a rip in the straw tick that was under the feather-bed, and\r\ncrammed it in a foot or two amongst the straw and said it was all\r\nright now, because a nigger only makes up the feather-bed, and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nturn over the straw tick only about twice a year, and so it warn\xe2\x80\x99t in\r\nno danger of getting stole now.\r\n\r\nBut I knowed better. I had it out of there before they was half-way\r\ndown-stairs. I groped along up to my cubby, and hid it there till I\r\ncould get a chance to do better. I judged I better hide it outside of\r\nthe house somewheres, because if they missed it they would give the\r\nhouse a good ransacking: I knowed that very well. Then I turned in,\r\nwith my clothes all on; but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 gone to sleep if I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99\r\nwanted to, I was in such a sweat to get through with the business. By\r\nand by I heard the king and the duke come up; so I rolled off my\r\npallet and laid with my chin at the top of my ladder, and waited to\r\nsee if anything was going to happen. But nothing did.\r\n\r\nSo I held on till all the late sounds had quit and the early ones\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t begun yet; and then I slipped down the ladder.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII\r\n\r\n\r\nI crept to their doors and listened; they was snoring. So I tiptoed\r\nalong, and got down-stairs all right. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t a sound anywheres. I\r\npeeped through a crack of the dining-room door, and see the men that\r\nwas watching the corpse all sound asleep on their chairs. The door was\r\nopen into the parlor, where the corpse was laying, and there was a\r\ncandle in both rooms. I passed along, and the parlor door was open;\r\nbut I see there warn\xe2\x80\x99t nobody in there but the remainders of Peter; so\r\nI shoved on by; but the front door was locked, and the key wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthere. Just then I heard somebody coming down the stairs, back behind\r\nme. I run in the parlor and took a swift look around, and the only\r\nplace I see to hide the bag was in the coffin. The lid was shoved\r\nalong about a foot, showing the dead man\xe2\x80\x99s face down in there, with a\r\nwet cloth over it, and his shroud on. I tucked the money-bag in under\r\nthe lid, just down beyond where his hands was crossed, which made me\r\ncreep, they was so cold, and then I run back across the room and in\r\nbehind the door.\r\n\r\nThe person coming was Mary Jane. She went to the coffin, very soft,\r\nand kneeled down and looked in; then she put up her handkerchief, and\r\nI see she begun to cry, though I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear her, and her back was\r\nto me. I slid out, and as I passed the dining-room I thought I\xe2\x80\x99d make\r\nsure them watchers hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen me; so I looked through the crack, and\r\neverything was all right. They hadn\xe2\x80\x99t stirred.\r\n\r\nI slipped up to bed, feeling ruther blue, on accounts of the thing\r\nplaying out that way after I had took so much trouble and run so much\r\nresk about it. Says I, if it could stay where it is, all right;\r\nbecause when we get down the river a hundred mile or two I could write\r\nback to Mary Jane, and she could dig him up again and get it; but that\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t the thing that\xe2\x80\x99s going to happen; the thing that\xe2\x80\x99s going to\r\nhappen is, the money\xe2\x80\x99ll be found when they come to screw on the lid.\r\nThen the king \xe2\x80\x99ll get it again, and it \xe2\x80\x99ll be a long day before he\r\ngives anybody another chance to smouch it from him. Of course I\r\n_wanted_ to slide down and get it out of there, but I dasn\xe2\x80\x99t try it.\r\nEvery minute it was getting earlier now, and pretty soon some of them\r\nwatchers would begin to stir, and I might get catched\xe2\x80\x94catched with\r\nsix thousand dollars in my hands that nobody hadn\xe2\x80\x99t hired me to take\r\ncare of. I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to be mixed up in no such business as that, I\r\nsays to myself.\r\n\r\nWhen I got down-stairs in the morning the parlor was shut up, and the\r\nwatchers was gone. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t nobody around but the family and the\r\nwidow Bartley and our tribe. I watched their faces to see if anything\r\nhad been happening, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell.\r\n\r\nTowards the middle of the day the undertaker come with his man, and\r\nthey set the coffin in the middle of the room on a couple of chairs,\r\nand then set all our chairs in rows, and borrowed more from the\r\nneighbors till the hall and the parlor and the dining-room was full. I\r\nsee the coffin lid was the way it was before, but I dasn\xe2\x80\x99t go to look\r\nin under it, with folks around.\r\n\r\nThen the people begun to flock in, and the beats and the girls took\r\nseats in the front row at the head of the coffin, and for a half an\r\nhour the people filed around slow, in single rank, and looked down at\r\nthe dead man\xe2\x80\x99s face a minute, and some dropped in a tear, and it was\r\nall very still and solemn, only the girls and the beats holding\r\nhandkerchiefs to their eyes and keeping their heads bent, and sobbing\r\na little. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t no other sound but the scraping of the feet on\r\nthe floor and blowing noses\xe2\x80\x94because people always blows them more at\r\na funeral than they do at other places except church.\r\n\r\nWhen the place was packed full the undertaker he slid around in his\r\nblack gloves with his softy soothering ways, putting on the last\r\ntouches, and getting people and things all ship-shape and comfortable,\r\nand making no more sound than a cat. He never spoke; he moved people\r\naround, he squeezed in late ones, he opened up passageways, and done\r\nit with nods, and signs with his hands. Then he took his place over\r\nagainst the wall. He was the softest, glidingest, stealthiest man I\r\never see; and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no more smile to him than there is to a\r\nham.\r\n\r\nThey had borrowed a melodeum\xe2\x80\x94a sick one; and when everything was\r\nready a young woman set down and worked it, and it was pretty skreeky\r\nand colicky, and everybody joined in and sung, and Peter was the only\r\none that had a good thing, according to my notion. Then the Reverend\r\nHobson opened up, slow and solemn, and begun to talk; and straight off\r\nthe most outrageous row busted out in the cellar a body ever heard; it\r\nwas only one dog, but he made a most powerful racket, and he kept it\r\nup right along; the parson he had to stand there, over the coffin, and\r\nwait\xe2\x80\x94you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear yourself think. It was right down awkward, and\r\nnobody didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to know what to do. But pretty soon they see that\r\nlong-legged undertaker make a sign to the preacher as much as to say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you worry\xe2\x80\x94just depend on me.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he stooped down and begun\r\nto glide along the wall, just his shoulders showing over the people\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheads. So he glided along, and the powwow and racket getting more and\r\nmore outrageous all the time; and at last, when he had gone around two\r\nsides of the room, he disappears down cellar. Then in about two\r\nseconds we heard a whack, and the dog he finished up with a most\r\namazing howl or two, and then everything was dead still, and the\r\nparson begun his solemn talk where he left off. In a minute or two\r\nhere comes this undertaker\xe2\x80\x99s back and shoulders gliding along the wall\r\nagain; and so he glided and glided around three sides of the room, and\r\nthen rose up, and shaded his mouth with his hands, and stretched his\r\nneck out towards the preacher, over the people\xe2\x80\x99s heads, and says, in a\r\nkind of a coarse whisper, \xe2\x80\x9c_He had a rat!_\xe2\x80\x9d Then he drooped down and\r\nglided along the wall again to his place. You could see it was a great\r\nsatisfaction to the people, because naturally they wanted to know. A\r\nlittle thing like that don\xe2\x80\x99t cost nothing, and it\xe2\x80\x99s just the little\r\nthings that makes a man to be looked up to and liked. There warn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nmore popular man in town than what that undertaker was.\r\n\r\nWell, the funeral sermon was very good, but pison long and tiresome;\r\nand then the king he shoved in and got off some of his usual rubbage,\r\nand at last the job was through, and the undertaker begun to sneak up\r\non the coffin with his screw-driver. I was in a sweat then, and\r\nwatched him pretty keen. But he never meddled at all; just slid the\r\nlid along as soft as mush, and screwed it down tight and fast. So\r\nthere I was! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know whether the money was in there or not. So,\r\nsays I, s\xe2\x80\x99pose somebody has hogged that bag on the sly?\xe2\x80\x94now how do\r\n_I_ know whether to write to Mary Jane or not? S\xe2\x80\x99pose she dug him up\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t find nothing, what would she think of me? Blame it, I says,\r\nI might get hunted up and jailed; I\xe2\x80\x99d better lay low and keep dark,\r\nand not write at all; the thing\xe2\x80\x99s awful mixed now; trying to better\r\nit, I\xe2\x80\x99ve worsened it a hundred times, and I wish to goodness I\xe2\x80\x99d just\r\nlet it alone, dad fetch the whole business!\r\n\r\nThey buried him, and we come back home, and I went to watching faces\r\nagain\xe2\x80\x94I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it, and I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t rest easy. But nothing come\r\nof it; the faces didn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me nothing.\r\n\r\nThe king he visited around in the evening, and sweetened everybody up,\r\nand made himself ever so friendly; and he give out the idea that his\r\ncongregation over in England would be in a sweat about him, so he must\r\nhurry and settle up the estate right away and leave for home. He was\r\nvery sorry he was so pushed, and so was everybody; they wished he\r\ncould stay longer, but they said they could see it couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be done.\r\nAnd he said of course him and William would take the girls home with\r\nthem; and that pleased everybody too, because then the girls would be\r\nwell fixed and amongst their own relations; and it pleased the girls,\r\ntoo\xe2\x80\x94tickled them so they clean forgot they ever had a trouble in the\r\nworld; and told him to sell out as quick as he wanted to, they would\r\nbe ready. Them poor things was that glad and happy it made my heart\r\nache to see them getting fooled and lied to so, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see no\r\nsafe way for me to chip in and change the general tune.\r\n\r\nWell, blamed if the king didn\xe2\x80\x99t bill the house and the niggers and all\r\nthe property for auction straight off\xe2\x80\x94sale two days after the\r\nfuneral; but anybody could buy private beforehand if they wanted to.\r\n\r\nSo the next day after the funeral, along about noon-time, the girls\xe2\x80\x99\r\njoy got the first jolt. A couple of nigger-traders come along, and the\r\nking sold them the niggers reasonable, for three-day drafts as they\r\ncalled it, and away they went, the two sons up the river to Memphis,\r\nand their mother down the river to Orleans. I thought them poor girls\r\nand them niggers would break their hearts for grief; they cried around\r\neach other, and took on so it most made me down sick to see it. The\r\ngirls said they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever dreamed of seeing the family separated or\r\nsold away from the town. I can\xe2\x80\x99t ever get it out of my memory, the\r\nsight of them poor miserable girls and niggers hanging around each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s necks and crying; and I reckon I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 stood it all,\r\nbut would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 had to bust out and tell on our gang if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t knowed\r\nthe sale warn\xe2\x80\x99t no account and the niggers would be back home in a\r\nweek or two.\r\n\r\nThe thing made a big stir in the town, too, and a good many come out\r\nflat-footed and said it was scandalous to separate the mother and the\r\nchildren that way. It injured the frauds some; but the old fool he\r\nbulled right along, spite of all the duke could say or do, and I tell\r\nyou the duke was powerful uneasy.\r\n\r\nNext day was auction day. About broad day in the morning the king and\r\nthe duke come up in the garret and woke me up, and I see by their look\r\nthat there was trouble. The king says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas you in my room night before last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, your majesty\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94which was the way I always called him when nobody\r\nbut our gang warn\xe2\x80\x99t around.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas you in there yisterday er last night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, your majesty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonor bright, now\xe2\x80\x94no lies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonor bright, your majesty, I\xe2\x80\x99m telling you the truth. I hain\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\na-near your room since Miss Mary Jane took you and the duke and showed\r\nit to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you seen anybody else go in there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, your grace, not as I remember, I believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop and think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI studied awhile and see my chance; then I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I see the niggers go in there several times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth of them gave a little jump, and looked like they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\nexpected it, and then like they _had_. Then the duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, _all_ of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94leastways, not all at once\xe2\x80\x94that is, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I ever see\r\nthem all come _out_ at once but just one time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello! When was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the day we had the funeral. In the morning. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t early,\r\nbecause I overslept. They tiptoed away; so I seen, easy enough, that they\xe2\x80\x99d shoved in\r\nthere to do up your majesty\xe2\x80\x99s room, or something, s\xe2\x80\x99posing you was up;\r\nand found you _warn\xe2\x80\x99t_ up, and so they was hoping to slide out of the\r\nway of trouble without waking you up, if they hadn\xe2\x80\x99t already waked you\r\nup.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat guns, _this_ is a go!\xe2\x80\x9d says the king; and both of them looked\r\npretty sick and tolerable silly. They stood there a-thinking and\r\nscratching their heads a minute, and the duke he bust into a kind of a\r\nlittle raspy chuckle, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does beat all how neat the niggers played their hand. They let on\r\nto be _sorry_ they was going out of this region! And I believed they\r\n_was_ sorry, and so did you, and so did everybody. Don\xe2\x80\x99t ever tell\r\n_me_ any more that a nigger ain\xe2\x80\x99t got any histrionic talent. Why, the\r\nway they played that thing it would fool _anybody._ In my opinion,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a fortune in \xe2\x80\x99em. If I had capital and a theater, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant a better lay-out than that\xe2\x80\x94and here we\xe2\x80\x99ve gone and sold \xe2\x80\x99em for\r\na song. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good business\xe2\x80\x94yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe king snarls around on him and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was trying to do for the best in sellin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99em out so quick. He give me down the banks for not\r\ncoming and _telling_ him I see the niggers come out of his room acting\r\nthat way\xe2\x80\x94said any fool would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 _knowed_ something was up. And then\r\nwaltzed in and cussed _himself_ awhile, and said it all come of him\r\nnot laying late and taking his natural rest that morning, and he\xe2\x80\x99d be\r\nblamed if he\xe2\x80\x99d ever do it again. So they went off a-jawing; and I felt\r\ndreadful glad I\xe2\x80\x99d worked it all off onto the niggers, and yet hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndone the niggers no harm by it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nBy and by it was getting-up time. So I come down the ladder and\r\nstarted for down-stairs; but as I come to the girls\xe2\x80\x99 room the door was\r\nopen, and I see Mary Jane setting by her old hair trunk, which was\r\nopen and she\xe2\x80\x99d been packing things in it\xe2\x80\x94getting ready to go to\r\nEngland. But she had stopped now with a folded gown in her lap, and\r\nhad her face in her hands, crying. I felt awful bad to see it; of\r\ncourse anybody would. I went in there and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Mary Jane, you can\xe2\x80\x99t a-bear to see people in trouble, and _I_\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94most always. Tell me about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo she done it. And it was the niggers\xe2\x80\x94I just expected it. She said\r\nthe beautiful trip to England was most about spoiled for her; she\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know _how_ she was ever going to be happy there, knowing the\r\nmother and the children warn\xe2\x80\x99t ever going to see each other no\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x94and then busted out bitterer than ever, and flung up her hands,\r\nand says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear, dear, to think they ain\xe2\x80\x99t _ever_ going to see each other\r\nany more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they _will_\xe2\x80\x94and inside of two weeks\xe2\x80\x94and I _know_ it!\xe2\x80\x9d says I.\r\n\r\nLaws, it was out before I could think! Then I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Mary Jane, is there any place out of town a little ways where\r\nyou could go and stay three or four days?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; Mr. Lothrop\xe2\x80\x99s. I got to\r\ntell the truth, and you want to brace up, Miss Mary, because it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbad kind, and going to be hard to take, but there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no help for\r\nit. There\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99_Royal Nonesuch, Bricksville._\xe2\x80\x99 Put it away, and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlose it. So\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d got to work _that_ off\xe2\x80\x94I never see such a girafft as the king\r\nwas for wanting to swallow _everything_. Well, whilst they was at it a\r\nsteamboat landed, and in about two minutes up comes a crowd a-whooping\r\nand yelling and laughing and carrying on, and singing out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Here\xe2\x80\x99s_ your opposition line! here\xe2\x80\x99s your two sets o\xe2\x80\x99 heirs to old\r\nPeter Wilks\xe2\x80\x94and you pays your money and you takes your choice!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX\r\n\r\n\r\nThey was fetching a very nice-looking old gentleman along, and a\r\nnice-looking younger one, with his right arm in a sling. Tired of our company,\r\nhey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, your majesty, we warn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94_please_ don\xe2\x80\x99t, your majesty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, then, and tell us what _was_ your idea, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll shake the\r\ninsides out o\xe2\x80\x99 you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHonest, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you everything just as it happened, your majesty.\r\nThe man that had a-holt of me was very good to me, and kept saying he\r\nhad a boy about as big as me that died last year, and he was sorry to\r\nsee a boy in such a dangerous fix; and when they was all took by\r\nsurprise by finding the gold, and made a rush for the coffin, he lets\r\ngo of me and whispers, \xe2\x80\x99Heel it now, or they\xe2\x80\x99ll hang ye, sure!\xe2\x80\x99 and I\r\nlit out. But the duke says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeggo the boy, you old idiot! Would _you_ \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done any different?\r\nDid you inquire around for _him_ when you got loose? _I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t remember\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo the king let go of me, and begun to cuss that town and everybody in\r\nit. For if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nfor that they\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 jailed us till them Englishmen\xe2\x80\x99s baggage come\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94the penitentiary, you bet! So the duke took his hands off and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you ever deny it again I\xe2\x80\x99ll drown you. Of\r\ncourse when they got to snoring we had a long gabble, and I told Jim\r\neverything.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI\r\n\r\n\r\nWe dasn\xe2\x80\x99t stop again at any town for days and days; kept right along\r\ndown the river. We was down south in the warm weather now, and a\r\nmighty long ways from home. We begun to come to trees with Spanish\r\nmoss on them, hanging down from the limbs like long, gray beards. It\r\nwas the first I ever see it growing, and it made the woods look solemn\r\nand dismal. So now the frauds reckoned they was out of danger, and\r\nthey begun to work the villages again.\r\n\r\nFirst they done a lecture on temperance; but they didn\xe2\x80\x99t make enough\r\nfor them both to get drunk on. Another time they tried to go at\r\nyellocution; but they didn\xe2\x80\x99t yellocute long till the audience got up\r\nand give them a solid good cussing, and made them skip out. They\r\ntackled missionarying, and mesmerizing, and doctoring, and telling\r\nfortunes, and a little of everything; but they couldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have\r\nno luck. Jim and me got uneasy. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the look of it. We judged\r\nthey was studying up some kind of worse deviltry than ever. We turned\r\nit over and over, and at last we made up our minds they was going to\r\nbreak into somebody\xe2\x80\x99s house or store, or was going into the\r\ncounterfeit-money business, or something. So then we was pretty\r\nscared, and made up an agreement that we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have nothing in the\r\nworld to do with such actions, and if we ever got the least show we\r\nwould give them the cold shake and clear out and leave them behind.\r\nWell, early one morning we hid the raft in a good, safe place about\r\ntwo mile below a little bit of a shabby village named Pikesville, and\r\nthe king he went ashore and told us all to stay hid whilst he went up\r\nto town and smelt around to see if anybody had got any wind of the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRoyal Nonesuch\xe2\x80\x9d there yet. (\xe2\x80\x9cHouse to rob, you _mean_,\xe2\x80\x9d says I to\r\nmyself; \xe2\x80\x9cand when you get through robbing it you\xe2\x80\x99ll come back here and\r\nwonder what has become of me and Jim and the raft\xe2\x80\x94and you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to\r\ntake it out in wondering.\xe2\x80\x9d) And he said if he warn\xe2\x80\x99t back by midday\r\nthe duke and me would know it was all right, and we was to come along.\r\n\r\nSo we stayed where we was. The duke he fretted and sweated around, and\r\nwas in a mighty sour way. He scolded us for everything, and we\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to do nothing right; he found fault with every little\r\nthing. Something was a-brewing, sure. I\r\ngot down there all out of breath but loaded up with joy, and sung out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSet her loose, Jim; we\xe2\x80\x99re all right now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no answer, and nobody come out of the wigwam. Jim was\r\ngone! I set up a shout\xe2\x80\x94and then another\xe2\x80\x94and then another one; and\r\nrun this way and that in the woods, whooping and screeching; but it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t no use\xe2\x80\x94old Jim was gone. Pretty soon I went out on the\r\nroad, trying to think what I better do, and I run across a boy\r\nwalking, and asked him if he\xe2\x80\x99d seen a strange nigger dressed so and\r\nso, and he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhereabouts?\xe2\x80\x9d says I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDown to Silas Phelps\xe2\x80\x99s place, two mile below here. Who nailed him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was an old fellow\xe2\x80\x94a stranger\xe2\x80\x94and he sold out his chance in him\r\nfor forty dollars, becuz he\xe2\x80\x99s got to go up the river and can\xe2\x80\x99t wait.\r\nThink o\xe2\x80\x99 that, now! No-sirree-_bob_, they\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t no trouble \xe2\x80\x99bout _that_ speculation, you bet you. Say, gimme a\r\nchaw tobacker, won\xe2\x80\x99t ye?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t have none, so he left. I went to the raft, and set down in\r\nthe wigwam to think. But I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come to nothing. I thought till I\r\nwore my head sore, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no way out of the trouble. After\r\nall this long journey, and after all we\xe2\x80\x99d done for them scoundrels,\r\nhere it was all come to nothing, everything all busted up and ruined,\r\nbecause they could have the heart to serve Jim such a trick as that,\r\nand make him a slave again all his life, and amongst strangers, too,\r\nfor forty dirty dollars.\r\n\r\nOnce I said to myself it would be a thousand times better for Jim to\r\nbe a slave at home where his family was, as long as he\xe2\x80\x99d _got_ to be a\r\nslave, and so I\xe2\x80\x99d better write a letter to Tom Sawyer and tell him to\r\ntell Miss Watson where he was. But I soon give up that notion for two\r\nthings: she\xe2\x80\x99d be mad and disgusted at his rascality and ungratefulness\r\nfor leaving her, and so she\xe2\x80\x99d sell him straight down the river again;\r\nand if she didn\xe2\x80\x99t, everybody naturally despises an ungrateful nigger,\r\nand they\xe2\x80\x99d make Jim feel it all the time, and so he\xe2\x80\x99d feel ornery and\r\ndisgraced. That\xe2\x80\x99s just the way: a person does a low-down thing,\r\nand then he don\xe2\x80\x99t want to take no consequences of it. Thinks as long\r\nas he can hide, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t no disgrace. That was my fix exactly. The\r\nmore I studied about this the more my conscience went to grinding me,\r\nand the more wicked and low-down and ornery I got to feeling. And at\r\nlast, when it hit me all of a sudden that here was the plain hand of\r\nProvidence slapping me in the face and letting me know my wickedness\r\nwas being watched all the time from up there in heaven, whilst I was\r\nstealing a poor old woman\xe2\x80\x99s nigger that hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever done me no harm,\r\nand now was showing me there\xe2\x80\x99s One that\xe2\x80\x99s always on the lookout, and\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to allow no such miserable doings to go only just so fur\r\nand no further, I most dropped in my tracks I was so scared. Well, I\r\ntried the best I could to kinder soften it up somehow for myself by\r\nsaying I was brung up wicked, and so I warn\xe2\x80\x99t so much to blame; but\r\nsomething inside of me kept saying, \xe2\x80\x9cThere was the Sunday-school, you\r\ncould \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 gone to it; and if you\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done it they\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 learnt you\r\nthere that people that acts as I\xe2\x80\x99d been acting about that nigger goes\r\nto everlasting fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt made me shiver. And I about made up my mind to pray, and see if I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t try to quit being the kind of a boy I was and be better. So I\r\nkneeled down. But the words wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come. Why wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they? It\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t no use to try and hide it from Him. Nor from _me_, neither. I\r\nknowed very well why they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t come. It was because my heart\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t right; it was because I warn\xe2\x80\x99t square; it was because I was\r\nplaying double. I was letting _on_ to give up sin, but away inside of\r\nme I was holding on to the biggest one of all. I was trying to make my\r\nmouth _say_ I would do the right thing and the clean thing, and go and\r\nwrite to that nigger\xe2\x80\x99s owner and tell where he was; but deep down in\r\nme I knowed it was a lie, and He knowed it. You can\xe2\x80\x99t pray a lie\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nfound that out.\r\n\r\nSo I was full of trouble, full as I could be; and didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to\r\ndo. At last I had an idea; and I says, I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and write the\r\nletter\xe2\x80\x94and _then_ see if I can pray. Why, it was astonishing, the way I\r\nfelt as light as a feather right straight off, and my troubles all\r\ngone. So I got a piece of paper and a pencil, all glad and excited,\r\nand set down and wrote:\r\n\r\nMiss Watson, your runaway nigger Jim is down here two mile below\r\nPikesville, and Mr. Phelps has got him and he will give him up for the\r\nreward if you send. HUCK FINN.\r\n\r\nI felt good and all washed clean of sin for the first time I had ever\r\nfelt so in my life, and I knowed I could pray now. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t do it\r\nstraight off, but laid the paper down and set there thinking\xe2\x80\x94thinking\r\nhow good it was all this happened so, and how near I come to being\r\nlost and going to hell. And went on thinking. And got to thinking over\r\nour trip down the river; and I see Jim before me all the time: in the\r\nday and in the night-time, sometimes moonlight, sometimes storms, and\r\nwe a-floating along, talking and singing and laughing. But somehow I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to strike no places to harden me against him, but only\r\nthe other kind. I\xe2\x80\x99d see him standing my watch on top of his\xe2\x80\x99n, \xe2\x80\x99stead\r\nof calling me, so I could go on sleeping; and see him how glad he was\r\nwhen I come back out of the fog; and when I come to him again in the\r\nswamp, up there where the feud was; and such-like times; and would\r\nalways call me honey, and pet me, and do everything he could think of\r\nfor me, and how good he always was; and at last I struck the time I\r\nsaved him by telling the men we had smallpox aboard, and he was so\r\ngrateful, and said I was the best friend old Jim ever had in the\r\nworld, and the _only_ one he\xe2\x80\x99s got now; and then I happened to look\r\naround and see that paper.\r\n\r\nIt was a close place. I took it up, and held it in my hand. I was\r\na-trembling, because I\xe2\x80\x99d got to decide, forever, betwixt two things,\r\nand I knowed it. I studied a minute, sort of holding my breath, and\r\nthen says to myself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, then, I\xe2\x80\x99ll _go_ to hell\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and tore it up.\r\n\r\nIt was awful thoughts and awful words, but they was said. And I let\r\nthem stay said; and never thought no more about reforming. I shoved the\r\nwhole thing out of my head, and said I would take up wickedness again,\r\nwhich was in my line, being brung up to it, and the other warn\xe2\x80\x99t. And\r\nfor a starter I would go to work and steal Jim out of slavery again;\r\nand if I could think up anything worse, I would do that, too; because\r\nas long as I was in, and in for good, I might as well go the whole hog.\r\n\r\nThen I set to thinking over how to get at it, and turned over some\r\nconsiderable many ways in my mind; and at last fixed up a plan that\r\nsuited me. So then I took the bearings of a woody island that was down\r\nthe river a piece, and as soon as it was fairly dark I crept out with\r\nmy raft and went for it, and hid it there, and then turned in. I slept\r\nthe night through, and got up before it was light, and had my\r\nbreakfast, and put on my store clothes, and tied up some others and\r\none thing or another in a bundle, and took the canoe and cleared for\r\nshore. I landed below where I judged was Phelps\xe2\x80\x99s place, and hid my\r\nbundle in the woods, and then filled up the canoe with water, and\r\nloaded rocks into her and sunk her where I could find her again when I\r\nwanted her, about a quarter of a mile below a little steam-sawmill\r\nthat was on the bank.\r\n\r\nThen I struck up the road, and when I passed the mill I see a sign on\r\nit, \xe2\x80\x9cPhelps\xe2\x80\x99s Sawmill,\xe2\x80\x9d and when I come to the farm-houses, two or\r\nthree hundred yards further along, I kept my eyes peeled, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee nobody around, though it was good daylight now. But I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind,\r\nbecause I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to see nobody just yet\xe2\x80\x94I only wanted to get the\r\nlay of the land. According to my plan, I was going to turn up there\r\nfrom the village, not from below. So I just took a look, and shoved\r\nalong, straight for town. Well, the very first man I see when I got\r\nthere was the duke. He was sticking up a bill for the \xe2\x80\x9cRoyal\r\nNonesuch\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94three-night performance\xe2\x80\x94like that other time. _They_ had the\r\ncheek, them frauds! I was right on him before I could shirk. He looked\r\nastonished, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHel-_lo!_ Where\xe2\x80\x99d _you_ come from?\xe2\x80\x9d Then he says, kind of glad and\r\neager, \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s the raft?\xe2\x80\x94got her in a good place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that\xe2\x80\x99s just what I was going to ask your grace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he didn\xe2\x80\x99t look so joyful, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was your idea for asking _me?_\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I see the king in that doggery yesterday I says\r\nto myself, we can\xe2\x80\x99t get him home for hours, till he\xe2\x80\x99s soberer; so I\r\nwent a-loafing around town to put in the time and wait. A man up and\r\noffered me ten cents to help him pull a skiff over the river and back\r\nto fetch a sheep, and so I went along; but when we was dragging him to\r\nthe boat, and the man left me a-holt of the rope and went behind him\r\nto shove him along, he was too strong for me and jerked loose and run,\r\nand we after him. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t have no dog, and so we had to chase him\r\nall over the country till we tired him out. We never got him till\r\ndark; then we fetched him over, and I started down for the raft. When\r\nI got there and see it was gone, I says to myself, \xe2\x80\x99They\xe2\x80\x99ve got into\r\ntrouble and had to leave; and they\xe2\x80\x99ve took my nigger, which is the\r\nonly nigger I\xe2\x80\x99ve got in the world, and now I\xe2\x80\x99m in a strange country,\r\nand ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no property no more, nor nothing, and no way to make my\r\nliving\xe2\x80\x99; so I set down and cried. I slept in the woods all night. But\r\nwhat _did_ become of the raft, then?\xe2\x80\x94and Jim\xe2\x80\x94poor Jim!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlamed if _I_ know\xe2\x80\x94that is, what\xe2\x80\x99s become of the raft. That old fool\r\nhad made a trade and got forty dollars, and when we found him in the\r\ndoggery the loafers had matched half-dollars with him and got every\r\ncent but what he\xe2\x80\x99d spent for whisky; and when I got him home late last\r\nnight and found the raft gone, we said, \xe2\x80\x98That little rascal has stole\r\nour raft and shook us, and run off down the river.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t shake my _nigger_, would I?\xe2\x80\x94the only nigger I had in the\r\nworld, and the only property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe never thought of that. Fact is, I reckon we\xe2\x80\x99d come to consider him\r\n_our_ nigger; yes, we did consider him so\xe2\x80\x94goodness knows we had\r\ntrouble enough for him. So when we see the raft was gone and we flat\r\nbroke, there warn\xe2\x80\x99t anything for it but to try the \xe2\x80\x98Royal Nonesuch\xe2\x80\x99\r\nanother shake. And I\xe2\x80\x99ve pegged along ever since, dry as a powder-horn.\r\nWhere\xe2\x80\x99s that ten cents? Give it here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had considerable money, so I give him ten cents, but begged him to\r\nspend it for something to eat, and give me some, because it was all\r\nthe money I had, and I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had nothing to eat since yesterday. He\r\nnever said nothing. The next minute he whirls on me and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you reckon that nigger would blow on us? We\xe2\x80\x99d skin him if he done\r\nthat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can he blow? Hain\xe2\x80\x99t he run off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! That old fool sold him, and never divided with me, and the\r\nmoney\xe2\x80\x99s gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Sold_ him?\xe2\x80\x9d I says, and begun to cry; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, he was _my_ nigger, and\r\nthat was my money. Where is he?\xe2\x80\x94I want my nigger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you can\xe2\x80\x99t _get_ your nigger, that\xe2\x80\x99s all\xe2\x80\x94so dry up your\r\nblubbering. Looky here\xe2\x80\x94do you think _you\xe2\x80\x99d_ venture to blow on us?\r\nBlamed if I think I\xe2\x80\x99d trust you. Why, if you _was_ to blow on us\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped, but I never see the duke look so ugly out of his eyes\r\nbefore. I went on a-whimpering, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to blow on nobody; and I ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no time to blow,\r\nnohow; I got to turn out and find my nigger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked kinder bothered, and stood there with his bills fluttering\r\non his arm, thinking, and wrinkling up his forehead. At last he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you something. We got to be here three days. If you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\npromise you won\xe2\x80\x99t blow, and won\xe2\x80\x99t let the nigger blow, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you\r\nwhere to find him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I promised, and he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA farmer by the name of Silas Ph\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and then he stopped. You see, he\r\nstarted to tell me the truth; but when he stopped that way, and begun\r\nto study and think again, I reckoned he was changing his mind. And so\r\nhe was. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t trust me; he wanted to make sure of having me out\r\nof the way the whole three days. So pretty soon he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man that bought him is named Abram Foster\xe2\x80\x94Abram G. Foster\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nhe lives forty mile back here in the country, on the road to\r\nLafayette.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cI can walk it in three days. And I\xe2\x80\x99ll start this\r\nvery afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo you won\xe2\x80\x99t, you\xe2\x80\x99ll start _now_; and don\xe2\x80\x99t you lose any time about\r\nit, neither, nor do any gabbling by the way. Just keep a tight tongue\r\nin your head and move right along, and then you won\xe2\x80\x99t get into trouble\r\nwith _us_, d\xe2\x80\x99ye hear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was the order I wanted, and that was the one I played for. I\r\nwanted to be left free to work my plans.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo clear out,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cand you can tell Mr. Foster whatever you\r\nwant to. Maybe you can get him to believe that Jim _is_ your\r\nnigger\xe2\x80\x94some idiots don\xe2\x80\x99t require documents\xe2\x80\x94leastways I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s such down South here. And when you tell him the handbill and\r\nthe reward\xe2\x80\x99s bogus, maybe he\xe2\x80\x99ll believe you when you explain to him\r\nwhat the idea was for getting \xe2\x80\x99em out. Go \xe2\x80\x99long now, and tell him\r\nanything you want to; but mind you don\xe2\x80\x99t work your jaw any _between_\r\nhere and there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nSo I left, and struck for the back country. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t look around, but\r\nI kinder felt like he was watching me. But I knowed I could tire him\r\nout at that. I went straight out in the country as much as a mile\r\nbefore I stopped; then I doubled back through the woods towards\r\nPhelps\xe2\x80\x99s. I reckoned I better start in on my plan straight off without\r\nfooling around, because I wanted to stop Jim\xe2\x80\x99s mouth till these\r\nfellows could get away. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want no trouble with their kind. I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nseen all I wanted to of them, and wanted to get entirely shut of them.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXII\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen I got there it was all still and Sunday-like, and hot and\r\nsunshiny; the hands was gone to the fields; and there was them kind of\r\nfaint dronings of bugs and flies in the air that makes it seem so\r\nlonesome and like everybody\xe2\x80\x99s dead and gone; and if a breeze fans\r\nalong and quivers the leaves it makes you feel mournful, because you\r\nfeel like it\xe2\x80\x99s spirits whispering\xe2\x80\x94spirits that\xe2\x80\x99s been dead ever so\r\nmany years\xe2\x80\x94and you always think they\xe2\x80\x99re talking about _you._ As a\r\ngeneral thing it makes a body wish _he_ was dead, too, and done with\r\nit all.\r\n\r\nPhelps\xe2\x80\x99s was one of these little one-horse cotton plantations, and\r\nthey all look alike. A rail fence round a two-acre yard; a stile made\r\nout of logs sawed off and up-ended in steps, like barrels of a\r\ndifferent length, to climb over the fence with, and for the women to\r\nstand on when they are going to jump onto a horse; some sickly\r\ngrass-patches in the big yard, but mostly it was bare and smooth, like\r\nan old hat with the nap rubbed off; big double log house for the white\r\nfolks\xe2\x80\x94hewed logs, with the chinks stopped up with mud or mortar, and\r\nthese mud-stripes been whitewashed some time or another; round-log\r\nkitchen, with a big broad, open but roofed passage joining it to the\r\nhouse; log smokehouse back of the kitchen; three little nigger cabins\r\nin a row t\xe2\x80\x99other side the smokehouse; one little hut all by itself\r\naway down against the back fence, and some outbuildings down a piece\r\nthe other side; ash-hopper and big kettle to bile soap in by the\r\nlittle hut; bench by the kitchen door, with bucket of water and a\r\ngourd; hound asleep there in the sun; more hounds asleep round about;\r\nabout three shade trees away off in a corner; some currant bushes and\r\ngooseberry bushes in one place by the fence; outside of the fence a\r\ngarden and a watermelon patch; then the cotton-fields begins, and\r\nafter the fields the woods.\r\n\r\nI went around and clumb over the back stile by the ash-hopper, and\r\nstarted for the kitchen. When I got a little ways I heard the dim hum\r\nof a spinning-wheel wailing along up and sinking along down again; and\r\nthen I knowed for certain I wished I was dead\xe2\x80\x94for that _is_ the\r\nlonesomest sound in the whole world.\r\n\r\nI went right along, not fixing up any particular plan, but just\r\ntrusting to Providence to put the right words in my mouth when the\r\ntime come; for I\xe2\x80\x99d noticed that Providence always did put the right\r\nwords in my mouth if I left it alone.\r\n\r\nWhen I got half-way, first one hound and then another got up and went\r\nfor me, and of course I stopped and faced them, and kept still. And\r\nsuch another powwow as they made! In a quarter of a minute I was a\r\nkind of a hub of a wheel, as you may say\xe2\x80\x94spokes made out of\r\ndogs\xe2\x80\x94circle of fifteen of them packed together around me, with their\r\nnecks and noses stretched up towards me, a-barking and howling; and\r\nmore a-coming; you could see them sailing over fences and around\r\ncorners from everywheres.\r\n\r\nA nigger woman come tearing out of the kitchen with a rolling-pin in\r\nher hand, singing out, \xe2\x80\x9cBegone! _you_ Tige! you Spot! begone sah!\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nshe fetched first one and then another of them a clip and sent them\r\nhowling, and then the rest followed; and the next second half of them\r\ncome back, wagging their tails around me, and making friends with me.\r\nThere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no harm in a hound, nohow.\r\n\r\nAnd behind the woman comes a little nigger girl and two little nigger\r\nboys without anything on but tow-linen shirts, and they hung on to\r\ntheir mother\xe2\x80\x99s gown, and peeped out from behind her at me, bashful,\r\nthe way they always do. And here comes the white woman running from\r\nthe house, about forty-five or fifty year old, bareheaded, and her\r\nspinning-stick in her hand; and behind her comes her little white\r\nchildren, acting the same way the little niggers was going. She was\r\nsmiling all over so she could hardly stand\xe2\x80\x94and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s _you_, at last!\xe2\x80\x94_ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI out with a \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x9d before I thought.\r\n\r\nShe grabbed me and hugged me tight; and then gripped me by both hands\r\nand shook and shook; and the tears come in her eyes, and run down\r\nover; and she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to hug and shake enough, and kept saying,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t look as much like your mother as I reckoned you would; but\r\nlaw sakes, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for that, I\xe2\x80\x99m _so_ glad to see you! Dear, dear,\r\nit does seem like I could eat you up! Children, it\xe2\x80\x99s your cousin\r\nTom!\xe2\x80\x94tell him howdy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut they ducked their heads, and put their fingers in their mouths,\r\nand hid behind her. So she run on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLize, hurry up and get him a hot breakfast right away\xe2\x80\x94or did you get\r\nyour breakfast on the boat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said I had got it on the boat. So then she started for the house,\r\nleading me by the hand, and the children tagging after. When we got\r\nthere she set me down in a split-bottomed chair, and set herself down\r\non a little low stool in front of me, holding both of my hands, and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I can have a _good_ look at you; and, laws-a-me, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been hungry\r\nfor it a many and a many a time, all these long years, and it\xe2\x80\x99s come\r\nat last! We been expecting you a couple of days and more. What kep\xe2\x80\x99\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x94boat get aground?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say yes\xe2\x80\x99m\xe2\x80\x94say Aunt Sally. Where\xe2\x80\x99d she get aground?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t rightly know what to say, because I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know whether the\r\nboat would be coming up the river or down. But I go a good deal on\r\ninstinct; and my instinct said she would be coming up\xe2\x80\x94from down\r\ntowards Orleans. That didn\xe2\x80\x99t help me much, though; for I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthe names of bars down that way. I see I\xe2\x80\x99d got to invent a bar, or\r\nforget the name of the one we got aground on\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94Now I struck an\r\nidea, and fetched it out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt warn\xe2\x80\x99t the grounding\xe2\x80\x94that didn\xe2\x80\x99t keep us back but a little. We\r\nblowed out a cylinder-head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood gracious! anybody hurt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x99m. Killed a nigger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s lucky; because sometimes people do get hurt. Two years ago\r\nlast Christmas your uncle Silas was coming up from Newrleans on the\r\nold Lally Rook, and she blowed out a cylinder-head and crippled a man.\r\nAnd I think he died afterwards. He was a Baptist. Your uncle Silas\r\nknowed a family in Baton Rouge that knowed his people very well. Yes,\r\nI remember now, he _did_ die. Mortification set in, and they had to\r\namputate him. But it didn\xe2\x80\x99t save him. Yes, it was mortification\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nwas it. He turned blue all over, and died in the hope of a glorious\r\nresurrection. They say he was a sight to look at. Your uncle\xe2\x80\x99s been up\r\nto the town every day to fetch you. But I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, m\xe2\x80\x99am, and I won\xe2\x80\x99t do it no\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x94I won\xe2\x80\x99t, honest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t, won\xe2\x80\x99t you? Well, I sh\xe2\x80\x99d _reckon_ you won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x99m, I\xe2\x80\x99m honest about it; I won\xe2\x80\x99t ever do it again\xe2\x80\x94till you ask\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill I _ask_ you! Well, I never see the beat of it in my born days! I\r\nlay you\xe2\x80\x99ll be the Methusalem-numskull of creation before ever _I_ ask\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94or the likes of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cit does surprise me so. I can\xe2\x80\x99t make it out,\r\nsomehow. They said you would, and I thought you would. But\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nstopped and looked around slow, like he wished he could run across a\r\nfriendly eye somewheres, and fetched up on the old gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\nsays, \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t _you_ think she\xe2\x80\x99d like me to kiss her, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no; I\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94well, no, I b\xe2\x80\x99lieve I didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he looks on around the same way to me, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, didn\xe2\x80\x99t _you_ think Aunt Sally \xe2\x80\x99d open out her arms and say, \xe2\x80\x99Sid\r\nSawyer\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy land!\xe2\x80\x9d she says, breaking in and jumping for him, \xe2\x80\x9cyou impudent\r\nyoung rascal, to fool a body so\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and was going to hug him, but he\r\nfended her off, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not till you\xe2\x80\x99ve asked me first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo she didn\xe2\x80\x99t lose no time, but asked him; and hugged him and kissed\r\nhim over and over again, and then turned him over to the old man, and\r\nhe took what was left. And after they got a little quiet again she\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, dear me, I never see such a surprise. We warn\xe2\x80\x99t looking for\r\n_you_ at all, but only Tom. Sis never wrote to me about anybody coming\r\nbut him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s because it warn\xe2\x80\x99t _intended_ for any of us to come but Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsays; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I begged and begged, and at the last minute she let me\r\ncome, too; so, coming down the river, me and Tom thought it would be a\r\nfirst-rate surprise for him to come here to the house first, and for\r\nme to by and by tag along and drop in, and let on to be a stranger.\r\nBut it was a mistake, Aunt Sally. This ain\xe2\x80\x99t no healthy place for a\r\nstranger to come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94not impudent whelps, Sid. You ought to had your jaws boxed; I\r\nhain\xe2\x80\x99t been so put out since I don\xe2\x80\x99t know when. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t care, I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mind the terms\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99d be willing to stand a thousand such jokes to\r\nhave you here. Well, to think of that performance! I don\xe2\x80\x99t deny it, I\r\nwas most putrified with astonishment when you give me that smack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had dinner out in that broad open passage betwixt the house and the\r\nkitchen; and there was things enough on that table for seven\r\nfamilies\xe2\x80\x94and all hot, too; none of your flabby, tough meat that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlaid in a cupboard in a damp cellar all night and tastes like a hunk\r\nof old cold cannibal in the morning. Uncle Silas he asked a pretty\r\nlong blessing over it, but it was worth it; and it didn\xe2\x80\x99t cool it a\r\nbit, neither, the way I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen them kind of interruptions do lots of\r\ntimes.\r\n\r\nThere was a considerable good deal of talk all the afternoon, and me\r\nand Tom was on the lookout all the time; but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use, they\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t happen to say nothing about any runaway nigger, and we was\r\nafraid to try to work up to it. But at supper, at night, one of the\r\nlittle boys says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPa, mayn\xe2\x80\x99t Tom and Sid and me go to the show?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d says the old man, \xe2\x80\x9cI reckon there ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to be any; and you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go if there was; because the runaway nigger told Burton and\r\nme all about that scandalous show, and Burton said he would tell the\r\npeople; so I reckon they\xe2\x80\x99ve drove the owdacious loafers out of town\r\nbefore this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo there it was!\xe2\x80\x94but _I_ couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it. Tom and me was to sleep in\r\nthe same room and bed; so, being tired, we bid good night and went up\r\nto bed right after supper, and clumb out of the window and down the\r\nlightning-rod, and shoved for the town; for I didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe anybody\r\nwas going to give the king and the duke a hint, and so if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhurry up and give them one they\xe2\x80\x99d get into trouble sure.\r\n\r\nOn the road Tom he told me all about how it was reckoned I was\r\nmurdered, and how pap disappeared pretty soon, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t come back no\r\nmore, and what a stir there was when Jim run away; and I told Tom all\r\nabout our \xe2\x80\x9cRoyal Nonesuch\xe2\x80\x9d rapscallions, and as much of the raft\r\nvoyage as I had time to; and as we struck into the town and up through\r\nthe middle of it\xe2\x80\x94it was as much as half after eight then\xe2\x80\x94here comes\r\na raging rush of people with torches, and an awful whooping and\r\nyelling, and banging tin pans and blowing horns; and we jumped to one\r\nside to let them go by; and as they went by I see they had the king\r\nand the duke astraddle of a rail\xe2\x80\x94that is, I knowed it _was_ the king\r\nand the duke, though they was all over tar and feathers, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlook like nothing in the world that was human\xe2\x80\x94just looked like a\r\ncouple of monstrous big soldier-plumes. Well, it made me sick to see\r\nit; and I was sorry for them poor pitiful rascals, it seemed like I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever feel any hardness against them any more in the world. It\r\nwas a dreadful thing to see. Human beings _can_ be awful cruel to one\r\nanother.\r\n\r\nWe see we was too late\xe2\x80\x94couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do no good. We asked some stragglers\r\nabout it, and they said everybody went to the show looking very\r\ninnocent; and laid low and kept dark till the poor old king was in the\r\nmiddle of his cavortings on the stage; then somebody give a signal,\r\nand the house rose up and went for them.\r\n\r\nSo we poked along back home, and I warn\xe2\x80\x99t feeling so brash as I was\r\nbefore, but kind of ornery, and humble, and to blame, somehow\xe2\x80\x94though\r\n_I_ hadn\xe2\x80\x99t done nothing. But that\xe2\x80\x99s always the way; it don\xe2\x80\x99t make no\r\ndifference whether you do right or wrong, a person\xe2\x80\x99s conscience ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngot no sense, and just goes for him _anyway._ If I had a yaller dog\r\nthat didn\xe2\x80\x99t know no more than a person\xe2\x80\x99s conscience does I would pison\r\nhim. It takes up more room than all the rest of a person\xe2\x80\x99s insides,\r\nand yet ain\xe2\x80\x99t no good, nohow. Tom Sawyer he says the same.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIV\r\n\r\n\r\nWe stopped talking, and got to thinking. By and by Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooky here, Huck, what fools we are to not think of it before! I bet\r\nI know where Jim is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! Where?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that hut down by the ash-hopper. Why, looky here. When we was at\r\ndinner, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you see a nigger man go in there with some vittles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you think the vittles was for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a dog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo \xe2\x80\x99d I. Well, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t for a dog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause part of it was watermelon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it was\xe2\x80\x94I noticed it. Well, it does beat all that I never thought\r\nabout a dog not eating watermelon. It shows how a body can see and\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see at the same time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, the nigger unlocked the padlock when he went in, and he locked\r\nit again when he came out. He fetched uncle a key about the time we\r\ngot up from table\xe2\x80\x94same key, I bet. Watermelon shows man, lock shows\r\nprisoner; and it ain\xe2\x80\x99t likely there\xe2\x80\x99s two prisoners on such a little\r\nplantation, and where the people\xe2\x80\x99s all so kind and good. Jim\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nprisoner. All right\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m glad we found it out detective fashion; I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give shucks for any other way. Now you work your mind, and\r\nstudy out a plan to steal Jim, and I will study out one, too; and\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll take the one we like the best.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat a head for just a boy to have! If I had Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x99s head I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t trade it off to be a duke, nor mate of a steamboat, nor clown\r\nin a circus, nor nothing I can think of. I went to thinking out a\r\nplan, but only just to be doing something; I knowed very well where\r\nthe right plan was going to come from. Pretty soon Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReady?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right\xe2\x80\x94bring it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy plan is this,\xe2\x80\x9d I says. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can easy find out if it\xe2\x80\x99s Jim in there.\r\nThen get up my canoe to-morrow night, and fetch my raft over from the\r\nisland. Then the first dark night that comes steal the key out of the\r\nold man\xe2\x80\x99s britches after he goes to bed, and shove off down the river\r\non the raft with Jim, hiding daytimes and running nights, the way me\r\nand Jim used to do before. Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t that plan work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Work?_ Why, cert\xe2\x80\x99nly it would work, like rats a-fighting. But it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntoo blame\xe2\x80\x99 simple; there ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing _to_ it. What\xe2\x80\x99s the good of a\r\nplan that ain\xe2\x80\x99t no more trouble than that? It\xe2\x80\x99s as mild as goose-milk.\r\nWhy, Huck, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make no more talk than breaking into a soap\r\nfactory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI never said nothing, because I warn\xe2\x80\x99t expecting nothing different;\r\nbut I knowed mighty well that whenever he got _his_ plan ready it\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have none of them objections to it.\r\n\r\nAnd it didn\xe2\x80\x99t. He told me what it was, and I see in a minute it was\r\nworth fifteen of mine for style, and would make Jim just as free a man\r\nas mine would, and maybe get us all killed besides. So I was\r\nsatisfied, and said we would waltz in on it. I needn\xe2\x80\x99t tell what it\r\nwas here, because I knowed it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay the way it was. I knowed\r\nhe would be changing it around every which way as we went along, and\r\nheaving in new bullinesses wherever he got a chance. And that is what\r\nhe done.\r\n\r\nWell, one thing was dead sure, and that was that Tom Sawyer was in\r\nearnest, and was actuly going to help steal that nigger out of\r\nslavery. That was the thing that was too many for me. Here was a boy\r\nthat was respectable and well brung up; and had a character to lose;\r\nand folks at home that had characters; and he was bright and not\r\nleather-headed; and knowing and not ignorant; and not mean, but kind;\r\nand yet here he was, without any more pride, or rightness, or feeling,\r\nthan to stoop to this business, and make himself a shame, and his\r\nfamily a shame, before everybody. I _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ understand it no way at\r\nall. It was outrageous, and I knowed I ought to just up and tell him\r\nso; and so be his true friend, and let him quit the thing right where\r\nhe was and save himself. And I _did_ start to tell him; but he shut me\r\nup, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you reckon I know what I\xe2\x80\x99m about? Don\xe2\x80\x99t I generly know what\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I _say_ I was going to help steal the nigger?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Well_, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s all he said, and that\xe2\x80\x99s all I said. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use to say any\r\nmore; because when he said he\xe2\x80\x99d do a thing, he always done it. But _I_\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out how he was willing to go into this thing; so I just\r\nlet it go, and never bothered no more about it. If he was bound to\r\nhave it so, _I_ couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\r\n\r\nWhen we got home the house was all dark and still; so we went on down\r\nto the hut by the ash-hopper for to examine it. We went through the\r\nyard so as to see what the hounds would do. They knowed us, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake no more noise than country dogs is always doing when anything\r\ncomes by in the night. When we got to the cabin we took a look at the\r\nfront and the two sides; and on the side I warn\xe2\x80\x99t acquainted\r\nwith\xe2\x80\x94which was the north side\xe2\x80\x94we found a square window-hole, up\r\ntolerable high, with just one stout board nailed across it. I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s the ticket. This hole\xe2\x80\x99s big enough for Jim to get through if\r\nwe wrench off the board.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s as simple as tit-tat-toe, three-in-a-row, and as easy as playing\r\nhooky. I should _hope_ we can find a way that\xe2\x80\x99s a little more\r\ncomplicated than _that_, Huck Finn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9chow\xe2\x80\x99ll it do to saw him out, the way I done\r\nbefore I was murdered that time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s more _like_,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s real mysterious, and troublesome,\r\nand good,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I bet we can find a way that\xe2\x80\x99s twice as long.\r\nThere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no hurry; le\xe2\x80\x99s keep on looking around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBetwixt the hut and the fence, on the back side, was a lean-to that\r\njoined the hut at the eaves, and was made out of plank. It was as long\r\nas the hut, but narrow\xe2\x80\x94only about six foot wide. The door to it was\r\nat the south end, and was padlocked. Tom he went to the soap-kettle\r\nand searched around, and fetched back the iron thing they lift the lid\r\nwith; so he took it and prized out one of the staples. The chain fell\r\ndown, and we opened the door and went in, and shut it, and struck a\r\nmatch, and see the shed was only built against a cabin and hadn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nconnection with it; and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no floor to the shed, nor nothing\r\nin it but some old rusty played-out hoes and spades and picks and a\r\ncrippled plow. The match went out, and so did we, and shoved in the\r\nstaple again, and the door was locked as good as ever. Tom was joyful.\r\nHe says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99re all right. We\xe2\x80\x99ll _dig_ him out. It \xe2\x80\x99ll take about a week!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen we started for the house, and I went in the back door\xe2\x80\x94you only\r\nhave to pull a buckskin latch-string, they don\xe2\x80\x99t fasten the doors\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nthat warn\xe2\x80\x99t romantical enough for Tom Sawyer; no way would do him but\r\nhe must climb up the lightning-rod. But after he got up half-way about\r\nthree times, and missed fire and fell every time, and the last time\r\nmost busted his brains out, he thought he\xe2\x80\x99d got to give it up; but\r\nafter he was rested he allowed he would give her one more turn for\r\nluck, and this time he made the trip.\r\n\r\nIn the morning we was up at break of day, and down to the nigger\r\ncabins to pet the dogs and make friends with the nigger that fed\r\nJim\xe2\x80\x94if it _was_ Jim that was being fed. The niggers was just getting\r\nthrough breakfast and starting for the fields; and Jim\xe2\x80\x99s nigger was\r\npiling up a tin pan with bread and meat and things; and whilst the\r\nothers was leaving, the key come from the house.\r\n\r\nThis nigger had a good-natured, chuckle-headed face, and his wool was\r\nall tied up in little bunches with thread. That was to keep witches\r\noff. He said the witches was pestering him awful these nights, and\r\nmaking him see all kinds of strange things, and hear all kinds of\r\nstrange words and noises, and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe he was ever witched so\r\nlong before in his life. He got so worked up, and got to running on so\r\nabout his troubles, he forgot all about what he\xe2\x80\x99d been a-going to do.\r\nSo Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the vittles for? Going to feed the dogs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe nigger kind of smiled around graduly over his face, like when you\r\nheave a brickbat in a mud-puddle, and he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mars Sid, _a_ dog. Cur\xe2\x80\x99us dog, too. Does you want to go en look\r\nat \xe2\x80\x99im?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI hunched Tom, and whispers:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou going, right here in the daybreak? _That_ warn\xe2\x80\x99t the plan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it warn\xe2\x80\x99t; but it\xe2\x80\x99s the plan _now._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo, drat him, we went along, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it much. When we got in\r\nwe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly see anything, it was so dark; but Jim was there,\r\nsure enough, and could see us; and he sings out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, _Huck!_ En good _lan\xe2\x80\x99!_ ain\xe2\x80\x99 dat Misto Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI just knowed how it would be; I just expected it. _I_ didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nnothing to do; and if I had I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done it, because that\r\nnigger busted in and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, de gracious sakes! do he know you genlmen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe could see pretty well now. Tom he looked at the nigger, steady and\r\nkind of wondering, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes _who_ know us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, dis-yer runaway nigger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t reckon he does; but what put that into your head?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _put_ it dar? Didn\xe2\x80\x99 he jis\xe2\x80\x99 dis minute sing out like he knowed\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom says, in a puzzled-up kind of way:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s mighty curious. _Who_ sung out? _When_ did he sing out?\r\n_What_ did he sing out?\xe2\x80\x9d And turns to me, perfectly ca\xe2\x80\x99m, and says,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid _you_ hear anybody sing out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course there warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing to be said but the one thing; so I\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; _I_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t heard nobody say nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he turns to Jim, and looks him over like he never see him before,\r\nand says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you sing out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sah,\xe2\x80\x9d says Jim; \xe2\x80\x9c_I_ hain\xe2\x80\x99t said nothing, sah.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sah, I hain\xe2\x80\x99t said a word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever see us before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sah; not as _I_ knows on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Tom turns to the nigger, which was looking wild and distressed, and\r\nsays, kind of severe:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you reckon\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you, anyway? What made you think\r\nsomebody sung out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s de dad-blame\xe2\x80\x99 witches, sah, en I wisht I was dead, I do.\r\nDey\xe2\x80\x99s awluz at it, sah, en dey do mos\xe2\x80\x99 kill me, dey sk\xe2\x80\x99yers me so.\r\nPlease to don\xe2\x80\x99t tell nobody \xe2\x80\x99bout it sah, er ole Mars Silas he\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nscole me; \xe2\x80\x99kase he say dey _ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ no witches. I jis\xe2\x80\x99 wish to goodness\r\nhe was heah now\xe2\x80\x94_den_ what would he say! I jis\xe2\x80\x99 bet he couldn\xe2\x80\x99 fine\r\nno way to git aroun\xe2\x80\x99 it _dis_ time. But it\xe2\x80\x99s awluz jis\xe2\x80\x99 so; people\r\ndat\xe2\x80\x99s _sot_, stays sot; dey won\xe2\x80\x99t look into noth\xe2\x80\x99n\xe2\x80\x99 en fine it out f\xe2\x80\x99r\r\ndeyselves, en when _you_ fine it out en tell um \xe2\x80\x99bout it, dey doan\xe2\x80\x99\r\nb\xe2\x80\x99lieve you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom give him a dime, and said we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell nobody; and told him to\r\nbuy some more thread to tie up his wool with; and then looks at Jim,\r\nand says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if Uncle Silas is going to hang this nigger. If I was to\r\ncatch a nigger that was ungrateful enough to run away, _I_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give\r\nhim up, I\xe2\x80\x99d hang him.\xe2\x80\x9d And whilst the nigger stepped to the door to\r\nlook at the dime and bite it to see if it was good, he whispers to Jim\r\nand says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ever let on to know us. And if you hear any digging going on\r\nnights, it\xe2\x80\x99s us; we\xe2\x80\x99re going to set you free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim only had time to grab us by the hand and squeeze it; then the\r\nnigger come back, and we said we\xe2\x80\x99d come again some time if the nigger\r\nwanted us to; and he said he would, more particular if it was dark,\r\nbecause the witches went for him mostly in the dark, and it was good\r\nto have folks around then.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXV\r\n\r\n\r\nIt would be most an hour yet till breakfast, so we left and struck\r\ndown into the woods; because Tom said we got to have _some_ light to\r\nsee how to dig by, and a lantern makes too much, and might get us into\r\ntrouble; what we must have was a lot of them rotten chunks that\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncalled fox-fire, and just makes a soft kind of a glow when you lay\r\nthem in a dark place. We fetched an armful and hid it in the weeds,\r\nand set down to rest, and Tom says, kind of dissatisfied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame it, this whole thing is just as easy and awkward as it can be.\r\nAnd so it makes it so rotten difficult to get up a difficult plan.\r\nThere ain\xe2\x80\x99t no watchman to be drugged\xe2\x80\x94now there _ought_ to be a\r\nwatchman. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t even a dog to give a sleeping-mixture to. And\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s Jim chained by one leg, with a ten-foot chain, to the leg of\r\nhis bed: why, all you got to do is to lift up the bedstead and slip\r\noff the chain. And Uncle Silas he trusts everybody; sends the key to\r\nthe punkin-headed nigger, and don\xe2\x80\x99t send nobody to watch the nigger.\r\nJim could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got out of that window-hole before this, only there\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be no use trying to travel with a ten-foot chain on his leg.\r\nWhy, drat it, Huck, it\xe2\x80\x99s the stupidest arrangement I ever see. You got\r\nto invent _all_ the difficulties. Well, we can\xe2\x80\x99t help it; we got to do\r\nthe best we can with the materials we\xe2\x80\x99ve got. Anyhow, there\xe2\x80\x99s one\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s more honor in getting him out through a lot of\r\ndifficulties and dangers, where there warn\xe2\x80\x99t one of them furnished to\r\nyou by the people who it was their duty to furnish them, and you had\r\nto contrive them all out of your own head. Now look at just that one\r\nthing of the lantern. When you come down to the cold facts, we simply\r\ngot to _let on_ that a lantern\xe2\x80\x99s resky. Why, we could work with a\r\ntorchlight procession if we wanted to, _I_ believe. Now, whilst I\r\nthink of it, we got to hunt up something to make a saw out of the\r\nfirst chance we get.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do we want of a saw?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do we _want_ of a saw? Hain\xe2\x80\x99t we got to saw the leg of Jim\xe2\x80\x99s bed\r\noff, so as to get the chain loose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you just said a body could lift up the bedstead and slip the\r\nchain off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if that ain\xe2\x80\x99t just like you, Huck Finn. You _can_ get up the\r\ninfant-schooliest ways of going at a thing. Why, hain\xe2\x80\x99t you ever read\r\nany books at all?\xe2\x80\x94Baron Trenck, nor Casanova, nor Benvenuto\r\nChelleeny, nor Henri IV., nor none of them heroes? Who ever heard of\r\ngetting a prisoner loose in such an old-maidy way as that? No; the\r\nway all the best authorities does is to saw the bed-leg in two, and\r\nleave it just so, and swallow the sawdust, so it can\xe2\x80\x99t be found, and\r\nput some dirt and grease around the sawed place so the very keenest\r\nseneskal can\xe2\x80\x99t see no sign of its being sawed, and thinks the bed-leg\r\nis perfectly sound. Then, the night you\xe2\x80\x99re ready, fetch the leg a\r\nkick, down she goes; slip off your chain, and there you are. Nothing\r\nto do but hitch your rope ladder to the battlements, shin down it,\r\nbreak your leg in the moat\xe2\x80\x94because a rope ladder is nineteen foot too\r\nshort, you know\xe2\x80\x94and there\xe2\x80\x99s your horses and your trusty vassles, and\r\nthey scoop you up and fling you across a saddle, and away you go to\r\nyour native Langudoc, or Navarre, or wherever it is. It\xe2\x80\x99s gaudy, Huck.\r\nI wish there was a moat to this cabin. If we get time, the night of\r\nthe escape, we\xe2\x80\x99ll dig one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do we want of a moat when we\xe2\x80\x99re going to snake him out from\r\nunder the cabin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he never heard me. He had forgot me and everything else. He had\r\nhis chin in his hand, thinking. Pretty soon he sighs and shakes his\r\nhead; then sighs again, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do\xe2\x80\x94there ain\xe2\x80\x99t necessity enough for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor what?\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, to saw Jim\xe2\x80\x99s leg off,\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood land!\xe2\x80\x9d I says; \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t _no_ necessity for it. And what\r\nwould you want to saw his leg off for, anyway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, some of the best authorities has done it. They couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get the\r\nchain off, so they just cut their hand off and shoved. And a leg would\r\nbe better still. But we got to let that go. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t necessity\r\nenough in this case; and, besides, Jim\xe2\x80\x99s a nigger, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nunderstand the reasons for it, and how it\xe2\x80\x99s the custom in Europe; so\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll let it go. But there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing\xe2\x80\x94he can have a rope ladder; we\r\ncan tear up our sheets and make him a rope ladder easy enough. And we\r\ncan send it to him in a pie; it\xe2\x80\x99s mostly done that way. And I\xe2\x80\x99ve et\r\nworse pies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Tom Sawyer, how you talk,\xe2\x80\x9d I says; \xe2\x80\x9cJim ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no use for a\r\nrope ladder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe _has_ got use for it. How _you_ talk, you better say; you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow nothing about it. He\xe2\x80\x99s _got_ to have a rope ladder; they all do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the nation can he _do_ with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Do_ with it? He can hide it in his bed, can\xe2\x80\x99t he? That\xe2\x80\x99s what they\r\nall do; and _he\xe2\x80\x99s_ got to, too. Huck, you don\xe2\x80\x99t ever seem to want to\r\ndo anything that\xe2\x80\x99s regular; you want to be starting something fresh\r\nall the time. S\xe2\x80\x99pose he _don\xe2\x80\x99t_ do nothing with it? ain\xe2\x80\x99t it there in\r\nhis bed, for a clue, after he\xe2\x80\x99s gone? and don\xe2\x80\x99t you reckon they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nwant clues? Of course they will. And you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t leave them any? That\r\nwould be a _pretty_ howdy-do, _wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ it! I never heard of such a\r\nthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cif it\xe2\x80\x99s in the regulations, and he\xe2\x80\x99s got to have it,\r\nall right, let him have it; because I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to go back on no\r\nregulations; but there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing, Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x94if we go to tearing up\r\nour sheets to make Jim a rope ladder, we\xe2\x80\x99re going to get into trouble\r\nwith Aunt Sally, just as sure as you\xe2\x80\x99re born. Now, the way I look at\r\nit, a hickry-bark ladder don\xe2\x80\x99t cost nothing, and don\xe2\x80\x99t waste nothing,\r\nand is just as good to load up a pie with, and hide in a straw tick,\r\nas any rag ladder you can start; and as for Jim, he ain\xe2\x80\x99t had no\r\nexperience, and so _he_ don\xe2\x80\x99t care what kind of a\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, shucks, Huck Finn, if I was as ignorant as you I\xe2\x80\x99d keep\r\nstill\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what _I\xe2\x80\x99d_ do. Who ever heard of a state prisoner escaping\r\nby a hickry-bark ladder? Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly ridiculous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, all right, Tom, fix it your own way; but if you\xe2\x80\x99ll take my\r\nadvice, you\xe2\x80\x99ll let me borrow a sheet off of the clothes-line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said that would do. And that gave him another idea, and he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBorrow a shirt, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do we want of a shirt, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWant it for Jim to keep a journal on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJournal your granny\xe2\x80\x94_Jim_ can\xe2\x80\x99t write.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cS\xe2\x80\x99pose he _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ write\xe2\x80\x94he can make marks on the shirt, can\xe2\x80\x99t he, if\r\nwe make him a pen out of an old pewter spoon or a piece of an old iron\r\nbarrel-hoop?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Tom, we can pull a feather out of a goose and make him a better\r\none; and quicker, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Prisoners_ don\xe2\x80\x99t have geese running around the donjon-keep to pull\r\npens out of, you muggins. They _always_ make their pens out of the\r\nhardest, toughest, troublesomest piece of old brass candlestick or\r\nsomething like that they can get their hands on; and it takes them\r\nweeks and weeks and months and months to file it out, too, because\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do it by rubbing it on the wall. _They_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t use a\r\ngoose-quill if they had it.
