It ain\xe2\x80\x99t regular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, what \xe2\x80\x99ll we make him the ink out of?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMany makes it out of iron-rust and tears; but that\xe2\x80\x99s the common sort\r\nand women; the best authorities uses their own blood. Jim can do that;\r\nand when he wants to send any little common ordinary mysterious\r\nmessage to let the world know where he\xe2\x80\x99s captivated, he can write it\r\non the bottom of a tin plate with a fork and throw it out of the\r\nwindow. The Iron Mask always done that, and it\xe2\x80\x99s a blame\xe2\x80\x99 good way,\r\ntoo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJim ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no tin plates. They feed him in a pan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing; we can get him some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t nobody _read_ his plates.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t got anything to _do_ with it, Huck Finn. All _he\xe2\x80\x99s_ got to\r\ndo is to write on the plate and throw it out. You don\xe2\x80\x99t _have_ to be\r\nable to read it. Why, half the time you can\xe2\x80\x99t read anything a prisoner\r\nwrites on a tin plate, or anywhere else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, what\xe2\x80\x99s the sense in wasting the plates?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, blame it all, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t the _prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s_ plates.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s _somebody\xe2\x80\x99s_ plates, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, spos\xe2\x80\x99n it is? What does the _prisoner_ care whose\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe broke off there, because we heard the breakfast-horn blowing. So we\r\ncleared out for the house.\r\n\r\nAlong during the morning I borrowed a sheet and a white shirt off of\r\nthe clothes-line; and I found an old sack and put them in it, and we\r\nwent down and got the fox-fire, and put that in too. I called it\r\nborrowing, because that was what pap always called it; but Tom said it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t borrowing, it was stealing. He said we was representing\r\nprisoners; and prisoners don\xe2\x80\x99t care how they get a thing so they get\r\nit, and nobody don\xe2\x80\x99t blame them for it, either. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t no crime in a\r\nprisoner to steal the thing he needs to get away with, Tom said; it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhis right; and so, as long as we was representing a prisoner, we had a\r\nperfect right to steal anything on this place we had the least use for\r\nto get ourselves out of prison with. He said if we warn\xe2\x80\x99t prisoners it\r\nwould be a very different thing, and nobody but a mean, ornery person\r\nwould steal when he warn\xe2\x80\x99t a prisoner. So we allowed we would steal\r\neverything there was that come handy. And yet he made a mighty fuss,\r\none day, after that, when I stole a watermelon out of the nigger patch\r\nand eat it; and he made me go and give the niggers a dime without\r\ntelling them what it was for. Tom said that what he meant was, we\r\ncould steal anything we _needed._ Well, I says, I needed the\r\nwatermelon. But he said I didn\xe2\x80\x99t need it to get out of prison with;\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s where the difference was. He said if I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 wanted it to hide\r\na knife in, and smuggle it to Jim to kill the seneskal with, it would\r\n\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been all right. So I let it go at that, though I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no\r\nadvantage in my representing a prisoner if I got to set down and chaw\r\nover a lot of gold-leaf distinctions like that every time I see a\r\nchance to hog a watermelon.\r\n\r\nWell, as I was saying, we waited that morning till everybody was\r\nsettled down to business, and nobody in sight around the yard; then\r\nTom he carried the sack into the lean-to whilst I stood off a piece to\r\nkeep watch. By and by he come out, and we went and set down on the\r\nwoodpile to talk. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything\xe2\x80\x99s all right now except tools; and that\xe2\x80\x99s easy fixed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTools?\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTools for what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, to dig with. We ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to _gnaw_ him out, are we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin\xe2\x80\x99t them old crippled picks and things in there good enough to dig\r\na nigger out with?\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\nHe turns on me, looking pitying enough to make a body cry, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuck Finn, did you _ever_ hear of a prisoner having picks and shovels,\r\nand all the modern conveniences in his wardrobe to dig himself out\r\nwith? Now I want to ask you\xe2\x80\x94if you got any reasonableness in you at\r\nall\xe2\x80\x94what kind of a show would _that_ give him to be a hero? Why, they\r\nmight as well lend him the key and done with it. Picks and shovels\xe2\x80\x94why,\r\nthey wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t furnish \xe2\x80\x99em to a king.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cif we don\xe2\x80\x99t want the picks and shovels, what do\r\nwe want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA couple of case-knives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo dig the foundations out from under that cabin with?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfound it, it\xe2\x80\x99s foolish, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt don\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference how foolish it is, it\xe2\x80\x99s the _right_\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94and it\xe2\x80\x99s the regular way. And there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no _other_ way, that\r\never _I_ heard of, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve read all the books that gives any\r\ninformation about these things. They always dig out with a\r\ncase-knife\xe2\x80\x94and not through dirt, mind you; generly it\xe2\x80\x99s through solid\r\nrock. And it takes them weeks and weeks and weeks, and for ever and\r\never. Why, look at one of them prisoners in the bottom dungeon of the\r\nCastle Deef, in the harbor of Marseilles, that dug himself out that\r\nway; how long was _he_ at it, you reckon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. A month and a half.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Thirty-seven year_\xe2\x80\x94and he come out in China. _That\xe2\x80\x99s_ the kind. I\r\nwish the bottom of _this_ fortress was solid rock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Jim_ don\xe2\x80\x99t know nobody in China.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s _that_ got to do with it? Neither did that other fellow. But\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re always a-wandering off on a side issue. Why can\xe2\x80\x99t you stick to\r\nthe main point?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right\xe2\x80\x94_I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t care where he comes out, so he _comes_ out; and\r\nJim don\xe2\x80\x99t, either, I reckon. But there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing, anyway\xe2\x80\x94Jim\xe2\x80\x99s too\r\nold to be dug out with a case-knife. He won\xe2\x80\x99t last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes he will _last_, too. You don\xe2\x80\x99t reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s going to take\r\nthirty-seven years to dig out through a _dirt_ foundation, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long will it take, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we can\xe2\x80\x99t resk being as long as we ought to, because it mayn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake very long for Uncle Silas to hear from down there by New Orleans.\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99ll hear Jim ain\xe2\x80\x99t from there. Then his next move will be to\r\nadvertise Jim, or something like that. So we can\xe2\x80\x99t resk being as long\r\ndigging him out as we ought to. By rights I reckon we ought to be a\r\ncouple of years; but we can\xe2\x80\x99t. Things being so uncertain, what I\r\nrecommend is this: that we really dig right in, as quick as we can;\r\nand after that, we can _let on_, to ourselves, that we was at it\r\nthirty-seven years. Then we can snatch him out and rush him away the\r\nfirst time there\xe2\x80\x99s an alarm. Yes, I reckon that \xe2\x80\x99ll be the best way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, there\xe2\x80\x99s _sense_ in that,\xe2\x80\x9d I says. \xe2\x80\x9cLetting on don\xe2\x80\x99t cost\r\nnothing; letting on ain\xe2\x80\x99t no trouble; and if it\xe2\x80\x99s any object, I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmind letting on we was at it a hundred and fifty year. It wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstrain me none, after I got my hand in. So I\xe2\x80\x99ll mosey along now, and\r\nsmouch a couple of case-knives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSmouch three,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cwe want one to make a saw out of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom, if it ain\xe2\x80\x99t unregular and irreligious to sejest it,\xe2\x80\x9d I says,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s an old rusty saw-blade around yonder sticking under the\r\nweather-boarding behind the smokehouse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked kind of weary and discouraged-like, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use to try to learn you nothing, Huck. Run along and\r\nsmouch the knives\xe2\x80\x94three of them.\xe2\x80\x9d So I done it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXVI\r\n\r\n\r\nAs soon as we reckoned everybody was asleep that night we went down\r\nthe lightning-rod, and shut ourselves up in the lean-to, and got out\r\nour pile of fox-fire, and went to work. We cleared everything out of\r\nthe way, about four or five foot along the middle of the bottom log.\r\nTom said we was right behind Jim\xe2\x80\x99s bed now, and we\xe2\x80\x99d dig in under it,\r\nand when we got through there couldn\xe2\x80\x99t nobody in the cabin ever know\r\nthere was any hole there, because Jim\xe2\x80\x99s counterpin hung down most to\r\nthe ground, and you\xe2\x80\x99d have to raise it up and look under to see the\r\nhole. So we dug and dug with the case-knives till most midnight; and\r\nthen we was dog-tired, and our hands was blistered, and yet you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t see we\xe2\x80\x99d done anything hardly. At last I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis ain\xe2\x80\x99t no thirty-seven-year job; this is a thirty-eight-year job,\r\nTom Sawyer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe never said nothing. But he sighed, and pretty soon he stopped\r\ndigging, and then for a good little while I knowed that he was\r\nthinking. Then he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use, Huck, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to work. If we was prisoners\r\nit would, because then we\xe2\x80\x99d have as many years as we wanted, and no\r\nhurry; and we wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get but a few minutes to dig, every day, while\r\nthey was changing watches, and so our hands wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get blistered,\r\nand we could keep it up right along, year in and year out, and do it\r\nright, and the way it ought to be done. But _we_ can\xe2\x80\x99t fool along; we\r\ngot to rush; we ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no time to spare. If we was to put in\r\nanother night this way we\xe2\x80\x99d have to knock off for a week to let our\r\nhands get well\xe2\x80\x94couldn\xe2\x80\x99t touch a case-knife with them sooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, what we going to do, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you. It ain\xe2\x80\x99t right, and it ain\xe2\x80\x99t moral, and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlike it to get out; but there ain\xe2\x80\x99t only just the one way: we got to\r\ndig him out with the picks, and _let on_ it\xe2\x80\x99s case-knives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_ you\xe2\x80\x99re _talking!_\xe2\x80\x9d I says; \xe2\x80\x9cyour head gets leveler and leveler\r\nall the time, Tom Sawyer,\xe2\x80\x9d I says. \xe2\x80\x9cPicks is the thing, moral or no\r\nmoral; and as for me, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care shucks for the morality of it,\r\nnohow. When I start in to steal a nigger, or a watermelon, or a\r\nSunday-school book, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t no ways particular how it\xe2\x80\x99s done so it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndone. What I want is my nigger; or what I want is my watermelon; or\r\nwhat I want is my Sunday-school book; and if a pick\xe2\x80\x99s the handiest\r\nthing, that\xe2\x80\x99s the thing I\xe2\x80\x99m a-going to dig that nigger or that\r\nwatermelon or that Sunday-school book out with; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t give a\r\ndead rat what the authorities thinks about it nuther.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s excuse for picks and letting on in a case\r\nlike this; if it warn\xe2\x80\x99t so, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t approve of it, nor I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand by and see the rules broke\xe2\x80\x94because right is right, and wrong is\r\nwrong, and a body ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no business doing wrong when he ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nignorant and knows better. It might answer for _you_ to dig Jim out\r\nwith a pick, _without_ any letting on, because you don\xe2\x80\x99t know no\r\nbetter; but it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t for me, because I do know better. Gimme a\r\ncase-knife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had his own by him, but I handed him mine. He flung it down, and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGimme a _case-knife._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know just what to do\xe2\x80\x94but then I thought. I scratched around\r\namongst the old tools, and got a pickax and give it to him, and he\r\ntook it and went to work, and never said a word.\r\n\r\nHe was always just that particular. Full of principle.\r\n\r\nSo then I got a shovel, and then we picked and shoveled, turn about,\r\nand made the fur fly. We stuck to it about a half an hour, which was\r\nas long as we could stand up; but we had a good deal of a hole to show\r\nfor it. When I got up-stairs I looked out at the window and see Tom\r\ndoing his level best with the lightning-rod, but he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t come it,\r\nhis hands was so sore. At last he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use, it can\xe2\x80\x99t be done. What you reckon I better do? Can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou think of no way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I reckon it ain\xe2\x80\x99t regular. Come up the stairs, and\r\nlet on it\xe2\x80\x99s a lightning-rod.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he done it.\r\n\r\nNext day Tom stole a pewter spoon and a brass candlestick in the\r\nhouse, for to make some pens for Jim out of, and six tallow candles;\r\nand I hung around the nigger cabins and laid for a chance, and stole\r\nthree tin plates. Tom says it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t enough; but I said nobody\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever see the plates that Jim throwed out, because they\xe2\x80\x99d fall\r\nin the dog-fennel and jimpson weeds under the window-hole\xe2\x80\x94then we\r\ncould tote them back and he could use them over again. So Tom was\r\nsatisfied. Then he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, the thing to study out is, how to get the things to Jim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake them in through the hole,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen we get it done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe only just looked scornful, and said something about nobody ever\r\nheard of such an idiotic idea, and then he went to studying. By and by\r\nhe said he had ciphered out two or three ways, but there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\nneed to decide on any of them yet. Said we\xe2\x80\x99d got to post Jim first.\r\n\r\nThat night we went down the lightning-rod a little after ten, and took\r\none of the candles along, and listened under the window-hole, and\r\nheard Jim snoring; so we pitched it in, and it didn\xe2\x80\x99t wake him. Then\r\nwe whirled in with the pick and shovel, and in about two hours and a\r\nhalf the job was done. We crept in under Jim\xe2\x80\x99s bed and into the cabin,\r\nand pawed around and found the candle and lit it, and stood over Jim\r\nawhile, and found him looking hearty and healthy, and then we woke him\r\nup gentle and gradual. He was so glad to see us he most cried; and\r\ncalled us honey, and all the pet names he could think of; and was for\r\nhaving us hunt up a cold-chisel to cut the chain off of his leg with\r\nright away, and clearing out without losing any time. But Tom he\r\nshowed him how unregular it would be, and set down and told him all\r\nabout our plans, and how we could alter them in a minute any time\r\nthere was an alarm; and not to be the least afraid, because we would\r\nsee he got away, _sure_. So Jim he said it was all right, and we set\r\nthere and talked over old times awhile, and then Tom asked a lot of\r\nquestions, and when Jim told him Uncle Silas come in every day or two\r\nto pray with him, and Aunt Sally come in to see if he was comfortable\r\nand had plenty to eat, and both of them was kind as they could be, Tom\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_ I know how to fix it. We\xe2\x80\x99ll send you some things by them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t do nothing of the kind; it\xe2\x80\x99s one of the most jackass\r\nideas I ever struck\xe2\x80\x9d; but he never paid no attention to me; went right\r\non. It was his way when he\xe2\x80\x99d got his plans set.\r\n\r\nSo he told Jim how we\xe2\x80\x99d have to smuggle in the rope-ladder pie and\r\nother large things by Nat, the nigger that fed him, and he must be on\r\nthe lookout, and not be surprised, and not let Nat see him open them;\r\nand we would put small things in uncle\xe2\x80\x99s coat pockets and he must\r\nsteal them out; and we would tie things to aunt\xe2\x80\x99s apron-strings or put\r\nthem in her apron pocket, if we got a chance; and told him what they\r\nwould be and what they was for. And told him how to keep a journal on\r\nthe shirt with his blood, and all that. He told him everything. Jim he\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t see no sense in the most of it, but he allowed we was white\r\nfolks and knowed better than him; so he was satisfied, and said he\r\nwould do it all just as Tom said.\r\n\r\nJim had plenty corn-cob pipes and tobacco; so we had a right down good\r\nsociable time; then we crawled out through the hole, and so home to\r\nbed, with hands that looked like they\xe2\x80\x99d been chawed. Tom was in high\r\nspirits. He said it was the best fun he ever had in his life, and the\r\nmost intellectural; and said if he only could see his way to it we\r\nwould keep it up all the rest of our lives and leave Jim to our\r\nchildren to get out; for he believed Jim would come to like it better\r\nand better the more he got used to it. He said that in that way it\r\ncould be strung out to as much as eighty year, and would be the best\r\ntime on record. And he said it would make us all celebrated that had a\r\nhand in it.\r\n\r\nIn the morning we went out to the woodpile and chopped up the brass\r\ncandlestick into handy sizes, and Tom put them and the pewter spoon in\r\nhis pocket. Then we went to the nigger cabins, and while I got Nat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnotice off, Tom shoved a piece of candlestick into the middle of a\r\ncorn-pone that was in Jim\xe2\x80\x99s pan, and we went along with Nat to see how\r\nit would work, and it just worked noble; when Jim bit into it it most\r\nmashed all his teeth out; and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t ever anything could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99\r\nworked better. Tom said so himself. Jim he never let on but what it\r\nwas only just a piece of rock or something like that that\xe2\x80\x99s always\r\ngetting into bread, you know; but after that he never bit into nothing\r\nbut what he jabbed his fork into it in three or four places first.\r\n\r\nAnd whilst we was a-standing there in the dimmish light, here comes a\r\ncouple of the hounds bulging in from under Jim\xe2\x80\x99s bed; and they kept on\r\npiling in till there was eleven of them, and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly room\r\nin there to get your breath. By jings, we forgot to fasten that\r\nlean-to door! The nigger Nat he only just hollered \xe2\x80\x9cWitches\xe2\x80\x9d once, and\r\nkeeled over onto the floor amongst the dogs, and begun to groan like\r\nhe was dying. Tom jerked the door open and flung out a slab of Jim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmeat, and the dogs went for it, and in two seconds he was out himself\r\nand back again and shut the door, and I knowed he\xe2\x80\x99d fixed the other\r\ndoor too. Then he went to work on the nigger, coaxing him and petting\r\nhim, and asking him if he\xe2\x80\x99d been imagining he saw something again. He\r\nraised up, and blinked his eyes around, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMars Sid, you\xe2\x80\x99ll say I\xe2\x80\x99s a fool, but if I didn\xe2\x80\x99t b\xe2\x80\x99lieve I see most a\r\nmillion dogs, er devils, er some\xe2\x80\x99n, I wisht I may die right heah in\r\ndese tracks. I did, mos\xe2\x80\x99 sholy. Mars Sid, I _felt_ um\xe2\x80\x94I _felt_ um,\r\nsah; dey was all over me. Dad fetch it, I jis\xe2\x80\x99 wisht I could git my\r\nhan\xe2\x80\x99s on one er dem witches jis\xe2\x80\x99 wunst\xe2\x80\x94on\xe2\x80\x99y jis\xe2\x80\x99 wunst\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s all _I_\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nast. But mos\xe2\x80\x99ly I wisht dey\xe2\x80\x99d lemme \xe2\x80\x99lone, I does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I tell you what _I_ think. What makes them come here just at\r\nthis runaway nigger\xe2\x80\x99s breakfast-time? It\xe2\x80\x99s because they\xe2\x80\x99re hungry;\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s the reason. You make them a witch pie; that\xe2\x80\x99s the thing for\r\n_you_ to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my lan\xe2\x80\x99, Mars Sid, how\xe2\x80\x99s I gwyne to make \xe2\x80\x99m a witch pie? I doan\xe2\x80\x99\r\nknow how to make it. I hain\xe2\x80\x99t ever hearn er sich a thing b\xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, I\xe2\x80\x99ll have to make it myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you do it, honey?\xe2\x80\x94will you? I\xe2\x80\x99ll wusshup de groun\xe2\x80\x99 und\xe2\x80\x99 yo\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfoot, I will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n[Illustration: TOM ADVISES A WITCH PIE]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it, seeing it\xe2\x80\x99s you, and you\xe2\x80\x99ve been good to us\r\nand showed us the runaway nigger. But you got to be mighty careful.\r\nWhen we come around, you turn your back; and then whatever we\xe2\x80\x99ve put\r\nin the pan, don\xe2\x80\x99t you let on you see it at all. And don\xe2\x80\x99t you look\r\nwhen Jim unloads the pan\xe2\x80\x94something might happen, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what.\r\nAnd above all, don\xe2\x80\x99t you _handle_ the witch-things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hannel_ \xe2\x80\x99m, Mars Sid? What _is_ you a-talkin\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99bout? I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 lay\r\nde weight er my finger on um, not f\xe2\x80\x99r ten hund\xe2\x80\x99d thous\xe2\x80\x99n billion\r\ndollars, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXVII\r\n\r\n\r\n_That_ was all fixed. So then we went away and went to the\r\nrubbage-pile in the back yard, where they keep the old boots, and\r\nrags, and pieces of bottles, and wore-out tin things, and all such\r\ntruck, and scratched around and found an old tin washpan, and stopped\r\nup the holes as well as we could, to bake the pie in, and took it down\r\ncellar and stole it full of flour and started for breakfast, and found\r\na couple of shingle-nails that Tom said would be handy for a prisoner\r\nto scrabble his name and sorrows on the dungeon walls with, and\r\ndropped one of them in Aunt Sally\xe2\x80\x99s apron pocket which was hanging on\r\na chair, and t\xe2\x80\x99other we stuck in the band of Uncle Silas\xe2\x80\x99s hat, which\r\nwas on the bureau, because we heard the children say their pa and ma\r\nwas going to the runaway nigger\xe2\x80\x99s house this morning, and then went to\r\nbreakfast, and Tom dropped the pewter spoon in Uncle Silas\xe2\x80\x99s coat\r\npocket, and Aunt Sally wasn\xe2\x80\x99t come yet, so we had to wait a little\r\nwhile.\r\n\r\nAnd when she come she was hot and red and cross, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly\r\nwait for the blessing; and then she went to sluicing out coffee with\r\none hand and cracking the handiest child\xe2\x80\x99s head with her thimble with\r\nthe other, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve hunted high and I\xe2\x80\x99ve hunted low, and it does beat all what _has_\r\nbecome of your other shirt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy heart fell down amongst my lungs and livers and things, and a hard\r\npiece of corn-crust started down my throat after it and got met on the\r\nroad with a cough, and was shot across the table, and took one of the\r\nchildren in the eye and curled him up like a fishing-worm, and let a\r\ncry out of him the size of a war-whoop, and Tom he turned kinder blue\r\naround the gills, and it all amounted to a considerable state of\r\nthings for about a quarter of a minute or as much as that, and I would\r\n\xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 sold out for half price if there was a bidder. But after that we\r\nwas all right again\xe2\x80\x94it was the sudden surprise of it that knocked us\r\nso kind of cold. Uncle Silas he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s most uncommon curious, I can\xe2\x80\x99t understand it. I know perfectly\r\nwell I took it _off_, because\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you hain\xe2\x80\x99t got but one _on_. Just _listen_ at the man! I know\r\nyou took it off, and know it by a better way than your wool-gethering\r\nmemory, too, because it was on the clo\xe2\x80\x99s-line yesterday\xe2\x80\x94I see it\r\nthere myself. But it\xe2\x80\x99s gone, that\xe2\x80\x99s the long and the short of it, and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll just have to change to a red flann\xe2\x80\x99l one till I can get time to\r\nmake a new one. And it \xe2\x80\x99ll be the third I\xe2\x80\x99ve made in two years. It\r\njust keeps a body on the jump to keep you in shirts; and whatever you\r\ndo manage to _do_ with \xe2\x80\x99m all is more\xe2\x80\x99n I can make out. A body\xe2\x80\x99d think\r\nyou _would_ learn to take some sort of care of \xe2\x80\x99em at your time of\r\nlife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it, Sally, and I do try all I can. But it oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to be\r\naltogether my fault, because, you know, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see them nor have\r\nnothing to do with them except when they\xe2\x80\x99re on me; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve ever lost one of them _off_ of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t _your_ fault if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t, Silas; you\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done it\r\nif you could, I reckon. And the shirt ain\xe2\x80\x99t all that\xe2\x80\x99s gone, nuther.\r\nTher\xe2\x80\x99s a spoon gone; and _that_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t all. There was ten, and now\r\nther\xe2\x80\x99s only nine. The calf got the shirt, I reckon, but the calf never\r\ntook the spoon, _that\xe2\x80\x99s_ certain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what else is gone, Sally?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTher\xe2\x80\x99s six _candles_ gone\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what. The rats could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got the\r\ncandles, and I reckon they did; I wonder they don\xe2\x80\x99t walk off with the\r\nwhole place, the way you\xe2\x80\x99re always going to stop their holes and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndo it; and if they warn\xe2\x80\x99t fools they\xe2\x80\x99d sleep in your hair,\r\nSilas\xe2\x80\x94_you\xe2\x80\x99d_ never find it out; but you can\xe2\x80\x99t lay the _spoon_ on the\r\nrats, and that I _know_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Sally, I\xe2\x80\x99m in fault, and I acknowledge it; I\xe2\x80\x99ve been remiss;\r\nbut I won\xe2\x80\x99t let to-morrow go by without stopping up them holes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hurry; next year \xe2\x80\x99ll do. Matilda Angelina Araminta\r\n_Phelps!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhack comes the thimble, and the child snatches her claws out of the\r\nsugar-bowl without fooling around any. Just then the nigger woman\r\nsteps onto the passage, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMissus, dey\xe2\x80\x99s a sheet gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA _sheet_ gone! Well, for the land\xe2\x80\x99s sake!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll stop up them holes to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d says Uncle Silas, looking sorrowful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _do_ shet up!\xe2\x80\x94s\xe2\x80\x99pose the rats took the _sheet?_ _Where\xe2\x80\x99s_ it\r\ngone, Lize?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClah to goodness I hain\xe2\x80\x99t no notion, Miss\xe2\x80\x99 Sally. She wuz on de\r\nclo\xe2\x80\x99s-line yistiddy, but she done gone: she ain\xe2\x80\x99 dah no mo\xe2\x80\x99 now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon the world _is_ coming to an end. I _never_ see the beat of\r\nit in all my born days. A shirt, and a sheet, and a spoon, and six\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMissus,\xe2\x80\x9d comes a young yaller wench, \xe2\x80\x9cdey\xe2\x80\x99s a brass cannelstick\r\nmiss\xe2\x80\x99n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCler out from here, you hussy, er I\xe2\x80\x99ll take a skillet to ye!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, she was just a-biling. I begun to lay for a chance; I reckoned I\r\nwould sneak out and go for the woods till the weather moderated. She\r\nkept a-raging right along, running her insurrection all by herself,\r\nand everybody else mighty meek and quiet; and at last Uncle Silas,\r\nlooking kind of foolish, fishes up that spoon out of his pocket. She\r\nstopped, with her mouth open and her hands up; and as for me, I wished\r\nI was in Jeruslem or somewheres. But not long, because she says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s _just_ as I expected. So you had it in your pocket all the time;\r\nand like as not you\xe2\x80\x99ve got the other things there, too. How\xe2\x80\x99d it get\r\nthere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reely don\xe2\x80\x99t know, Sally,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, kind of apologizing, \xe2\x80\x9cor you\r\nknow I would tell. I was a-studying over my text in Acts Seventeen\r\nbefore breakfast, and I reckon I put it in there, not noticing,\r\nmeaning to put my Testament in, and it must be so, because my\r\nTestament ain\xe2\x80\x99t in; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see; and if the Testament is where\r\nI had it, I\xe2\x80\x99ll know I didn\xe2\x80\x99t put it in, and that will show that I laid\r\nthe Testament down and took up the spoon, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for the land\xe2\x80\x99s sake! Give a body a rest! Go \xe2\x80\x99long now, the whole\r\nkit and biling of ye; and don\xe2\x80\x99t come nigh me again till I\xe2\x80\x99ve got back\r\nmy peace of mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_I\xe2\x80\x99d_ \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 heard her if she\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 said it to herself, let alone speaking\r\nit out; and I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got up and obeyed her if I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been dead. As we\r\nwas passing through the setting-room the old man he took up his hat,\r\nand the shingle-nail fell out on the floor, and he just merely picked\r\nit up and laid it on the mantel-shelf, and never said nothing, and\r\nwent out. Tom see him do it, and remembered about the spoon, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it ain\xe2\x80\x99t no use to send things by _him_ no more, he ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nreliable.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he says: \xe2\x80\x9cBut he done us a good turn with the spoon,\r\nanyway, without knowing it, and so we\xe2\x80\x99ll go and do him one without\r\n_him_ knowing it\xe2\x80\x94stop up his rat-holes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a noble good lot of them down cellar, and it took us a whole\r\nhour, but we done the job tight and good and ship-shape. Then we heard\r\nsteps on the stairs, and blowed out our light and hid; and here comes\r\nthe old man, with a candle in one hand and a bundle of stuff in\r\nt\xe2\x80\x99other, looking as absent-minded as year before last. He went\r\na-mooning around, first to one rat-hole and then another, till he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen to them all. Then he stood about five minutes, picking\r\ntallow-drip off of his candle and thinking. Then he turns off slow and\r\ndreamy towards the stairs, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, for the life of me I can\xe2\x80\x99t remember when I done it. I could\r\nshow her now that I warn\xe2\x80\x99t to blame on account of the rats. But never\r\nmind\xe2\x80\x94let it go. I reckon it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do no good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so he went on a-mumbling up-stairs, and then we left. He was a\r\nmighty nice old man. And always is.\r\n\r\nTom was a good deal bothered about what to do for a spoon, but he said\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99d got to have it; so he took a think. When he had ciphered it out\r\nhe told me how we was to do; then we went and waited around the\r\nspoon-basket till we see Aunt Sally coming, and then Tom went to\r\ncounting the spoons and laying them out to one side, and I slid one of\r\nthem up my sleeve, and Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Aunt Sally, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t but nine spoons _yet_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo \xe2\x80\x99long to your play, and don\xe2\x80\x99t bother me. I know better, I counted\r\n\xe2\x80\x99m myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve counted them twice, Aunty, and _I_ can\xe2\x80\x99t make but nine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked out of all patience, but of course she come to\r\ncount\xe2\x80\x94anybody would.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI declare to gracious ther\xe2\x80\x99 _ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ but nine!\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what\r\nin the world\xe2\x80\x94plague _take_ the things, I\xe2\x80\x99ll count \xe2\x80\x99m again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I slipped back the one I had, and when she got done counting, she\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHang the troublesome rubbage, ther\xe2\x80\x99s _ten_ now!\xe2\x80\x9d and she looked huffy\r\nand bothered both. But Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Aunty, _I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t think there\xe2\x80\x99s ten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou numskull, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you see me _count_ \xe2\x80\x99m?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll count \xe2\x80\x99m again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I smouched one, and they come out nine, same as the other time.\r\nWell, she _was_ in a tearing way\xe2\x80\x94just a-trembling all over, she was\r\nso mad. But she counted and counted till she got that addled she\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nstart to count in the _basket_ for a spoon sometimes; and so, three\r\ntimes they come out right, and three times they come out wrong. Then\r\nshe grabbed up the basket and slammed it across the house and knocked\r\nthe cat galley-west; and she said cler out and let her have some\r\npeace, and if we come bothering around her again betwixt that and\r\ndinner she\xe2\x80\x99d skin us. So we had the odd spoon, and dropped it in her\r\napron pocket whilst she was a-giving us our sailing orders, and Jim\r\ngot it all right, along with her shingle-nail, before noon. We was\r\nvery well satisfied with this business, and Tom allowed it was worth\r\ntwice the trouble it took, because he said _now_ she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\ncount them spoons twice alike again to save her life; and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbelieve she\xe2\x80\x99d counted them right if she _did_; and said that after\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99d about counted her head off for the next three days he judged\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99d give it up and offer to kill anybody that wanted her to ever\r\ncount them any more.\r\n\r\nSo we put the sheet back on the line that night, and stole one out of\r\nher closet; and kept on putting it back and stealing it again for a\r\ncouple of days till she didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how many sheets she had any more,\r\nand she didn\xe2\x80\x99t _care_, and warn\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to bullyrag the rest of her\r\nsoul out about it, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t count them again not to save her life;\r\nshe druther die first.\r\n\r\nSo we was all right now, as to the shirt and the sheet and the spoon\r\nand the candles, by the help of the calf and the rats and the mixed-up\r\ncounting; and as to the candlestick, it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no consequence, it\r\nwould blow over by and by.\r\n\r\nBut that pie was a job; we had no end of trouble with that pie. We\r\nfixed it up away down in the woods, and cooked it there; and we got it\r\ndone at last, and very satisfactory, too; but not all in one day; and\r\nwe had to use up three washpans full of flour before we got through,\r\nand we got burnt pretty much all over, in places, and eyes put out\r\nwith the smoke; because, you see, we didn\xe2\x80\x99t want nothing but a crust,\r\nand we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t prop it up right, and she would always cave in. But of\r\ncourse we thought of the right way at last\xe2\x80\x94which was to cook the\r\nladder, too, in the pie. So then we laid in with Jim the second night,\r\nand tore up the sheet all in little strings and twisted them together,\r\nand long before daylight we had a lovely rope that you could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 hung\r\na person with. We let on it took nine months to make it.\r\n\r\nAnd in the forenoon we took it down to the woods, but it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t go\r\ninto the pie. Being made of a whole sheet, that way, there was rope\r\nenough for forty pies if we\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 wanted them, and plenty left over\r\nfor soup, or sausage, or anything you choose. We could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 had a whole\r\ndinner.\r\n\r\nBut we didn\xe2\x80\x99t need it. All we needed was just enough for the pie, and\r\nso we throwed the rest away. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t cook none of the pies in the\r\nwashpan\xe2\x80\x94afraid the solder would melt; but Uncle Silas he had a noble\r\nbrass warming-pan which he thought considerable of, because it\r\nbelonged to one of his ancesters with a long wooden handle that come\r\nover from England with William the Conqueror in the _Mayflower_ or one\r\nof them early ships and was hid away up garret with a lot of other old\r\npots and things that was valuable, not on account of being any\r\naccount, because they warn\xe2\x80\x99t, but on account of them being relicts,\r\nyou know, and we snaked her out, private, and took her down there, but\r\nshe failed on the first pies, because we didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how, but she come\r\nup smiling on the last one. We took and lined her with dough, and set\r\nher in the coals, and loaded her up with rag rope, and put on a dough\r\nroof, and shut down the lid, and put hot embers on top, and stood off\r\nfive foot, with the long handle, cool and comfortable, and in fifteen\r\nminutes she turned out a pie that was a satisfaction to look at. But\r\nthe person that et it would want to fetch a couple of kags of\r\ntoothpicks along, for if that rope ladder wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t cramp him down to\r\nbusiness I don\xe2\x80\x99t know nothing what I\xe2\x80\x99m talking about, and lay him in\r\nenough stomach-ache to last him till next time, too.\r\n\r\nNat didn\xe2\x80\x99t look when we put the witch pie in Jim\xe2\x80\x99s pan; and we put\r\nthe three tin plates in the bottom of the pan under the vittles; and\r\nso Jim got everything all right, and as soon as he was by himself he\r\nbusted into the pie and hid the rope ladder inside of his straw tick,\r\nand scratched some marks on a tin plate and throwed it out of the\r\nwindow-hole.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXVIII\r\n\r\n\r\nMaking them pens was a distressid tough job, and so was the saw; and\r\nJim allowed the inscription was going to be the toughest of all.\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s the one which the prisoner has to scrabble on the wall. But he\r\nhad to have it; Tom said he\xe2\x80\x99d _got_ to; there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no case of a\r\nstate prisoner not scrabbling his inscription to leave behind, and his\r\ncoat of arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at Lady Jane Grey,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9clook at Gilford Dudley; look at\r\nold Northumberland! Why, Huck, s\xe2\x80\x99pose it _is_ considerble\r\ntrouble?\xe2\x80\x94what you going to do?\xe2\x80\x94how you going to get around it? Jim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_got_ to do his inscription and coat of arms. They all do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mars Tom, I hain\xe2\x80\x99t got no coat o\xe2\x80\x99 arm; I hain\xe2\x80\x99t got nuffn but\r\ndish yer ole shirt, en you knows I got to keep de journal on dat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t understand, Jim; a coat of arms is very different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cJim\xe2\x80\x99s right, anyway, when he says he ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no\r\ncoat of arms, because he hain\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI reckon _I_ knowed that,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom says, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you bet he\xe2\x80\x99ll have one before\r\nhe goes out of this\xe2\x80\x94because he\xe2\x80\x99s going out _right_, and there ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ngoing to be no flaws in his record.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo whilst me and Jim filed away at the pens on a brickbat apiece, Jim\r\na-making his\xe2\x80\x99n out of the brass and I making mine out of the spoon,\r\nTom set to work to think out the coat of arms. By and by he said he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nstruck so many good ones he didn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly know which to take, but\r\nthere was one which he reckoned he\xe2\x80\x99d decide on. He says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the scutcheon we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a bend _or_ in the dexter base, a saltire\r\n_murrey_ in the fess, with a dog, couchant, for common charge, and\r\nunder his foot a chain embattled, for slavery, with a chevron _vert_\r\nin a chief engrailed, and three invected lines on a field _azure_,\r\nwith the nombril points rampant on a dancette indented; crest, a\r\nrunaway nigger, _sable_, with his bundle over his shoulder on a bar\r\nsinister; and a couple of gules for supporters, which is you and me;\r\nmotto, _Maggiore fretta, minore atto_. Got it out of a book\xe2\x80\x94means the\r\nmore haste the less speed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeewhillikins,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cbut what does the rest of it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe ain\xe2\x80\x99t got no time to bother over that,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cwe got to dig in\r\nlike all git-out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s _some_ of it? What\xe2\x80\x99s a fess?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fess\xe2\x80\x94a fess is\xe2\x80\x94_you_ don\xe2\x80\x99t need to know what a fess is. I\xe2\x80\x99ll show\r\nhim how to make it when he gets to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9cI think you might tell a person. What\xe2\x80\x99s a bar\r\nsinister?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _I_ don\xe2\x80\x99t know. But he\xe2\x80\x99s got to have it. All the nobility does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was just his way. If it didn\xe2\x80\x99t suit him to explain a thing to\r\nyou, he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it. You might pump at him a week, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make\r\nno difference.\r\n\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99d got all that coat-of-arms business fixed, so now he started in to\r\nfinish up the rest of that part of the work, which was to plan out a\r\nmournful inscription\xe2\x80\x94said Jim got to have one, like they all done. He\r\nmade up a lot, and wrote them out on a paper, and read them off, so:\r\n\r\n  _1. Here a captive heart busted.\r\n\r\n   2. Here a poor prisoner, forsook by the world and\r\n   friends, fretted his sorrowful life.\r\n\r\n   3. Here a lonely heart broke, and a worn spirit went\r\n   to its rest, after thirty-seven years of solitary captivity.\r\n\r\n   4. Here, homeless and friendless, after thirty-seven\r\n   years of bitter captivity, perished a noble stranger,\r\n   natural son of Louis XIV._\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s voice trembled whilst he was reading them, and he most broke\r\ndown. When he got done he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t no way make up his mind which one\r\nfor Jim to scrabble onto the wall, they was all so good; but at last\r\nhe allowed he would let him scrabble them all on. Jim said it would\r\ntake him a year to scrabble such a lot of truck onto the logs with a\r\nnail, and he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how to make letters, besides; but Tom said he\r\nwould block them out for him, and then he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have nothing to do\r\nbut just follow the lines. Then pretty soon he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome to think, the logs ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to do; they don\xe2\x80\x99t have log\r\nwalls in a dungeon: we got to dig the inscriptions into a rock. We\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nfetch a rock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim said the rock was worse than the logs; he said it would take him\r\nsuch a pison long time to dig them into a rock he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever get\r\nout. But Tom said he would let me help him do it. Then he took a look\r\nto see how me and Jim was getting along with the pens. It was most\r\npesky tedious hard work and slow, and didn\xe2\x80\x99t give my hands no show to\r\nget well of the sores, and we didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to make no headway, hardly;\r\nso Tom says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know how to fix it. We got to have a rock for the coat of arms and\r\nmournful inscriptions, and we can kill two birds with that same rock.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s a gaudy big grindstone down at the mill, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll smouch it,\r\nand carve the things on it, and file out the pens and the saw on it,\r\ntoo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt warn\xe2\x80\x99t no slouch of an idea; and it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no slouch of a\r\ngrindstone nuther; but we allowed we\xe2\x80\x99d tackle it. It warn\xe2\x80\x99t quite\r\nmidnight yet, so we cleared out for the mill, leaving Jim at work. We\r\nsmouched the grindstone, and set out to roll her home, but it was a\r\nmost nation tough job. Sometimes, do what we could, we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep\r\nher from falling over, and she come mighty near mashing us every time.\r\nTom said she was going to get one of us, sure, before we got through.\r\nWe got her half-way; and then we was plumb played out, and most\r\ndrownded with sweat. We see it warn\xe2\x80\x99t no use; we got to go and fetch\r\nJim. So he raised up his bed and slid the chain off of the bed-leg,\r\nand wrapt it round and round his neck, and we crawled out through our\r\nhole and down there, and Jim and me laid into that grindstone and\r\nwalked her along like nothing; and Tom superintended. He could\r\nout-superintend any boy I ever see. He knowed how to do everything.\r\n\r\nOur hole was pretty big, but it warn\xe2\x80\x99t big enough to get the\r\ngrindstone through; but Jim he took the pick and soon made it big\r\nenough. Then Tom marked out them things on it with the nail, and set\r\nJim to work on them, with the nail for a chisel and an iron bolt from\r\nthe rubbage in the lean-to for a hammer, and told him to work till the\r\nrest of his candle quit on him, and then he could go to bed, and hide\r\nthe grindstone under his straw tick and sleep on it. Then we helped\r\nhim fix his chain back on the bed-leg, and was ready for bed\r\nourselves. But Tom thought of something, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou got any spiders in here, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sah, thanks to goodness I hain\xe2\x80\x99t, Mars Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, we\xe2\x80\x99ll get you some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut bless you, honey, I doan\xe2\x80\x99 _want_ none. I\xe2\x80\x99s afeard un um. I jis\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\xe2\x80\x99s soon have rattlesnakes aroun\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom thought a minute or two, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a good idea. And I reckon it\xe2\x80\x99s been done. It _must_ \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been\r\ndone; it stands to reason. Yes, it\xe2\x80\x99s a prime good idea. Where could\r\nyou keep it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep what, Mars Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, a rattlesnake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe goodness gracious alive, Mars Tom! Why, if dey was a rattlesnake\r\nto come in heah I\xe2\x80\x99d take en bust right out thoo dat log wall, I would,\r\nwid my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Jim, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid of it after a little. You could tame\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Tame_ it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94easy enough. Every animal is grateful for kindness and petting,\r\nand they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t _think_ of hurting a person that pets them. Any book\r\nwill tell you that. You try\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s all I ask; just try for two or\r\nthree days. Why, you can get him so in a little while that he\xe2\x80\x99ll love\r\nyou; and sleep with you; and won\xe2\x80\x99t stay away from you a minute; and\r\nwill let you wrap him round your neck and put his head in your mouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Please_, Tom\xe2\x80\x94_doan_\xe2\x80\x99 talk so! I can\xe2\x80\x99t _stan\xe2\x80\x99_ it! He\xe2\x80\x99d _let_ me\r\nshove his head in my mouf\xe2\x80\x94fer a favor, hain\xe2\x80\x99t it? I lay he\xe2\x80\x99d wait a\r\npow\xe2\x80\x99ful long time \xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99 I _ast_ him. En mo\xe2\x80\x99 en dat, I doan\xe2\x80\x99 _want_ him\r\nto sleep wid me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJim, don\xe2\x80\x99t act so foolish. A prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s _got_ to have some kind of a\r\ndumb pet, and if a rattlesnake hain\xe2\x80\x99t ever been tried, why, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmore glory to be gained in your being the first to ever try it than\r\nany other way you could ever think of to save your life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mars Tom, I doan\xe2\x80\x99 _want_ no sich glory. Snake take \xe2\x80\x99n bite Jim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchin off, den _whah_ is de glory? No, sah, I doan\xe2\x80\x99 want no sich\r\ndoin\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame it, can\xe2\x80\x99t you _try?_ I only _want_ you to try\xe2\x80\x94you needn\xe2\x80\x99t keep\r\nit up if it don\xe2\x80\x99t work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut de trouble all _done_ ef de snake bite me while I\xe2\x80\x99s a-tryin\xe2\x80\x99 him.\r\nMars Tom, I\xe2\x80\x99s willin\xe2\x80\x99 to tackle mos\xe2\x80\x99 anything \xe2\x80\x99at ain\xe2\x80\x99t onreasonable,\r\nbut ef you en Huck fetches a rattlesnake in heah for me to tame, I\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngwyne to _leave_, dat\xe2\x80\x99s _shore_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, let it go, let it go, if you\xe2\x80\x99re so bull-headed about it.\r\nWe can get you some garter-snakes, and you can tie some buttons on\r\ntheir tails, and let on they\xe2\x80\x99re rattlesnakes, and I reckon that \xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhave to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI k\xe2\x80\x99n stan\xe2\x80\x99 _dem_, Mars Tom, but blame\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99f I couldn\xe2\x80\x99 get along widout\r\num, I tell you dat. I never knowed b\xe2\x80\x99fo\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99twas so much bother and\r\ntrouble to be a prisoner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it _always_ is when it\xe2\x80\x99s done right. You got any rats around\r\nhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sah, I hain\xe2\x80\x99t seed none.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we\xe2\x80\x99ll get you some rats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mars Tom, I doan\xe2\x80\x99 _want_ no rats. Dey\xe2\x80\x99s de dadblamedest creturs\r\nto \xe2\x80\x99sturb a body, en rustle roun\xe2\x80\x99 over \xe2\x80\x99im, en bite his feet, when\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s tryin\xe2\x80\x99 to sleep, I ever see. No, sah, gimme g\xe2\x80\x99yarter-snakes, \xe2\x80\x99f\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99s got to have \xe2\x80\x99m, but doan\xe2\x80\x99 gimme no rats; I hain\xe2\x80\x99 got no use f\xe2\x80\x99r\r\num, skasely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Jim, you _got_ to have \xe2\x80\x99em\xe2\x80\x94they all do. So don\xe2\x80\x99t make no more\r\nfuss about it. Prisoners ain\xe2\x80\x99t ever without rats. There ain\xe2\x80\x99t no\r\ninstance of it. And they train them, and pet them, and learn them\r\ntricks, and they get to be as sociable as flies. But you got to play\r\nmusic to them. You got anything to play music on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ain\xe2\x80\x99 got nuffn but a coase comb en a piece o\xe2\x80\x99 paper, en a\r\njuice-harp; but I reck\xe2\x80\x99n dey wouldn\xe2\x80\x99 take no stock in a juice-harp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes they would. _They_ don\xe2\x80\x99t care what kind of music \xe2\x80\x99tis. A\r\njews-harp\xe2\x80\x99s plenty good enough for a rat. All animals like music\xe2\x80\x94in a\r\nprison they dote on it. Specially, painful music; and you can\xe2\x80\x99t get no\r\nother kind out of a jew\xe2\x80\x99s-harp. It always interests them; they come\r\nout to see what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you. Yes, you\xe2\x80\x99re all right; you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfixed very well. You want to set on your bed nights before you go to\r\nsleep, and early in the mornings, and play your jew\xe2\x80\x99s-harp; play \xe2\x80\x99The\r\nLast Link is Broken\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the thing that \xe2\x80\x99ll scoop a rat quicker \xe2\x80\x99n\r\nanything else; and when you\xe2\x80\x99ve played about two minutes you\xe2\x80\x99ll see all\r\nthe rats, and the snakes, and spiders and things begin to feel worried\r\nabout you, and come. Blest if I kin see de pint. But I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it ef I got to. I\r\nreck\xe2\x80\x99n I better keep de animals satisfied, en not have no trouble in\r\nde house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom waited to think it over, and see if there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing else; and\r\npretty soon he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, there\xe2\x80\x99s one thing I forgot. Some other prisoners has done it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne er dem big cat-tail-lookin\xe2\x80\x99 mullen-stalks would grow in heah,\r\nMars Tom, I reck\xe2\x80\x99n, but she wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be wuth half de trouble she\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ncoss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you believe it. And don\xe2\x80\x99t call it mullen, call\r\nit Pitchiola\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s its right name when it\xe2\x80\x99s in a prison. And you\r\nwant to water it with your tears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I got plenty spring water, Mars Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t _want_ spring water; you want to water it with your tears.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s the way they always do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mars Tom, I lay I kin raise one er dem mullen-stalks twyste wid\r\nspring water whiles another man\xe2\x80\x99s a _start\xe2\x80\x99n_ one wid tears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t the idea.
