But he studied it over, and then said Jim would\r\nhave to worry along the best he could with an onion. He promised he\r\nwould go to the nigger cabins and drop one, private, in Jim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncoffee-pot, in the morning. Jim said he would \xe2\x80\x9cjis\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99s soon have\r\ntobacker in his coffee\xe2\x80\x9d; and found so much fault with it, and with the\r\nwork and bother of raising the mullen, and jew\xe2\x80\x99s-harping the rats, and\r\npetting and flattering up the snakes and spiders and things, on top of\r\nall the other work he had to do on pens, and inscriptions, and\r\njournals, and things, which made it more trouble and worry and\r\nresponsibility to be a prisoner than anything he ever undertook, that\r\nTom most lost all patience with him; and said he was just loadened\r\ndown with more gaudier chances than a prisoner ever had in the world\r\nto make a name for himself, and yet he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know enough to\r\nappreciate them, and they was just about wasted on him. So Jim he was\r\nsorry, and said he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t behave so no more, and then me and Tom\r\nshoved for bed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXIX\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the morning we went up to the village and bought a wire rat-trap\r\nand fetched it down, and unstopped the best rat-hole, and in about an\r\nhour we had fifteen of the bulliest kind of ones; and then we took it\r\nand put it in a safe place under Aunt Sally\xe2\x80\x99s bed. But while we was\r\ngone for spiders little Thomas Franklin Benjamin Jefferson Elexander\r\nPhelps found it there, and opened the door of it to see if the rats\r\nwould come out, and they did; and Aunt Sally she come in, and when we\r\ngot back she was a-standing on top of the bed raising Cain, and the\r\nrats was doing what they could to keep off the dull times for her. So\r\nshe took and dusted us both with the hickry, and we was as much as two\r\nhours catching another fifteen or sixteen, drat that meddlesome cub,\r\nand they warn\xe2\x80\x99t the likeliest, nuther, because the first haul was the\r\npick of the flock. I never see a likelier lot of rats than what that\r\nfirst haul was.\r\n\r\nWe got a splendid stock of sorted spiders, and bugs, and frogs, and\r\ncaterpillars, and one thing or another; and we like to got a hornet\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnest, but we didn\xe2\x80\x99t. The family was at home. We didn\xe2\x80\x99t give it right\r\nup, but stayed with them as long as we could; because we allowed we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\ntire them out or they\xe2\x80\x99d got to tire us out, and they done it. Then we\r\ngot allycumpain and rubbed on the places, and was pretty near all\r\nright again, but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t set down convenient. And so we went for the\r\nsnakes, and grabbed a couple of dozen garters and house-snakes, and\r\nput them in a bag, and put it in our room, and by that time it was\r\nsupper-time, and a rattling good honest day\xe2\x80\x99s work: and hungry?\xe2\x80\x94oh,\r\nno, I reckon not! And there warn\xe2\x80\x99t a blessed snake up there when we\r\nwent back\xe2\x80\x94we didn\xe2\x80\x99t half tie the sack, and they worked out somehow,\r\nand left. But it didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter much, because they was still on the\r\npremises somewheres. So we judged we could get some of them again. No,\r\nthere warn\xe2\x80\x99t no real scarcity of snakes about the house for a\r\nconsiderable spell. You\xe2\x80\x99d see them dripping from the rafters and\r\nplaces every now and then; and they generly landed in your plate, or\r\ndown the back of your neck, and most of the time where you didn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nthem. Well, they was handsome and striped, and there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no harm in\r\na million of them; but that never made no difference to Aunt Sally;\r\nshe despised snakes, be the breed what they might, and she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand them no way you could fix it; and every time one of them flopped\r\ndown on her, it didn\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference what she was doing, she\r\nwould just lay that work down and light out. I never see such a woman.\r\nAnd you could hear her whoop to Jericho. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get her to take\r\na-holt of one of them with the tongs. And if she turned over and found\r\none in bed she would scramble out and lift a howl that you would think\r\nthe house was afire. She disturbed the old man so that he said he\r\ncould most wish there hadn\xe2\x80\x99t ever been no snakes created. Why, after\r\nevery last snake had been gone clear out of the house for as much as a\r\nweek Aunt Sally warn\xe2\x80\x99t over it yet; she warn\xe2\x80\x99t near over it; when she\r\nwas setting thinking about something you could touch her on the back\r\nof her neck with a feather and she would jump right out of her\r\nstockings. It was very curious. But Tom said all women was just so. He\r\nsaid they was made that way for some reason or other.\r\n\r\nWe got a licking every time one of our snakes come in her way, and she\r\nallowed these lickings warn\xe2\x80\x99t nothing to what she would do if we ever\r\nloaded up the place again with them. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mind the lickings,\r\nbecause they didn\xe2\x80\x99t amount to nothing; but I minded the trouble we had\r\nto lay in another lot. But we got them laid in, and all the other\r\nthings; and you never see a cabin as blithesome as Jim\xe2\x80\x99s was when\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d all swarm out for music and go for him. Jim didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the\r\nspiders, and the spiders didn\xe2\x80\x99t like Jim; and so they\xe2\x80\x99d lay for him,\r\nand make it mighty warm for him. And he said that between the rats and\r\nthe snakes and the grindstone there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no room in bed for him,\r\nskasely; and when there was, a body couldn\xe2\x80\x99t sleep, it was so lively,\r\nand it was always lively, he said, because _they_ never all slept at\r\none time, but took turn about, so when the snakes was asleep the rats\r\nwas on deck, and when the rats turned in the snakes come on watch, so\r\nhe always had one gang under him, in his way, and t\xe2\x80\x99other gang having\r\na circus over him, and if he got up to hunt a new place the spiders\r\nwould take a chance at him as he crossed over. He said if he ever got\r\nout this time he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever be a prisoner again, not for a salary.\r\n\r\nWell, by the end of three weeks everything was in pretty good shape.\r\nThe shirt was sent in early, in a pie, and every time a rat bit Jim he\r\nwould get up and write a line in his journal whilst the ink was fresh;\r\nthe pens was made, the inscriptions and so on was all carved on the\r\ngrindstone; the bed-leg was sawed in two, and we had et up the\r\nsawdust, and it give us a most amazing stomach-ache. We reckoned we\r\nwas all going to die, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t. It was the most undigestible sawdust\r\nI ever see; and Tom said the same. But as I was saying, we\xe2\x80\x99d got all\r\nthe work done now, at last; and we was all pretty much fagged out,\r\ntoo, but mainly Jim. The old man had wrote a couple of times to the\r\nplantation below Orleans to come and get their runaway nigger, but\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t got no answer, because there warn\xe2\x80\x99t no such plantation; so he\r\nallowed he would advertise Jim in the St. Louis and New Orleans\r\npapers; and when he mentioned the St. Louis ones it give me the cold\r\nshivers, and I see we hadn\xe2\x80\x99t no time to lose. So Tom said, now for the\r\nnonnamous letters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s them?\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWarnings to the people that something is up. Sometimes it\xe2\x80\x99s done one\r\nway, sometimes another. But there\xe2\x80\x99s always somebody spying around that\r\ngives notice to the governor of the castle. When Louis XVI. was going\r\nto light out of the Tooleries a servant-girl done it. It\xe2\x80\x99s a very good\r\nway, and so is the nonnamous letters. We\xe2\x80\x99ll use them both. And it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nusual for the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s mother to change clothes with him, and she\r\nstays in, and he slides out in her clothes. We\xe2\x80\x99ll do that, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut looky here, Tom, what do we want to _warn_ anybody for that\r\nsomething\xe2\x80\x99s up? Let them find it out for themselves\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s their\r\nlookout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know; but you can\xe2\x80\x99t depend on them. It\xe2\x80\x99s the way they\xe2\x80\x99ve acted\r\nfrom the very start\xe2\x80\x94left us to do _everything_. They\xe2\x80\x99re so confiding\r\nand mullet-headed they don\xe2\x80\x99t take notice of nothing at all. So if we\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t _give_ them notice there won\xe2\x80\x99t be nobody nor nothing to\r\ninterfere with us, and so after all our hard work and trouble this\r\nescape \xe2\x80\x99ll go off perfectly flat; won\xe2\x80\x99t amount to nothing\xe2\x80\x94won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nnothing _to_ it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as for me, Tom, that\xe2\x80\x99s the way I\xe2\x80\x99d like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShucks!\xe2\x80\x9d he says, and looked disgusted. So I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to make no complaint. Any way that suits you suits\r\nme. What you going to do about the servant-girl?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be her. You slide in, in the middle of the night, and hook\r\nthat yaller girl\xe2\x80\x99s frock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Tom, that \xe2\x80\x99ll make trouble next morning; because, of course, she\r\nprob\xe2\x80\x99bly hain\xe2\x80\x99t got any but that one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know; but you don\xe2\x80\x99t want it but fifteen minutes, to carry the\r\nnonnamous letter and shove it under the front door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, then, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do it; but I could carry it just as handy in my\r\nown togs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t look like a servant-girl _then_, would you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but there won\xe2\x80\x99t be nobody to see what I look like, _anyway_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ain\xe2\x80\x99t got nothing to do with it. The thing for us to do is just\r\nto do our _duty_, and not worry about whether anybody _sees_ us do it\r\nor not. Hain\xe2\x80\x99t you got no principle at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t saying nothing; I\xe2\x80\x99m the servant-girl. Who\xe2\x80\x99s Jim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m his mother. I\xe2\x80\x99ll hook a gown from Aunt Sally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, you\xe2\x80\x99ll have to stay in the cabin when me and Jim leaves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much. I\xe2\x80\x99ll stuff Jim\xe2\x80\x99s clothes full of straw and lay it on his\r\nbed to represent his mother in disguise, and Jim \xe2\x80\x99ll take the nigger\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x99s gown off of me and wear it, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll all evade together. When\r\na prisoner of style escapes it\xe2\x80\x99s called an evasion. It\xe2\x80\x99s always called\r\nso when a king escapes, f\xe2\x80\x99rinstance. And the same with a king\xe2\x80\x99s son;\r\nit don\xe2\x80\x99t make no difference whether he\xe2\x80\x99s a natural one or an unnatural\r\none.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo Tom he wrote the nonnamous letter, and I smouched the yaller\r\nwench\xe2\x80\x99s frock that night, and put it on, and shoved it under the front\r\ndoor, the way Tom told me to. It said:\r\n\r\n   _Beware. Trouble is brewing. Keep a sharp lookout.\r\n      UNKNOWN FRIEND._\r\n\r\nNext night we stuck a picture, which Tom drawed in blood, of a skull\r\nand crossbones on the front door; and next night another one of a\r\ncoffin on the back door. I never see a family in such a sweat. They\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been worse scared if the place had \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 been full of\r\nghosts laying for them behind everything and under the beds and\r\nshivering through the air. If a door banged, Aunt Sally she jumped and\r\nsaid \xe2\x80\x9couch!\xe2\x80\x9d if anything fell, she jumped and said \xe2\x80\x9couch!\xe2\x80\x9d if you\r\nhappened to touch her, when she warn\xe2\x80\x99t noticing, she done the same;\r\nshe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t face no way and be satisfied, because she allowed there\r\nwas something behind her every time\xe2\x80\x94so she was always a-whirling\r\naround sudden, and saying \xe2\x80\x9couch,\xe2\x80\x9d and before she\xe2\x80\x99d got two-thirds\r\naround she\xe2\x80\x99d whirl back again, and say it again; and she was afraid to\r\ngo to bed, but she dasn\xe2\x80\x99t set up. So the thing was working very well,\r\nTom said; he said he never see a thing work more satisfactory. He said\r\nit showed it was done right.\r\n\r\nSo he said, now for the grand bulge! So the very next morning at the\r\nstreak of dawn we got another letter ready, and was wondering what we\r\nbetter do with it, because we heard them say at supper they was going\r\nto have a nigger on watch at both doors all night. Tom he went down\r\nthe lightning-rod to spy around; and the nigger at the back door was\r\nasleep, and he stuck it in the back of his neck and come back. This\r\nletter said:\r\n\r\n_Don\xe2\x80\x99t betray me, I wish to be your friend. There is a desprate gang\r\nof cutthroats from over in the Indian Territory going to steal your\r\nrunaway nigger to-night, and they have been trying to scare you so as\r\nyou will stay in the house and not bother them. I am one of the gang,\r\nbut have got religgion and wish to quit it and lead an honest life\r\nagain, and will betray the helish design. They will sneak down from\r\nnorthards, along the fence, at midnight exact, with a false key, and\r\ngo in the nigger\xe2\x80\x99s cabin to get him. I am to be off a piece and blow a\r\ntin horn if I see any danger; but stead of that I will BA like a sheep\r\nsoon as they get in and not blow at all; then whilst they are getting\r\nhis chains loose, you slip there and lock them in, and can kill them\r\nat your leasure. Don\xe2\x80\x99t do anything but just the way I am telling you;\r\nif you do they will suspicion something and raise whoop-jamboreehoo. I\r\ndo not wish any reward but to know I have done the right thing._\r\n\r\n_UNKNOWN FRIEND._\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XL\r\n\r\n\r\nWe was feeling pretty good after breakfast, and took my canoe and went\r\nover the river a-fishing, with a lunch, and had a good time, and took\r\na look at the raft and found her all right, and got home late to\r\nsupper, and found them in such a sweat and worry they didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhich end they was standing on, and made us go right off to bed the\r\nminute we was done supper, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell us what the trouble was,\r\nand never let on a word about the new letter, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t need to,\r\nbecause we knowed as much about it as anybody did, and as soon as we\r\nwas half up-stairs and her back was turned we slid for the cellar\r\ncubboard and loaded up a good lunch and took it up to our room and\r\nwent to bed, and got up about half past eleven, and Tom put on Aunt\r\nSally\xe2\x80\x99s dress that he stole and was going to start with the lunch, but\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s the butter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI laid out a hunk of it,\xe2\x80\x9d I says, \xe2\x80\x9con a piece of a corn-pone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you _left_ it laid out, then\xe2\x80\x94it ain\xe2\x80\x99t here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can get along without it,\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can get along _with_ it, too,\xe2\x80\x9d he says; \xe2\x80\x9cjust you slide down\r\ncellar and fetch it. And then mosey right down the lightning-rod and\r\ncome along. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and stuff the straw into Jim\xe2\x80\x99s clothes to\r\nrepresent his mother in disguise, and be ready to _ba_ like a sheep\r\nand shove soon as you get there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo out he went, and down cellar went I. The hunk of butter, big as a\r\nperson\xe2\x80\x99s fist, was where I had left it, so I took up the slab of\r\ncorn-pone with it on, and blowed out my light, and started up-stairs\r\nvery stealthy, and got up to the main floor all right, but here comes\r\nAunt Sally with a candle, and I clapped the truck in my hat, and\r\nclapped my hat on my head, and the next second she see me; and she\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou been down cellar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you been doing down there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNoth\xe2\x80\x99n.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Noth\xe2\x80\x99n!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, what possessed you to go down there this time of night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know \xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t _know?_ Don\xe2\x80\x99t answer me that way. Tom, I want to know what\r\nyou been _doing_ down there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hain\xe2\x80\x99t been doing a single thing, Aunt Sally, I hope to gracious if\r\nI have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI reckoned she\xe2\x80\x99d let me go now, and as a generl thing she would; but I\r\ns\xe2\x80\x99pose there was so many strange things going on she was just in a\r\nsweat about every little thing that warn\xe2\x80\x99t yard-stick straight; so she\r\nsays, very decided:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou just march into that setting-room and stay there till I come. You\r\nbeen up to something you no business to, and I lay I\xe2\x80\x99ll find out what\r\nit is before _I\xe2\x80\x99m_ done with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo she went away as I opened the door and walked into the\r\nsetting-room. My, but there was a crowd there! Fifteen farmers, and\r\nevery one of them had a gun. I was most powerful sick, and slunk to a\r\nchair and set down. They was setting around, some of them talking a\r\nlittle, in a low voice, and all of them fidgety and uneasy, but trying\r\nto look like they warn\xe2\x80\x99t; but I knowed they was, because they was\r\nalways taking off their hats, and putting them on, and scratching\r\ntheir heads, and changing their seats, and fumbling with their\r\nbuttons. I warn\xe2\x80\x99t easy myself, but I didn\xe2\x80\x99t take my hat off, all the\r\nsame.\r\n\r\nI did wish Aunt Sally would come, and get done with me, and lick me,\r\nif she wanted to, and let me get away and tell Tom how we\xe2\x80\x99d overdone\r\nthis thing, and what a thundering hornet\xe2\x80\x99s nest we\xe2\x80\x99d got ourselves\r\ninto, so we could stop fooling around straight off, and clear out with\r\nJim before these rips got out of patience and come for us.\r\n\r\nAt last she come and begun to ask me questions, but I _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\nanswer them straight, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know which end of me was up; because\r\nthese men was in such a fidget now that some was wanting to start\r\nright _now_ and lay for them desperadoes, and saying it warn\xe2\x80\x99t but a\r\nfew minutes to midnight; and others was trying to get them to hold on\r\nand wait for the sheep-signal; and here was Aunty pegging away at the\r\nquestions, and me a-shaking all over and ready to sink down in my\r\ntracks I was that scared; and the place getting hotter and hotter, and\r\nthe butter beginning to melt and run down my neck and behind my ears;\r\nand pretty soon, when one of them says, \xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99m_ for going and getting\r\nin the cabin _first_ and right _now_, and catching them when they\r\ncome,\xe2\x80\x9d I most dropped; and a streak of butter come a-trickling down my\r\nforehead, and Aunt Sally she see it, and turns white as a sheet, and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the land\xe2\x80\x99s sake, what _is_ the matter with the child? He\xe2\x80\x99s got\r\nthe brain-fever as shore as you\xe2\x80\x99re born, and they\xe2\x80\x99re oozing out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd everybody runs to see, and she snatches off my hat, and out comes\r\nthe bread and what was left of the butter, and she grabbed me, and\r\nhugged me, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what a turn you did give me! and how glad and grateful I am it\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t no worse; for luck\xe2\x80\x99s against us, and it never rains but it\r\npours, and when I see that truck I thought we\xe2\x80\x99d lost you, for I knowed\r\nby the color and all it was just like your brains would be if\xe2\x80\x94Dear,\r\ndear, whyd\xe2\x80\x99nt you _tell_ me that was what you\xe2\x80\x99d been down there for,\r\n_I_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 cared. Now cler out to bed, and don\xe2\x80\x99t lemme see no\r\nmore of you till morning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was up-stairs in a second, and down the lightning-rod in another\r\none, and shinning through the dark for the lean-to. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t hardly\r\nget my words out, I was so anxious; but I told Tom as quick as I could\r\nwe must jump for it now, and not a minute to lose\xe2\x80\x94the house full of\r\nmen, yonder, with guns!\r\n\r\nHis eyes just blazed; and he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x94is that so? _Ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ it bully! Why, Huck, if it was to do over\r\nagain, I bet I could fetch two hundred! If we could put it off till\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurry! _hurry!_\xe2\x80\x9d I says. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight at your elbow; if you reach out your arm you can touch him.\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99s dressed, and everything\xe2\x80\x99s ready. Now we\xe2\x80\x99ll slide out and give the\r\nsheep-signal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut then we heard the tramp of men coming to the door, and heard them\r\nbegin to fumble with the padlock, and heard a man say:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _told_ you we\xe2\x80\x99d be too soon; they haven\xe2\x80\x99t come\xe2\x80\x94the door is locked.\r\nHere, I\xe2\x80\x99ll lock some of you into the cabin, and you lay for \xe2\x80\x99em in the\r\ndark and kill \xe2\x80\x99em when they come; and the rest scatter around a piece,\r\nand listen if you can hear \xe2\x80\x99em coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo in they come, but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see us in the dark, and most trod on us\r\nwhilst we was hustling to get under the bed. But we got under all\r\nright, and out through the hole, swift but soft\xe2\x80\x94Jim first, me next,\r\nand Tom last, which was according to Tom\xe2\x80\x99s orders. Now we was in the\r\nlean-to, and heard trampings close by outside. So we crept to the\r\ndoor, and Tom stopped us there and put his eye to the crack, but\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out nothing, it was so dark; and whispered and said he\r\nwould listen for the steps to get further, and when he nudged us Jim\r\nmust glide out first, and him last. So he set his ear to the crack and\r\nlistened, and listened, and listened, and the steps a-scraping around\r\nout there all the time; and at last he nudged us, and we slid out, and\r\nstooped down, not breathing, and not making the least noise, and\r\nslipped stealthy towards the fence in Injun file, and got to it all\r\nright, and me and Jim over it; but Tom\xe2\x80\x99s britches catched fast on a\r\nsplinter on the top rail, and then he hear the steps coming, so he had\r\nto pull loose, which snapped the splinter and made a noise; and as he\r\ndropped in our tracks and started somebody sings out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that? Answer, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll shoot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut we didn\xe2\x80\x99t answer; we just unfurled our heels and shoved. Then\r\nthere was a rush, and a _bang_, _bang_, _bang!_ and the bullets fairly\r\nwhizzed around us! We heard them sing out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere they are! They\xe2\x80\x99ve broke for the river! After \xe2\x80\x99em, boys, and turn\r\nloose the dogs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo here they come, full tilt. We could hear them because they wore\r\nboots and yelled, but we didn\xe2\x80\x99t wear no boots and didn\xe2\x80\x99t yell. We was\r\nin the path to the mill; and when they got pretty close onto us we\r\ndodged into the bush and let them go by, and then dropped in behind\r\nthem. They\xe2\x80\x99d had all the dogs shut up, so they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t scare off the\r\nrobbers; but by this time somebody had let them loose, and here they\r\ncome, making powwow enough for a million; but they was our dogs; so we\r\nstopped in our tracks till they catched up; and when they see it\r\nwarn\xe2\x80\x99t nobody but us, and no excitement to offer them, they only just\r\nsaid howdy, and tore right ahead towards the shouting and clattering;\r\nand then we up-steam again, and whizzed along after them till we was\r\nnearly to the mill, and then struck up through the bush to where my\r\ncanoe was tied, and hopped in and pulled for dear life towards the\r\nmiddle of the river, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t make no more noise than we was\r\nobleeged to. Then we struck out, easy and comfortable, for the island\r\nwhere my raft was; and we could hear them yelling and barking at each\r\nother all up and down the bank, till we was so far away the sounds got\r\ndim and died out. And when we stepped onto the raft I says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_, old Jim, you\xe2\x80\x99re a free man _again_, and I bet you won\xe2\x80\x99t ever be\r\na slave no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEn a mighty good job it wuz, too, Huck. It \xe2\x80\x99uz planned beautiful, en\r\nit \xe2\x80\x99uz _done_ beautiful; en dey ain\xe2\x80\x99t _nobody_ kin git up a plan dat\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmo\xe2\x80\x99 mixed up en splendid den what dat one wuz.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe was all glad as we could be, but Tom was the gladdest of all\r\nbecause he had a bullet in the calf of his leg.\r\n\r\nWhen me and Jim heard that we didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel as brash as what we did\r\nbefore. It was hurting him considerable, and bleeding; so we laid him\r\nin the wigwam and tore up one of the duke\xe2\x80\x99s shirts for to bandage him,\r\nbut he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGimme the rags; I can do it myself. Don\xe2\x80\x99t stop now; don\xe2\x80\x99t fool around\r\nhere, and the evasion booming along so handsome; man the sweeps, and\r\nset her loose! Shut the door and blindfold the doctor tight\r\nand fast, and make him swear to be silent as the grave, and put a\r\npurse full of gold in his hand, and then take and lead him all around\r\nthe back alleys and everywheres in the dark, and then fetch him here\r\nin the canoe, in a roundabout way amongst the islands, and search him\r\nand take his chalk away from him, and don\xe2\x80\x99t give it back to him till\r\nyou get him back to the village, or else he will chalk this raft so he\r\ncan find it again. It\xe2\x80\x99s the way they all do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I said I would, and left, and Jim was to hide in the woods when he\r\nsee the doctor coming till he was gone again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XLI\r\n\r\n\r\nThe doctor was an old man; a very nice, kind-looking old man when I\r\ngot him up. I told him me and my brother was over on Spanish Island\r\nhunting yesterday afternoon, and camped on a piece of a raft we found,\r\nand about midnight he must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 kicked his gun in his dreams, for it\r\nwent off and shot him in the leg, and we wanted him to go over there\r\nand fix it and not say nothing about it, nor let anybody know, because\r\nwe wanted to come home this evening and surprise the folks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is your folks?\xe2\x80\x9d he says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Phelpses, down yonder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. It\r\nlooks foolish enough _now_, in the daytime; but I says to myself,\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s my two poor boys asleep, \xe2\x80\x99way up-stairs in that lonesome room,\r\nand I declare to goodness I was that uneasy \xe2\x80\x99t I crep\xe2\x80\x99 up there and\r\nlocked \xe2\x80\x99em in! Because, you know, when you\r\nget scared that way, and it keeps running on, and getting worse and\r\nworse all the time, and your wits gets to addling, and you get to\r\ndoing all sorts o\xe2\x80\x99 wild things, and by and by you think to yourself,\r\nspos\xe2\x80\x99n _I_ was a boy, and was away up there, and the door ain\xe2\x80\x99t locked,\r\nand you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She stopped, looking kind of wondering, and then she turned\r\nher head around slow, and when her eye lit on me\xe2\x80\x94I got up and took a\r\nwalk.\r\n\r\nSays I to myself, I can explain better how we come to not be in that\r\nroom this morning if I go out to one side and study over it a little.\r\nSo I done it. But I dasn\xe2\x80\x99t go fur, or she\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 sent for me. And when\r\nit was late in the day the people all went, and then I come in and\r\ntold her the noise and shooting waked up me and \xe2\x80\x9cSid,\xe2\x80\x9d and the door\r\nwas locked, and we wanted to see the fun, so we went down the\r\nlightning-rod, and both of us got hurt a little, and we didn\xe2\x80\x99t never\r\nwant to try _that_ no more. And then I went on and told her all what I\r\ntold Uncle Silas before; and then she said she\xe2\x80\x99d forgive us, and maybe\r\nit was all right enough anyway, and about what a body might expect of\r\nboys, for all boys was a pretty harum-scarum lot as fur as she could\r\nsee; and so, as long as no harm hadn\xe2\x80\x99t come of it, she judged she\r\nbetter put in her time being grateful we was alive and well and she\r\nhad us still, stead of fretting over what was past and done. So then\r\nshe kissed me, and patted me on the head, and dropped into a kind of a\r\nbrown-study; and pretty soon jumps up, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, lawsamercy, it\xe2\x80\x99s most night, and Sid not come yet! What _has_\r\nbecome of that boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI see my chance; so I skips up and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll run right up to town and get him,\xe2\x80\x9d I says.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo you won\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she says. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll stay right wher\xe2\x80\x99 you are; _one\xe2\x80\x99s_\r\nenough to be lost at a time. If he ain\xe2\x80\x99t here to supper, your uncle\r\n\xe2\x80\x99ll go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWell, he warn\xe2\x80\x99t there to supper; so right after supper uncle went.\r\n\r\nHe come back about ten a little bit uneasy; hadn\xe2\x80\x99t run across Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntrack. Aunt Sally was a good _deal_ uneasy; but Uncle Silas he said\r\nthere warn\xe2\x80\x99t no occasion to be\xe2\x80\x94boys will be boys, he said, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nsee this one turn up in the morning all sound and right. So she had to\r\nbe satisfied. But she said she\xe2\x80\x99d set up for him awhile anyway, and\r\nkeep a light burning so he could see it.\r\n\r\nAnd then when I went up to bed she come up with me and fetched her\r\ncandle, and tucked me in, and mothered me so good I felt mean, and\r\nlike I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t look her in the face; and she set down on the bed and\r\ntalked with me a long time, and said what a splendid boy Sid was, and\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to want to ever stop talking about him; and kept asking me\r\nevery now and then if I reckoned he could \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 got lost, or hurt, or\r\nmaybe drownded, and might be laying at this minute somewheres\r\nsuffering or dead, and she not by him to help him, and so the tears\r\nwould drip down silent, and I would tell her that Sid was all right,\r\nand would be home in the morning, sure; and she would squeeze my hand,\r\nor maybe kiss me, and tell me to say it again, and keep on saying it,\r\nbecause it done her good, and she was in so much trouble. And when she\r\nwas going away she looked down in my eyes so steady and gentle, and\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe door ain\xe2\x80\x99t going to be locked, Tom, and there\xe2\x80\x99s the window and\r\nthe rod; but you\xe2\x80\x99ll be good, _won\xe2\x80\x99t_ you? And you won\xe2\x80\x99t go? For _my_\r\nsake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLaws knows I _wanted_ to go bad enough to see about Tom, and was all\r\nintending to go; but after that I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 went, not for kingdoms.\r\n\r\nBut she was on my mind and Tom was on my mind, so I slept very\r\nrestless. And twice I went down the rod away in the night, and slipped\r\naround front, and see her setting there by her candle in the window\r\nwith her eyes towards the road and the tears in them; and I wished I\r\ncould do something for her, but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t, only to swear that I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t never do nothing to grieve her any more. And the third time I\r\nwaked up at dawn, and slid down, and she was there yet, and her candle\r\nwas most out, and her old gray head was resting on her hand, and she\r\nwas asleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XLII\r\n\r\n\r\nThe old man was up-town again before breakfast, but couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get no\r\ntrack of Tom; and both of them set at the table thinking, and not\r\nsaying nothing, and looking mournful, and their coffee getting cold,\r\nand not eating anything. And by and by the old man says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I give you the letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one I got yesterday out of the post-office.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you didn\xe2\x80\x99t give me no letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 forgot it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he rummaged his pockets, and then went off somewheres where he had\r\nlaid it down, and fetched it, and give it to her. She says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s from St. Petersburg\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s from Sis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI allowed another walk would do me good; but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t stir. But\r\nbefore she could break it open she dropped it and run\xe2\x80\x94for she see\r\nsomething. And so did I. It was Tom Sawyer on a mattress; and that old\r\ndoctor; and Jim, in _her_ calico dress, with his hands tied behind\r\nhim; and a lot of people. I hid the letter behind the first thing that\r\ncome handy, and rushed. She flung herself at Tom, crying, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99s dead, he\xe2\x80\x99s dead, I know he\xe2\x80\x99s dead!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Tom he turned his head a little, and muttered something or other,\r\nwhich showed he warn\xe2\x80\x99t in his right mind; then she flung up her hands,\r\nand says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s alive, thank God! And that\xe2\x80\x99s enough!\xe2\x80\x9d and she snatched a kiss of\r\nhim, and flew for the house to get the bed ready, and scattering\r\norders right and left at the niggers and everybody else, as fast as\r\nher tongue could go, every jump of the way.\r\n\r\nI followed the men to see what they was going to do with Jim; and the\r\nold doctor and Uncle Silas followed after Tom into the house. The men\r\nwas very huffy, and some of them wanted to hang Jim for an example to\r\nall the other niggers around there, so they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be trying to run\r\naway like Jim done, and making such a raft of trouble, and keeping a\r\nwhole family scared most to death for days and nights. But the others\r\nsaid, don\xe2\x80\x99t do it, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t answer at all; he ain\xe2\x80\x99t our nigger, and\r\nhis owner would turn up and make us pay for him, sure. So that cooled\r\nthem down a little, because the people that\xe2\x80\x99s always the most anxious\r\nfor to hang a nigger that hain\xe2\x80\x99t done just right is always the very\r\nones that ain\xe2\x80\x99t the most anxious to pay for him when they\xe2\x80\x99ve got their\r\nsatisfaction out of him.\r\n\r\nThey cussed Jim considerble, though, and give him a cuff or two side\r\nthe head once in a while, but Jim never said nothing, and he never let\r\non to know me, and they took him to the same cabin, and put his own\r\nclothes on him, and chained him again, and not to no bed-leg this\r\ntime, but to a big staple drove into the bottom log, and chained his\r\nhands, too, and both legs, and said he warn\xe2\x80\x99t to have nothing but\r\nbread and water to eat after this till his owner come, or he was sold\r\nat auction because he didn\xe2\x80\x99t come in a certain length of time, and\r\nfilled up our hole, and said a couple of farmers with guns must stand\r\nwatch around about the cabin every night, and a bulldog tied to the\r\ndoor in the daytime; and about this time they was through with the job\r\nand was tapering off with a kind of generl good-by cussing, and then\r\nthe old doctor comes and takes a look, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be no rougher on him than you\xe2\x80\x99re obleeged to, because he ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\na bad nigger. When I got to where I found the boy I see I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t cut\r\nthe bullet out without some help, and he warn\xe2\x80\x99t in no condition for me\r\nto leave to go and get help; and he got a little worse and a little\r\nworse, and after a long time he went out of his head, and wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nme come a-nigh him any more, and said if I chalked his raft he\xe2\x80\x99d kill\r\nme, and no end of wild foolishness like that, and I see I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do\r\nanything at all with him; so I says, I got to have _help_ somehow; and\r\nthe minute I says it out crawls this nigger from somewheres and says\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll help, and he done it, too, and done it very well. Of course I\r\njudged he must be a runaway nigger, and there I _was!_ and there I had\r\nto stick right straight along all the rest of the day and all night.\r\nIt was a fix, I tell you! I had a couple of patients with the chills,\r\nand of course I\xe2\x80\x99d of liked to run up to town and see them, but I\r\ndasn\xe2\x80\x99t, because the nigger might get away, and then I\xe2\x80\x99d be to blame;\r\nand yet never a skiff come close enough for me to hail. So there I had\r\nto stick plumb until daylight this morning; and I never see a nigger\r\nthat was a better nuss or faithfuler, and yet he was risking his\r\nfreedom to do it, and was all tired out, too, and I see plain enough\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d been worked main hard lately. I liked the nigger for that; I tell\r\nyou, gentlemen, a nigger like that is worth a thousand dollars\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nkind treatment, too. I had everything I needed, and the boy was doing\r\nas well there as he would \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 done at home\xe2\x80\x94better, maybe, because it\r\nwas so quiet; but there I _was_, with both of \xe2\x80\x99m on my hands, and\r\nthere I had to stick till about dawn this morning; then some men in a\r\nskiff come by, and as good luck would have it the nigger was setting\r\nby the pallet with his head propped on his knees sound asleep; so I\r\nmotioned them in quiet, and they slipped up on him and grabbed him and\r\ntied him before he knowed what he was about, and we never had no\r\ntrouble. And the boy being in a kind of a flighty sleep, too, we\r\nmuffled the oars and hitched the raft on, and towed her over very nice\r\nand quiet, and the nigger never made the least row nor said a word\r\nfrom the start. He ain\xe2\x80\x99t no bad nigger, gentlemen; that\xe2\x80\x99s what I think\r\nabout him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomebody says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it sounds very good, doctor, I\xe2\x80\x99m obleeged to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the others softened up a little, too, and I was mighty thankful\r\nto that old doctor for doing Jim that good turn; and I was glad it was\r\naccording to my judgment of him, too; because I thought he had a good\r\nheart in him and was a good man the first time I see him. Then they\r\nall agreed that Jim had acted very well, and was deserving to have\r\nsome notice took of it, and reward. So every one of them promised,\r\nright out and hearty, that they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t cuss him no more.\r\n\r\nThen they come out and locked him up. I hoped they was going to say he\r\ncould have one or two of the chains took off, because they was rotten\r\nheavy, or could have meat and greens with his bread and water; but\r\nthey didn\xe2\x80\x99t think of it, and I reckoned it warn\xe2\x80\x99t best for me to mix\r\nin, but I judged I\xe2\x80\x99d get the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s yarn to Aunt Sally somehow or\r\nother as soon as I\xe2\x80\x99d got through the breakers that was laying just\r\nahead of me\xe2\x80\x94explanations, I mean, of how I forgot to mention about\r\nSid being shot when I was telling how him and me put in that dratted\r\nnight paddling around hunting the runaway nigger.\r\n\r\nBut I had plenty time. Aunt Sally she stuck to the sick-room all day\r\nand all night, and every time I see Uncle Silas mooning around I\r\ndodged him.\r\n\r\nNext morning I heard Tom was a good deal better, and they said Aunt\r\nSally was gone to get a nap. So I slips to the sick-room, and if I\r\nfound him awake I reckoned we could put up a yarn for the family that\r\nwould wash. But he was sleeping, and sleeping very peaceful, too; and\r\npale, not fire-faced the way he was when he come. So I set down and\r\nlaid for him to wake. In about half an hour Aunt Sally comes gliding\r\nin, and there I was, up a stump again! Dear,\r\ndear, out of his head again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_No_, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t out of my HEAD; I know all what I\xe2\x80\x99m talking about. Turn him loose! he ain\xe2\x80\x99t no slave; he\xe2\x80\x99s as free as any cretur\r\nthat walks this earth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _does_ the child mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean every word I _say_, Aunt Sally, and if somebody don\xe2\x80\x99t go,\r\n_I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ go. I\xe2\x80\x99ve knowed him all his life, and so has Tom, there. Old\r\nMiss Watson died two months ago, and she was ashamed she ever was\r\ngoing to sell him down the river, and _said_ so; and she set him free\r\nin her will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what on earth did _you_ want to set him free for, seeing he was\r\nalready free?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that _is_ a question, I must say; and _just_ like women! And I peeped\r\nout, and in a little while Tom\xe2\x80\x99s Aunt Polly shook herself loose and\r\nstood there looking across at Tom over her spectacles\xe2\x80\x94kind of\r\ngrinding him into the earth, you know. And then she says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you _better_ turn y\xe2\x80\x99r head away\xe2\x80\x94I would if I was you, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, deary me!\xe2\x80\x9d says Aunt Sally; \xe2\x80\x9c_is_ he changed so? Why, that ain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n_Tom_, it\xe2\x80\x99s Sid; Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94why, where is Tom? He was here a minute\r\nago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean where\xe2\x80\x99s Huck _Finn_\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what you mean! I reckon I hain\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nraised such a scamp as my Tom all these years not to know him when I\r\n_see_ him. That _would_ be a pretty howdy-do. Come out from under that\r\nbed, Huck Finn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I done it. But not feeling brash.\r\n\r\nAunt Sally she was one of the mixed-upest-looking persons I ever\r\nsee\xe2\x80\x94except one, and that was Uncle Silas, when he come in and they\r\ntold it all to him. It kind of made him drunk, as you may say, and he\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t know nothing at all the rest of the day, and preached a\r\nprayer-meeting sermon that night that gave him a rattling ruputation,\r\nbecause the oldest man in the world couldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 understood it. So\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s Aunt Polly, she told all about who I was, and what; and I had to\r\nup and tell how I was in such a tight place that when Mrs. Phelps took\r\nme for Tom Sawyer\xe2\x80\x94she chipped in and says, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, go on and call me\r\nAunt Sally, I\xe2\x80\x99m used to it now, and \xe2\x80\x99taint no need to change\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nwhen Aunt Sally took me for Tom Sawyer I had to stand it\xe2\x80\x94there warn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nno other way, and I knowed he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind, because it would be nuts\r\nfor him, being a mystery, and he\xe2\x80\x99d make an adventure out of it, and be\r\nperfectly satisfied. And so it turned out, and he let on to be Sid,\r\nand made things as soft as he could for me.\r\n\r\nAnd his Aunt Polly she said Tom was right about old Miss Watson\r\nsetting Jim free in her will; and so, sure enough, Tom Sawyer had gone\r\nand took all that trouble and bother to set a free nigger free! and I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ever understand before, until that minute and that talk, how\r\nhe _could_ help a body set a nigger free with his bringing-up.\r\n\r\nWell, Aunt Polly she said that when Aunt Sally wrote to her that Tom\r\nand _Sid_ had come all right and safe, she says to herself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at that, now! I might have expected it, letting him go off that\r\nway without anybody to watch him. So now I got to go and trapse all\r\nthe way down the river, eleven hundred mile, and find out what that\r\ncreetur\xe2\x80\x99s up to _this_ time, as long as I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to get any\r\nanswer out of you about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I never heard nothing from you,\xe2\x80\x9d says Aunt Sally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wonder! Why, I wrote you twice to ask you what you could mean\r\nby Sid being here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I never got \xe2\x80\x99em. Sis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAunt Polly she turns around slow and severe, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Tom!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94_what?_\xe2\x80\x9d he says, kind of pettish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you what _me_, you impudent thing\xe2\x80\x94hand out them letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat letters?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Them_ letters. I be bound, if I have to take a-holt of you I\xe2\x80\x99ll\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re in the trunk. There, now. And they\xe2\x80\x99re just the same as they\r\nwas when I got them out of the office. I hain\xe2\x80\x99t looked into them, I\r\nhain\xe2\x80\x99t touched them. But I knowed they\xe2\x80\x99d make trouble, and I thought\r\nif you warn\xe2\x80\x99t in no hurry, I\xe2\x80\x99d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you _do_ need skinning, there ain\xe2\x80\x99t no mistake about it. And I\r\nwrote another one to tell you I was coming; and I s\xe2\x80\x99pose he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it come yesterday; I hain\xe2\x80\x99t read it yet, but _it\xe2\x80\x99s_ all right,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve got that one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI wanted to offer to bet two dollars she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t, but I reckoned maybe\r\nit was just as safe to not to. So I never said nothing.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER THE LAST\r\n\r\n\r\nThe first time I catched Tom private I asked him what was his idea,\r\ntime of the evasion?\xe2\x80\x94what it was he\xe2\x80\x99d planned to do if the evasion\r\nworked all right and he managed to set a nigger free that was already\r\nfree before? And he said, what he had planned in his head from the\r\nstart, if we got Jim out all safe, was for us to run him down the\r\nriver on the raft, and have adventures plumb to the mouth of the\r\nriver, and then tell him about his being free, and take him back up\r\nhome on a steamboat, in style, and pay him for his lost time, and\r\nwrite word ahead and get out all the niggers around, and have them\r\nwaltz him into town with a torchlight procession and a brass-band, and\r\nthen he would be a hero, and so would we. But I reckoned it was about\r\nas well the way it was.\r\n\r\nWe had Jim out of the chains in no time, and when Aunt Polly and Uncle\r\nSilas and Aunt Sally found out how good he helped the doctor nurse\r\nTom, they made a heap of fuss over him, and fixed him up prime, and\r\ngive him all he wanted to eat, and a good time, and nothing to do. And\r\nwe had him up to the sick-room, and had a high talk; and Tom give Jim\r\nforty dollars for being prisoner for us so patient, and doing it up so\r\ngood, and Jim was pleased most to death, and busted out, and says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Dah_, now, Huck, what I tell you?\xe2\x80\x94what I tell you up dah on Jackson\r\nIslan\xe2\x80\x99? I _tole_ you I got a hairy breas\xe2\x80\x99, en what\xe2\x80\x99s de sign un it; en I\r\n_tole_ you I ben rich wunst, en gwineter to be rich _ag\xe2\x80\x99in;_ en it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncome true; en heah she _is! Dah_, now! doan\xe2\x80\x99 talk to _me_\xe2\x80\x94signs is\r\n_signs_, mine I tell you; en I knowed jis\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x99s well \xe2\x80\x99at I \xe2\x80\x99uz gwineter\r\nbe rich ag\xe2\x80\x99in as I\xe2\x80\x99s a-stannin\xe2\x80\x99 heah dis minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd then Tom he talked along and talked along, and says, le\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nthree slide out of here one of these nights and get an outfit, and go\r\nfor howling adventures amongst the Injuns, over in the territory, for\r\na couple of weeks or two; and I says, all right, that suits me, but I\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t got no money for to buy the outfit, and I reckon I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nnone from home, because it\xe2\x80\x99s likely pap\xe2\x80\x99s been back before now, and\r\ngot it all away from Judge Thatcher and drunk it up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he hain\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom says; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s all there yet\xe2\x80\x94six thousand dollars\r\nand more; and your pap hain\xe2\x80\x99t ever been back since. Hadn\xe2\x80\x99t when I come\r\naway, anyhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJim says, kind of solemn:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-comin\xe2\x80\x99 back no mo\xe2\x80\x99, Huck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Jim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNemmine why, Huck\xe2\x80\x94but he ain\xe2\x80\x99t comin\xe2\x80\x99 back no mo\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I kept at him; so at last he says:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoan\xe2\x80\x99 you \xe2\x80\x99member de house dat was float\xe2\x80\x99n down de river, en dey wuz\r\na man in dah, kivered up, en I went in en unkivered him and didn\xe2\x80\x99 let\r\nyou come in? Well, den, you kin git yo\xe2\x80\x99 money when you wants it, kase\r\ndat wuz him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s most well now, and got his bullet around his neck on a\r\nwatch-guard for a watch, and is always seeing what time it is, and so\r\nthere ain\xe2\x80\x99t nothing more to write about, and I am rotten glad of it,\r\nbecause if I\xe2\x80\x99d \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 knowed what a trouble it was to make a book I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t \xe2\x80\x99a\xe2\x80\x99 tackled it, and ain\xe2\x80\x99t a-going to no more. But I reckon I\r\ngot to light out for the territory ahead of the rest, because Aunt\r\nSally she\xe2\x80\x99s going to adopt me and sivilize me, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it. I\r\nbeen there before.\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE END\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE ADVENTURES OF HUCKLEBERRY FINN ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 32325-0.txt or 32325-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n    https://www.gutenberg.org/3/2/3/2/32325/\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one\xe2\x80\x94the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
